# 集注太玄经

**The Collected Commentary on the *Taixuan Jing***

**Title:** *The Collected Commentary on the Taixuan Jing*
Composed by **Yang Xiong** of the Western Han, annotated by **Sima Guang** of the Northern Song (6 volumes).
Supplemented by **Xu Han** of Shuanling (4 volumes).
Original source: *The Canon of Orthodox Daoist Teachings (Zhengtong Daozang)*, Taqing Section.

**Collated Editions:**
1. *The Complete Book of a Hundred Masters* (shadow-copied Song manuscript) – hereafter, *Quan Shu* Edition;
2. *The Four Branches of Essential Works*, published by Shanghai Zhonghua Shuju based on a Ming-engraved edition – hereafter, *Bei Yao* Edition;
3. *Ming Wan Yutang’s Reproduction of the Song Edition*, annotated by **Fan Wang** – hereafter, *Fan* Edition.

---

*Reading the Mysterious*

**Preface to *Reading the Mysterious***
When I was young, I had heard of the *Taixuan Jing* but had never seen it. I had only observed Yangzi’s *Self-Preface*, which praised the *Taixuan Jing* highly.

Later, when Ban Gu wrote his *Transmission*, he said: Liu Xin once observed the *Taixuan Jing* and told Yang Xiong, “You have only labored in vain.” Today’s scholars seek wealth and profit, yet they still cannot fully grasp the *Yijing*, so what can they do with the *Taixuan Jing*? I fear that future generations will treat it like an overturned jar of fermented soybeans. Xiong laughed but did not respond.

Some scholars criticized this, saying that since Xiong was not a sage, his composing a classic was like the rulers of Wu and Chu in the Spring and Autumn period, who usurped the title of king—an offense deserving of extermination. Gu preserved these words, and though his intent surpassed that of Xin, he did not acknowledge the *Taixuan Jing* as being as excellent as Yangzi claimed.

I too privately wondered why Yangzi did not endorse the *Yijing* but instead created the *Taixuan Jing*. The *Yijing* exhaustively encompasses the principles of Heaven and humanity. How could Yangzi surpass it, yet compose another book? Moreover, I did not know its purpose, so I also did not believe Yangzi was suited to author the *Taixuan Jing*.

Later, as I studied the *Yijing*, I found its profundity and obscurity difficult to grasp. I thought the *Taixuan Jing* must be a work by a virtuous scholar—simpler in meaning and easier in text than the *Yijing*. Those who ascend the Jiao Mountains must first traverse the lowlands; those who journey to the distant seas must first follow the Yangtze and Han rivers. Thus, I wished to begin with the *Taixuan Jing*, gradually progressing to the *Yijing*, hoping to attain even a distant glimpse of its depths.

After years of seeking, I finally obtained a copy. At first, its meaning was murky and indistinct, barely comprehensible. I then devoted myself to careful study, excluding all distractions, and read it dozens of times, comparing its beginning and end, gradually discerning its general outline. Then, sighing deeply, I placed the book down and exclaimed: Ah! Was Yangzi truly a great scholar?

After Confucius’ passing, who else could have known the Dao of the Sage if not Yangzi? Mencius and Xunzi are hardly worthy of comparison, let alone the rest. The *Taixuan Jing* reveals the manifest as it reaches humanity and the profound as it exhausts the divine; it encompasses the vast as it embraces the cosmos, and delves into the minute as it enters hair and thread. It unifies the Dao of Heaven, Earth, and humanity into one, scraping away at its roots to reveal its origin, nurturing all things as their primal mother. Like the earth bearing weight without sinking, like the sea yielding water without depletion. Though there may be those who practice the Dao in the world, none can surpass this in ease.

Examining it from the undifferentiated origin, the *Taixuan* was already born. Observing it in the present, the *Taixuan* is not without effect. Pursuing it to the end of Heaven and Earth, the *Taixuan* cannot be extinguished. Questioning it with the nature of all things reveals no gaps; probing it with the forms of spirits and gods finds no contradiction; aligning it with the Six Classics shows no inconsistency. Even if the Sage were to return, upon seeing the *Taixuan*, he would surely smile with delight, deeming it the fulfillment of his own heart.

Thus, it is clear that the *Taixuan* serves to complement the *Yijing*—not as a separate work competing with it. How shallow He Qin and Gu Xi knew it, yet how profound their misjudgment!

Some say: *"The principles of the Yijing differ from those of the Taixuan. Yangzi did not follow the Yijing but created his own system. How then does the Taixuan complement the Yijing?"*

Moreover, if the Taixuan shares the same Dao as the Yijing, then why have the Yijing at all? Why the Taixuan?

I reply: The hunter’s tools serve to capture game—whether by net or by arrow, what difference does it make? Books serve to convey the Dao: the Yijing is the net, the Taixuan is the arrow. What harm is there?

If one rejects the arrow to rely solely on the net, your pursuit of the Dao will be stifled. Besides, Yangzi composed the *Fayan* to align with the *Analects*; he wrote the *Taixuan* to align with the *Yijing*. If you abandon the *Fayan* yet wish to discard the *Taixuan*, are you not confused?

The Dao of the *Fayan* and the *Analects*—do they truly differ? The relationship between the *Taixuan* and the *Yijing* is the same.

When a great hall is about to collapse, one beam may prop it up, but nothing secures it like many beams. When the Great Dao is about to fade, one book may clarify it, but nothing illuminates it like many books. If a scholar can devote himself entirely to the *Yijing*, that is indeed sufficient. Yet the *Yijing* is Heaven itself, and the *Taixuan* is its ladder. Would you ascend to Heaven and abandon its ladder?

Earlier scholars have expounded the *Taixuan*, and their interpretations are numerous. Yet Yangzi’s writing is rich in exegesis, pointing toward depths so profound that the *Taixuan* itself is the most difficult to grasp. Thus, I doubt whether earlier scholars fully captured Yangzi’s intent. The world must have those who can truly comprehend it—I shall study it until old age.

---

**On the *Taixuan* and the *Yijing*:**
At their core, the *Yijing* and the *Taixuan* share the same Dao but differ in method.

The *Yijing* employs two symbols: Yang and Yin.
The *Taixuan* employs three: One, Two, and Three.

The *Yijing* has six ranks; the *Taixuan* has four tiers:
The highest is called *Fang* (方), followed by *Zhou* (州), then *Bu* (部), and finally *Jia* (家).
This is what the *Benzhuan* refers to as "threefold modeling and fourfold division," culminating in eighty-one.

The *Yijing* combines eight trigrams to form sixty-four hexagrams.
The *Taixuan* arranges One, Two, and Three across *Fang*, *Zhou*, *Bu*, and *Jia* to produce eighty-one chapters.

Each *Jia* chapter shifts with every variation—three chapters return to the initial state, as in *Zhong* (中), *Zhou* (周), and *Lian* (礥).
Each *Bu* shifts every three chapters—nine chapters return to the initial state, as in *Zhong* (中), *Xian* (闲), and *Shang* (上).
Each *Zhou* shifts every nine chapters—twenty-seven chapters return to the initial state, as in *Zhong* (中), *Xian* (羡), and *Cong* (从).
Each *Fang* shifts every twenty-seven chapters—eighty-one chapters return to the initial state, as in *Zhong* (中), *Geng* (更), and *Jian* (减).

Beyond eighty-one chapters, no further addition is possible—thus, it is called the Dao of Nature.

The *Yijing* has six lines per hexagram, totaling three hundred eighty-four lines.
The *Taixuan* has nine *zan* (赞) per chapter, totaling seven hundred twenty-nine *zan*.

The Diagram states: *"The Taixuan has two Dào—one arising from Three, one generated by Three.*
*The Three that arise: Fang, Zhou, Bu, Jia.*
*The Three that generate: the threefold division of yang-qi, forming three layers, culminating in nine camps.*
*This is the same origin but separate manifestation—the fundamental pattern of Heaven and Earth."*

The *Ben Chuan* states: *"The雄潭 contemplates the revolving Heaven, modeling it in threefold divisions and quartering it, culminating at eighty-one—this refers to the *Taixuan* chapters. Again, it says: 'Lateral divisions of threefold modeling and nine foundations, culminating at seven hundred twenty-nine *zan*—this refers to the *Taixuan zan*.' Chapters are akin to hexagrams, while *zan* are akin to lines.

Again, it says: 'Those who observe the *Yijing* name the hexagrams they see; those who observe the *Taixuan* count its strokes to determine it.' The *Taixuan*’s four-layered chapters are not hexagrams but numerical patterns. Thus, the *Yijing*’s six lines per hexagram each bear words, while the *Taixuan*’s four-layered chapters are supplemented by nine *zan*, governing what follows below. Therefore, chapters and *zan* proceed along separate paths, not interdependent. Both must be reckoned by days.

The *Yijing*’s hexagram *qi* arises from *Zhongfu*, and excluding the four cardinal hexagrams—*Zhen*, *Li*, *Dui*, and *Kan*—their twenty-four lines govern the twenty-four *qi*#9, while the remaining sixty hexagrams each span six days and seven fractions, totaling three hundred sixty-five days and one fraction. *Zhongfu*’s initial line marks the start of the winter solstice. *Yi*’s upper line marks the end of the great snow. Thus, it cycles and returns to its beginning.

The *Taixuan*’s eighty-one chapters, each with nine *zan*, total seven hundred twenty-nine *zan*. Every two *zan* combine into one day—one for day, one for night—yielding three hundred sixty-four days and a half. Adding the *Zhuolei* two *zan* completes three hundred sixty-five days and one fraction. The first *zan* marks the start of the winter solstice, while the *Zhuolei* two *zan* mark the end of the great snow. It too cycles and returns to its beginning.

All *Taixuan* chapters follow the *Yijing*’s hexagram *qi* sequence but vary in nomenclature. The first is *Zhongfu*; the cyclical return is *Fu*; *Li* and *Xian* correspond to *Lun*; *Shao* to *Qian*; *Li* to *Kui*. The rest follow this pattern. Thus, the *Taixuan* chapters declare: *"Eighty-one chapters, all affairs are divinely verified. The *Ce* says: 'Perform the *Xun Cheng* ritual, aligning with the Dipper’s encounter. The calendar marks the years, and the grains flourish in season.' All this is as stated.*

The *Yijing* has *Yuan*, *Heng*, *Li*, and *Zhen*; the *Taixuan* has *Wang*, *Zhi*, *Meng*, *Qiu*, and *Ming*. These five are the *Taixuan*’s virtues. *Wang* is the north, corresponding to *Zhen* in the *Yijing*. *Zhi* is the east, corresponding to *Yuan*. *Meng* is the south, corresponding to *Heng*. *Qiu* is the west, corresponding to *Li*#10. *Ming* is formless. Thus, the *Taixuan* text states: *"Wang and Meng reach their extremes, Zhi and Qiu command each other; emerging from Ming and returning to Ming, new and old alternate."*

Since the *Taixuan* begins at the winter solstice, it divides *Zhen* into *Wang* and *Ming*. *Wang* is the period after the winter solstice; *Ming* is before the great snow.

The *Yijing*’s *Great Correlation* number is fifty, with forty-nine in use. The *Taixuan*’s Heaven and Earth strategies are eighteen each, totaling thirty-six. Earth omits three, using thirty-three. The *Yijing* casts with four; the *Taixuan* casts with three. The *Taixuan* casting method: divide one hexagram in half, then use three casts per unit—one as *sou*, one as *huan*. Combine the remainder with *tiao*, and after one *tiao*, count the rest: seven as one, eight as two, nine as three. The *Yijing* has seven, nine, eight, and six—called the four symbols. The *Taixuan* has one, two, and three—called the threefold models. Both are the numerical foundations of hexagram chapters.

The *Yijing* has *Tuan*, the *Taixuan* has chapters. *Tuan* is the hexagram’s text; chapters likewise summarize the meaning of a single chapter. The *Yijing* has lines, the *Taixuan* has *zan*. The *Yijing* has symbols, the *Taixuan* has *Ce*. *Ce* interprets the *zan*. The *Yijing* has *Wenyan*, the *Taixuan* has *Wen*. *Wen* elucidates the Five Virtues, alongside the central chapter’s nine *zan*—akin to *Wenyan*.

The *Yijing* has *Xici*, the *Taixuan* has *Chui*, *Ying*, *Lian*, *Tu*, and *Gao*. These five expound upon the *Taixuan zan*—akin to *Xici*. The *Yijing* has *Shuo Guà*, the *Taixuan* has *Shu*. *Shu* discusses the symbols of the nine *zan*—akin to *Shuo Guà*. The *Yijing* has *Xu Guà*, the *Taixuan* has *Chong*. *Chong* sequences the eighty-one chapters, resolving yin and yang oppositions—akin to *Xu Guà*. The *Yijing* has *Za Guà*, the *Taixuan* has *Cuo*. *Cuo* interweaves and expounds upon the eighty-one chapters. Though paths diverge, they converge; myriad thoughts unite in harmony, all rooted in the *Taiji*, Two Principles, Three Talents, Four Seasons, and Five Phases, returning to Dao, virtue, benevolence, righteousness, and propriety.

#9 *"Kan"* originally *"Zhao"*, *"xiao"* originally *"zhi"*, *"zhu"* originally *"yu"*—corrected according to *Beiyao* and *Quanshu* editions.
#10 *"Li"* originally *"He"*, corrected according to *Beiyao*, *Quanshu* editions and *"Yijing* has *Yuan*, *Heng*, *Li*, *Zhen."*

**Annotations from *Ji Zhu* of *Yangzi Taixuan Jing Xu* by Song Zhong (Han Five Business Official):**
Song Zhong first composed *Jie Gu* for the *Taixuan*, Wu Yu Lin’s Governor Lu Ji authored *Shi Zheng*, Jin Shangshu Lang Fan Wang wrote *Jie Zan*, Tang Menxia Shilang Pingzhangshi Wang Ye annotated the *Jing* and chapters, *Ce*, Song Xing Du Guan Langzhong Zhi Zhao Wen Guan Song Weigan comprehensively commented, Qinzhou Tian Shu Yu Chen Jian authored *Yanxuan*, and Sifeng Yuanwailang Wu Mi wrote *Yinyi*.

「During the *Qingli* era, Guang Shi first obtained and read the *Taixuan*, composing *Du Xuan* ["Reading of the *Xuan*"].
Since then, he sought and gathered these several works, all of which he acquired,
and further wrote *Shuo Xuan* ["Explanations of the *Xuan*"].
Exhausting his vital *qi* and straining his spirit over thirty years,
he still could not penetrate their defenses.
Given the depth of his dedication, abandoning them seemed regrettable,
so he followed the *Fayan* ["Speaking of the *Fa*"],
and compiled the *Jizhu* ["Collected Commentaries"] for it.
Truly he did not measure his own capacity,
yet perhaps future scholars may find some use in his efforts.

As for those who simply say "Song," they refer to Zhongzi;
those who say "Xiao Song," refer to the *Zhaowen Langzhong*.

Written in the sixth month, *Dingchou* day, of the fifth year of *Yuanfeng*.

#11 *"Shi Zheng"* ["Explanation of *Zheng*"] originally *"Shi Shi"* ["Explanation of *Shi*"],
corrected according to the *Beiyao* and *Quanshu* editions,
and the *Jin* Fan Wang’s *Jizhu* on the *Taixuan Jing* (as per the *Ming* Wan Yutang’s edition of the *Song* text,
hereafter referred to as the *Fan* edition),
where Lu Ji expounds on the *Xuan*: *"Its lost aspects are rectified through elucidation"*—thus altered.

**Collected Commentaries on the *Taixuan Jing*, Volume One?**」

**Collected Commentaries on the *Taixuan Jing*, Volume Two**

One *Zhou* and two *Fang*, one *Shi* and three *Tong*:
The *Yin* *Shi* is wood, aligning with *Meng*.

The *Yang* *Qi* begins to emerge, and things take form but are yet unknown.
*Fan* says: At the *Lichun* node, all things sprout in their initial forms, branches and leaves not yet unfurled, hence called *Tong*.
*Guang* says: The *Yang* *Qi* faintly appears upon the earth, hence "begins to emerge."

**#11** *Zheng* [Explanation of *Zheng*]:
*Zhuan* does not awaken, meeting me in *Menghun*.
*Ce* says: *Zhuan* does not awaken, lest it remain utterly obscured.
One represents *thought at its beginning*—yet at night, the *Zhuan* person does not awaken to learning and ultimately returns to *Menghun*.

**#12** *Error* at the *Lingshi*, *Zhao* at the *Guizi*, emerging from mud and entering fat.
*Ce* says: *Error* at the *Lingshi*, *Zhao* at the *Guizi*—this compares to the *Guang*’s *Dao*.
*Wang*’s edition renders *Guizi* as *Yuanguiz*, and his note states: "Illuminated by the *Yuanguiz*, the auspicious signs of fortune or misfortune are drawn."
This is clearly a textual error. *Zhao* is synonymous with *Zhao* (illuminate).
*Bi* is pronounced *pinmei* (repeatedly).
Mud symbolizes stagnation; fat, the source of clarity.
Two represents *thought in its midst*—yet at day, though one can seek clarity in darkness, like divining with the *Lingshi* and *Guizi* to resolve doubts, emerging from stagnation and entering brightness, hence "comparing to the *Guang*’s *Dao*".
*Bi* also means "near." *Cuo* refers to the intricate divination of *shi* (yarrow stalks) to perform *shi* (fortune-telling).

**#13** The *Dongchen* shines, yet cannot act.
*Ce* says: If the *Dongchen* shines, why does it not proceed?
The *Xiaosong* edition renders *why does it not proceed* as *why cannot it proceed*, and I follow the other editions.
Three represents *thought at completion*—like the east where light has already dawned, and one could act, yet cannot, symbolizing those who fail to learn and lose time.

**#14** Sometimes it follows, sometimes precedes, the husband—
first bestowing its light. #1
*Ce* says: Sometimes it follows, sometimes precedes, the husband—
this is the *Guang*’s greatness. #2

**#15** *Meng* seeks the *Ru* with brushwood,
its gain is not beautiful.
*Ce* says: *Meng* seeks the *Ru* with brushwood—
its gain is not joyous.

**#16** Wide opening of curtains and screens,
to welcome guests from afar.
*Ce* says: Wide opening of curtains and screens—
this is surveying all the lights.
Curtains and screens are what obscures light.
Six represents *uppermost fortune*, and also *abundance*—
like a ruler receiving scholars from all directions, without concealment.
*Shun* hosted guests at the four gates, illuminating the four eyes, and attaining the four ears.

**#17** Cultivating the *Zhu*, comparing to the *Zhu* scholar. #4
*Ce* says: The cultivation of the *Zhu*—
there is nothing to be done.
*Zhu* is pronounced like *zhu* (stump).
*Xiu* means "long."
*Zhu* refers to a tall stature.
Seven represents seventy—age has grown long,
yet at day’s night, though tall in stature,
its wisdom compares to that of a *Zhu* scholar,
still not free from ignorance.
This symbolizes occupying the *Junzi*’s station yet following the *Xiaoren*’s *Dao*.

**#18** Sometimes struck, sometimes pierced,
cultivating its *Xuanjian*,
yet it fails.
*Ce* says: Struck and pierced—
this is decline from excess.
*Ce* (pierce) is pronounced *qi* (seven).
Not learning and thus being foolish,
leading to decay and old age,
falling into misfortune,
hence "sometimes struck, sometimes pierced."
Yet at day’s light,
if still capable of learning,
cultivating its *Xuanjian*,
it may yet transform misfortune into fortune.
*Jin Ping Gong* asked *Shi Guang*:
"My age is seventy, and I wish to learn—
fear it is already too late."
*Shi Guang* replied:
"Youthful love of learning is like the radiance of the sun at dawn;
Mature love of learning is like the brightness of day;
Old age love of learning is like the glow of a candle.
The glow of a candle—
who can compare to the darkness of night?"
*Uppermost Nine*:
The *Tong* deer collides with the rhinoceros,
its head is ashen.
*Ce* says: The *Tong* deer collides with the rhinoceros—
this is returning to its own burden.
The other editions render *burden* as *scenery*, but I follow the *Xiaosong* edition, pronounced *li* (force).
*Wang* says: At the extreme of *Tong*,
in the night’s position,
in the deepest *Menghun*,
those who cannot reflect upon themselves.
The *Tong* deer is a deer without horns.
*Guang* says: It is like being pulverized. #5
Nine is at the extreme of *Tong*, encountering the utmost misfortune.
Like the *Tong* deer colliding,
its head is crushed, #6
not measuring its own strength—
the depth of its foolishness.

**One *Zhou*, Two *Fang*, Two *Shi*, One *Jia* Added:**
The *Yang* *Shi* is metal, aligning with *Yi*.

**One *Zhōu*, Two *Fāng*, Two *Shì*, One *Jiā* Added:**

The *Yáng* **qi** flourishes and grows—
things thereby increase and multiply,
day by day spreading and nurturing.

The *Sòng* and *Lù* editions render *Yáng qi* flourishes and grows* as *Yáng qi* this flourishes and grows*, the *Wáng* and *Xiaosong* editions as *Yáng* this flourishes and grows*; I follow the *Fàn* edition.
The *Lù*, *Fàn*, and *Wáng* editions render *increase* as *adorned increase*; I follow the *Sòng* and *Xiaosong* editions.
The *Sòng*, *Xiaosong*, *Lù*, and *Wáng* editions render *while* as *like*; I follow the *Fàn* edition.

**First (hexagram) line:** *Hearing* (augury) *increase*—
growth in silence, unseen by others.
**Cè** says: *Hearing* (augury) *increase* in silence—
this is knowing the inner.
The *Wáng* edition renders *hearing* as *interval*; I follow the other editions.
*Silence* should be read as *silent reflection*.

One is the beginning of thought,
and should be marked with a stroke.
The virtuous person listens to the **Dao**/Way,
to increase their **virtue/power**,
and silently reflects upon it,
not revealing it outwardly.

**Second line:** It does not increase its **Fāng** (method/aspect),
but increases its brightness—
*obscured* (darkness).

**Cè** says: To not increase its **Fāng** (method/aspect),
merely to adorn the outer—
this is vain display.
**Fàn** says: **Fāng** is the **Dao**/Way.
*Obscured* is *dimmed*.
**Wáng** says: To not increase the **Dao**/Way,
yet outwardly boast and seek to enhance brightness,
one’s own light is obscured instead.
**Guāng** says: The virtuous person increases and refines the **Dao**/Way,
and honor follows naturally.
The petty person abandons the inner to adorn the outer,
seeking brightness while only growing more dim.

**Third line:** Wood halts its growth—
gradual increase.
**Cè** says: Wood halts, then gradually increases—
it cannot be covered.
The *Fàn* edition renders *covered* as *increased*; I follow the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and *Wáng* editions.
**Wáng** says: *Covered* means *concealed*.
**Guāng** says: The virtuous person’s learning,
like wood whose roots halt in the soil where it grows,
while its branches and leaves gradually lengthen.
The virtuous person halts at the **Dao**/Way they guard,
while their **virtue/power** grows ever new.
*The Law of Speech* says: Someone asks: “Wood grows gradually.”
It replies: “Wood that halts below and grows above—
is that not wood?
It is also like water.”

**Fourth line:** The *Yáo* (binding) is not overcome,
yet it may increase—
*wearing* it as a burden.

**Cè** says: The *Yáo* (binding) is not overcome—
this can lead to ruin.
Four is the position of *lowered fortune*,
and corresponds to night.
To enjoy fortune without **virtue/power**,
like one weak yet burdened with increase,
is to be certain of failing in their task.

**Fifth line:** The *Zé* (marsh) expands its form,
all nourished, their blessings shared alike.
**Cè** says: The *Zé* (marsh) expands its form—
this is greatness in humility.
**Wáng** says: Like the marsh spreading below,
where all nourishment gathers.
**Guāng** says: Five is the height of increase.
All share in enhancing it—
how else could it be so widely spread,
if not through humility?

**Sixth line:** A *red chariot* (augury) divides the *torch*—
one reading: *Increase me three thousand* (fortunes).
The virtuous rejoice; the petty grieve.
**Cè** says: The *red chariot*’s increase—
this is not fitting for the petty.

**Seventh line:** It increases its height,
sharpens its edge—
the *hill* (augury) stands firm.

**Cè** says: To increase its height and sharpen its edge—
this is the realm where both increase and decrease occur.
Seven is the upper body,
and the foundation for misfortune—
a place of dread.
The *Jiā* (line) nature is to increase;
if it increases without restraint,
it will surely suffer harm.

Seven should be marked as the *trigram* **Zhuì** (gradual),
the **Dao**/Way of the virtuous.
Thus, they can each time cultivate self-restraint,
to preserve their stability.

The reason a *hill* tilts is its sharpness.
If one can each time increase its height,
yet also sharpen its edge,
making it gentle and sloping,
then it will never tilt.
This is the *hill*’s true **Dao**/Way.
*Zhèng Kāo Fù*’s three commandments—
now even more reverent.
The first commandment, he bent his back;
the second, he stooped his body;
the third, he bowed his head.

**Eighth line:** It combines *shells* (augury) to serve—
benefiting in the going, harm in the coming.
**Cè** says: It combines *shells* (augury) to serve—
this is rejoicing now, ruin later.
*Harm* is pronounced *xuē* (to shave).
*Shells* are *wealthy resources*.
*To serve* is *menial labor*.
To use wealthy resources for menial labor,
greed without end.
Though benefiting in the going,
harm comes in the coming—
thus, it is rejoicing now, ruin later.

**Uppermost Nine:**
*Lofty and towering*, it does not collapse—
relying on the *supporting marsh*.
**Cè** says: *Lofty and towering*, it does not collapse—
this is the gathering of many virtuous.
The *Sòng* and *Lù* editions render *supporting marsh* as *yǐng yōng* (unclear), I follow the *Fàn*, *Wáng*, and *Xiaosong* editions.
*Lofty* is pronounced *cuī* (to depart).
*Supporting* is pronounced *yǒng* (two).
*Marsh* is pronounced *mǐ* (to conceal).
*Gathering* is pronounced *qú* (channel).

**Fàn** says: *Supporting marsh* is the *foot of the mountain*.
**Wáng** says: *Gathering* is the appearance of *support*.
**Guāng** says: Nine is at the extreme of increase,
encountering the utmost misfortune.
Yet it escapes blame,
for the many virtuous serve as its aid.
Like a high mountain that does not collapse,
relying on the *supporting marsh* as its foundation.

**One *Zhōu*, Two *Fāng*, Two *Shì*, Two *Jiā*, Two *Bù* Sharpened:**

**Yin Family Earth, measured by gradualness.**

**First Sharpness (Hexagram 5):**
Entering the fifth rank, thirty-one degrees and thirteen seconds;
responding to the *Rain Water* qi.
The Yang qi is lofty and sharp—
all things in their generation
are single-minded and undivided.
*Lofty* (*cén*): pronounced *chú zān* (plow-pin).
*Lofty* (*cénrán*): the appearance of sharpness.
The Dao of sharpness values singularity.

**First line (yín):**
The crab’s encircling retreat,
later the earthworm drinks from the Yellow Spring.
**Commentary:** The crab’s encircling retreat—
its heart is not singular.
**Fàn** says: *Encircling retreat*—the form of many legs.
**Wáng** says: *Encircling retreat*—encompassing abundance.
**Wú** says: *Encompassing abundance*—the appearance of restless agitation.
**Guāng** cites *Xúnzǐ*:
*The earthworm lacks sharp claws or strong bones,
yet it eats the dust above and drinks from the Yellow Spring below—
all because its mind is singular.*
The crab walks on six legs yet wields two claws,
and without the holes of snakes and tortoises has nowhere to shelter—
its mind is restless.
One is the beginning of thought,
and corresponds to night,
when family nature is sharp,
thus this symbol arises.
*Dì* (kneel): *qù wěi* (abandoned), meaning *foot*.

**Second line (yáng):**
Sharp and singular—
nothing it does not reach.
**Commentary:** The reach of sharp singularity—
it must grasp the Dao.
Two is the midpoint of thought,
and corresponds to day,
thus it is said: *Sharp and singular—
nothing it does not reach.*
All possess one virtue:
*"Virtue is singular—
in action, nothing is unauspicious;
virtue is two or three—
in action, nothing is auspicious."*
**Xúnzǐ** says:
*"He who walks the crossroads does not arrive,
he who serves two lords is not contained.
The eye does not see two things and yet is clear,
the ear does not hear two things and yet is keen.
The flying snake has no feet,
the five-skilled dormouse is exhausted.
The *Shī* says:*
*The cuckoo in the mulberry,
its seven younglings.
The virtuous gentleman’s bearing is singular—
his bearing is singular,
his heart is knotted.*
Thus the gentleman binds himself to singularity."*

**Third line (yín):**
Mad sharpness—
drift.
**Commentary:** The drift of mad sharpness—
it cannot dwell in singularity.
Three is the end of thought,
and corresponds to night.
*Mad*—to advance without singularity.
Drifting without foundation,
success is unseen.
*The Yì* says:
*"Advancing like a weasel-mouse—
perseverance brings peril."*

**Fourth line (yáng):**
Sharp in season—
nothing is unprofitable.
**Commentary:** Sharp in season—
it finds its due.
Four is the beginning of fortune,
and corresponds to the *huà* (drawing),
for sharpness that meets its time—
thus nothing is unprofitable.

**Fifth line (yín):**
Sharp to the east, forgetting the west;
seeing its back, not seeing its heart.
**Commentary:** Sharp to the east, forgetting the west—
it cannot turn aside.
*Back*—the exterior.
*Heart*—the interior.
Five is peak fortune,
and corresponds to night,
when the petty man knows gain but not loss,
sees profit without heeding harm,
greedily pursues the front while abandoning the rear,
discards the interior to chase the exterior.

**Sixth line (yáng):**
Sharp at *chǒu* (the multitude),
containing the five tracks and ten thousand *zhòng*,
persevering.
**Commentary:** Sharp at *chǒu*—
fortune and rank are boundless.
*Chǒu*—the multitude.
Six is supreme fortune,
yet also extreme abundance,
corresponding to the *huà* (drawing),
for the sharpness of its beauty.
The gentleman’s advancement
seeks only to harmonize the multitude—
thus it can contain the five tracks and ten thousand *zhòng*
without losing its rectitude.
In ancient times, *xú* was measured by tracks.
*A track*—the space between two wheel ruts,
eight *chǐ* wide.
Ten *dù* make a *zhòng*.
*A zhòng*—six *hú* and four *dòu*.
Five tracks—explaining breadth;
ten thousand *zhòng*—discussing multitude.

**Seventh line (yín):**
Sharp at profit—
shame and evil arrive.
**Commentary:** Sharp at profit—
humiliation lies in one direction.
**Fàn** says: *Shame*—humiliation.
**Guāng** says: Seven is the beginning of calamity,
and corresponds to night,
when the petty man, sharp at profit,
incurs humiliation and evil.
*Direction*—toward.
Humiliation lies in one direction—
speaking of how one incurs shame
through a single pursuit of profit,
without considering righteousness.

**Eighth line (yáng):**
Sharpening its sharpness,
rescuing its failures.
**Commentary:** Sharpening its sharpness—
fearing it may turn to disaster.
Eight is the midpoint of calamity,
and corresponds to day,
when the gentleman, seeing gain, considers righteousness,
looks ahead while looking back,
though sharpening its sharpness,
yet always rescues its failures—
thus it avoids disaster and blame.

**Ninth line (yáng):**
Lofty and precipitous cliffs—
*tuò*.
**Commentary:** Lofty and precipitous cliffs—
sharpness at its extreme must collapse.
*Tuò*: *zhí ěr* (straight).
**Fàn** says: *Tuò*—to fall.
*Jīng*—referring to *jīngróng* (towering).
*Qiào*—steep.
Towering and steep, about to fall below—
thus it is said *tuò*.

**One *Fāng*, Two *Zhōu*, Two *Bù*, Three *Jiā* Reach.**
**Yang Family Water #8, measured by *Tài*.**

**Initial One (yīn):**
Entering at the first day—
**She East Wall.**
Yang-qi branches, sprouts, and tendrils emerge—
All things attain their reach.

**Song** says:
What branches off is called *méi*,
What sprouts from *méi* is called *tiáo*.
Speaking of yang-qi stirring outward—
All things receive their principle,
None entangled by hooks yet fail to reach.

**Lu** says:
*Branches, sprouts, tendrils emerge*—#9
This speaks of yang’s boundless dissemination,
None left unpenetrated.

**Initial One (yáng):**
Midnight’s lone clarity—
Piercing, unyielding.
**Commentary:** Midnight’s lone clarity—
Inner illumination without bounds.
*Jiǒng*—*jiǒngtú nòngqiè*.
**Fan** says: *Jiǒng*—to traverse.
*Qū*—to exhaust.
**Guāng** says: One—
The subtlest of thought,
Thus called *midnight’s clarity*.
At the day’s dawn,
The gentleman’s inner light is silent yet knowing,
Piercing all directions without bound.

**Second Two:**
Confused in the belly, clarity in the eyes.
**Commentary:** Confused in the belly, clarity in the eyes—
Because the Dao is not illuminated.
**Song and Lu** originally said "not illuminated by the Dao,"
Now following **Fan**.
**Guāng** says: Two—
The midpoint of thought,
Yet at night,
Inner clarity obscured,
Then outward sight is also unexamined.
Thus, failing to illuminate the Dao,
Yet relying on external perception—
No benefit.
*Yǐ*—to use.
Why confused in the belly, clear in the eyes?
Because the Dao’s use is not illuminated.

**Third Three:**
Sapling wood, its tendrils flowing—
Its virtue may reach to the gourd’s calyx.
**Commentary:** Sapling wood, its tendrils flowing—
Inner benevolence measuring outward.
*Liàng*—*lì zhāng qiè*.
**Fan** says: *Wéiliú*—branches and sprouts descending.
The gourd’s calyx clings to the earth;
If wood does not lower its tendrils,
Then it cannot be bound and led upward.
**Guāng** says: *Bāo*—same as *páo*.
Three—
The advancing person,
Near fortune, at day’s dawn.
The virtuous: what one wishes to establish, one establishes for others;
What one wishes to reach, one reaches for others—
Not hoarding its beauty.
Like wood lowering its branches to reach below,
Thus gourds and calyxes are bound and led upward.
The *Odes* say:
*"South stands the twisted wood,
Sweet gourds hang in clusters."*

**Fourth Four:**
Small gain, small reach—
Great confusion, flat and flat—
No salvation.
**Commentary:** Small reach, great confusion—
Lone clarity in a corner and a direction.
*Biān*—*bì miǎn qiè*.
**Lu** says: Lone clarity in a corner and a direction—
Speaking of not knowing the four-way reach.
**Guāng** says: *Biānbiān*—narrow and confined.
Four—
Low fortune, at night,
Thus small gain.
The petty man knows only a corner and a direction,
Not the great Dao,
His gains are narrow,
Unable to save what he loses.
**Pénchéng Kuò** served in Qi;
Mencius knew he would die.
Saying: *"His talents are small,
He has not heard the gentleman’s great Dao—
Enough to kill his body, but nothing more."*

**Fifth Five:**
Reaching the central thoroughfare—
Great and small, no confusion.
**Commentary:** Reaching the central thoroughfare—
The Dao’s four-way passage.
Five—
Central harmony, also clarity,
At day’s dawn,
The peak of reach.

**Six Six:**
Great reach, no boundaries—
Do not neglect ditches and levees—
**Opposition.**
**Commentary:** Great reach, no boundaries—
Cannot follow one-sidedly.
*Zhěn*—*zhī rén qiè*.
Six—
Extreme greatness, past the midpoint, at night,
Thus *great reach, no boundaries*.
*Zhěn*—field boundaries.
*Xù*—what marks field boundaries.
*Zuò*—to govern.
The gentleman’s Dao must have altars and halls.
Like vast fields without boundaries—
If levees and ditches are not maintained,
Governance within becomes neglected.
Cannot follow one-sidedly—
Fields are vast; this way loses that.

**Seventh Seven:**
Reaching to acupuncture and incision—
What precedes is lost, what follows is relied upon.
**Commentary:** Reaching to acupuncture and incision—
Ultimately not abandoned.
*Biān*—*bǐ yàn qiè*.
**Wang** says: Though there be the pain of acupuncture and incision,
Ultimately gain the cure’s benefit.
*Lài*—benefit.
**Guāng** says: *Biān*—stone used to probe disease.
Seven—
The knife, also the start of calamity,
At day’s dawn.
The gentleman reaches to changes—
Knowing calamity’s approach,
Severing attachments, discarding evil,
Like acupuncture and incision removing disease.
Though some is lost,
Later gains its benefit—
Not a wasted cure.

**Eighth Eight:**
Confused in the eyes, clear in the belly.
**Commentary:** Confused in the eyes, clear in the belly—
Outer deception of the inner.
Two is in the inner body,
Thus *clear in the belly*.
Eight is in the outer body,
Thus *confused in the eyes*.
Deceived by external things,
With inner clarity,
Though the heart knows it false,
Yet cannot restrain itself—
Thus it ends in ruin.

**Ninth Nine:**
Reaching to blame—
**Authentic, ending in praise.**
**Commentary:** Reaching to blame, ending in praise—
Withdrawing well by the Dao.
Nine—
Calamity’s end, at day’s dawn.
The gentleman knows calamity’s extremity,
Guarding correctness and withdrawing,
Not losing his good name.

---
**One *Fāng*, Two *Zhōu*, Three *Bù*, One *Jiā* Exchange**
**Yin Family Fire**, also measured by *Tài*.
Yang exchanges with yin, yin with yang.
Things ascend to the Hall of Clarity—
*Yūyū huánghuáng*.
**Small Song** originally: *"Yin and yang exchange in harmony,
Endless grace."*
Now following other editions.
*Yū*—sounds like *yù*.
**Song** says: At the seventh division, the hexagram is *Tài*.
Ascending yang at Three—
Already above the earth.
**Lu** says: Below the earth is the Yellow Palace,
Thus above the earth is the Hall of Clarity.
*Yūyū huánghuáng*—restful and beautiful.
**Wang** says: *Yūyū huánghuáng*—the appearance of brilliant clarity.

**Initial One (yīn):**
Dark exchange with the spirit—
Not solemn in its authenticity.
**Commentary:** Dark exchange, not authentic.
Holding deceit, containing shame.
*Sī*—same as *zhāi*.
**Fan** says: *Míng*—obscurity.
Exchanging with ghosts and spirits,
Must be solemn and respectful.
*Sī*—fasting.
*Zhēn*—sincerity.
Exchanging with ghosts and spirits,
Though in darkness,
Without sincerity,
The spirits do not bless.
**Guāng** says: One—
The beginning of thought,
At night,
Thus this symbol.

「Second Two: Dark exchange with sincerity, bright and clear.
**Xie** says: Sincere dark exchange—trustworthy communion with the numinous.
Two is the midpoint of thought#14 and corresponds to day;
The virtuous one can thus exchange with ghosts and spirits in bright sincerity.

Third Three: Exchange with wood and stone.
**Ce** says: Exchange with wood and stone—unable to benefit others.
Three is the completion of intent#15, the first external exchange, occurring at nightfall.
To exchange with the foolish as with wood and stone—
No mutual benefit can be gained.
**Confucius** said: Do not befriend those not as good as yourself.

Fourth Four: Difficult to approach, reeking with incense, entering the gate of loss.
**Ce** says: Coming and going with incense—conducting exchanges with the spirits.
Wang’s original text reads *fú* (福) for *shén* (神), now following other editions.
**Wang** says: *Xūnxūn*—the appearance of multitude.

Fifth Five: Exchange with parrots and gibbons, gaining no honor.
**Ce** says: Exchange with parrots and gibbons—aligning with beasts.
Parrots, though speaking, do not depart from birds;
Gibbons, though speaking, do not depart from beasts.
Five occupies the position of maturity, yet corresponds to night#18—
Exchanging with things without propriety.
To seek honor without ritual—how can it be attained?#19
The *Shijing* says#20:
*"The rhinoceros horn cup is filled,
Sweet wine, thoughts of gentleness.
Their exchange was not arrogant,
Ten thousand blessings come seeking."*

Sixth Six: The great feast is vast and splendid, the small feast exchanges within it.
I have sacred viands, and I share them with you.
**Ce** says: The exchange of great and small—awaiting the virtuous with radiant brilliance.
The original editions of Song, Lu, and Wang read *gégé* (阁阁) for *hónghóng* (闳闳); now following Fan’s edition.
Fan and Wang’s editions read *xiǎo* (殽) for *yáo* (肴); now following Song, Lu, and Wang’s editions.
Fan’s edition sometimes reads *sàn* (散) for *xiǎo* (殽).
*Quān* (圈) is pronounced *qiú* (求晚切).
**Wang** says: *Quān*—the vessel for prepared dishes.
The way of awaiting the worthy shines brightly—
The peak of exchange.
**Guang** says: *Quān*—nurturing and storing leisurely.
*Hónghóng*—vast in appearance.
*Líng*—excellent.
*Yáo*—the body of the offering.
The character *xiǎo* (殽) should be *yáo* (肴).
*Yáo*—to eat.
The great feast speaks to the wealthy virtuous,
The small feast to those without rank.
Six is abundance and corresponds to the evening.
The virtuous, with their blessings, rejoice to share with the worthy.
The *Yijing* says: *"I have fine wine, I share it with you."*

Seventh Seven: Exchange with birds and mice, depleting the millet.
**Ce** says: Exchange with birds and mice—mere deception and waste.
Seven is ruin and corresponds to night.
To exchange with the wrong people—only waste remains.
The *Fayan* says: *"Frequent associations,
Worse than [blank]#24,
This is but robbing praise and food."*

Eighth Eight: Spear and halberd coming and going, with sincerity—no regret.
**Ce** says: Spear and halberd coming and going—military action cannot be abandoned.
Eight is the midpoint of calamity; exchanges not made with benevolence but with arms,
Thus it is said: spear and halberd coming and going.
Yet it corresponds to the evening of the day—
The virtuous use arms to suppress chaos and curb tyranny,
Employing them only when unavoidable.
Not deviating from the proper way, hence no regret.
The rod and flog cannot be relaxed in the home,
Punishments cannot be abolished in the state,
Military campaigns cannot be halted under Heaven—
Their use has beginnings and ends, their conduct has order and sequence.

Upper Nine: Exchange in warfare#21—unauthentic.
Overthrown at the city, fierce and devouring.
**Ce** says: Exchange in warfare#22—how can it be pursued?
Nine is the extremity of calamity, corresponding to night.
The petty exchange in victorious warfare#23,
Not in accordance with the proper way—
Those who overturn states and ruin families.
Attacking *Jié* with *Jié*#25,
Their virtue not differing,
Then only the fierce devour the weak—
No superiority or inferiority exists.
One region, two states, three departments, two families—
*Rǎn* (耎), same as *ruǎn* (软).»

The **Yang** family’s wood, aligned with *Xū* (需).
Entering *Rǎn* (耎) at the first day, called **"Sheltering Kuí"** (舍奎).
Entering the second nine, eight *fēn* twenty seconds, called **"Subsequent Descent of Lóu"** (次降娄).

At the **Awakening of Insects** (*Jǐngzhé*), the Dipper (*Fùxiàng*) aligns with the *Mǎo* position, the *Lǜ* (律) resonates with *Jiá Zhōng* (夹钟).
The **Yang qi** can be firm yet yielding, can act yet rest, retreating when encountering difficulty.
Difficulty—this is the *dàn* (旦) cutting.
On the first day, the **red sprouts** are sharp and pointed, advantageous to advance by withdrawing.
**Ce** says: The red sprouts sharp and pointed—withdraw to move.
**Fàn** says: The red sprouts—when grass and trees first sprout their buds.
**Guāng** says: When buds first emerge, they must first curve and bend#24—like the virtuous withdrawing to advance themselves.

On the second day, **softening the heart**, illness arises.
**Ce** says: Softening the heart—there is no courage within.
The second is **thought within**, and it corresponds to night.
The family’s nature is softness, and its disease is the lack of courage.
The *Spring and Autumn Annals Transmission* says: *"Ren without martial virtue cannot achieve its purpose."*

On the third day, **softening the knees**, guarding its limits.
Though not unrestrained, ultimately there is no defiance.
**Ce** says: Softening the knees—the body cannot be unrestrained.
Knees—same as *xī* (膝).
Defiance—*fú* (拂), pronounced *fú-wù*.
**Wáng** says: Defiance—*lì* (戾).
**Guāng** says: The third pertains to the lower body, hence "softening the knees."
Though it cannot act freely, it ultimately does not violate the proper Way.

On the fourth day, **softening the throat**, three years without swallowing.
**Ce** says: Softening the throat, swallowing lost—timing and measure are missed.
Throat—pronounced *wā* (哇), *yú-jiā* cutting.
Swallowing—*nuò* (噣);

「Zhújiǎo qiè, yòu yīn huà.
Wáng yuē: *Wā*, hóu yě.
Guāng wèi: Sì wéi xià lù, yòu wéi tiáo chàng, ér dāng yè, kě yǔ ér mò zhě yě.
Kǒngzǐ yuē: *Yán jí zhī ér bù yán, wèi zhī yǐn.*

**Cì wǔ, huángjūn bù yàn, sì yú qìngyún.**
Cè yuē: *Huángjūn bù yàn, sì qúǒu-ǒu yě.*
Èr Sòng, Lù zuò jiù huò yě, Wáng zuò sì ǒu yě.
Wú yuē: [空缺], gǔ chóu zì.
Jīn cóng Fàn běn. *Qú*, yīn qiú.
Wáng yuē: *Jū zhōng tǐ zhèng, wéi nì zhī zhǔ, ér yòu dé wèi dāng zhòu.*
Huángjūn, wèi língzhī yě. *Yàn*, shēng yě.
Língzhī wèi shēng, jiāng dài qìngyún;
Tóng biǎo jiāruì, xū dài zhī yì yě.
Guāng wèi: *Qú*, pǐ yě.
Zhī bù shēng zhě sì qìngyún, shì bù jìn zhě sì míngjūn.
Jūn míng chén xián, xiāng pǐ-ǒu yě.

**Cì liù, suō shī shí, huò chéng zhī zāi.**
Cè yuē: *Suō shī shí, zuò tōng hòu yě.*
Zāi, yǔ zāi tóng.
Wáng yuē: *Dì jū guò mǎn; ér yòu shī wèi yù yè, kuí yú chǔ nì zhī yí, shì suō ér shī shí zhě yě.*
Guāng wèi: *Jūnzǐ jìn tuì xiāo xī, yǔ shí jiē xíng. Liù guò zhōng ér dāng yè, tuì suō hòu shí, zé zāi chéng zhī yǐ.*

**Cì qī, qū#25 qí jié, zhí qí shù, gòng suǒ kūn.**
Cè yuē: *Qū jié gòng kūn, nèi yǒu zhǔ yě.*
Qū, yǔ qū tóng. *Kūn*, yǔ mò tóng.
Wáng yuē: *Néng zhí qí xīn, zé wéi yǒu zhǔ.*
Guāng wèi: *Qī wéi huò shǐ, ér dāng huà. Jūnzǐ qū shēn ér shēn dào zhě yě, gù yuē qū qí jié, zhí qí shù. Jūnzǐ wài suī xùn shùn, ér nèi zhǔ zhèngzhí, zhí shì dào yě. Yǔ zhī gòng, mò qí shēn, ér bù biàn zhě yě.*

**Cì bā, jiā kūmù dīng chōng#26, zhèn qí zhī.**
Xiǎorén yǒu nì, sān què gōulǔ.
Cè yuē: *Kūn mù zhī zhèn, xiǎorén jiàn wǔ yě.*
Wáng, Xiǎo Sòng běn *zhèn* zuò è, Xiǎo Sòng běn *zhī* zuò kē, jīn cóng Sòng, Lù, Fàn běn.
Fàn běn *què* zuò xì, jīn cóng zhū jiā. *Kūn*, yīn kuǎn.
Shàng jiǔ, huǐ suō, wǎng qù lái fù.
Cè yuē: *Huǐ suō zhī fù, dé zài hòu yě. Jū wù zhī shàng, xiàn yú huò jí, qǔ néng huǐ ér zì suō, yóu bù shī dào yě. Gù wǎng zé suì qù bù fǎn, lái zé fù dé qí suǒ yě.*

Yīfāng èr zhōu sān bù sān jiā xiàn
Yīn jiā jīn, yì zhǔn xū.
Lù yuē: *Xiàn*, dài yě. *Yáng qì yǒu xiàn, kě yǐ jìn ér jìn, wù xiān dé qí yuàn.*

**Chū yī, míng zéi, jù tiān xiōng.**
Cè yuē: *Míng zéi zhī xiàn, shí wú jí yě. Yī wéi sī shǐ, ér dāng yè, xiǎorén bāo cáng huò zéi zhī xīn, bì shòu qí yāng.*

**Cì èr, míng dé, xiàn tiān chāng.**
Cè yuē: *Míng dé zhī xiàn, chāng jiāng rì yě. Xiǎo Sòng běn chāng jiāng rì zuò chāng jiāng yǒu rì, jīn cóng zhū jiā.*
Wáng yuē: *Yǐ dé xiàn shí, chāng shí bù rì ér zhì.*
Guāng wèi: *Èr wéi sī zhōng, ér dāng zhòu. Gǔ, jūnzǐ jī dé yú yǐn, ér méng fú yú xiǎn, chāng měi zhī zhì, jiāng wú rì yě.*

**Cì sān, xiàn hòu shí.**
Cè yuē: *Xiàn ér hòu zhī, jiě yě. Wáng zuò xiàn ér hòu shí, jīn cóng zhū jiā. Jiě, yǔ xiè tóng. Sān wéi sī zhōng, bù dé qí zhōng, ér dāng yè, xiè màn hòu shí zhě yě.*

**Cì sì, qū qí jiǎo, zhí qí zú, wéi yǐ xiàn gǔ.**
Cè yuē: *Qū jiǎo zhí zú, bù jì cì yě. Jì, lì yuē zhì. Cì, láng dá qiè. Sì wéi jiǎo wéi xià lù, ér dāng zhòu. Lù yě. Jì, yǔ zhì tóng, hěn yě. Cì, lì yě. Qū qí jiǎo, bù yǔ wù jiào yě. Zhí qí zú, xíng bù shī zhèng yě. Bù wéi hěn lì, kě yǐ dài fú lù yě.*

**Cì wǔ, dà jué jí yú gōng yōng, xiǎorén bì, xiàn kōng.**
Cè yuē: *Gōng yōng zhī jué, bù kě kōng dé yě. Wǔ, wéi gōng wéi zhái. Xiǎorén ér féng shèng fú, rú dà jué jí yú gōng yōng. Jué jí yú cāng, kě yǐ dé shí; jí yú gōng yōng, hé suǒ dài yě. Xiǎo dé bì ér wèi gāo zhī xiàng yě.*

**Cì liù, jù fú zhēn zhēn, shí yú jīn.**
Cè yuē: *Xiàn fú zhēn zhēn, zhèng kě fú yě. Jīn zhě, jiān gāng zhī wù. Liù wéi shàng fú, ér dāng zhòu, xiàn zhī shèng zhě yě. Zhèng yǐ dài fú, suī jīn kě shí, sǐ qí yú hū? Zhèng kě fú zhě, kě yǐ fú xíng, yǐ dài fú yě.*

**Cì qī, xiàn huò jiè jiè, xiōng rén zhī yóu.**
Cè yuē: *Xiàn huò jiè jiè, yǔ huò qī yě. Jiè jiè, pì xié zhī mào. Yóu, guò yě. Qī wéi huò shǐ, ér dāng yè, gù yǒu shì xiàng.*

**Cì bā, bù huò huò, xiàn tiān huó wǒ.**
Cè yuē: *Huò bù huò, fēi jué yóu yě. Wáng běn huó zuò zhì, jīn cóng zhū jiā. Yóu, yīn yóu, zuì yě. Bā wéi huò zhōng, ér dāng zhòu, jūnzǐ fēi zuì ér féng huò zhě yě. Tǎng shěn jǐ zhī dào, bù yǐ huò wéi huò, tiān dào fú shàn, bì jiāng shēng wǒ yě.*

The *Yì* says:
*"Constrained yet not losing its place—this is *héng*."*

**Upper Nine:** *Xiàn yǒu yǒu, tiān pū zhī sǎng.*
**Cè yuē:** *Xiàn yǒu yǒu zhī pū, zhōng bù kě zhì yě.*
*Pū, pǔ-bǔ qiè.*
*Wáng yuē: Yǒu zhě, jí bìng yǎng xiàng tiān.*
*Guāng wèi: Pū, jī yě. Sǎng, é yě.*
*"The petty man does not heed his beginning, hidden in the extremity of calamity, now only *yǒu yǒu* in wait for Heaven’s salvation. Heaven will but further descend calamity upon him—hence, *tiān pū zhī sǎng*."*

*"One region, three states, one department, one household follow—*
*Yáng family’s water, follow as it comes.*
*Yáng yuè, at the yuàn, at the zé, at the tián, at the yuè—*
*Things aspire with their feet.*
*Sòng yuē: Yáng qì yuèyuè, zài yuàn, zé, tián, yuè zhě, wèi qí gāo xià bèi yě, wàn wù yì qǐ qí zú ér suí zhī."*

**First:** *Rén yōu fēi zhī, yuè míng suí zhī, jī.*
**Cè yuē:** *Rì fēi yuè suí, chén yìng jī yě.*
*Fēi, fù yě. Jī, shǐ yě.*
*"One for the beginning of thought—hence, *yōu míng*.*
*The moon, newly past the new moon, follows the sun in secret,*
*Like a wife following her husband.*
*When the ruler acts, his thoughts first arise in mind,*
*And virtuous ministers respond in harmony without deliberation."*

**Second:** *Fāng chū xù xù, péng cóng ěr chǒu.*
**Cè yuē:** *Fāng chū péng cóng, bù zhī suǒ zhī yě.*
*Xù xù, rì chū zhī mào.*
*Chǒu, lèi yě.*
*"Two for the middle of thought, yet at night.*
*The petty man’s heart is confused, about to manifest externally,*
*Like the sun just rising, *xù xù* in radiance.*
*Wavering in thought, unsure of its course—*
*If toward good, good companions follow; if toward evil, evil companions follow—*
*Thus, *péng cóng ěr chǒu*."*

**Third:** *Rén bù gōng zhī, zì qiān cóng zhī.*
**Cè yuē:** *Rén bù gōng zhī, zìrán zhèng yě.*
*Sān wéi sī zhōng, yòu wéi jìn rén ér dāng zhòu.*
*"The Sage follows nature, spontaneously aligning with the good,*
*Without needing coercion or correction.*
*Zhèng, should be *zhèng* (correct)."*

**Fourth:** *Míng cóng bù zāng, yǒu nǚ chéng qí xuè, kuāng wáng.*
**Cè yuē:** *Wū cóng zhī wáng, hé zú péng yě.*
*Wáng běn péng zuò míng, jīn cóng zhū jiā.*
*Kuāng, yǔ kuāng tóng.*
*"The Sage cultivates virtue, and people follow naturally.*
*To wail for followers is insufficient virtue."*

*"If virtue is bestowed upon a husband and wife,*
*then they lose their mates,*
*and their desires remain unfulfilled.*

*The *Yì* says:*
*'The maiden carries the empty basket,*
*The scholar flays the sheep without blood,*
*No benefit is gained.'*

**Fifth:** *From the Water hexagram, the line of fullness.*
**Cè yuē:** *From the Water hexagram, the line of fullness—*
*It does not exceed its bounds.*
**Wáng yuē:** *Water flows downward by nature,*
*the principle of spontaneity.*
*Five, having attained its place at midday,*
*is the master of following.*
*Things that follow, like water in its channel,*
*the channel being *Kǎn* (Abyss).*
*Fullness in the channel alone,*
*without further transgression,*
*embodies the *Zhōng* (Middle/Moderate) *Way.*

**Sixth:** *Following their eyes,*
*losing their hearts.*
**Cè yuē:** *Following their eyes,*
*losing their hearts—*
*this is to seek excessive following.*
**Wáng yuē:** *Following the pleasures of ear and eye,*
*while neglecting the tranquility of mind and body.*
*Greatly indulging their desires,*
*this is the path of ruin.*
**Guāng wèi:** *Sixth, though central,*
*falls at night—*
*those who pursue external desires while abandoning inner virtue.*

**Seventh:** *Repelling their evil,*
*following their virtue,*
*as *xiónghuáng* (雄黄) transforms corrupt flesh into pure.*
**Cè yuē:** *Repelling evil, following virtue—*
*this is to rescue the dire.*
*Xiónghuáng* (雄黄) in *xiǎoshòng* (小宋) is *tiānhuáng* (赨黄),*
*pronounced like *xiónghuáng*,*
*now adopted from other scholars.*
*Seventh marks the onset of calamity,*
*yet occurs at midday.*
*The Sage abandons evil and embraces good,*
*like *xiónghuáng* (雄黄) purifying corrupt flesh and nurturing virtuous.*

**Eighth:** *Following what is not virtuous,*
*calamity flies unchecked.*
**Cè yuē:** *Following what is not virtuous,*
*calamity cannot be redressed.*
**Shòng yuē:** *It cannot be discerned or rectified.*
**Guāng wèi:** *The petty follow what is not virtuous,*
*and calamity arises swiftly—*
*unable to be pursued or rectified.*

**Ninth (Upper):** *Following the subtle and refined,*
*the ruler ascends,*
*then mounts the throne,*
*and reaches completion.*
**Cè yuē:** *Following the subtle and refined,*
*the ruler attains success.*
*In *Fàn* (范), *shēng* (升) is *dēng* (登),*
*now adopted from other scholars.*
*One province, three states; one division, two families advance [#27].*
*Yīn (阴) family—Fire,*
*aligned with *Jìn* (晋).*
*Entering the *Jìn* (晋) division,*
*the sixth line: *Day sheds light on Lóu* (舍娄).*
*The *Yáng* (阳) line advances,*
*and all things emerge in abundance,*
*opening clarity before them.*
**Wáng yuē:** *In *Wáng* (王), *yǐn* (引) is *chéng* (承),*
*now adopted from other scholars.*
**Shòng yuē:** *All things follow and flourish,*
*abundantly and harmoniously.*

**First (Lower):** *Obscurely advancing the *Pǐ* (否) hexagram,*
*it becomes the mother of retreat.*
**Cè yuē:** *Obscurely advancing the *Pǐ* (否) hexagram,*
*this is the retreat of perversity.*
**Wáng yuē:** *Losing the path of proper advance,*
*this is the root of retreat,*
*hence, it is called the mother of retreat.*
**Guāng wèi:** *First marks the onset of thought,*
*yet occurs at night—*
*those who advance covertly,*
*not by their true nature.*

**Second:** *Advancing with moderate restraint,*
*the great person sees alone.*
**Cè yuē:** *Advancing with moderate restraint,*
*restraint cannot be externalized.*
**Wáng yuē:** *In *Wáng* (王), there is no *kě* (可) character,*
*now adopted from other scholars.*

**Third:** *Madly rushing forward,*
*unable to walk the Middle Way.*
**Cè yuē:** *Madly rushing forward,*
*this is uncentered advance.*
**Wáng yuē:** *In *Wáng* (王), *kuáng* (狂) is *wǎng* (往),*
*now adopted from other scholars.*
*Three marks the end of thought,*
*exceeding the Middle at night—*
*the recklessly advancing,*
*hence, *madly rushing forward*.*
*Chaptered and chaptered,*
*losing their foundation.*

**Fourth:** *The sun ascends, suspended in the sky,*
*all things melt in harmony.*
**Cè yuē:** *The sun ascends, suspended in the sky,*
*the ruler’s Way flourishes [#28].*
*Fourth marks the onset of fortune,*
*yet occurs at midday.*
*The Sage’s virtue advances in abundance,*
*illuminating all without obstruction,*
*like the sun mounting to heaven [#29],*
*separating from darkness,*
*all things harmonized and made clear.*

**Fifth:** *Advancing with *zhuó* (欋) and *sū* (疏) restraint,*
*sometimes supported by staff,*
*sometimes by embrace.*
**Cè yuē:** *Advancing with *zhuó* (欋) and *sū* (疏) restraint,*
*this is governed by the *Zōng* (宗) [line].*
**Wáng yuē:** *In *Wáng* (王), *quán* (权) is *zhuó* (擢),*
*and *zhì* (制) is *chè* (掣);*
*in *Fàn* (范), *Zōng* (宗) is *Zūn* (尊),*
*now adopted from *Sòng* (宋) and *Lù* (陆) editions.*
**Sòng, Lù, Wáng yuē:** *In *Sòng* (宋), *Lù* (陆), and *Wáng* (王),*
*zhàng* (杖) is *zhī* (枝),*
*now adopted from *Fàn* (范) edition.*

**Sixth:** *Advancing with lofty clarity,*
*receiving blessings without limit.*
**Cè yuē:** *Advancing with lofty clarity,*
*its Way is circuitous.*
**Fàn yuē:** *Circuitous, meaning distant.*
**Guāng wèi:** *Sixth marks supreme fortune,*
*yet occurs at dusk.*
*The Sage’s virtue advances in lofty clarity,*
*receiving blessings without end.*

**Seventh:** *Advancing not by its proper means,*
*listening to blame clogs the ears.*
**Cè yuē:** *Advancing not by its proper means,*
*defamation spreads unchecked.*
*Seventh marks the onset of calamity,*
*yet occurs at night.*
*The petty do not advance by proper means,*
*but by false elevation to high rank,*
*defamation flourishes,*
*and their ears are sealed by it.*

**Eighth:** *Advancing into the depths,*
*the Sage uses a boat.*
**Cè yuē:** *Advancing into the depths,*
*using a boat—*
*this is to follow the Way.*
*In *Fàn* (范), it reads:*
*'Advancing into the depths, yet the boat is used,'*
*now adopted from other scholars.*
*The depths symbolize perilous difficulty.*
*Eighth marks calamity’s peak,*
*hence, *advancing into the depths*.*
*Using a boat,*
*the depths can be crossed;*
*relying on the Way,*
*difficulties can be traversed.*
*One province, three states; one division, three families explain:*
*The *Yáng* (阳) family—Wood,*
*aligned with *Jiě* (解)."*

**First:** *Upon entering the **Shí (释)**, three lines follow,*
*twenty-six divisions, eleven seconds,*
*the **Spring Equinox** *Qi* **responds.**
*The **Yáng (阳)** *Qi harmonizes with **Zhèn (震)***—
*dynamic, rounded, and warm,*
*all things under **Shí (释)** wither and dry,*
*while their shells are loosened.*
*Loosened is synonymous with **shed (脱)**.*

**Cè (测) says:** *Harmonizing with **Shí (释)**—*
*the **Zhèn (震)** *line is dynamic yet formless,*
*the **Yáng (阳)** *Qi is rounded and warm.*
*At the start, the first line is motion without name,*
*and success is attained.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Motion without name,*
*cannot be named.*

**Rán (范) says:** *Rounded, the form of **Yáng (阳)**.*
**Sòng (宋) says:** *Dynamic—*
*the motion of **Zhèn (震)**.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Warm—*
*the radiance of **Yáng (阳)** *Qi.*

*The first line: dynamic yet without form,*
*it is **Qiú (酋)**—*
*meaning **success (成功)**.*
*One marks the beginning of thought,*
*and aligns with **Hùa (画)**.*
*The Sage moves at the slightest stir,*
*nurturing all things,*
*while the common folk see only **success (成功)**,*
*yet cannot name its source.*
*Thus it is said:*
*"Dynamic yet without name,*
*it is **Qiú (酋)**."*

**Second:** *Motion at **Xiǎng (响)** and **Jǐng (景)**.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Motion at **Xiǎng (响)** and **Jǐng (景)**,*
*is not worthy of observation or listening.*
*Echo (**Xiǎng**) follows sound,*
*while shadow (**Jǐng**) follows form—*
*both are movements beyond one’s control.*
*The second line marks the midpoint of thought,*
*and aligns with **night (夜)**.*
*The petty follow others in their motion,*
*like echoes and shadows,*
*thus it is not worthy of observation or listening.*

**Third:** *Wind stirs, thunder rises,*
*following its lofty grandeur.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Wind stirs, thunder rises,*
*this is motion with purpose.*
*Original texts from **Sòng (宋)** and **Lù (陆)** read:*
*"Following its lofty **Zōng (宗)**,"*
*while **Wáng (王)** reads:*
*"Following its **Dào (道)**."*
*Now adopting **Rán (范)**'s text:*
*"Following its lofty grandeur."*
*The third line marks the completion of thought,*
*and aligns with **day (昼)**.*
*The Sage’s actions now manifest externally,*
*like wind and thunder nourishing all things,*
*thus their **virtue/power (功业)** grows ever loftier and grander.*

**Fourth:** *Motion at the hills and mounds,*
*losing the favor of allies.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Motion at the hills and mounds,*
*is to lose the peril of descent.*
*Hills and mounds signify height,*
*while allies signify the lowly.*
*The fourth line marks the beginning of fortune,*
*and aligns with **night (夜)**.*
*The petty’s motion seeks only elevation,*
*ignoring those below,*
*thus they do not escape isolation and danger.*

**Fifth:** *Harmonizing with **Shí (释)**,*
*the **Zhī (脂)** of **Yáng (阳)**—*
*the **Yí (夷)** of four lands.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Harmonizing with **Shí (释)**,*
*the **Zhī (脂)** of **Yáng (阳)**,*
*the people rejoice without bound.*
*Original texts from **Sòng (宋)** and **Lù (陆)** read:*
*"Harmonizing with **Shí (释)**,*
*the **Zhī (脂)** of the people,"*
*while **Wáng (王)** and **Xiǎo Sòng (小宋)** read:*
*"Harmonizing with **Shí (释)**,*
*the **Zhī (脂)**."*
*Now adopting **Rán (范)**'s text.*
*Rejoice is synonymous with **delight (悦)**.*
**Rán (范) says:** *Harmonizing with **Zhī (脂)**,*
*illustrating **moisture and accord (濡协)**.*
**Yí (夷)** means **level (平)**.*
**Guāng (光) says:** *The fifth line resides at the peak of moderate harmony,*
*and aligns with **day (昼)**.*
*The Sage, having attained their position,*
*spreads their **virtue/power (德)** to harmonize the four lands,*
*all of which become **level and joyous (夷怿)**.*

**Sixth:** *Shaking at the court,*
*losing harmony and rectitude.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Shaking at the court,*
*harmony and rectitude both perish.*
*The sixth line is within the second **tì (体)**,*
*near the fifth line,*
*the court is its symbol.*
*Indeed, **virtue/power (德)** softens the central lands,*
*while **punishments (刑)** awe the distant barbarians.*
*Using **virtue/power (德)** to embrace those near,*
*brings harmony;*
*using **awe (威)** to deter those far,*
*brings rectitude.*
*Now, shaking at the court,*
*loses its proper place,*
*thus harmony and rectitude both perish.*

**Seventh:** *Shaking with shaking,*
*unassailable,*
*cleansing their shame.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Shaking with shaking,*
*unassailable,*
*this is to dissolve shame without restraint.*
*The seventh line marks the beginning of calamity,*
*and aligns with **day (昼)**.*
*The Sage possesses **virtue/power (德)** of unassailable might,*
*none dare to insult them,*
*thus they can wash away their shame.*

**Eighth:** *Shaking at what is advantageous,*
*overturned, fallen, and dead.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Shaking at what is advantageous,*
*advantage and death go hand in hand.*
*The petty move at the sight of profit,*
*and fall into the depths of calamity,*
*profit and death accompany them,*
*yet they do not realize it.*

**Ninth:** *Now in prison behind the valley,*
*finally freed from bonds.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Now in prison behind the valley,*
*this is the release from affliction.*
**Sòng (宋)** and **Lù (陆)** originally read:
*"behind the **Pí (皮)**,"*
*now adopting **Rán (范)**'s text.*
*Freed is synonymous with **shed (脱)**.*
*Valley means **birth (生)**.*
*The ninth line marks the exhaustion of calamity,*
*thus now in prison.*
*Within **Shí (释)** and aligning with **day (昼)**,*
*it is behind the valley.*
*One province, three states; two divisions, one family explain:*
*The **Yīn (阴)** family—Metal,*
*aligned with **Dà Zhùang (大壮)**.*
*Metal signifies **repulse (拒)**.*
*The **Yáng (阳)** *Qi strengthens within,*
*able to repulse the **Yīn (阴)** and drive them back.*
*The first line: repulse within the good,*
*losing rectitude and law.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Repulse within the good,*
*the center does not **reflect (宵)**.*
**Rán (范)** originally read:
*"**reflect (省)**,"*
*now adopting the common texts.*
**Wú (吴) says:** *Reflect is synonymous with **resemble (肖)**,*
*citing the **Hànshū (汉书)** for "people resembling heaven and earth."*
**Sòng (宋) says:** *Reflect means **law (类)**;*
*law means **model (法)**.*
**Guāng (光) says:** *The origin of good and evil*
*lies in thought.*
*The first line marks the beginning of thought,*
*and aligns with **night (夜)**,*
*repulsing the good while admitting the evil,*
*thus losing proper rectitude and law.*

*The second line marks the midpoint of thought,*
*and aligns with **day (昼)**,*
*repulsing the evil while admitting the good,*
*thus **hidden rectitude (幽正)** is attained.*

**Second:** *Repulse within the evil,*
*hidden rectitude.*
**Cè (测) says:** *Repulse within the evil,*
*hidden rectitude is profound.*
**Rán (范)** originally read:
*"**rectitude and law (贞类)**,"*
*now adopting the common texts.*
*Hidden signifies*
*that which lies concealed within the heart.*

**Third:** *The hem repels the belt-hook,*
*it is **Yú (渝)**—*
*unrestrained.*
**Cè (测) says:** *The hem repels the belt-hook,*
*there is no means to govern it.*

**Second:** The *Song* and *Lu* versions read *"making and governing"* (制作制), while the *Wang* version reads *"drawing"* (掣). The present text follows the *Fan* version.
**Fan** says: *"Revolutionary leather belts are called **é** (鞶)."*
**Hook** (钩) is what attaches the writing brush.
**Wang** says: *"The **é** and hook are what bind one’s robes, yet they repel and resist—thus it is **Yú** (渝), a deviation from propriety."*
**Guang** says: *"The third hexagram resides in the lower body, hence it is called **shang** (裳).*
*The third line marks the lower-upper position and aligns with **night (夜)**—when a subject repels the sovereign’s commands, refusing restraint, change must follow.*

**Fourth:** *The completion of repulsion—*
*the **Zhen** of birds.*
**Cè** says: *"The completion of repulsion in birds—*
*the proper alignment of law and position."*
**Fan** says: *"**Bì** (毕) means **wǎng** (罔), i.e., net."*
**Guang** says: *"The fourth line marks the beginning of **lù** (禄) and aligns with **day (昼)**—*
*when the virtuous first attain their station.*
*With station comes the power to enforce law,*
*to distinguish right from wrong and restrain violence."*

**Fifth:** *The glue and lacquer dissolve,*
*the bow cannot shoot,*
*the horn and wood separate.*
**Cè** says: *"The glue and lacquer dissolve—*
*faith is not bound.*
*Shoot (射) also rhymes with *food (食)."*
**Wang** says: *"No bond is stronger than glue and lacquer.*
*If the horn and wood of the bow are repelled and separated,*
*how can it shoot?"*
**Guang** says: *"The fifth line embodies **xìn** (信)—*
*as glue, as lacquer, as bow and arrows.*
*Repulsion (格) signifies things resisting and failing to unite.*
*The bow unites horn and wood with glue and lacquer,*
*thus it can shoot.*
*The sovereign unites subjects with trust,*
*thus they can be commanded.*
*The fifth line, though a petty person in a position of power,*
*cannot bind things with trust—*
*hearts above and below grow estranged,*
*hence: the glue and lacquer dissolve,*
*the bow cannot shoot,*
*the horn and wood separate."*

**Sixth:** *The metal breathes, the stone melts,*
*the small departs, the great arrives.*
**Cè** says: *"The metal breathes, the stone melts—*
*beauty is greatness."*
**Fan** says: *"**Yì** (奕) means *great.*"*
**Wang** says: *"What breathes is metal, what melts is stone,*
*what is lost is the smallest,*
*what is gained is radiant greatness."*
**Guang** says: *"**Xī** (息) means *to generate.**
*To generate metal and melt stone—*
*the symbol of repelling evil with virtue.*
*The sixth line marks the upper **lù** (禄) and aligns with **day (昼)**—*
*when the Dao of the virtuous flourishes,*
*and petty men are diminished,*
*hence: the small departs, the great arrives."*

**Seventh:** *Repulse its precious kind—*
*the tortoise and jade bring peril.*
**Cè** says: *"Repulse its precious kind—*
*there is no means to defend oneself."*
**Jì** (緺) is pronounced *wā* (蛙).
**Fan** says: *"The tortoise is the seal, the **jì** is the sash."*
**Guang** says: *"The virtuous align themselves with virtue,*
*thus preserving their blessings and station.*
*The seventh line is the sash,*
*and the beginning of calamity,*
*aligning with **night (夜)**—*
*when petty men repel virtue*
*and refuse to receive it.*
*To repel virtue is to repel blessings and station.*
*Abandoning virtue and losing station—*
*what greater peril is there?"*

**Eighth:** *Repulse its firm **é** (鞶)—*
*the virtuous find their time,*
*the petty man—*
*worry and distress.*
**Cè** says: *"Repulse its firm **é**—*
*justice cannot prevail."*
*The *Wang* version reads **"pán" (盘)** for **é**,*
*but the present text follows the common versions.*
**[Tì] (忧否)** is pronounced *tì*.
**Uppermost (上九):** *Its eyes are girded,*
*its horns are raised,*
*its body is not bowed—*
*struck.*
**Cè** says: *"Girded eyes, raised horns—*
*self-inflicted harm."*
**[Jiāo] (矫)** is pronounced *jiāo*.
**Fan** says: *"**Pū** (扑) means *to strike.*"*
**Wang** says: *"Girded eyes are wide eyes.*
*Raised horns are high horns."*
**Guang** says: *"Repulsion (格) is the use of strength to resist.*
*The ninth line, at its peak, exhausts strength,*
*reaches the end of misfortune,*
*and aligns with **night (夜)**.*
*Petty men widen their eyes and raise their horns*
*to resist others,*
*not humbling their bodies,*
*thus they are struck by others*
*and harm themselves.*

*One region, three states, two departments, two households,*
*Yí (夷) and Yáng (阳) families,*
*earth of the **Zhǔn** (准) and **Yù** (豫) regions.*

**Entering Yí in threes, the sun lodges in the stomach.**
**Yí**—harm, also leveling.
If not harmed by things, then it cannot be level.

The old *Zhǔn* and *Dàzhuàng*—incorrect.
When **Yáng**-*qi* is harmed,
**Yīn** lacks remedy for **huì**,
Things then become level and easy.
**[Tì]**—pronounced *tì*.
**Huì**—pronounced *huì* (as in *hù-huì*).
**Sòng** says: **[Tì]**—to remove.
**Lù** says: When **Yáng**-*qi* is strong,
It harms **Yīn**, and **[tì]** removes it.
**Huì**—disease.
When harmed by **Yáng**, it is disease.
All things without **Yīn** harm are thus level and easy.

**Guāng** says: **[Tì]**—to shave the head.
The ancients call it *kūn* (for adults), *tì* (for children).
When **Yáng**-*qi* shaves and trims all **Yīn**,
**Yīn** cannot self-heal its disease,
Then things attain birth and growth, level and easy.

**First line:** *Zǎi yōu èr*, holding **Yí** within.
**Cè** says: *"Zǎi yōu èr, holding **Yí**—*
*ease within."*
**Fàn** says: *Zǎi*—beginning.
**Guāng** says: *Yī*—the start of thought, at day.
The beginning of contemplation, yet still hidden.
*Èr*—right and profit.
When the two contend, the virtuous take right and abandon profit.
Holding the **Tǎn-Yí** heart,
Nurturing the boundless *hé*-*qi*,
Self-attained within,
Seeking nothing from without.

**Zǐ Xià** said: *"I conquered in battle, thus I grew fat."*
*The Fǎyán* says: *"The joy of **Jūhuí** with gold in one’s embrace—external;
The joy of the **Yánshì** child—internal."*

**Second line:** **Yīn-Yí**, presuming at **Tiānwǎng**.
**Cè** says: *"**Yīn-Yí** presuming at **Tiānwǎng**—*
*loose yet not lost."*
**Lù** says: Though **Tiānwǎng** is loose,
It does not lose evil.
**Guāng** says: *Èr*—**wǎng**, again *èr*—thought’s midpoint, at night.
The petty conceal their guilt,
**Yīn** harmed by things,
Believing none can know.
Yet presuming at **Tiānwǎng**,
Heaven will surely punish them.
**Lǎozǐ** says: *"**Tiānwǎng** vast and wide,
Loose yet never misses."*

**Third line:** **Róu**, the infant’s cry—
Three days without **gǎ**.
**Cè** says: *"The infant’s cry—*
*harmony within."*
(Sòng, Lù, and Wáng manuscripts read *gǎ* as *yōu*; here following Fàn’s *gǎ*.)
**Hào**—pronounced *hào* (as in *hú-dāo*).
**Tàn**—pronounced *tàn* (as in *suǒ-jià*).
**Ài**—pronounced *ài* (as in *yú-qiú*).
**Wáng** says: *Yōu*—reversed *qi*.
**Guāng** says: *Gǎ*—voice altered.
*Sān*—fulfilled intent, at day.
The virtuous, thick with virtue,
Level and easy.
Like the infant, though three days of wailing,
Its voice unaltered—
Harmonious and yielding.
**Lǎozǐ** says: *"The newborn cries all day yet never hoarse,
Harmony’s peak."*

**Fourth line:** **Yí** its teeth,
Or gluttony’s retinue.
**Cè** says: *"**Yí** its teeth—*
*food not savored."*
**Yù**—pronounced *yù* (as in *jū-jù*).
**Wáng** says: Teeth leveled, nothing to eat.
*Tú*—empty, like void.
Some feed with deceit,
Yet gluttony’s retinue remains empty.
**Guāng** says: *Sì*—bone, tooth,
Also fortune’s start, at night.
The petty, greedy for office,
Self-harming—
Thus *"**Yí** its teeth."*
Teeth harmed, though fine food exists,
They cannot eat—
Only gluttony’s retinue is fed.

**Fifth line:** **Zhōng-Yí**, without fail.
**Cè** says: *"**Zhōng-Yí**’s utility—*
*its Way is manifold."*
(Sòng and Lù manuscripts read *"lì qí duō"*;
here following Fàn and Wáng’s *"qí dào duō"*.)
**Wáng** says: Level to govern things.
Where things gather, what failure could there be?
*Wǔ* at center, body upright, rightful—
Thus its level ease.

**Guāng** says: *Wǔ* at peak position, at day,
If one levels the heart to await things,
Then things, near or far, all turn to it.
*The Yì* says: *"Simple and unadorned,
And all under Heaven’s order is attained."*

**Sixth line:** **Yí** at the hut,
Its dwelling mound is empty.
**Cè** says: *"**Yí** at the hut—*
*its virtue lost."*
Virtue—the virtuous’ constant abode.
*Liù* past center, at night,
The petty begin to harm their virtue,
Lose their secure dwelling.

**Seventh line:** **Gānróu gānruò**,
Separating wood, **ài**, metal—**Yí**.
**Cè** says: *"**Gānróu**—*
*yield overcomes strength."*
(Sòng and Lù manuscripts read *"gānróu"* as *"zhuàng"*;
Fàn’s *"gānróu àijīn"* as *"ruò shèng qiáng"*#34.
Wáng’s *"gānróu"* as *"ruò shèng qiáng"*#35.
Small Sòng’s *"zhuàng gānróu"* as *"ruò shèng qiáng"*#36.
Here following Wáng’s *"gānróu"*, Sòng/Lù’s *"ruò shèng qiáng"*.)
**Ài**—pronounced *ài* (as in *yú-fèi*).
**Wáng** says: Though overfull,
Yet rightful, at the boundary,
Attaining **Yí**’s Way—
Thus yielding-weak things level the firm and rigid.

**Guāng** says: Separating wood—
Like drawing rope to dry a well.
**Ài** metal—
Like sharpening a blade on a whetstone.
Yielding overcomes strength,
Ultimately leveling.

**Eighth line:** **Yí** its horns—**lì**.
**Cè** says: *"**Yí** its horns—*
*injury through threat."*
The petty use threat and harm themselves—
A path to peril.
*Bā*—mid-calamity, at night.

**Ninth line:** **Yí** at **[ ]**—
Utility, reverent of sickness and age.
**Cè** says: *"**Yí** at **[ ]**’s *zhēn*—*
*retiring the chariot to the village."*
**Lù** says: *"Retiring from office to return to kin."*
**Wáng** says: Revering decay, sickness, and elders—
The way of *zhēn*.
**Guāng** says: *Jiǔ*—ninety, also extremity.
The virtuous, aged, decline from office,
Self-placing in level ease.
The worthy, through age and sickness, return—
What the ruler should revere.

**One region, three states, two departments, three households—**
**Lè**, pronounced *luò*.

The **Yīn** family’s water—
also governs **Yù**’s domain.

Entering the **Lè** sequence, seventh in rank.
Three parts, eighteen seconds—
daily aligned with **Dà Liáng**,
responding to **Qīngmíng**’s qi.

The Dipper stands at **Chén**,
the pitch-pipe tuned to **Gūxǐ**.

**Yáng** first emerges from the hidden depths,
layered things receiving harmony and softness—
all things rejoice and delight.
**Nào**—*nùjiào* (harmony).
At **Qīngmíng**’s onset, **Yáng** first stirs,
unfurling from obscurity.
Layered accumulations all attain harmony and softness,
thus rejoicing.

**First day:** Solitary joy, gentle and intimate,
reaching not far.
**Cè** says: *"Solitary joy, gentle and intimate—*
*licentious within."*
**Fàn** says: *"Gentle and intimate—*
*the appearance of solitary joy."*
**Guāng** says: *"One marks the beginning of thought,*
*falling upon night—*
*the petty man takes solitary joy in himself,*
*yet cannot share it with others."*

**Second day:** Joy unknowable#37,
**Chén** in heaven.
**Cè** says: *"Joy unknowable—*
*by season and year."*
**Lù** says#38: *"It means practicing virtuous governance,*
*bringing people joy.*
*Like the seasons,*
*making people unaware of its workings#39."*

**Third day:** Neither feasting nor solemn,
*guāyǎzé*, *hàotáo yǐhù*.
**Cè** says: *"Neither feasting nor solemn—*
*ritual and music abandoned."*
**Wáng**’s original text reads *hāo*,
now following the others.
**#**, ancient *diào* (five), *diào* (two).

「Yǎ, pronounced *eh* (as in "heh"). **Zé**, pronounced *zéi* (as in "zei").

**Wáng** says: *"Guāyǎzé*—all are sounds of joyous laughter."*

**Guāng** says: *"Yàn (feasting), is tranquility. **Yǎ (solemn)**, is correctness. The third day marks the completion of intention while in darkness—abandoning ritual and music, drowning in licentiousness and dissipation. Abandoning ritual, one cannot find tranquility; abandoning music, one cannot find correctness. Though they guāyǎzé, if they seek only fleeting, immediate pleasure, how far is their suffering from them? It lies just beyond the door, no farther."*

From the *Shī·Yúzhào* (Book of Poetry: "The Fishes and Reeds"), it censures King Yōu’s words: *"All things lose their nature, the king dwelling in Hào and Jīng, shall find no joy in himself."*#

The fourth day: *"To stroke their bonds, to sever their cords, to abandon their thoughts."*#
**Cè** says: *"Stroking bonds and severing cords—truly a heart at ease. Bonds, the same as *system* (系). Cords, pronounced *shuāng* (as in 'thread'). To stroke, is to depart. Cords, are the central threads in the weft of a net."*#
The fourth day marks *tiáochàng* (harmony), while in daylight. The virtuous man follows the Dao/Way, finding joy in forgetting his cares, external things unable to burden him, and none are more free than he.#
Thus it says: *"To stroke their bonds, to sever their cords, to abandon their thoughts."*

The fifth day: *"Bell and drum clamor—clamor—pipes and strings sigh—sigh—perhaps to follow its decline."*#
**Cè** says: *"Bell and drum clamor—joy followed by sorrow. Sigh—both pronounced *cuò* (as in 'wrong'). Sigh-sigh, the sound of fading thoughts."*#
The fifth day marks the petty man indulging in great fortune, unrestrained in his licentious pleasures. Joy to its extreme must bring sorrow; prosperity to its peak must decline.

The sixth day: *"Great joy without interruption, people and spirits, birds and beasts all find their delight."*#
**Cè** says: *"Great joy without interruption—none are left without embracing it."*#
**Dàn** (as in "charm"), pronounced *púgān* (as in "to float").
**Wáng** says: *"The sixth day resides in a position of greatness, at the time of daylight, as the leader of the group. Without interruption means that between heaven and earth, all things find joy, and people, spirits, birds, and beasts each fulfill their nature and float in delight."*
**Guāng** says: *"Dàn (float), is joy."*#
The sixth day marks *lóngfú* (abundant blessings), *shèngduō* (great prosperity), and *jímǎ* (ultimate greatness). The virtuous man of the *Tàipíng* (Great Peace) era benefits all things, with no distinctions—people, spirits, birds, and beasts all find their place, and what joy is greater than this? The fourth day marks *xiàlù* (lower fortune), benefiting only oneself; the sixth day marks *shànglù* (higher fortune), benefiting all under heaven.

The seventh day: *"Men laugh, ghosts laugh, heaven’s appointed term."*#
**Cè** says: *"Men laugh, ghosts laugh—celebrating the completion of joy."*#
The *Sòng* and *Lù* versions read *bēi* (low) instead of *bì* (completion); now following **Fàn**’s version.
**Yāo** (term), pronounced *yúxiāo* (as in "nightfall").

The **King** says: *"Yào (term), is yuē (restraint/limit)."*
**Guāng** says: *"Xī (laugh), is an exclamation of joy."*

The seventh day marks *huòshǐ* (beginning of calamity), yet it falls at night.
The petty man, when his joy reaches its peak, invites calamity—both men and ghosts exclaim in lament, unaware that the great decree is near its end.

The eighth day: *"Laughter and exclamations, self-fear, no guilt nor misfortune."*
**Cè** says: *"Laughter and exclamations in self-fear—ultimately self-preservation."*

The **King** says: *"The Dao of joy is nearing its extreme, yet one still lingers within calamity. This is the perilous path.*
Yet if one holds a position of day, excels in rectifying faults, then even amid laughter and exclamations of mirth, one can still self-fear—thus there will be no guilt nor misfortune."

The ninth day, highest: *"The brink of extreme joy, sorrow without waiting for the day to shift—then the resources for weeping arise."*
**Cè** says: *"The brink of extreme joy—truly something to repent."*
**Jī** (brink), pronounced *jī* (as in "machine").
**Zī** (resources), pronounced *chēn* (as in "anger"), is the swelling of flesh.
**Lù** says: *"Jī (brink), is danger."*
The **King** says: *"At the extreme of joy, yet still at night—sorrow comes immediately, thus the weeping and exclamations of lament.*
**Guāng** says: *"The three [days] warn against excess in thought; the five [days] warn against excess in fortune; the nine [days] warn against excess in calamity.*
The overarching principle is that joy cannot be pushed to its extreme, urging one to remain ever mindful and reflective throughout."*

---

**One family in three states and three departments contends for the Yang family’s fire—litigation.**
The **Yang** *qì* (qi) spreads evenly, neither partial nor biased.
Things involved in litigation each follow their proper order.
**Shì** (spread), pronounced *shì* (as in "style").
**Shī** (apply), pronounced *shì* (as in "sauce").
**Pō** (bias), pronounced *pǔhé* (as in "universal").
**Yí** (order), is what is fitting.
The **Yang** *qì* spreads evenly and balanced—all things vie to advance, seeking to fulfill their proper order.
