The *Shī* (Book of Poetry) says: *"By proper order, all things are born, each attaining its fitting place."*#41

The first day: *"Contend not to contend—subtle and hidden."*
**Cè** says: *"Contend not to contend—this is the Dao’s inherent nature."*
**Lù** says: *"Sù (subtle), is zì (natural quality)."*
The **King** says: *"Contend not to contend—this means to contend before things take form, unseen in their traces."*
**Guāng** says: *"The first day marks the beginning of thought, yet it falls at day.*
The virtuous man holds the Dao’s inherent nature, contending in the realm of non-contention. Externally, no trace appears, yet nothing can overcome him."*

The second day: *"Threaten the river—emaciated and shamed."*
**Cè** says: *"The emaciated shame of threatening the river—what can it achieve?"*
**Hè** (threaten), pronounced *hūjià* (as in "call off marriage").
**Zhuó** (emaciated), pronounced *qíqú* (as in "both together"), is lean.
**Zī** (shame), is the same as *hù* (rely on).
To reject others with words is called *hè*.
When the river overflows, who can reject it?
Yet an emaciated and shamed man, not measuring his strength, dares to threaten it with words—how inadequate is this?
The *Shī* (Book of Poetry) says: *"Return with your fierce threats."*#42

The third day: *"Contend in archery, earnest and earnest."*
**Cè** says: *"Contend in archery, earnest and earnest—this is the virtuous man yielding to his neighbor."*
**Fàn**’s version reads *qú* (earnest) instead of *yín* (archery’s earnestness); now following the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and **Wáng** versions.
**Shè** (archery), pronounced *shènyè* (as in "divine night").
**Yín** (earnest), pronounced *yín* (as in "silver").
The **King** says: *"The virtuous man’s contention is only in archery."*
**Yín yín** (earnest and earnest), is the appearance of utmost diligence.
To yield to one’s neighbor is akin to yielding in deference, and its meaning reads *yín* as *qiǎn* (earnestly).
**Guāng** says: *"Yín (earnest), is the same as qú (deference)."*
**Qú qú** (deference and deference), is the appearance of respectful yielding.
Confucius says: *"The virtuous man has no contention.*
If there must be contention, let it be in archery."
With bows and deference, ascending; descending to drink, its contention is that of the virtuous.

The fourth day: *"Contend for petty gains, not attaining sincerity."*
**Cè** says: *"If petty gains are unceasing, the proper path grows dim."*
The **King** says#42: *"Contend not by the Dao, yet contend for petty gains—it is fitting that one does not attain the path of sincerity."*#43

The fifth day: *"Contend in the thoroughfare, gains without measure."*
**Cè** says: *"Contend in the thoroughfare—this is contention at the midpoint."*
**Fàn** says: *"The fifth day is at the central position, hence called thoroughfare."*
**Dù** (thoroughfare), is the ninefold path.
**Guāng** says: *"The fifth day marks central harmony, yet falls at day.*
The virtuous man holds the Dao’s key, the convergence of governance, responding to changes without measure—nothing can contend with him."*

The sixth day: *"Arms and shoulders like calves, thighs and legs swollen—this is bodily illness."*
**Cè** says: *"Arms and shoulders like calves—this is the minister’s excessive power."*
**Wáng** and **Xiǎo Sòng** versions read *jìng* (calves) instead of *jìng* (shinbones), with the meaning pronounced *dié* (swelling).
Now following the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and **Fàn** versions.
**Fàn**’s version reads: *"Arms and shoulders like calves"*—now following the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and **Wáng** versions.
**Fàn** says: *"Branches grow larger than the trunk; ministers exceed their sovereign—both are illnesses."*
**Guāng** says: *"Arms and shoulders like calves—this means arms are as large as shinbones, unmanageable."*
**Chēn** (swollen), pronounced *chēn* (as in "anger"), is the swelling of flesh.
The sixth day, with its **Yīn** essence, surpasses the midpoint and reaches its peak—like a minister’s overpowering strength, which the sovereign cannot control.
**Jiǎ Yì** (Jia Yi) says: *"The world suffers from great swelling; a shinbone’s bulk nearly matches the waist, a finger’s size nearly matches the thigh."*

The seventh day: *"Contend for the trunk and spear, offering sacrifices at the King’s procession."*
**Cè** says: *"The contention of the trunk and spear—this is guarding the sovereign’s person."*
**Wáng**’s version reads *"contend at the spear"* instead of *"contend for the trunk and spear"*—**Cè** says *"contend at the spear"*—now following all versions.
*Sòng* and *Lù* versions read *"offering sacrifices to the King at the procession"* instead of *"offering sacrifices at the King’s procession"*—now following **Fàn** and **Wáng** versions.
**Zhuó** (spear), is the same as *zhuì* (helmet).
**Xíng** (procession), pronounced *hùláng* (as in "door").

The seventh hexagram marks the beginning of calamity—
its use of arms is for contention.
Thus it says: *"Contend for the trunk and spear, helmet and shield."*

To contend with arms, not for private gain but for the public cause—
what greater fortune could there be?

The *Odes* say:
*"The prime chariot leads with ten teams,
to open the way."*

The eighth hexagram: *"The wolf’s jaws are full,
but arrows lie behind."*
**Cè** says: *"The wolf’s jaws are full—
it heeds no danger."*

**Wáng** says: *"When the wolf devours its prey,
its jaws are full in contention,
yet it knows not of the arrows behind."*

**Guāng** says: *"The wolf’s nature is greedy,
striving to fill its jaws,
yet unaware of the arrows behind—
like petty men who seize profit
while heedless of their peril."*

The celestial constellation *Húshǐ* (Wolf’s Arrow) lies behind the Wolf.

The ninth hexagram, at the peak:
*"Two tigers gnash their teeth—
he who knows retreat is whole."*
**Cè** says: *"Two tigers gnash their teeth—
they know where to pull back."*

The *Sòng* and *Lù* versions read *"know where to control"* instead of *"know where to pull back"*—
**Fàn**’s version also reads *"control"*—
now following **Wáng**’s version.

*"Pull back"* is pronounced *chè* (as in *"measure"*).

**Wáng** says: *"The utmost of contention
is like tigers fighting, gnashing their teeth—
there must be death or injury.
If one fears harm and withdraws in retreat,
then one may preserve what is whole.
One cannot persist in contention,
so at its height, the symbol shows self-retreat.
The reason one can know the way of retreat
is because the Yang is at its zenith."*

**Guāng** says: *"Pull back"* means *"to draw back."*

One region, three states, three departments, two households—
the Wood family of the Yin clan,
aligned with *Guà* (the hexagram of *Snakes*).

「When entering the *Mù* (務) hexagram, the second line is: *"Day rests at Mǎo (昴)."*#

The **Yáng** *qi* (陽氣) strives in its undertakings,
and all things conform to its will while gathering their affairs.
When the **Yáng** *qi* strives in its undertakings and brings forth things,
all things follow its will and rejoice in themselves,
gathering their affairs without hesitation.

The first line:
*"At the outset, there is no fixed direction—
even the petty man may act upon it—
**wǎng** (罔)."*
**Chè** (测) says:
*"At the outset, there is no fixed direction—
this is not the petty man’s proper concern."*
**Fàn**’s version reads *"At the outset, it begins without fixed direction,"*
now following the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and **Wáng** versions.
The *Sòng*, *Lù*, and **Wáng** versions lack the character *"man"* (人),
now following **Fàn**’s version.
The first line represents the beginning of contemplation,
and one may respond to change without fixed direction.
Yet during the day’s night (the hour of *Mǎo*),
this is not the petty man’s proper undertaking,
so it says: *"Even the petty man may act upon it—
**wǎng** (罔),"* meaning *"without proper measure."*

The second line:
*"Freshly seeking treasures of its own,
pure and refined in its fragrance,
the noble man proceeds with purpose."*
**Chè** says:
*"Freshly seeking treasures of its own—
this is self-illumination."*
**Wáng**’s version reads *"fragrance"* (芳) as *"rectitude"* (方),
and *"proceeds"* (行) as *"virtuous"* (臧),
now following the other versions.
The second line represents the middle of contemplation,
and it corresponds to the day.
The noble man refines and purifies his heart,
preparing to act with resolve.

The third line:
*"Without restraint or retreat,
its heart rots and decays."*
**Chè** says:
*"Without restraint or retreat—
its body is incomplete."*
The *Sòng*, *Lù*, and **Wáng** versions read *"its body is incomplete"* (其体不全) as *"its body is whole"* (其体全),
now following **Fàn**’s and *Xiǎo Sòng* versions.
**Chè** (掣) is pronounced *chè* (尺制切).
The third line represents the end of contemplation,
and it corresponds to the night,
when the petty man is moved by fleeting emotions,
losing his heart and damaging his body.

The fourth line:
*"Seeing arrows rise on their own,
benefiting from the companionship of feathers,
it covers the carriage and loads it."*
**Chè** says:
*"Arrows reach the carriage—
this is their proper course."*
The *Sòng* and *Lù* versions read *"Seeing arrows reach the carriage"* (见矢及盖) as *"Seeing arrows reach the carriage"* (见矢及盖车),
**Fàn**’s version reads *"Arrows reach the carriage"* (矢及盖),
now following **Wáng**’s version.
**Fàn** says:
*"Arrows rise on their own—
this is the power of feathers."*
Feathers unite with the arrow’s shaft,
and then they may fly.
Just as the ruler and minister act in harmony,
they drive the wind and transform customs.
The carriage carries its load,
just as the noble man aids the world in its affairs.

The fifth line:
*"The weaver’s labor
is not as beneficial as the silkworm’s cocoon."*
**Chè** says:
*"The weaver’s labor—
it brings no benefit to others."*
The silkworm’s cocoon (*suō* 緰) is pronounced *xū* (息浪切),
meaning *"raw silk or cloth."* **Wáng** alters it to *"cloth"* (褕), pronounced *yáo* (遥).
**Fàn** says:
*"Though the spider produces silk,
its labor is not used by people,
so it is less beneficial than the silkworm’s single cocoon."*
**Guāng** says:
*"The fifth line represents weaving, making cloth, and raising silkworms—
the petty man’s affairs are not his own,
laboring without reward,
so this symbol arises."*
**Lǚ Ào** (旅獒) says:
*"If one does not engage in what is harmful to others
and does what is beneficial,
then the labor is properly rewarded."*

The sixth line:
*"Flowering fruit, fragrant and abundant,
its use is virtuous and proper."*
**Chè** says:
*"Flowering fruit is fragrant,
its use is virtuous—
this benefits the current year."*
**Fàn**’s version lacks the character *"fruit"* (实),
now following the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and **Wáng** versions.
The sixth line represents the utmost of contemplation,
the completion of undertakings.
The completion of undertakings
is nothing greater than fulfilling one’s **virtue/power** (德).
When flowers and fruit both flourish,
the year is at its peak,
then what use is not virtuous and proper?

The seventh line:
*"Losing its fragrance,
there is nowhere to go."*
**Chè** says:
*"Losing its fragrance—
**virtue/power** (德) begins to decline."*
**Fàn** says:
*"The seventh line represents lost resolve,
so **virtue/power** (德) declines."*

The eighth line:
*"Central and yellow,
free from calamity,
**zhēn** (貞)."*
**Chè** says:
*"Central and yellow, free from calamity—
harmony and rectitude prevail."*
**Wáng** says:
*"The eighth line sits at the point of excess,
nearly inviting calamity.
Yet it finds its proper place in the middle,
dwelling in the upper body’s center,
this is the way of attaining central yellow,
free from blame and regret."*
**Guāng** says:
*"The eighth line represents calamity at its peak,
yet it aligns with the proper course.
The noble man takes harmony and rectitude as his undertaking,
so even calamity does not harm him."*

The ninth line:
*"Undertakings completed, self-destruction;
rains completed, they fall in ruin."*
**Chè** says:
*"Undertakings completed, self-destruction—
this is not their proper fate."*
Ruined (*duì* 队) is the same as fallen (*zhuì* 坠).
**Wáng** says:
*"At the utmost of undertakings,
losing its proper place in the night,
the completed work,
though achieved, is bound to fail.
Like the **Yīn** (阴) completing the rain’s fall,
this is an inevitable outcome—
can it be prevented?"*
**Guāng** says:
*"All things strive to create,
yet undertakings completed inevitably fail—
this is why.
The petty man, when his work is done,
becomes arrogant and indolent,
unable to fulfill his natural destiny."*

---
One region, three states, three departments, three households’ affairs:
The **Yáng** (陽) family of the **Yīn** (阴) clan,
also aligned with *Guà* (蛊).
The **Yáng** *qi* (陽氣) greatly exhorts and clarifies its roles,
and all things stretch forth in earnest,
each exerting its utmost strength.

**Fàn**’s version reads *"exhorts"* (勖) as *"overtakes"* (冒),
now following the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and **Wáng** versions.
Stretch (*shēn* 信) is the same as extend (*shēn* 伸).
It speaks of the **Yáng** *qi* exhorting all things,
as the black oil and kernels of fruits
each fulfill their proper functions.
All things extend themselves,
exhausting their strength,
and follow their own affairs.
Undertakings (*shì* 事) are what one strives to create;
affairs (*shì* 事) are each fulfilling their proper roles.

The first line:
*"Affairs without affairs,
the utmost of affairs without fail."*
**Chè** says:
*"Affairs without affairs—
this is the way of acting."*
**Wáng** says:
*"All affairs are properly ordered,
why should there be any action at all?
Simply rectify their roots.
Yet when the **Yáng** *qi* is at its zenith during the day,
at the start of affairs,
one may rectify their roots."*

**Guāng** says:
*"The first line represents the beginning of contemplation,
the source of purity of heart,
the root of all affairs.
The noble man clears his source,
rectifies his root,
and then there are no affairs he cannot manage."*#
**Lǎo Zǐ** says:
*"Without action, yet nothing remains undone."*

The second line:
*"Affairs lie at the pivot,
neither consulting nor divining,
losing its wise symbol."*
**Chè** says:
*"Neither consulting nor divining—
its wisdom is lost."*
Divining (*zōu* 诹) is pronounced *zǐ hóu* (子侯切) or *zǐ yú* (子于切).
Wisdom (*zhì* 知) is the same as intelligence (*zhì* 智).
A symbol (*fú* 符) is the auspicious token one guards.
The second line represents the middle of contemplation,
so it says: *"Affairs lie at the pivot."*
The pivot is where honor and disgrace,
safety and danger,
are decided.
When affairs are at the pivot,
contemplation has yet to unfold—
one should seek counsel from the virtuous,
to find the proper course.*

But on that very night, foolish and self-reliant,
he neither consults nor divines,
thus losing his wise symbol.

Emperor Yao sought counsel from the masses,
Shun took joy in accepting others’ virtues as his own,
and Confucius asked questions in every matter.

The third line:
*"Time departs and returns,
no space for delay."*
**Chè** says:
*"Time departs and returns—
it does not lose its course."*
The *Wang* text omits the character *container* (容), following the *Song*, *Lǚ*, and *Fàn* editions.
The third line represents *completed intent*.
When contemplation is complete,
one must center his will and act.
If one misses the moment,
regret comes too late—
thus it says: *"Time departs and returns,
no space for delay,"*
speaking of how gain and loss
lie in a hair’s breadth.

The fourth line:
*"Male and female affairs,
no substitution of characters."*
**Chè** says:
*"Male and female affairs—
this is not their proper duty."*
**Wang** says:
*"If it is not their affair,
it must lead to misfortune."*
For a male to attend to female affairs
is a grave dereliction—
why not substitute the character *nurture* (育)?
The fourth line exceeds its proper measure at night,
contrary to its due place,
hence the saying.

**Guāng** says:
*"If a man substitutes for a woman’s duties, his household will suffer misfortune;
if a subject usurps the ruler’s authority, the state will fall into disorder.
This makes clear that each affair has its proper constant."*

The fifth line:
*"Attend to one’s own affairs,
the king grants him sustenance."*
**Chè** says:
*"Attend to one’s own affairs—
this is the duty one is tasked with."*
**Wang** says:
*"Grant" (假) means "to bestow upon."*
**Guāng** says:
*"To attend to one’s own affairs means
to attend to what one ought to attend to.
Each holds his proper station,
thus the king bestows upon him sustenance—
receiving blessings and emoluments."*

The sixth line:
*"Bearing great responsibility alone,
when it arrives, no aid comes."*
**Chè** says:
*"Bearing great responsibility alone—
how can it be borne?"*
The sixth line exceeds the midpoint and reaches its extreme,
where strength is small yet the burden is heavy—
thus it says: *"Bearing great responsibility alone."*
The matter is about to arrive,
thus collapse and ruin cannot be averted.

The seventh line:
*"The elder supports the orphaned,
the young boy carries the pitcher."*
**Chè** says:
*"The elder supports the orphaned—
the young boy knows his place."*
**Wang** says:
*"Though the seventh line exceeds its measure,
it finds its proper place in the day,
not losing the suitability of its affairs.
Thus the elder has the duty of supporting the orphaned,
and the young boy also knows to carry the pitcher to provide nourishment."*

The eighth line:
*"Female affairs for males,
ten years without instruction."*
**Chè** says:
*"Female affairs for males—
the household will never prosper."*
The *Fàn* edition reads *"male and female affairs"* (男女事), but we follow the *Song*, *Lǚ*, and *Wang* editions.
**Wang** says:
*"Exceeding its proper measure,
yet losing its place at night.
Contrary to the suitability of affairs,
this is a female substituting for male duties.
Ten years—
the extreme of numbers.
Without instruction—
incorrigible."*
**Guāng** says:
*"If a woman takes on male duties,
the household will not prosper;
if a subject encroaches upon the ruler’s authority,
the state will not flourish.
Beyond ten years,
strength and influence are already established,
and cannot be undone.
Thus the female does not accept male instruction,
nor does the subject obey the ruler’s commands."*

The ninth line:
*"Arriving at *ear-obedience*, it stops,
its affairs are steadfast."*
**Chè** says:
*"Arriving at *ear-obedience*, it stops—
hearing in reverse, acting in accord."*
*Arrive* (到) is the same as *reverse* (倒).
*Stop* (止) is the same as *foot* (趾).
The ninth line represents the end of calamity while in the day,
able to accept loyal counsel and rectify faults,
not losing the proper order.
Loyal words, though harsh to hear, benefit action;
good medicine, though bitter, benefits the ailment.

*Completion of the Commentary on the Taixuan Jing, Volume Two.*

「#28 *Lóng yě* (隆也) was originally incomplete; restored according to the *Fànběn* (范本) and *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#29 *Tiān* (天) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#30 *Sān* (三) was originally *èr* (二); corrected according to *Fànběn* (范本) and *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#31 *Shāng yú* (伤於) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#32 *Jiàqiè* (嫁切) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#33 *Tǎn* (坦) was originally *wù* (物); corrected according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#34 *Yě* (也) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Fànběn* (范本) and *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#35 *Wángběn* (王本) was originally incomplete; restored according to the following text: *"Now the dry (gān) yields to the soft (róu), and the soft obeys the King (wáng)."*

#36 *Zhī lí* (之离) was originally *yě lí* (也离); corrected according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#37 *Lè* (乐) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Fànběn* (范本) and *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#38 *Lù* (陆) was originally omitted; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#39 *Shǐ* (使) and *suǒ* (所) were originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#41 *Wànwù* (万物) was originally *měi wànwù* (美万物), with *měi* (美) as a superfluous addition; *yě* (也) was originally omitted; deleted and restored according to the *Shísān Jīng Zhùshū* (十三经注疏) edition.

#42 *Wáng* (王) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#43 *Jiù* (就), *zhèng* (正), and *Dào* (Dao/Way) were originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#44 *Zhī* (知) was originally *dāng* (当); corrected according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#45 *Běn* (本) was originally omitted; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#46 *Kě* (可) was originally *hé* (何); corrected according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#47 *Zé hé* (则何) was originally *hé zé* (何则); corrected according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#48 *Shàngtǐ* (上体) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#49 *Zhī* (之) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#50 *Dà* (大) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Fànběn* (范本) and *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#51 *Zào* (皂) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#52 *Shì* (事) was originally incomplete; restored according to the correct text.

#53 *Wáng yě* (亡也) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Fànběn* (范本) and *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#54 *Shǒuzhī* (守之) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#55 *Èr* (二) was originally *sān* (三); corrected according to the correct text and *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#56 *Wáng* (王) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#57 *Shì zhàng* (是丈) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#58 *Jū* (居) was originally *jūn* (君); corrected according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

#59 *Bǔguò* (补过) was originally incomplete; restored according to *Bèiyào* (备要), as in the full text.

**Collected Commentary on the *Tàixuān Jīng* (太玄经), Volume Three**

**Two *Fāng* (方), One *Zhōu* (州), One *Bù* (部), One *Jiā* (家) Further Corrections**
*"Further"* (更), *jū* (居), *hēng* (亨) rhyme.

The *Yīn* (阴) family is *shuǐ* (水, water), with the *Gé* (革) rhyme.

On the first day of *Rùgēng* (入更), at one *fēn* (分) and nine *miǎo* (秒), the *Gǔ* (谷) and *Liáng* (梁) *qì* (气) align.
On the eighth day, the *Shè* (舍) day concludes as *Tiān* (天) ends.

The *Yáng* (阳) *qì* (气) having ascended,
its transformative influence shifts and alters forms,
and all things undergo change in their essence.

*Song* (宋) says:
*"In the heavens, it is called *fēi* (飞, soaring)."*

On the first day,
the *Mínghuà* (冥化) *pǔzhēn* (否贞) aligns with *xìng* (性, nature).
*Cè* (测) says:
*"The *Mínghuà* (冥化) *pǔzhēn* (否贞) aligns—
the *Shǎogēng* (少更) phase begins."*
*"Shǎo"* (少), *shāo* (照) rhyme.

One marks the beginning of *sī* (思, contemplation),
and corresponds to the night.
In youthful immaturity,
one becomes accustomed to improper ways,
like inherent nature itself,
and such habits cannot be altered again.

*Jiǎ Yì* (贾谊) says:
*"Early formation resembles inherent nature,
habits become as natural as spontaneity."*

The *Shǎogēng* (少更) phase is when
the Dao/Way transforms during youthful immaturity.

On the second day,
the *Shí* (时) *qī* (气) of seven and nine,
the *Zhěn* (轸) influence shifts its Dao/Way.
*Cè* (测) says:
*"The *Shí* (时) *qī* (气) of seven and nine—
it does not deviate from its proper course."*
Seven denotes the peak of *Yáng* (阳, yang),
Nine denotes the decline of *Yáng* (阳, yang).
*Zhěn* (轸) means *lún* (轮, wheel).

Two marks the midpoint of *sī* (思, contemplation),
and corresponds to the *huà* (画, division).
The virtuous person harmonizes *yáng* (盈, fullness) and *kū* (虚, emptiness),
adapting to the *shèng* (盛, flourishing) and *kuī* (衰, waning) of the times,
like a wheel turning endlessly,
responding to changes without fail,
never deviating from the perfect course.

On the third day,
the *Huà* (化) *bái* (白) in *ní* (泥, mud),
*Zī* (淄).
*Cè* (测) says:
*"The *Huà* (化) *bái* (白) in *ní* (泥, mud)—
its transformation becomes obscured."*

*Wáng* (王) says:
*"In all matters of change and transformation,
the value lies in converting evil to virtue.
Now, instead, it becomes muddy and *Zī* (淄),
losing the proper essence of transformation."*

*Guāng* (光) states:
*"*Zī* (淄) means *hēi* (黑, black)."*
Three marks the upper phase of *sī* (思, contemplation),
and corresponds to the night.
To dwell with those who are not virtuous,
is like staining white objects in mud—
all become black together.

On the fourth day,
the small gains of transformation,
its application brings no disadvantage.
*Cè* (测) says:
*"The small gains of transformation—
what the people aspire to."*

*Wángběn* (王本) originally had six additional characters below *"bùlì"* (不利): *"wǒ pǔ fēi qí yǒu chǐ"* (我否非其有耻),
but these are now omitted in favor of other editions.
Four marks the beginning of *fú* (福, blessings),
and corresponds to the day—
the small gains of transformation.
Small gains in transformation align with the people’s hopes,
and its application brings no disadvantage.

On the fifth day,
the *tóngniú jiǎomǎ* (童牛角马) phase,
neither of today nor of antiquity.
*Cè* (测) says:
*"The *tóngniú jiǎomǎ* (童牛角马) phase—
a deviation from heavenly order."*

*Wáng* (王) says:
*"Five occupies the peak position yet corresponds to the night—
this is the Dao/Way of transformation reaching its height,
finding no proper place."*

The cow is made to revert to its calfhood,
the horse is made to lose its horns,
neither of today nor of antiquity—
there is no such thing.

*Guāng* (光) states:
*"Hornless is called *tóng* (童)."*
When the petty person holds high position,
they arbitrarily alter laws and ordinances,
reversing the natural order of heaven.
This neither suits the present age,
nor conforms to antiquity—
it is like those of the *Liú Xīn* (刘歆) and *Wáng Máng* (王莽) ilk.

「Sixth, the phase of *rùshuǐ zàichē* (入水载车) and *chūshuǐ zàiháng* (出水载杭) —
it is fitting for the *Wáng* (王) to transform.

*Cè* (测) says:
*"The emergence and withdrawal of the chariot and the boat—
this is the Dao/Way of transformation."*

All other schools lack the *Wáng* (王) character;
now I follow the *Fàn* (范) edition.

*Háng* (杭) is the same as *háng* (航) —
a boat.

Watercraft and land chariots are the natural order of things.
Just as new shoes must be worn on the feet,
and though a hat may be worn out,
it must still be placed on the head.

*Shāng Wǔ* (商武) and *Zhōu Wǔ* (周武) reached their limits,
harmonizing with Heaven and following the people,
as sovereign and ministers exchanged roles—
this was the natural course of transformation,
which could not be avoided.

Seventh, *gēng bù gēng* (更不更) —
to feign transformation while actually causing harm.
*Cè* (测) says:
*"Feigning transformation without transformation—
this is the inability to govern oneself properly."*

The original editions of all schools read *"able to govern oneself properly"* (能自臧也),
but now I follow the *Wáng* (王) edition.

Seven marks the beginning of calamity,
yet corresponds to the night.
The decay of *súhuà* (俗化) is due to
failing to transform when transformation is needed—
thus, no transformation occurs.

*Dǒng Zhòngshū* (董仲舒) said:
*"To govern without implementing policies,
the most severe cases must be transformed and altered—
only then can order be restored."*

Eighth, *sìmǎ xiāngxiāng* (驷马跙跙) —
the four horses stumbling, yet able to transform their guidance.
*Cè* (测) says:
*"The four horses stumbling in *sìmǎ xiāngxiāng* (驷马跙跙)—
transforming their guidance is indeed good."*

The *Fàn* (范) edition reads *"transforming their guidance"* (而更其御),
but now I follow the *Sòng* (宋), *Lù* (陆), and *Wáng* (王) editions.

*Xiāng* (跙) is pronounced with the tone of *cái* (才) and the *qiè* (切) rhyme.

*Fàn* (范) says:
*"Stumbling (*xiāngxiāng* 跙跙) means disarray."*

*Wáng* (王) says:
*"When one holds high position during the day,
they should transform according to the Dao/Way.
The four horses stumbling (*xiāngxiāng* 跙跙) —
their movement does not advance.
Transforming with proper guidance (*liáng yù* 良御),
only then does it suit its purpose."*

*Guāng* (光) states:
*"Eight is the midpoint of calamity,
thus it is called *sìmǎ xiāngxiāng* (驷马跙跙),
to symbolize the instability of the state.
Yet it corresponds to the day’s brightness,
hence it says ‘transform their guidance’ (更其御).
Transforming guidance symbolizes
replacing incompetent officials with virtuous ones
to govern the state properly."*

Uppermost Nine, *bùzhōng qí dé* (不终其德) —
not completing one’s virtue/power,
and within three years, being replaced.
*Cè* (测) says:
*"The unfinished replacement—
this cannot endure long."*

The *Fàn* (范) edition lacks the *"long"* (久) character,
but now I follow the *Sòng* (宋), *Lù* (陆), and *Wáng* (王) editions.

Nine marks the extreme of calamity,
yet corresponds to the night.
The petty person does not complete their virtue/power,
becoming arrogant and indulgent,
losing their position—
others will replace them.

The *Yáng* (阳) family’s fire phase aligns with this decision.
The two *fāng* (方) regions, one *zhōu* (州), and one *bù* (部) —
two *jiā* (家) schools break the sequence.
」

The **Yang** qi is strong within and firm without,
its movement capable of decisive action.
*The Fàn* edition reads *"able to have shameful decisions,"*
*The Wáng* edition reads *"able to make decisions."*
I follow the *Sòng* and *Lù* editions.

**Initial Nine (1):**
The heart is severed, the axe extinguished,
its measuring cords and squares obscured.
*The Cè* says:
*"Severing the heart, extinguishing the axe—
self-governance within."*
One marks the beginning of thought,
yet corresponds to day.
Able to internally sever by law within the heart,
while no trace is seen by others.

**Second Nine (2):**
Obscured severance—
the ears are stopped.
*The Cè* says:
*"Obscured severance—
doubt within the heart."*
Two marks the middle of thought,
yet corresponds to night.
The mind is shrouded in darkness,
unable to decide—
though fine counsel exists,
it knows not what to follow.
Listening is not keen—
hence, *"the ears are stopped."*

**Third Nine (3):**
Severing its deafness,
profitable for purifying filth.
*The Cè* says:
*"Severing its deafness—
profitable counsel."*
*The Wáng* says: *"Deafness"* refers to nasal illness.
*Guāng* says:
Three marks the completion of intent,
yet corresponds to day.
Able to sever obstructions,
admitting wise counsel.

**Fourth Nine (4):**
Severing my *"no"*—
eating what is shameful.
*The Cè* says:
*"Severing my 'no'—
eating what is shameful."*
Four marks lower blessings,
yet corresponds to night.
Occupying a position unfit,
receiving blessings unearned—
unable to sever and depart—
truly shameful.

**Fifth Nine (5):**
The great belly is severed,
its thighs detached.
*The Cè* says:
*"The great belly severed—
just severance."*
*The Wáng* edition reads *"thighs"* as *"clothing"*;
I follow the other editions.
Five marks the manifestation of clarity,
yet corresponds to the *huà* (画).
Severance at its peak—
the great belly cannot contain deceit.
Its thighs detached—
the essence remains.

**Sixth Nine (6):**
Severance without severance—
your enemy is not distant,
only then comes the axe.
*The Cè* says:
*"Severance without severance—
guilt falls upon oneself."*
Six exceeds the midpoint,
yet corresponds to night.
When one should sever but does not,
enmity draws near—
inevitably receiving humiliation.

**Seventh Nine (7):**
*Gēng* severs *Jiǎ*—
my heart is vast,
only then comes refinement.
*The Cè* says:
*"Gēng severs Jiǎ—
justice severs benevolence."*
*The Fàn* says:
*"Gēng"* is justice,
*"Jiǎ"* is benevolence.
*"Kǒng"* means *"extreme,"*
*"shuò"* means *"great,"*
*"shuò"* also means *"beauty."*
*Guāng* says:
*"Gēng"* is metal, representing justice,
*"Jiǎ"* is wood, representing benevolence.
Seven marks the blade—
the symbol of punishment.
The virtuous sever benevolence with justice,
abandoning the minor for the major,
only then achieving governance’s beauty.
*The Shū of Xià* says:
*"Threaten to overcome their affection,
truly aiding them."*

**Eighth Nine (8):**
The bold and stunted assault—
the thief is shrouded in severance.
*The Cè* says:
*"The thief’s severance—
arbitrary judgment."*
*The Xiǎo Sòng* edition reads *"assault"* as *"[phonetic],"* sound altered;
I follow the other editions.
*The Wáng* edition omits *"guài"*;
I follow the two *Xiǎo Sòng*, *Lù*, and *Fàn* editions.
*"Zhū"* sounds *"zhū"* (株).
*"Fá"* sounds *"fá"* (伐).
*The Fàn* says:
*"Without the Way is 'stunted,'*
*contradicting principle is 'assault.'"*

**Uppermost Nine (9):**
The axe’s edge is fluttering,
profitable for the carpenter’s sincerity.
*The Cè* says:
*"The fluttering axe—
profitable for征乱 (zhēngluàn)."*
Nine marks war and the axe,
again the extreme of calamity—
hence, *"the axe’s edge is fluttering."*
The carpenter wields the axe to fell wood;
the virtuous uphold justice to quell chaos.

---
**The *Yǐn* family’s Wood aligns with this decision.**
*Poised resolve* is achieved—
*guài* flourishes in the royal court,
thus *poised resolve* also bears the symbol of speech.
The **Yang** qi is now strong—
resolute and daring in action,
all things trust its will.
*"Shēn"* is synonymous with *"shēn"* (伸, "extend").

*The Sòng* says:
*"Good without flexibility is 'strong.'"*

**Initial Nine (1):**
Threat is harbored, the void is filled.
*The Cè* says:
*"Threat harbored, the void filled—
virtue and the Way are lost."*
One marks the beginning of thought,
yet corresponds to night.
The petty person harbors threat within,
relying on force to extinguish righteousness.

**Second Nine (2):**
Poised resolve in heart and vitals—
sincerity.
*The Cè* says:
*"Poised resolve in heart and vitals—
internal firmness and rigidity."*
Two marks the middle of thought,
yet corresponds to day.
The virtuous uphold correctness,
firm and unyielding.

**Third Nine (3):**
Threat is donned upon the head,
the virtuous lack it, the petty possess it.
*The Cè* says:
*"Threat donned upon the head—
the petty’s strength."*
Three marks the completion of intent,
while rigidity and resolve appear externally—
hence, *"threat donned upon the head."*
If the virtuous occupy it,
they deem themselves insufficient;
if the petty occupy it,
they deem themselves excessive.

**Fourth Nine (4):**
The virtuous are pleased with their tools,
their speech gentle yet resolute.
*The Cè* says:
*"The virtuous pleased with their tools—
speech is measured."*
*The Fàn* edition reads *"people"* for *"speech"*;
I follow the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and *Wáng* editions.
Four marks lower blessings,
yet corresponds to day.
The virtuous’ words all follow measure,
applied like tools—
gentle yet not weak,
resolute yet not obstinate.

**Fifth Nine (5):**
Without tilling yet reaping grain,
poised resolve in selecting blessings.
*The Cè* says:
*"Without tilling yet reaping grain—
blessings unearned."*
*The Sòng*, *Lù*, and *Wáng* editions read *"selecting"* as *"supporting"*;
I follow the *Fàn* edition.
Five corresponds to the night of day.
Without virtue yet enjoying great blessings—
rigidity and fruit apply only to choosing blessings.
Thus: *"Without tilling yet reaping grain,
poised resolve in selecting blessings."*
*The Shījīng* says:
*"Neither plowing nor harvesting—
how do you take three hundred measures of grain?"*

**Sixth Nine (6):**
Poised resolve in beams and pillars,
profitable for stabilizing the great lord.
*The Cè* says:
*"Poised resolve in beams and pillars—
the state’s strength is mighty."*
Six marks upper blessings,
yet corresponds to day.
The state’s great ministers—
loyal, strong, and resolute—
capable of bearing their duties,
stabilizing the realm.

**Seventh Nine (7):**
The *gōng-yáng*’s resolve—
its cry is unlike.
*The Cè* says:
*"The gōng-yáng’s resolve—
speech is unlawful."*
*The Fàn* says:
*"Gōng-yáng"* is the great ram.
*Guāng* says:
*"Yáng"* is a *"very"* thing,
*"lèi"* means *"good."*
Seven marks the stage of calamity,
yet corresponds to night.
The petty are rigid and extreme,
speech without discrimination,
heedless of measure.

**Eighth Nine (8):**
Poised resolve in *huò-zhēn* calamity,
where the **virtuous** one earns renown.
*The Cè* says:
*"Poised resolve in *huò-zhēn* calamity—
it cannot be concealed or obscured.*
The *Sòng* and *Lù* versions render *yǎn* as *dū*, while the *Wáng* version renders it as *què*; here we follow the *Fàn* version.
*Nuò*, pronounced as *Pǔ-kǒu* (蒲口) cutting.
The *Wáng* says:
*"Eight resides within calamity,
so its resolve is poised in *huò*.
Yet it corresponds to the *wéi* (位) position and to day,
not losing its constancy—
this is what earns the **virtuous** one renown."*
*The Guāng* says:
*"The **virtuous** one upholds constancy,
encountering calamity with unyielding resolve—
though the body may be slain,
renown cannot be buried."*
*Yǎn*, to cover.

**Uppermost Nine (9):**
The *zì* boar’s resolve—
its tusks are poised.
It shoots forth with a drawn bow.
*The Cè* says:
*"The *zì* boar’s resolve—
it is hunted by officials."*
The *Wáng* version renders *lì* (吏) as *rén* (人); here we follow the other households.
*Zì*, standing—*xǐ* (喜).
The *Wáng* says:
*"Residing in the extreme position of poised resolve,
and corresponding to night,
if like the wild boar’s resolve in its tusks,
it will surely meet the fate of a drawn bow’s arrow."*
*The Guāng* says:
*"Zì*, the great boar.
The petty, in their extreme resolve, invite calamity—
like the boar’s resolve in its tusks,
sufficient only to provoke hunting and slaughter."*

---
**Annotations (to be removed unless requested):**
# *Huò-zhēn* (祸贞) – Likely refers to a state of crisis or adversity (*huò*) where constancy (*zhēn*) is tested.
# *Wéi* (位) – Position or station (here, likely referring to the *yáng* (阳) hexagram’s position in the *shàng* (上) half).
# *Zì* (豨) – A wild boar; *xǐ* (喜) suggests a joyful or aggressive stance.
# *Yǎn* (蔀) – To cover or obscure; *dū* (都) and *què* (却) are variant readings.
# *Dào* (道) – Not present in this section, but retained in prior conventions.
# *Qi* (氣) / *Dharma* (佛教) – Not present in this section, but retained in prior conventions.
# *Lì* (吏) – Officials or functionaries; *rén* (人) is a variant reading.
# *Sòng, Lù, Wáng, Fàn, Guāng* – Commentarial traditions (e.g., *Sòng* for Song dynasty scholars, *Guāng* for *Guāngyùn* 光尹).

The **Way** enters the **Array** in the fourth position, thirty-eight divisions and thirty-two seconds, the sun’s course truly sinks, and the **Summer Qi** responds.

The **Ladder** stands in the **Peril** position, the **Lü** pitch of the **Compass Bells** is central.

The **Array**, a regulated march. Though the **Yang Qi** dominates in its great activity, a subtle **Yin** rests below, the **Array** is formed yet desiring departure.

Lü says: The **Yin Qi** rests below, so the **Yang** is bound in the **Array**, its resolve set on departure.

The first day: A **Dark Array**, no visible march.
Mèng says: The **Dark Array** is unseen, the heart already straying outward.
One is the beginning of contemplation, yet it occurs in daylight.
The **Noble One**, perceiving subtleties, harbors a hidden resolve to depart, though none know it.

The second day: The **Wildfowl** are distressed upon the ice, winging toward the southern wind, inwardly cherishing their **Pairs**.
Mèng says: The **Wildfowl’s** distress is a burden of sorrow, finding no comfort.
The *Sòng* and *Lü* readings render *惨* as *掺*, but now follow the *Fàn* and *Wáng* readings.
**Pairs**, pronounced *shí-líng* or *shí-líng* (variant).
Wáng says: **Wildfowl**, meaning **Geese**. Having lost their companions, they are distressed by the cold ice, then wing toward the southern wind, inwardly cherishing their **Pairs**, sorrowful yet finding no comfort.
**Pairs** refers to the four **Geese** in flight.
Guāng says: In *Fāngyán* (Regional Speech), flying birds are called **Pairs**, **Geese** are called **Pairs**—meaning **mates** or **companions**. **Geese** flee cold and seek warmth, migrating from north to south, just as people depart from danger to seek safety. The second day is the middle of contemplation, occurring at night.
The **Lesser One**, indulging in favor and wealth, cannot avoid calamity in its subtlety, like the **Goose** that inwardly cherishes its **Pairs** yet cannot journey far.

The *Yì* (Changes) says: **Bound Retreat** brings sickness—**Peril**.

The third day: **Advancing** in resolve, or **receiving** its joy.
Mèng says: **Advancing** in resolve meets what brings comfort.
The third day is the upper stage of contemplation, occurring in daylight.
Though bound in travel, **advancing** fulfills its resolve, hence it **receives** its joy.

The fourth day: The **Black-Cock** flies at dawn, shattered in the north;
**Ming-ming** they harmonize, yet never cease their feeding.
Mèng says: What reliance can the **Black-Cock’s** dawn flight have?
The *Sòng* and *Lü* readings render *踤* as *夸*, while *Wáng* renders it as *跨*; now follow the *Fàn* reading.
**Black-Cock**, same as *鹍*, pronounced *gǔ-hún*.
**Tread**, same as *萃*, also pronounced *cí-xù*.
Wáng says: The great **Crow** flies at dawn, yet it should have sought the **Yang** for its own safety.
Instead, it reverses toward the north, losing its direction.
Though they harmonize with **ming-ming**, they never abandon their intent to feed.
Having lost its **Way**, what benefit can it find?

The fifth day: The **Great Wildfowl** is bound in **Zī** (variant: *Zhī*),
Feasting with **yí-yí** contentment.
Mèng says: The **Great Wildfowl** bound in **Zī** signifies a great commander achieving its resolve.

The sixth day: Passing through six **Crossroads**, circling nine **Paths**, yet no restraint on its **Marching Merchants**.
Mèng says: Passing through six **Crossroads** is the affair of **Merchants**.
Wáng says: Six **Crossroads**, nine **Paths**—there is no place it does not traverse.
Those who labor yet seek profit are the peculiar affairs of the **Lesser Ones**.
Guāng says: Six signifies abundance and multiplicity, occurring at night.
The **Lesser Ones** wander throughout the land, with no restraint on their movement, not for the sake of the **Way**.
Their resolve is merely to pursue profit, differing in no way from **Merchants**.

The seventh day: The **Array** lacks **Pairs**,
Profit in **Campaigning**—**Fault**.
Mèng says: The **Array** lacks **Pairs**, calamity is near.
The *Sòng*, *Lü*, and *Wáng* readings render *离* as *lì*, but now follow the *Fàn* and minor *Sòng* readings.
**Pairs**, same as *俪*, pronounced *lì*.
The eighth day: The **Late Middle** scatters its **Tracks**, weeping upon the **Path**, its journey sent forth.

Mèng says: The **Late Middle** scatters its **Tracks**, sending it to its death.
The *Fàn* and *Wáng* readings render *泣于之道* as *泣于道*; now follow the *Sòng* and *Lü* readings.

The ninth day: The **Array** is bound at **Dusk**.

The **Array** at **Dusk**—still may be avoided.

The **King** says: In the **Way** of the **Array**, one should act first.
Now occupying the extreme position, it has lost the **Way** of avoiding difficulty.
Yet, when in position at **Day**, like one preparing to avoid calamity in the murky hour,
though late, it may still be avoided.

**Guāng** says: Nine marks the end of calamity, yet it is at **Day**.
The **Noble One**, when encountering the extremity of calamity,
prepares and departs—though the time is late, it is better than lingering in ease and not leaving.

**Two Regions, One Province, Two Prefectures, Two Families, the Multitude**
**Yin-Family Earth**, following the **Master**.

Entering the Multitude, the fourth day: **Day-Rest** at *Zhuì* and *Zhì*.
The eighth day: **Day-Rest** at *Shēn*.

**Yang-qi** is indeed lofty, the **Heart** harmonized—
All things flourish and expand, the Multitude is vast.
*Shēn*, same as *shēn* (to extend).
*Mǎo*, pronounced *hù* (to adorn).
**Lü** says: *Mǎo*, beautiful appearance.

The first day: **Dark Soldiers** begin—**Fire** enters the **Ear**.
Farming ceases, the **Grain-Valley** is abandoned;
The **Corpse** will be arrayed upon the **Field**.

**Mèng** says: The beginning of **Dark Soldiers**—
At its start, it is not yet preserved.

The **King’s** text renders *毅* as *gǔ* (wheel), now following all other readings.

The **King** says: *Bān*, to deploy.

**Guāng** says: One, with its obscurity, marks the beginning of **Soldiers**—
The onset of war has already sprouted, yet it has not yet taken form, hence "the beginning of **Dark Soldiers**."
For **Soldiers** are an inauspicious instrument—
When people hear of them, they are startled and alarmed, hence "the **Fire** enters the **Ear**."
Farming halts its plowing, to provide provisions and supplies.
Feeding the **Horse** with *yì* (strength), for the **General’s** use.

The **Corpse** arrayed upon the **Field**—
This speaks of many dead.

The second hexagram:
**Soldiers** without blades,
The army without formation,
The **Lynx** comes as a guest—**gentleness**.

**Mèng** says: **Soldiers** without blades—
Their **virtue/power** subdues without measure.

The **King** renders *麟* as *lín* (neighbor), now following all other readings.
*陈* (formation) is pronounced *zhèn* (straight blade).

**Fàn** says: The **Lynx** is a beast,
With horns that do not strike.

The **King** says: The second hexagram resides in the lower body’s center,
Yet also holds the position of day.
It gains the favor of the multitude,
Thus **Soldiers** do not clash blades,
The army does not set formations,
Yet strong neighboring enemy states come as guests.

**Guāng** says: The second hexagram is the midpoint of **Thought**,
And aligns with day.
The **Noble** cultivates **virtue/power** in the heart,
And all under heaven submit in turn—
**Soldiers** have no need to strike,
Thus the army has no need of formation.
*Guests* are those who come from without.
The **Lynx** comes as a guest—
Symbolizing martial might yet unused.
*Gentleness* means no harsh coercion.

The third hexagram:
The army laden with carts,
The elder crushes his kin,
Within, he treads upon the **Flaw**.

**Mèng** says: The army laden with carts—
Temple battles, internal wounds.

The **Sòng** and **Lù** readings render *丈* as *dà* (great) and *孥* as *nú* (crossbow),
The **King** renders *摧* as *tuī* (push) and *孥* as *nú* (slave).
Now all follow **Fàn’s** readings.

The third hexagram marks the end of **Thought**.
Before battle, internal scheming has already begun.
Carts are bound and entangled,
Overturning what they bear.
The *elder* is the head of the household.
*Kin* refers to wife and children.
Planning proves as flawed as the elder’s own self-destruction.
Defeat comes not from without—
It stems from internal discord already sown,
Thus the enemy gains the advantage.

**Sūnzǐ** says: To lose before battle, in temple calculations,
Is to lack foresight.

The fourth hexagram:
The **Tiger** roars, shaking the hall,
The **Leopard** subdues its private passions—**restraint**.

**Mèng** says: The **Tiger** roars, shaking the hall,
Like an eagle taking flight.
The **King** renders *胜* as *téng* (soar), now following **Sòng**, **Lù**, and **Fàn** readings.
The **Sòng**, **Lù**, and **Fàn** readings render *私* as *mì* (secret), now following the **King’s** reading.
*虓* is pronounced *xiāo* (angry cry).
*振廞* is pronounced *zhènyīn* (stirring).
*否* is pronounced *pǐ* (blocked).

**Fàn** says: *虓* is the sound of rage.
*Shaking the hall* depicts extreme fury.

The **King** says: Positioned among the multitude yet near the honored seat,
This is the duty of generalship.
Holding the position of day,
Skilled in leading the masses,
Like the **Tiger’s** roar, stirring and rising.
**Guāng** says: The fourth hexagram is the lower **Luck** in position—
One who wields the multitude.
The **Tiger** and **Leopard**, both symbols of martial ferocity.
Though the warrior rages like a **Tiger**,
His might is tempered like an eagle’s flight.
Yet he does not use force to settle disputes or vent anger,
Mastering his private desires,
Thus he may be used yet remains unused.
*The Law of Speech* says:
*"Asked of martial virtue, it was answered: ‘Conquest.’*
To conquer one’s private heart is called conquest."

The fifth hexagram:
**Drums** clash *jiējiē*,
Like **Bear** or **Dragon**.
**Mèng** says: **Drums** clash *jiējiē*—
Relying on strength to rule.
*躆* (drum) is the same as *踞* (sit), should read *剧* (excessive).
The fifth hexagram resides in the peak position yet aligns with night,
Relying on strength for victory—
This is insufficient to subdue all under heaven.

The sixth hexagram:
Great **Soldiers** like thunder and lightning,
Shaking the ears,
Yet bend to fill the belly—**submission**.

**Mèng** says: Great **Soldiers** like thunder and lightning—
Their awe-inspiring might knows no bounds.
The **King** renders *维* as *hòu* (await), now following all other readings.
*诎* is the same as *屈* (yield).

「Submission of the belly—
This is akin to submitting one’s heart.

The sixth hexagram reaches its greatest height while corresponding to day.
The **King’s** armies do not seek to slaughter and maim;
They merely instill dread.
Thus, like thunder and lightning,
They strike with awe-inspiring might,
Forcing hearts to submit.
The *Shijing* [Classic of Poetry] states:
*"The lands of Xu tremble in fear,
Like thunder, like lightning—
The lands of Xu tremble in fear."*

The *Baihu Tong* [White Tiger's Discourse] says:
*"War is to instill dread."*

The seventh hexagram:
**Banners** and **flags** flutter in disarray,
**Weapons** clash with a sinister din.
The **army** is burdened with grief,
Weeping and sighing.
**Mèng** says:
**Banners** and **flags** flutter in disarray—
This reflects the people’s deep resentment.
The *Fan* and *Wang* readings of *dry* (钺) as *ge* (戈) are now corrected to follow the *Song* and *Lu* readings.
*絓* (flutter) is pronounced as in *Hù* (户).
*唁* (weeping) is pronounced *Yàn* (彦).
*Sighing* is pronounced *Mòjiā* (莫佳).
**Fan** says:
*"To mourn the living is called *yàn* (唁), and to peer secretly is called *mòjiā* (窃视称 )."*
**Guang** states:
*"The seventh hexagram marks the beginning of calamity while corresponding to night,
A sign of the army’s defeat and ruin.
**Banners** and **flags** flutter in disarray,
**Weapons** clash with a sinister din—
This depicts the chaos of defeat.
The **army** represents the multitude.
When a husband dies and a wife is pregnant,
The people’s sorrow is most acute.
The multitude weeping in mourning,
Both crying and peering secretly at their rulers,
Express their resentment and hatred."*

The eighth hexagram:
The **army** grows weary,
**Victory** is seen in sickness,
Not in corpses or fallen chariots.
**Mèng** says:
*"The **army** grows weary—
This means no bloodshed is required.
*衰衰* (weary) depicts exhaustion and decline.
The eighth hexagram signifies **stagnant illness**, **consumption**, **peeling away**, and **calamity within**,
Yet corresponds to day.
It represents the ability to exhaust and weaken an enemy state,
Bending their strength without battle."*

The ninth hexagram:
**Axe-blades** are chipped,
Their handles broken—
They can halt, but cannot strike.
**Blood** is shed.
**Mèng** says:
*"Chipped blades and broken handles—
The **army** is ill-equipped to advance.
**Wang** says:
*"To advance will only bring self-inflicted bloodshed."*
**Guang** states:
*"The ninth hexagram marks the extreme of military use,
The culmination of misfortune—
Pursuing war without knowing when to stop."*"

*"Two lands, one state, two departments, three families—**sealed**,
The **Yang Family’s** water, **measured**."*

The ninth hexagram:
**Sealed** is **measured**—
The **Way** of the **Yang Family’s** water.

*"**Sealed** means **comparison** and **proximity**, as well as **completeness** and **perfection**.
**Yang qi** is close to Heaven, all things flourish and thrive—
Everything is **sealed** without gaps.
**Wang** originally wrote *caì* (咸) as *shèng* (盛), but now follows other scholars.
**Wang** says:
*'Flourish and thrive'—a description of greatness.
All things, riding the **Yang qi**, are great and perfect—
**Completeness** and **perfection** without flaw.
**Guang** states:
*'**Yang qi** ascends and is close to Heaven—
The utmost height.'*

*First line: Peering without gaps—
The gateway to great obscurity.*
**Mèng** says:
*'Peering without gaps—
**Sealed** without direction.'*
**Sòng** states:
*'All matters are **sealed**, hence the description of "without direction."'*
**Guang** says:
*'The first line marks the beginning of contemplation,
And corresponds to day.
The virtuous one focuses their mind on **sealed** matters,
To establish deeds and secure achievements—
No one can peer into their intentions.'*
The *Yì* says: *"If even minor matters are not **sealed**, then harm will arise."*#

*Second line: Neither **sealed** nor **comparison**,
Yet my heart is merely **traveling**.*
**Mèng** says:
*'Neither **sealed** nor **comparison**—
This is to stray from one’s native place.'*
**Comparison** (比) is pronounced *bǐ* (频寐切).
The virtuous one loves what is near to embrace what is distant,
While the petty one does the opposite.
The second line marks the middle of contemplation,
And corresponds to night.
Unable to embrace what is near,
Yet laboring their heart for what is distant.
**Traveling** (次) means to lodge at an inn.
To stray from one’s native place,
Yet seek to lodge at an inn—
This is abandoning what is near for what is distant.*
The *Shī* says:
*"No fields of my own, only weeds flourishing—
The distant, unthought-of, weigh heavy on my heart."*

*Third line: **Sealed** in what is close,
Beneficial for nurturing others.*
**Mèng** says:
*'**Sealed** in what is close—
This is the ultimate benefit.'*
The third line marks the upper stage of contemplation,
And corresponds to day.
The virtuous one loves their own kin,
And thus knows how to love others’ kin—
Extending their heart to include all,
Hence it is said: "Beneficial for nurturing others."*

*Fourth line: **Sealed** in what is foul and turbulent,
Three days without tasting the **soup**—*
**Mèng** says:
*'**Sealed** in what is foul and rank—
This is the passage of minor evils.'*
**Wang** says:
*'**Sealed** in what is foul and rank—
This is the **virtue/power** of embracing what is evil.'*
**Guang** states:
*'The fourth line marks external lodging and corresponds to night—
This is the one who associates with those who are not virtuous.
Associating with those who are not virtuous,
Over time, one will transform into them.
Confucius said:
*"To dwell with those who are not virtuous,
Is like entering a fishmonger’s stall—
If one stays long without noticing the stench,
They will come to regard it as their own.
Minor evils, if not removed,
Will gradually become familiar,
And one will eventually align with them.*
**Soup** (殽) is a mixture of flavors.
**Lodging** (次) means to lodge at an inn.*

*Fifth line: **Sealed** and **measured** without flaw,
In harmony with Heaven.*
**Mèng** says:
*'**Sealed** and **measured** without **altar**—
This is to merge with Heaven’s function.'*
**Crack** (罅) is pronounced *xià* (呼嫁切).
**Harmony** (嫔) is pronounced *pín* (频).
**Wang** says:
*'Residing in the center with uprightness,
Obtaining their proper position and corresponding to day.
As a wise ruler in their time,
They embrace what is truly close.
Hence it is said: "**Sealed** and **measured** without flaw"—
This means without gaps.
Thus, their achievements align with Heaven’s.'*
**Guang** states:
*'"**Sealed** and **measured** without flaw"—
The ruler and subjects, the people—
All are **sealed** in closeness,
Without any gaps.'*

*Sixth line: **Comparison** with great evil,
Perhaps it will bring added sorrow.*
**Mèng** says:
*'**Comparison** with great evil—
This is to match opposites.*
The sixth line marks the end of fortune and nearing misfortune,
Hence they **compare** with great evil,
And perhaps it will bring added sorrow.
Those not of my kind,
Their hearts must differ.
Matching with opposites,
Only invites self-inflicted burden.*
The *Yì* says: *"**Comparison** with those who are not of one’s kind."*

*Seventh line: **Sealed** with a small mouth and **gills**,
The great ruler has no successor.*
**Mèng** says:
*'**Sealed** with a small mouth and **gills**—
This is relying on the ruler’s reach.'*
**Wang** originally omitted the *wú* (无) character,
While **Xiǎo Sòng** omitted it as *qí* (其).
Now follows the **Sòng**, **Lǚ**, and **Fàn** versions.
**Fàn** and **Xiǎo Sòng** versions have *féng* (逢) instead of *dá* (达),
But now follows the **Sòng**, **Lǚ**, and **Wang** versions.
**Gills** (鳃) is pronounced *sāi* (苏来切).
**Ruler** (君) means to lodge at an inn (次),*
*And the eighth line is entered.
At **16° 7'**, the **qi** of **Great Fullness** responds.*

*Those who are **close**—
This means mutual love and deep affection.
The **Yang** side embodies **ren** (仁) and love,
Perfecting **zhēn** (真) with sincerity and earnestness,
All things are **close** and harmonious.
**Yang qi** is pure and refined,
Hence it is said: "Perfecting **zhēn**."*

*First line: **Close** not to their own flesh,
Their intentions are **clashing**.*
**Mèng** says:
*'**Close** not to their own flesh—
This is a heart at ease.'*
**Wang** originally omitted the *qīn* (亲) character,
Now follows the **Èr Sòng**, **Lǚ**, **Fàn**, and **Ràng** versions.
**Wang** says:
*'At the beginning of **closeness**, yet losing their proper position and corresponding to night,
This is straying from the **Way** of **closeness**.
Not their own flesh—
This refers to those who are distant,
Not truly **close** in flesh and blood,
Yet are **close** to them—
Their intentions will **clash**,
And not align.
Their hearts are mutually guarded,
Straying from the **Way** of **closeness**.
**Guang** states:
*'The first line marks the beginning of contemplation,
And corresponds to night—
This is **closeness** that is not truly **close**.
Though outwardly **close**,
Their inner intentions do not match,
And they will ultimately separate.
**Ease** (闲) means obstruction or lack of communication.'*

*Second line: Trust within,
Their intentions are **close** to **affection**.*
**Mèng** says:
*'Trust within—
No one can intervene.'*
**Fàn** version has *ròu* (肉) instead of *nèi* (内),
And *wén* (闻) instead of *jiàn* (间);
**Wang** version has *ròu* (肉) instead of *nèi* (内),
And *xián* (闲) instead of *jiàn* (间);
Now follows the **Sòng** and **Lǚ** versions.
**Wang** says:
*'**Close** to **affection** (戚)—
This is to take (资) what is **close**.
Their intentions are solely taken from what is **close** and attached.*
**Guang** states:
*'The second line marks the middle of contemplation,
And corresponds to day.
The virtuous one embodies sincerity in deep **closeness**,
Hence it is said: "Trust within."*

Next follows the third hexagram:

**Third line:**
The silkworm moth’s offspring belong not to it,
Yet the mantis takes them—
No resistance is met.

**Mèng** says:
*The silkworm moth’s offspring belong not to it—
It has lost its own body.*

The **Wang** version reads *"no resistance is met"* as *"蠃侮"* (shāng wǔ), now following the **Sòng**, **Lǚ**, and **Fàn** versions.

**Phonetic glosses:**
*蜾* (guǒ), *蠃* (láng guǒ), *螟* (míng), *蛉* (líng), *属* (zhī yù).
*The silkworm moth* refers to the silkworm’s larvae.
*The mantis* is the *Chuánlú* insect.
*属* means "to attach."
*近侮* is akin to "warding off insult."

The third line marks the end of contemplation,
And corresponds to night.
The petty person cannot attach to their kin
To ward off external threats,
Thus causing estrangement and aligning with others.
Like the silkworm moth failing to raise its own young,
Only to have them taken by the mantis.

As the *Shī* says:
*The silkworm moth’s offspring,*
*The mantis carries them away.*
Teach your children well,
And let them emulate this.

---

**Fourth line:**
Guests are honored with propriety,
Feasts are measured and orderly.

**Mèng** says:
*Guests are honored with propriety—
Host and guest unite in harmony.*

**Wang** says:
*几几* (jī jī) means "ordered by law."

**Guang** states:
The fourth line marks flourishing clarity,
And corresponds to day.
The virtuous one uses ceremonial feasting
To foster bonds of kinship and affection.
Guests are honored not for the food,
But because propriety is observed.
Confucius said:
*"I ate at the home of Shàoshī,*
*And was filled.*
*Shàoshī fed me with propriety."*

Host and guest unite—
Both act with decorum.

---

**Fifth line:**
Generosity without substance,
Those who cling will flee.

**Mèng** says:
*Generosity without substance—
It lacks kindred and direction.*

**Wang** says:
Though the fifth line is central,
It is misplaced and corresponds to night.
When generosity should be given but is not,
Those who cling to it will abandon it.
**Guang** states:
Unable to honor those who should be honored,
Or to show generosity where it is due,
It drives away those who are near,
Leaving one isolated and perilous.
As **Zǐ Tàishū** said:
*"Jìn is no longer a neighbor—*
*Who would say otherwise?"*

---

**Sixth line:**
Generosity in generosity,
The virtuous one wields the Dipper.

**Mèng** says:
*The virtuous one’s generosity—
Boundless in gathering people.*

The sixth line marks abundance and corresponds to day,
For it honors those who should be honored
And shows generosity where it is due.
The virtuous one enriches the near and draws the distant,
Nurtures the close and attracts the distant.
Like the Dipper at the center,
With all stars revolving around it,
Thus it is said: *The virtuous one wields the Dipper.*

---

**Seventh line:**
Lofty in station, yet trivial in deeds,
Base in execution.

**Mèng** says:
*Lofty in station, base in deeds—
Virtue cannot sustain it.*

The **Sòng** and **Lǚ** versions read *"周"* (zhōu) for *"从"* (cóng),
While the **Fàn** version reads *"同"* (tóng).
Now following the **Wang** version.

*庳* (bì) is pronounced *"便是"* (biàn shì) or *"必至"* (bì zhì), also *"卑"* (bēi).

**Wang** says:
The seventh line occupies an overfull position,
Misplaced and corresponding to night,
Though its station is lofty,
Its actions are trivial and base.

---

**Eighth line:**
Dried meat clings to the bundle,
Yet the resentment has already formed—
The virtuous one departs.

**Mèng** says:
*The clinging of dried meat—
It lacks my worth.*

The **Sòng** and **Wang** versions read *"脯"* (fǔ) for *"肺"* (fèi),
*"乾"* (qián) for *"干"* (gān),
And *"往"* (wǎng) for *"行"* (xíng).
The **Wang** version reads *"腑"* (fǔ) for *"附"* (fù).
Now following the **Fàn** and **Xiǎo Sòng** versions.

*糇* (hóu).
**Fàn** says: *"削"* (xuē) is *"肺"* (fèi).
*肺附* is like the lung clinging to wood.
**Wang** says:
Though there is the clinging of dried meat as kin,
The resentment of the bundle has already formed—
Thus the virtuous one departs.

As the *Shī* says:
*The people’s loss of virtue,*
*The bundle’s resentment grows.*

**Guang** states:
*肺附* illustrates how kin cling to their clan leader.
Though the people are humble,
They should still share the *jiān hóu* (dried meat and grain)
To aid their kin.
The clinging of dried meat is the thinnest of bonds,
Yet even this should be honored with provisions—
How much more so when the resentment is justified?
This is why **Wēizǐ** returned to **Zhōu**.

---

**Ninth line (topmost):**
Youthful kin are unfaithful.

**Mèng** says:
*Youthful kin are unfaithful—
They revert to their roots.*

**Sòng** says: *"荄"* (zhì) means *"exhausting one’s roots."*

**Wang** says:
At the extreme of kinship,
Misplaced and corresponding to night,
Like the foolish and senile,
Those they are close to will never be upright.

---
**Appendix:**
Two regions, one state, three departments, two families—
**Yáng** family, **Mù** (wood).

The **Yáng**-qi (阳气) is fully manifest in the external,
While the subtle **Yīn** (阴) is gathered inward.

**Lù** says:
*"The subtle **Yīn** is gathered inward, exhausting the roots of all things."*

**Initial One (初一):**
*Small gathering without remission,*
*Favorable for governing and stabilizing the people, rectifying the state.*
**Mèng** says:
*Small gathering without remission—*
*This is the proper **Dao/Way** (道).*
*Remission* (贷), *read as "huò"* (货, *tǔ dé* cut).

From **One** to **Three** (三),
*Subtle and yet to take root,*
*Hence all bear the image of smallness.*
**One** (初一) corresponds to the *stroke of day* (日之画),
*Where the noble one practices modest gathering with constancy,*
*Not burdening the people with remission of taxes,*
*Thus favorable for governing and stabilizing the people, rectifying the state.*
**One** (初一) also says:
*"Remission"* (贷) should be read as *"huò"* (货, *tǔ dé* cut).

**Second (次二):**
*Blackened gathering, ever so slight,*
*I am not upright.*
**Mèng** says:
*Blackened gathering, ever so slight—*
*This is not for illumination.*
*Xiao Song* (小宋) originally read *截* (jié) for *铁* (tiě), while *Wang* (王) read *寝* (qǐn).
*Now following Song, Lu, and Fan* (范), we read *铁* (tiě).
*铁* (tiě), *read as "xī lián"* (息廉).
**Fàn** (范) says:
*铁铁* (tiě tiě), *means "slight" or "small."*
*Illumination* (光) refers to:
*Blackened* (墨) means *greed* (贪).
The petty one is greedy in gathering,
Taking delight in trifling gains,
Gradually descending into what is not upright—
This is not the way to bring forth illumination and beauty.

**Third (次三):**
*Seeing smallness, do not use it,*
*With me, to nurture the sparse.*
**Mèng** says:
*Seeing smallness, do not use it,*
*Await my fullness.*
**Wáng** (王) says:
*Three* (次三) *holds its proper position at midday,*
*Fitting for the gathering of **Yīn** (阴).*
*Seeing smallness, do not use it—*
*This is because with me, the growth is flourishing,*
*Thus reaching the stage of nurturing and spreading vastness.*
*Illumination* (光) says:
*When things are yet subtle and small,*
*The noble one cultivates them,*
*Awaiting their fullness,*
*And then takes them.*

The *Rites* (礼) state:
*"Do not pluck, do not break eggs, do not kill the fetus,
Nor cut down the young—*
*Grass and trees must first wither and fall,*
*Then one may enter the mountains and forests.*
*All this is of the same intent."*

---

**Fourth (次四):**
*Gathering profit with minor punishments,*
*Small advances, great withdrawals.*
**Mèng** says:
*Gathering profit with minor punishments—*
*This is because governance is in retreat.*

*(The text of) the *Rites* (政) is established as correct,*
*Now following the Sòng, Lù, and Wáng editions.*

**Fifth (次五):**
*Store the **Yīn** (阴) and spread it forth,*
*The cocoon’s purity in the fields.*
**Mèng** says:
*Store the **Yīn** (阴) and nurture its purity—*
*This is to avoid disrupting the seasons.*

**Yīn** (阴) is *joy* (乐).
**Purity** (纯) is *beauty* (美).
Fifth (五) is the stage of the cocoon,
And it holds the position of *flourishing* (盛) while corresponding to *drawing* (画).

*(The Way) does not act in haste,*
*Life is born at its proper time,*
*Its use is measured with moderation—*
*Thus the six livestock thrive and multiply,*
*And the silkworms yield abundant beauty.*

**Sixth (次六):**
*Lamenting and extending, yet failing to heed,*
*Creating great beginnings—*
*The petty man does not guard against ruin.*
**Mèng** says:
*The warning of lamenting and extending—*
*This is because he does not recognize subtleties.*

*(The Wáng edition reads) "Lamenting and extending, yet failing to heed,"*
*Now following the Sòng, Lù, and Fàn editions.*

**Lamenting** (闵) and **extending** (绵) are minor appearances.
**Beginning** (元) is *origin* (始).
Sixth (六) is in the gathering phase,
Exceeding the midpoint and corresponding to night—
*The harbinger of resentment.*

Resentment begins in small things,
Yet grows to great.
The petty man does not guard against ruin,
Thus resentment reaches him,
And he does not even realize it.

**Seventh (次七):**
*The husband is drawn to the chariot,*
*The wife becomes the stripper of **chá** (茶),*
*Profit for the king’s sisters,*
*Not for the public realm—*
*This is perilous.*

**Mèng** says:
*Drawn to the chariot, stripping **chá** (茶)—*
*These are the means of gathering profit.*

**Lù** says:
*Means* (资) *is* ***wealth** (财).

*The husband and wife gather wealth,*
*Profit for the household, not for the state.*

**Wáng** says:
*Drawn to the chariot* (牵于车) *is heavy labor with meager gain;*
*Stripping **chá** (茶)* (剥荼) *is bitter herbs with little reward.*
*It can suffice for private chambers,*
*Profit for the king’s sisters,*
*Yet is insufficient to support the public realm.*

**Light** (光) says:
*The *Ěryǎ* (尔雅) states that the sisters of the king’s father are called *king’s sisters* (王姑).*

**Eighth (次八):**
*Great gathering, great reversal.*
**Mèng** says:
*The reversal of great gathering—*
*The gathered profits are not right.*

*(The Lù, Fàn, and Wáng editions read) "reversal" (颠) as "peak" (巅),*
*Now following the two Sòng editions.*

**Wáng** says:
*Eighth (八) resides at the extreme of gathering,*
*Yet loses its proper position, corresponding to night—*
*The stage of great gathering.*
*Amidst calamity,*
*To seek great gathering,*
*One must surely face the disaster of reversal and collapse.*

**Ninth (上九):**
*Gathering at the proper time,*
*Profit in guarding against extreme calamity.*
**Mèng** says:
*Gathering at the proper time—*
*How can this be criticized?*

**Wáng** says:
*Guarding* (圉) *is the same as* ***driving/warding** (御).

**Light** (光) says:
*Ninth (九) is the stage of extreme calamity,*
*Yet corresponds to midday.*
*The noble one, in times of abundance,*
*Implements heavy gathering—*
*The people do not regard it as tyranny,*
*This is how one prepares in advance for years of famine,*
*And wards off this extreme disaster.*

*(Criticized* (几) *should be read as* ***mocked/reproached** (讥).

*Though gathering is heavy,*
*If done at the proper time,*
*There is no reproach.*

Mencius condemns:
*Dogs and pigs devouring human food yet unaware of restraint,*
*And the land having starving corpses yet unaware of relief.*
*In these two cases,*
*One state, one district, three families—*
*The **Yīn** (阴) family hoards gold,*
*Following the *Kàn* (乾) family’s measure.*

The **Yáng** (陽) *qi* is pure and rigid, **Kàn** (乾) **Kàn** (乾).
All things are strong and flourishing#20.
*(Range and Wang originally read* **chún** (純) *as* **tǒng** (統) *; now following Song and Lu’s readings.)*

**First** (初) **one** (一), *Strong within* **fǒu** (否) *is steadfast#35—*
*No use may be made of it.*
**Zhè** (测) says:
*Strong within* **fǒu** (否) *is steadfast—*
*It cannot be conspired with.*
*(Fǒu, pronounced as* **fāng jiǔ** (方久) *.)*

**One** (一) *is the beginning of thought,*
*And corresponds to night.*
*Its strength is unbridled yet unaligned,*
*Hence no use may be found.*

**Second** (次) **two** (二), *The phoenix bird takes flight,*
*Polishing its plumage;*
*The noble one, at the* **chén** (辰) *hour,*
*Endures no warding.*
**Zhè** (测) says:
*The phoenix bird takes flight—*
*The noble one finds the proper time.*
**Fàn** (范) says:
*Warding* (圉) *is restraint.*
**Guāng** (光) says:
*The* **chén** (辰) *hour is the time.*
**Two** (二) *is the middle of thought,*
*And corresponds to day.*
*The noble one finds the proper time,*
*Strong in righteousness,*
*None can restrain him.*
*Like the phoenix’s flight,*
*Its plumage refined and long,*
*None can control it#21.*

**All** (凡) *who are in the middle*
*Bear the likeness of finding position and time.*

**Third** (次) **three** (三), *The pillar does not align,*
*The beam does not rise,*
*The great hall is slight.*
**Zhè** (测) says:
*The pillar does not align—*
*It cannot establish a firm foundation.*
**Three** (三) *resides at the head of the lower body,*
*And bears the burden above,*
*The likeness of the beam and pillar.*
*At the night of day,*
*The commoner is not strong but weak,*
*Unable to fulfill its duty.*
*If it cannot fulfill its duty,*
*Then the state’s foundation will collapse.*

**Fourth** (次) **four** (四), *Then it is wise and clear,*
*Assisted on both sides.*
**Zhè** (测) says:
*Then it is wise and clear—*
*The ministers now come forward.*
*(Song, Lu, and Wang originally read* **sāo** (搔) *as* **zhuā** (橿) *; Xiao Song as* **qiáng** (强) *; now following Fan’s reading.)*
*(Wang originally read* **fāng** (方) *as* **yǒng** (永) *; now following other readings.)*
**Fàn** (范) says:
*Four* (四) *is in its course,*
*The numbers of course mutually support.*
**Xī** ( ) *is flourishing.*
**Wáng** (王) says:
*Xī* ( ) *is indeed the appearance of mutual support.*
**Wú** (吴) says:
*The character* **xī** ( ) *should be* **jiāng** (姜) *with the radical* **shǒu** (手) *; the character books have no such form, but with the radical* **mù** (木) *its sound is* **jiāng** (姜).*
*Since* **four** (四) *is metal,*
*And the metal family heads with metal,*
*It is said that the numbers of course mutually support,*
*Not taking* **qiáng** (强) *as support.*
**Guāng** (光) says:
*Four* (四) *is the likeness of unobstructed flow,*
*And corresponds to day.*
*The noble one possesses the virtue of wisdom and clarity,*
*Hence the ministers now come forward,*
*Assisting on both sides,*
*Thus making it strong.*

**Fifth** (次) **five** (五), *The noble one is strong and flourishing by virtue#22,*
*The commoner is strong and flourishing by force#23.*
**Zhè** (测) says:
*The commoner is strong and flourishing#24—*
*Finding position brings even greater fault.*
**Wáng** (王) says:
*Five* (五) *resides in the ruler’s position,*
*Yet loses the proper time, corresponding to night.*
*Without the ruler’s virtue,*
*It is the commoner’s strength and flourishing#25.*
*Strong and flourishing by force#26,*
*It must bring about* **diān** (颠) *and peril.*

**Sixth** (次) **six** (六), *Conquering my strength and flourishing,*
*With Heaven, it knows no bounds#28.*
**Zhè** (测) says:
*Conquering my strength and flourishing—*
*The great beauty lacks foundation.*
*(Wang originally read* **wú jī** (无基) *as* **wú jiāng** (无疆) *; now following Song, Lu, and Fan’s readings.)*
**Sòng** (宋) says:
*Without foundation—*
*It means without boundaries,*
*Speaking of vastness and remoteness.*
**Wáng** (王) says:
*Six* (六) *is strength,*
*The master of finding position, corresponding to day.*
*If it can conquer strength and flourishing,*
*And fortify its own virtue,*
*Then the ruler’s way grows ever brighter,*
*Hence reaching Heaven with no end.*
**Guāng** (光) says:
*From five onward, it is* **xiāo** (消) *;*
*Six exceeds the middle yet corresponds to day.*
*The noble one can harmonize with the times,*
*Conquering its own strength,*
*Thus attaining vast blessings,*
*And knowing no bounds with Heaven#32.*

**Seventh** (次) **seven** (七), *Metal is rigid, flesh is soft,*
*Blood flows in the fields.*
**Zhè** (测) says:
*Metal is rigid, flesh is soft—*
*The law is too severe.*
*(Wang originally omitted the character* **liú** (流) *; now following other readings.)*
**Seven** (七) *is ruin and loss,*
*And corresponds to night.*
*The commoner applies the law too severely,*
*Severing soft flesh with rigid metal—*
*None can resist it.*
*Blood flows in the fields,*
*The extreme of cruelty#33.*

**Eighth** (次) **eight** (八), *Strengthen its decline,*
*Encourage its weakness.*
**Zhè** (测) says:
*Strengthen its decline—*
*It strives to strengthen itself.*
**Eight** (八) *is illness and stagnation,*
*And corresponds to day.*
*The noble one can strengthen decline and encourage weakness,*
*Not sinking into ruin itself.*
**Upper nine** (上九), *Great mountain uprooted,*
*The beam and pillar broken,*
*Its person is* **diān** (颠) *and* **jué** (蹶) *.*
**Zhè** (测) says:
*The mountain uprooted, the beam broken—*
*It ends in ferocity.*
*(Wang originally read* **tài shān bá** (太山拔) *as* **dà zhī bá** (大枝拔) *; now following other readings.)*
**Wáng** (王) says:
*At the extreme of strength,*
*Yet losing position, corresponding to night,*
*It is strong yet overbearing.*
**Guāng** (光) says:
*The commoner’s strength and flourishing are excessive#33,*
*The mountain uprooted, the beam broken#34,*
*It brings about its own* **diān** (颠) *and* **jué** (蹶) *.*

**Two states, two districts, one department, one family—** *The* **yīn** (阴) *family holds water,*
*Also measured by* **Kàn** (乾).

「**睟** (Zhuàn), same as **粹** (cuì).
**Lù** (陆) says: *乾* (Kàn) is pure.
**粹** (cuì) is refined. The **yáng** (阳) *qi* (气) is **zhuàn** (袀) and **zhuàn** (睟) in purity and clarity, all things flourish in radiance, preserving their resplendent **yáng** (阳).
*(Wang originally read **zhuàn** (袀) as **chū** (初), now following other readings.)*
**袀** (zhuàn), same as **jūn** (均).
**Sòng** (宋) says: *保* (bǎo) means to secure.
At this time, the **yīn** (阴) *qi* (气) withdraws and hides below, while the **yáng** (阳) *qi* (气) is **zhuàn** (袀) and **zhuàn** (睟) in purity and clarity. Thus, all things—whether high or low—flourish in radiance, securing their vitality, and embody their **yáng** (阳) *virtue/power* (德).

**Lù** (陆) says: *袀睟* (zhuàn zhuàn), akin to *pure and refined* (纯粹).

**First line**, *睟* (Zhuàn) within, pure without blemish.
**Zhè** (测) says: *睟* (Zhuàn) within, pure without blemish.
One signifies the beginning of thought, and corresponds to day.
The virtuous person maintains purity and refinement in heart, clear and unadulterated, thus able to gather all principles and accomplish myriad tasks.

**Second line**, *dim and impure* (冥驳) *concealing* (冒) *睟* (Zhuàn), within.
**Zhè** (测) says: *冥驳冒睟* (dim and impure concealing refinement), internally flawed.
**Zhuì** (瘱), pronounced as *yú jì* (於计).
**Zhuì** (瘱), pronounced as *yú jì* (於计).
**Lù** (陆) says: *瘱* (Zhuì) means to hide.
**Fàn** (范) says: *瘱* (Zhuì) means shame.
**驳** (Bó) means impure.
**Guāng** (光) says: The second line signifies thought in its middle, yet corresponding to night.
The petty person, amidst dimness and obscurity, conceals impure and mixed thoughts—though outwardly they may deceive others, their heart remains inwardly ashamed.

**Third line**, *eyes raised to heaven, ears lowered to the abyss, reverent* (恭).
**Zhè** (测) says: *目上耳下* (eyes raised to heaven, ears lowered to the abyss), the peak of insight and discernment.
*(Wang originally omitted the word *恭* (reverent), now following other readings.)*
The third line signifies thought at its end, yet still corresponding to day.
The virtuous person’s thoughts, if pure and refined, attain boundless clarity—*eyes raised to heaven, ears lowered to the abyss*. Though they do not rely on this, they remain solemn and vigilant, thus preserving their refinement.

**Fourth line**, petty persons *covet* (慕) *睟* (Zhuàn), yet lose their *luck and uprightness* (禄贞).
**Zhè** (测) says: Petty persons *covet* (慕) *睟* (Zhuàn), yet fail to attain it.
**Wáng** (王) says: Losing position and corresponding to night, their path is already impure.
Though they covet the pure and refined Dao, they lose their fortune, uprightness, and integrity.

**Fifth line**, *睟* (Zhuàn) in the *mysterious yellow* (幽黄), *uprightness and origin* (元贞) without measure.
**Zhè** (测) says: *睟* (Zhuàn) in the *mysterious yellow* (幽黄), the true and just principle.
**Lù** (陆) says: *则* (Zé) means law.
**Wáng** (王) says: Dwelling in the center and embodying uprightness, attaining position corresponding to day, this is the master of *睟* (Zhuàn).
Pure and great virtue shining clear, *睟* (Zhuàn) in the mysterious depths, yet bearing the *yellow center’s* (黄中) harmonizing principle. The origin and uprightness are boundless, their Dao without measure, ineffable.

**Guāng** (光) says: *元* (yuán) is the longest of the good.
The fifth line signifies *central harmony* (中和), yet still corresponding to day.
