The virtuous person, though hidden, does not lose the Dao of central harmony—thus they are refined. Guarding their origin and uprightness, they respond to all tasks without fail.
Like the earth’s virtue, it too completes all things through mysterious yellow, origin, and uprightness.

**Sixth line**, *great* (大) *睟* (Zhuàn) *bears* (承) *faults* (愆), *perilous* (易).
**Zhè** (测) says: *大睟承愆* (great refinement bears faults), the petty person cannot preserve it.
*(Wang originally read: Great refinement bears faults, the petty person cannot preserve it. Zhè says: The Dao of great refinement, the petty person cannot preserve it. Now following other readings.)*
**Wáng** (王) says: The sixth line dwells in a position of excess, the greatest of *睟* (Zhuàn); yet losing position and corresponding to night, thus bearing faults.
**Guāng** (光) says: Lines five and above are *dissolving* (消), the sixth exceeds the center and corresponds to night, unable to preserve its purity and refinement.
For even white jade is prone to flaws, and clear water easily stained—thus, the great refinement is not what petty persons can fully preserve; they must inevitably bear faults and errors.

**Seventh line**, *睟* (Zhuàn) *at dawn* (辰) *bears faults* (愆), the virtuous person *mends faults* (补愆).
**Zhè** (测) says: *睟* (Zhuàn) *at dawn* (辰) *bears faults* (愆), skilled at mending errors.
**Wáng** (王) says: The seventh line dwells at the beginning of misfortune, the fault of the *睟* (Zhuàn) *time*. Yet with position attained and corresponding to day, they do not lose the virtue of the noble, thus able to mend faults without blame.
**Guāng** (光) says: Faults in time, only the virtuous can mend them to complete their refinement.
The *Shī* (《诗》) says: *The grand robes have gaps, only Zhòngshānfǔ* (仲山甫) *mends them.*

**Eighth line**, *睟* (Zhuàn) *in evil, without good.*
**Zhè** (测) says: *睟* (Zhuàn) *in evil, without good*, ultimately unassailable.
**Wáng** (王) says: Losing position and corresponding to night, pure in evil virtue, then good has no way to enter.
**Guāng** (光) says: The eighth line is *rapidly stagnant* (疾瘀) and corresponds to night—those entirely pure in evil, how can they be aided?

**Upper ninth line**, *睟* (Zhuàn) *at its end, ever as the beginning* (永初), upright.
**Zhè** (测) says: The uprightness of *睟* (Zhuàn) *at its end*, truly praiseworthy.
**Wáng** (王) says: The ninth line dwells at the extreme of the hexagram, yet attains position corresponding to day—this is the ability to preserve its purity and refinement, never losing the uprightness of the beginning.
**Guāng** (光) says: The ninth line is the peak of refinement, able to be cautious at the end as at the beginning, fully preserving its purity and uprightness.

**Two states, two districts, one department, two families flourish—**
*The* **yīn** (阴) *family holds fire,*
*Also measured by* **Dàyǒu** (大有).

---

**Entering the state of flourishing, second in sequence**—the thirty-third division, thirty seconds, corresponds to the day of *Chúnshǒu* (Blind Pheasant), aligning with the *qi* of *Mángzhǒng* (Grain in Beard).

The Dipper stands at the noon position, and the musical pitch is *Ruìbīn* (Reui-pin).
**Yang-qi** (Yang *qi*) ascends in overwhelming abundance, filling all things with its fullness, as if they were entirely satisfied.

**The original character *窴* (Yuàn) is pronounced *tián* (like "field").** The *Sòng* (Song) commentary states: *窴然* (Yuànrán), "fullness." On the first line: *Flourishing yet not silent* (盛不墨), *loses the virtue of darkness* (失冥德).
**Zhè** (测) says: *Flourishing yet not silent* means the center does not restrain itself.
The *Sòng* and *Lù* (Lu) texts originally read *终* (Zhōng, "end") instead of *中* (Zhōng, "center"), but now follow the *Fàn* (Fan) and *Wáng* (Wang) readings. *Lù* says: *克* (Kè), "to overcome." Unable to overcome its own desires.
**Wáng** says: The first line, dwelling at the onset of flourishing yet corresponding to night—*flourishing yet unable to silence itself*, a state that loses its virtue of darkness.
Thus, *it forfeits the virtue of its hidden and profound nature*.
**Guāng** (光) says: *Mò* (墨), "law/measure." In the *Way* of flourishing, the difficulty is not achieving it, but *how to dwell within it*. The first line is *contemplating the beginning* (思始), yet it corresponds to night. *Flourishing without law to restrain itself*, it thus *loses its virtue of concealment and subtlety*.

**Second in sequence**—*not leaning on its own power* (作不恃), *overcoming Great Possession* (克大有).
**Zhè** says: *Not leaning on its own power*, *it aligns with the virtue of the Mysterious* (称玄德).
**Wáng** says: Dwelling in the time of flourishing, yet attaining the position of *drawing* (画)—*clear in the natural Way*, it has the *function of action* (作为之功), yet does not *rely on that function*. Thus, it can reach *Great Possession*.
**Guāng** says: The second line is *contemplating the center* (思中), yet corresponding to day. *Acting yet not relying*, *doing yet not possessing*. *It is only because it does not possess* that it can achieve *Great Possession*.
(*Chēng* (称), "fit/appropriate.")

**Third in sequence**—*embracing profit, its chest full of satisfaction* (怀利满匈), *not beneficial for the public* (不利于公).
**Zhè** says: *Embracing profit, its chest full of satisfaction*, *it does not favor private interests*. The third line is *contemplating the upper* (思上), yet corresponding to night. The *gentleman* (君子) is guided by *righteousness* (义), while the *petty man* (小人) is guided by *profit* (利). The petty man, in seeking flourishing, only *pursues profit* (营利). Thus, it is said: *embracing profit, its chest full of satisfaction*. *Profit for the private* (利於私), *this is harm for the public* (斯害於公).

**Fourth in sequence**—*small flourishing, subject to subjects* (小盛臣臣), *the gateway to great flourishing* (大人之门).
**Zhè** says: *Small flourishing, subject to subjects*, *it serves the virtuous and wise* (事仁贤).
(*The original text reads "人" (rén, "person"), but follows the Sòng, Lù readings.* The fourth line is the *beginning of prosperity* (福始), hence *small flourishing*. *臣臣* (Chénchén), *a state of self-humility and lowliness*. In the initial phase of small flourishing, the gentleman can *humble himself* (自卑贱), *attending to the virtuous and wise* (承事仁贤), thus *leading to great flourishing*. For those who become *great persons* (大人), there is none who does not emerge through this *Way*, hence it is called *the gateway to great flourishing*.

**Fifth in sequence**—*what prosperity fills the shoulders* (何福满肩), *lifting calamity, swaying and swaying* (提祸挥挥).
**Zhè** says: *What prosperity lifts calamity*, *this is the petty man’s Way*.
(*The Sòng text originally reads *掸掸* (Shànshàn), but now follows the Fàn, Wáng readings.* *何* (Hé), pronounced *húkě* (like "how").
**Wáng** says: *掸* (Shàn), pronounced *chán* (like "entangling"), *its meaning taken from the image of entanglement that does not depart*. (*Chén* notes: *掸*#35, pronounced *dān* (like "red"), also *túdān*#36.) The fifth line dwells at the *peak of flourishing* (盛位), hence it is said: *what prosperity*. *Prosperity reaching its peak* (福至盛), hence *fills the shoulders*. *Extreme flourishing must reverse* (极盛必反), hence *lifting calamity*. **Guāng** says: All praises corresponding to night are the *Way of petty men*. *For petty men to enjoy great prosperity*, calamity must follow, hence: *what prosperity fills the shoulders, lifting calamity, swaying and swaying*.

**Sixth in sequence**—*the light bestowed by Heaven* (天赐之光), *the mighty strength of great opening* (大开之强), *for At-Qian there is joy* (于谦有庆).
**Zhè** says: *The light bestowed by Heaven*, *the humility of Great Possession* (谦大有).
(*The Fàn and Wáng texts originally read *赐* (Cì, "bestow"), but now follow the Sòng, Lù readings.* The sixth line is *flourishing to its greatest extent* (盛多极大), yet corresponding to day. The gentleman *receives Heaven’s bright command* (受天明命), *greatly opening the upper realm* (大启上宇). *Extreme flourishing is difficult to dwell within*, hence *humility* (谦) must be used, *then there is joy*.

**Seventh in sequence**—*riding the fire, cold springs arrive* (乘火寒泉至).
**Zhè** says: *Riding the fire, cold springs arrive*, *calamity does not ensue*.
(*The Fàn text originally reads: *riding the fire, springs arrive*, *calamity does not approach*. Now follows the Sòng, Lù, and Wáng readings.*)
**Wáng** says: In the time of flourishing, the seventh line *dwells beyond fullness*, yet also shares the *fire number* (火数) with the original stem. The *overwhelming force of two fires* (二火之盛), *ascending fiercely* (炎炎上干), is *detested by the sixth line*, hence *cold springs are about to arrive*, with the *fear of being extinguished*.
**Guāng** says: The seventh line is the *beginning of calamity* (祸始), yet corresponding to night. *Riding the fire* is the *peak of flourishing*. *Cold springs arriving* means *extinction is imminent*.

**Eighth in sequence**—*drawing from the full brilliance* (挹于满荧), *nearing the danger of later collapse* (几后之倾).
**Zhè** says: *Drawing from the full brilliance*, *it is nearly unavoidable*.
(*The Sòng and Lù texts originally read *拘* (Jū, "restrain"), but now follow the Fàn and Wáng readings. The Wáng text originally lacked the *荧* (Yíng) character, now follows the Sòng, Lù, and Fàn readings. The Fàn text originally read *几危也* (Jīwēi yě, "nearing peril"), now follows the Sòng, Lù, and Wáng readings.* *几* (Jī), pronounced *jī* (like "machine") or *qí* (like "border").
**Wáng** says: *Attaining the position of day*, *skilled in dwelling within flourishing and fullness* (善於处盛满), yet able to *draw from the fullness*—*it nears the danger of later collapse, yet does not collapse*, *for it avoids ruin through humility and restraint*. Dwelling at the *peak of flourishing*, *without the Way of drawing from fullness*, *it is nearly unavoidable*.

**Ninth line, uppermost**—*extreme flourishing cannot be saved* (极盛不救), *calamity descends from Heaven* (祸降自天).
**Zhè** says: *Extreme flourishing cannot be saved*, *Heaven’s Way reverses*.
The ninth line dwells at *the extreme of flourishing* (盛极), *the day’s night*—*the end of calamity’s course* (逢祸之穷), *a state where extreme flourishing must decline*.

---
**Two states, two districts, one department, three families dwell**
**The Yang family holds wood** (阳家木),
**Measured by *Jiārén* (Family of Man)** (准家人).

**The Yang state, vigorous and resplendent** (阳方躆肤赫赫),
*is the fortress and ramparts of all things* (为物城郭),
*all things thrive within it* (万物咸度).
(*The Fàn text originally reads *万物咸得度* (wànwù xián dédù, "all things attain thriving"), now follows the Sòng, Lù, and Wáng readings.*)
(*Lù* says: *Lù* (躆), "the appearance of fullness and vigor." The Yang state *is the fortress and ramparts*, *all things dwell within it*, hence: *all things thrive*. **Wáng** says: *Fortress and ramparts* (城郭) are *external symbols*. **Guāng** says: *Fú* (肤) should also be read as *Lù* (躆).)
(*Lù* (躆), *Lù* (躆), *a form of vigorous and forceful movement* (动作强梁貌).)

**The Yang state, which shelters all things**, is said to nurture and defend them,
*so that the Yin qi does not harm them*.
**Thrive (度)**, should be read as **attain (得)**.
**#38 (躆躆)**, an ancient form of *residence (宅)*.
**Residence (宅)**, means *dwelling (居)*.

**First (初一)**, neither praise nor blame,
*successfully upholding one’s own family*.
**#39 (测)** states: *Neither praise nor blame*—
*this is the constant Dao*.
**One (一)** is the lowest rank of dwelling in a family,
*the symbol of descendants*,
*and corresponds to the day’s brightness (昼)*.
**To uphold the constant Dao**,
*without blame or praise*,
*is the master of preserving the family*.

**Second (次二)**, the family lacks a *vessel (壶)*,
*the wife serves her mother-in-law*,
*or washes and anoints her*.
**#39 (测)** states: *The family lacks a vessel*—
*there is no means to pass it on*.
*The vessel*—
*that which receives from above to nourish below*.
**If the family lacks a vessel**, then the lower does not provide sustenance.
**Two (二)** is the middle rank of the lower body,
*the symbol of the wife*.
*The wife*—
*that which receives from the mother-in-law*.
**Now instead, the mother-in-law serves her**,
*washing and anointing her*,
*and bearing the laborious and humiliating tasks*,
*thus disrupting order above and below*—
*no greater inversion exists*.

**Third (次三)**, the order of elders and juniors is established,
*the son assists his father*.
**#39 (测)** states: *The son assists his father*—
*thus he can prosper*.
*The Fàn text originally reads* **long and young sequence (长幼序序)**,
*now follows the Sòng, Lù, and Wáng readings*.
**Wáng** states: *When one attains position, it is the day’s brightness*—
*thus, the dwelling has proper ranks*,
*elders and juniors each hold their place*,
*and the son can undertake his father’s affairs*.
**Guāng** states: *Three (三)* is the upper rank of the lower body,
*and corresponds to the day’s brightness*.
*The young can serve the old*,
*and the son can fulfill his father’s duties*.

**Fourth (次四)**, seeing a *wild boar (豕)* in the hall,
*the dog bounds it repeatedly (击之迒)*.
**#39 (测)** states: *Seeing a wild boar in the hall*—
*its form is not auspicious*.
**Derè (迒)**, pronounced *gāng*, also *hù-láng* (户郎).
**System (系)**, is the same as *tie (系)*.
**Derè (迒)**, *the tracks of a beast*.
*The dog and the wild boar*—
*both are unclean things*.
*The hall*—
*the place of the honored*.
**Four (四)** is the middle rank of the lower body,
*and yet it corresponds to the night*—
*a small person’s Dao*.
**The proper Dao for a family**,
*is to rectify oneself*,
*and then one can govern the family*.
**Now, four (四)** is in the hall,
*its own wild boar conduct present*,
*then those below will also act like dogs*,
*bounding forward in its tracks*—
*it cannot be restrained*.
**Auspicious (庆)**, means *good (善)*.

**Fifth (次五)**, the *stern and prow of vessels (舳舻)* are harmonized and settled,
*benefiting wealth and propriety*.
**#39 (测)** states: *The stern and prow are harmonized*—
*it follows its boundaries*.
**Zhú (舳)**, *straight-six* (直六).
**Lú (舻)**, *fall-Hu* (落胡).
**Wáng** states: *It does not lose its dwelling*—
*and yet nothing is too distant to reach*—
*the meaning of being on a vessel*.
**Five (五)**, having attained its proper position in the day’s brightness,
*is the master of the dwelling*—
*one who, when departing, will surely succeed*.
**Thus, the stern and prow are harmonized and settled**,
*and there is benefit in wealth and propriety*—
*does it not uphold the Dao of the dwelling?*
**Guāng** states: *Zhú (舳)*—
*the rear of a boat where the steering oar is placed*;
**Lú (舻)**—
*the front of a boat where the paddle is fixed*.
*Five (五)* is in the highest rank of dwelling,
*and corresponds to the day’s brightness*—
*a junzi who can govern his family*.
**If the stern and prow are harmonized and settled**,
*then the multitude relies on it for tranquility*;
**If superiors and inferiors are in accord**,
*then the family relies on it for governance*.
*Wealth*—
*the blessing of the family*.
*Wealth that does not lose its rectitude*—
*this is what makes it beautiful*.
**Its (其)**, should be read as **none (无)**.

**Sixth (次六)**, its *well and hearth (井灶)* are cast outside,
*three years and it sees betrayal*.
**#39 (测)** states: *Its well and hearth are cast outside*—
*three years and it does not enjoy (sustenance)*.
**Wáng** states: *Three years*—
*the end of a numerical cycle*.
**Guāng** states: *The well and hearth*—
*the resources for drink and food*,
*the essential duties of the family*.
**If they are cast outside**, then how can the family sustain itself?
**Six (六)** is beyond the middle and corresponds to the night—
*a small person who cannot harmonize his clan*,
*and instead drives them away*—
*not exceeding three years before kin all rebel*.
**Not enjoy (不享)**—
*not obtaining drink and food*.

**Seventh (次七)**, the *aged father harnesses the cart (老父擐车)*,
*the young maiden lifts the vessel (少女提壶)*,
*benefiting the father and the family*.
**#39 (测)** states: *The aged father harnesses the cart*—
*its form is now solemn*.
*The Wáng text reads* **consider (考)** as *at (于)*,
*now follows the Sòng, Lù, and Fàn readings*.
**Propose (拟)**, pronounced *huàn*.
**Harness (擐)**, means *pierce (贯)*.
*Piercing the cart’s pole with his hand to move it*—
*this is how things are carried*.
*Lifting the vessel*—
*receiving from above to nourish below*.
**Consider (考)**, means *complete (成)*.
**Seven (七)** is the upper rank of the dwelling,
*the symbol of the honored and lowly*.
*The aged father*—
*the most honored in the family*.
*The young maiden*—
*the most lowly in the family*.
*The honored can bear the multitude*,
*the lowly can receive from above*—
**thus it benefits in completing the family**.
*To govern the family*—
*one cannot do so without solemnity*—
**thus its form is solemn and majestic**,
*and only then can it bear the multitude*.
*The **Yì** states: *The Family of Man, resounding and bright*—
*regret, peril, and auspice*.
*The wife and children, laughing and cheerful*—
*end in sorrow*.
**Propose (拟)**—

**Eighth (次八)**, its *table (几)* is overturned,
*its legs doubled (朼)*,
*and its family does not prosper*.
**#39 (测)** states: *Its table overturned, its legs doubled*—
*the family’s affairs are not flourishing*.
*The Sòng and Lù readings* both have *female (牝)* for *leg (朼)*,
*Wáng has male (牡)*,
*now follows the Fàn reading*.
*The family*—
*Sòng and Lù read* **nine families (九家)**,
*Fàn and Xiǎo Sòng read* **table family (几家)**.
*According to the text*—
*ancient* **its (其)** *was written as* **not (丌)**,
*thus the error arose*.
*Now follows the Wáng reading*.

「**Legs (朼)** are the same as *bì* (匕).
When the *table (几)* must bear above, *bì* must be single—this is the natural order.
Eight is the midpoint of calamity and belongs to night; to overturn its *table* means the young do not uphold the old, and the superior finds no rest.

To double its *bì* is for the family not to be governed by its elders, while those who wield authority multiply.
**Edible (旨)** means *good*.
When the young fail to uphold the old and the elders do not govern the lowly, the family’s Dao is in ruin—thus it is not good.

**Upper Nine (上九)**, a stump sprouts suckers—its lineage does not perish.
*The divination states*: A stump sprouts suckers—its kind thrives.
*Sucker (蘖)*—read as *yúlìè* or *wǔgé*.
*Lineage (种)*—read as *zhāngyǒng*.
Wáng says: Nine resides in excess, a symbol of withered decay, yet it holds its proper position by day—thus it is a stump that sprouts suckers.
Guāng says: When wood is cut and regrows, it is called a sucker. #43
Nine marks the extreme of calamity—the family is already extinct.
Yet it falls upon the day’s night, meaning there remains a remnant lineage, capable of reviving the family.

**Two Regions, Two Prefectures, Two Departments, One Family Law**
The Yin family’s metal aligns with *Jǐng* (井).
To change a city but not its well—no loss, no gain—symbolizing orderly movement between wells by law.
In Yáng Gāo County, its laws are upheld; all things emulate its standards, none failing to conform.
*County (县)*—pronounced *xuán*.
Sòng says: *Mò (墨)* refers to the measuring lines of law.
Guāng says: At this time, the Yang qi reaches its peak, and all things mirror it, none escaping its governance.

**First (初一)**, establishing law without law.
*The divination states*: Establishing law without law—it is insufficient.
Wáng says: At the outset of making law, yet it fails its proper position by night—unworthy of emulation.
Guāng says: One signifies the beginning of thought, hence *establishing law*.
*The Law of Speech (法言)* says: *Mó* not modeling, *fàn* not ranging—how much harm has been done!

**Second (次二)**, modeling law with moderation—successful.
*The divination states*: Modeling law with moderation—what all share.
*Modeling (摹)* is the same as *mó*.
*Modeling* is like *making*.
Two signifies central thought, yet it falls by day.
To make law with moderation is only then effective.
*The Great Plan (洪范)*: The Royal Extremity—what all follow.

**Third (次三)**, the plumb-line fails to be upright—losing its compass and square.
*The divination states*: The plumb-line fails to be upright—its use is flawed.
*Pǔ (甫)* means *good*.
*Shuǎng (爽)* means *erroneous*.
If one cannot rectify oneself, how can one rectify others?

**Fourth (次四)**, the plumb-line and square—none dare defy my implementation.
*The divination states*: The plumb-line and square—this is personal conduct.
The gentleman first cultivates himself.
When his conduct is upright, orders need not be given for them to follow.

**Fifth (次五)**, deceptive and deceptive—jar with a hollow belly, well overflowing—yet none may drink.
*The divination states*: Jar with a hollow belly—this is not the Way of learning.
Sòng and Lù originally read *bīn* as *míng*; now follows the Fàn and Wáng reading.
*Plait (繘)*—pronounced *jú*.
*Hollow (窴)*—pronounced *tián*.
*Overflowing (潢)*—pronounced *huáng*.
Lù says: *Fāng (方)* means *Dao*.
Wáng says: A jar with a hollow belly cannot hold.
A well overflowing—this is the appearance of excess water.
Yet none may drink—this is because the method of drawing is wrong.
Guāng says: *Plait (繘)* is the rope for drawing.
*Deceptive and deceptive (谲陆陆)*—the sound of the rope descending.
If the jar’s belly fills first, water cannot enter.
Though the well overflows, none may drink.
The scholar who is empty receives others gladly.
Fifth resides at the well’s peak yet falls upon the night—petty men grow proud and full, unable to accept others.

**Sixth (次六)**, at the threads and the cords—demonstrate the pure radiance.
*The divination states*: At the threads and the cords—the great governance is clear.
Wáng says: To demonstrate the Dao of upright radiance, so that none are confused.
Guāng says: Six resides in the upper blessing, applying its law—able to govern the threads and cords of the world, demonstrating the pure radiance to others.

**Seventh (次七)**, a dense net cast into the abyss—unfavorable to scales.
*The divination states*: A dense net cast into the abyss—tyrannical laws are spread.
Wáng says: Seven resides in excess, yet falls by night.
To make laws too dense, nets hanging in the depths—fish cannot rest, laws applied to the state—people find no ease.
Guāng says: Seven is both *net* and *ruin*—falling upon the night’s day, laws are harsh and the people are terrified.
Thus it is said: A dense net cast into the abyss—unfavorable to scales.

**Eighth (次八)**, rectify the guilty—drive them to righteousness.
*The divination states*: Rectify the guilty—drive them onward.
*Drive (驱)* is the same as *lí*.
Sòng says: Drive the people to righteousness.
Wáng says: Eight resides in the upper body’s center, yet falls by day.
Able to apply its law, rectifying the guilty to bring none to wickedness.
Guāng says: *Gé (格)* means *to reach*.
To rectify the guilty and reach the wicked—this is the excellence of punishment.
Shùn says: Punishment aims at no punishment.

**Upper Nine (上九)**, the well lacks its rim—water flows straight—neither ravine nor valley—ending in transgression.
*The divination states*: The well lacks its rim—law is wantonly indulged.
Wáng’s reading of *fěixī fěigǔ* as *lìxīn fěixī*—now follows the Sòng, Lù, and Fàn reading.
*Rim (干)*—pronounced *hán*.
Fàn says: The rim checks and supports the well; drainage has its time.
Wáng says: At the extreme of law yet falling by night—this is lawlessness.
A state without law—people easily transgress.
A well without its rim—water overflows.
Guāng says: Nine marks the extreme of calamity, yet falls by night.
Law is wantonly indulged without restraint—equivalent to lawlessness.
The people’s will is confused, knowing not what to follow, thus they transgress and end in chaos.
Like a well without its rim, water flows straight—neither ravine nor valley—yet pours recklessly, ending in error.

**Two Regions, Two Prefectures, Two Departments, Two Family Laws**
The Yang family’s earth aligns with *Xián* (咸).*

The **Sixth Entry** follows in sequence, marking **eighteen divisions and five seconds**, corresponding to the **Summer Solstice qi**. Hence, it aligns with *Lí* (離).

When **Yang qi** reaches its peak above, **Yin’s omens** begin to stir below—**above and below resonate in harmony**.
*"Omens"* (信) are akin to **auspicious signs**.

On the **first day**, the **Six Stems** align as *Luó* (羅), the **Five Branches** as *Lí* (離).
The **Divination** states: *"Six Stems as Luó"*—**clinging to the Lord of Lí**.

On the **second day**, above follows the **Lunar Stations** (歷), below harmonizes with the **Lü** (律).
If misaligned, it is **not so**.
The **Divination** states: *"Above follows the Stations, below harmonizes with the Lü*—**this is not genuine**."
Fàn’s original text reads *"kěn"* (肯), now following the Sòng, Lù, and Wáng readings.
*"Harmonize"* (和) is pronounced *húwò* (胡卧).
*"Stations"* (歷) refers to the **Twelve Celestial Stems** (十二辰).
*"Lü"* (律) refers to the **Twelve Pitch Pipes** (十二管).
The **Celestial Stem** establishes the **Twelve Stems** above, while the **Lü** arranges the **Twelve Pipes** below—**above and below resonate in harmony**.
If they do not align, there is **no resonance**.

On the **third day**, **one vertical and one horizontal**—**the Celestial Net** (天網) is woven.
The **Divination** states: *"One vertical and one horizontal"*—**the warp and weft are arrayed**.
*"Vertical"* (从) is pronounced *róng* (容切).
*"Arrayed"* (，陈) is pronounced *lángwú* (郎吴) or *lángyán* (郎岩).
Fàn says: *"Arrayed"*—**a vast and expansive appearance**.

The King says: **"The Celestial Net"** (天網) embodies the meaning of **being sparse yet not leaking**.

**Master Guang** states: The **third day** (三) corresponds to **Thought** (思) above, and **daytime** (昼) is when. The **virtuous person** (君子) can **weave the warp and weft of Heaven and Earth** (经纬天地).

**Li** (离) signifies **cultural refinement** (文明), and also bears the **symbol of nets and traps** (网罟之象).

On the **fourth day** (四), **"I lift my nets and traps"** (援我罘罟), **"entangling snares spread across the fields"** (絓罗于野至). The **Divination** states: **"Lifting nets and traps"** (援次罘罟) is **unable to act with benevolence** (不能以仁). **"Lift"** (援) is pronounced *yuán* (爰切). **"Net"** (罘) is pronounced *fú* (浮切). **"Entangling"** (絓) is pronounced *húguà* (胡卦切). The **fourth day** (四) marks when **yang-qi** (陽氣) is about to **extinguish** (熄), while **yin-qi** (陰氣) is about to **arise** (生); **virtue** (德) departs and **punishment** (刑) arrives. Again, **Li** (离) bears the **symbol of nets and traps** (网罟之象), hence the saying **"I lift my nets and traps"** (援我罘罟), **"entangling snares spread across the fields"** (絓罗于野). **"Arrive"** (至) speaks of matters **drawing near**, as in the saying **"treading frost"** (履霜), **"solid ice arrives"** (坚冰至).

On the **fifth day** (五), **"the dragon soars in Heaven"** (龙翰于天), **"its scales tremble with solemnity"** (贞栗其鳞). The **Divination** states: **"The dragon’s trembling"** (龙翰之栗) signifies **uttermost fear of collapse** (极惧坠). **"Soar"** (翰) is pronounced *huán’ān* (胡安切). The King says: **Residing in the center** (居中) and **embodying rectitude** (体正), **obtaining its proper position** (得位) and **marking the time for response** (当画), it is the **master of correspondence** (应之主), hence the **symbol of the dragon flying in Heaven** (龙飞于天). **Master Guang** states: **The dragon** (龙) is used to **discuss yang** (论阳). **"Soar"** (翰) means **to fly** (飞). The **fifth day** (五) is **pure yang** (纯阳), the **peak of greatness** (盛大之极), hence the saying **"the dragon soars in Heaven"** (龙翰于天). When the **virtuous person** (君子) stands at the **peak of greatness** (盛大之极), he **must not fail to be upright** (不可不正) and **must not fail to be solemn** (不可不惧), hence the saying **"its scales tremble with solemnity"** (贞栗其鳞).

On the **sixth day** (六), **"the blazing yang ascends to Heaven"** (炽承于天), **"the ice begins to sprout from Earth"** (冰萌于地). The **Divination** states: **"Ascending Heaven, sprouting from Earth"** (承天萌地) signifies **the beginning of yang’s retreat** (阳始退). The **King’s reading** (王本) takes **"blazing yang"** (炽承) as **"ascending yang"** (烝云), where **"ascending"** (烝) is the ancient form of **"blazing"** (炽), now following the **Sòng, Lù, and Fàn readings** (今从宋、陆、范本). **"Blazing"** (炽) is the **peak of yang** (陽之盛). **"Ice"** (冰) is the **ultimate of yin** (陰之极). The **sixth day** (六) falls at the **start of the Summer Solstice** (夏至之初), the **juncture of yang’s peak and yin’s rise** (陽极陰生之际); **the Way of the petty man grows long** (小人道长), while **the Way of the virtuous person diminishes** (君子道消), hence the saying: **"the blazing yang ascends to Heaven"** (炽承于天), **"the ice begins to sprout from Earth"** (冰萌于地). **The subtle warnings of reverence and caution** (敬戒之微) are fully contained here.

On the **seventh day** (七), **"the sun strengthens its decline"** (日强其衰), **"response flourishes with solemnity"** (应蕃贞). The **Divination** states: **"Strengthening its decline"** (自强其衰) is **to hate the proliferation of ruinous influences** (恶败类). **"Hate"** (恶) is pronounced *wùgù* (乌故切). The **seventh day** (七) marks the **beginning of calamity** (祸始) and **the time for response** (当画). The **virtuous person**, if he **can strengthen his declining Way** (能自强其衰), will **find many responding to him** (则应之者蕃多) and **will not lose his rectitude** (而不失其正).

On the **eighth day** (八), **"at the peak of yang, summoning yin"** (极阳征阴), **"response follows without delay"** (不移日而应). The **Divination** states: **"At the peak of yang, summoning yin"** (极阳征阴) is **the response to its manifestation** (应其发). The **King’s reading** (王本) takes **"summoning"** (征) as **"subtle"** (微), and **"response"** (应) as **"time"** (时), now following the **Sòng, Lù, and Fàn readings** (今从宋、陆、范本). **When governance reaches its peak, disorder is summoned** (治极召乱), **when greatness reaches its peak, decline is summoned** (盛极召衰), **when fortune reaches its peak, calamity is summoned** (福极召祸), hence the response **without delay** (不移日而应).

On the **ninth day** (上九), **"at the ultimate of Li"** (元离之极), **the virtuous person responds with the Great Millet** (君子应以大稷). The **Divination** states: **"At the ultimate of Li"** (元离之极) is **unable to be restrained** (不可遏). **Fàn’s reading** (范本作) takes it as **"unable to be restrained"** (不可遏), now following the **Sòng, Lù, and Wáng readings** (今从宋、陆、王本). **Master Lù** (陆) says: **"Restrained"** (遏) means **to stop** (止). The **King** (王) says: **"Great"** (大) signifies **the sun approaching its setting** (日将暮). **Master Wú** (吴) says: **"Millet"** (稷) is pronounced and shares meaning with **"Wu"** (吴). **Master Guang** (光) states: **"Li"** (元离) is **Great Clarity** (大明). At the **ultimate of Great Clarity** (大明之极), **greatness at its peak must decline** (极盛必衰). The **virtuous person responds to the times** (君子应时), **aligning with their waxing and waning** (与之消息), hence the saying **"the virtuous person responds with the Great Wu"** (君子应以大吴).

*(End of the third volume of the "太玄经" (Tàixuān Jīng) annotations. The original text has been corrected as follows:)*

#2 *"二"* (two) was originally *"之"* (zhī), now corrected according to **Bèiyào** (备要) and **Quánshū** (全书) editions.

#6 *"得"* (dé) was originally missing, now supplemented according to **Bèiyào** (备要) and **Quánshū** (全书) editions.
#7 *"帅"* (shuài) was originally *"师"* (shī), now corrected according to **Bèiyào** (备要) and **Quánshū** (全书) editions.
#8 *"《易》曰"* (The *Yì* says) was originally missing, now supplemented according to **Bèiyào** (备要) and **Quánshū** (全书) editions.

**Collected Commentary on the *Tàixuān Jīng* (太玄经) Scroll Four: Two Directions, Two States, Two Divisions, Three Families in Welcome**

**Collected Commentary on the *Tàixuān Jīng* (太玄经) Scroll Four: Two Directions, Two States, Two Divisions, Three Families in Welcome**

---

**The Yin Family: Water, Aligned with *Xián* (咸)**
Water of the Yin Family is also aligned with *Xián*.
When welcoming in the second position, it is the day of *Shèyú Guǐ* (舍舆鬼).
Yin *qi* takes form below, and all things ascend to meet it.
*Sù* (溯), pronounced *sù*, as in *sù* (素).
Song [Commentary] says: *Sù* means "to turn toward."

**First Position:**
When welcoming others without proper response—no proper omen, only evil.
Cè [Commentary] says: Welcoming others without proper response means it is not something to be joined with.
Wang [Commentary] says: At the beginning of welcoming, if one loses proper position at night, welcoming without the correct method.
When things are not as they should be, and one responds by welcoming them, one loses the proper path and falls into evil.
Guāng [Commentary] says: Welcoming aligns with *Xián*.
*Xián* means "to be moved by."
The first position is the beginning of thought, corresponding to night—being moved by external things that are not proper responses.

**Second Position:**
The *jiāo* (蛟) hides in the depths, and the *líng* (陵) transforms its eggs.
People may speak in secret, and the common folk agree with it.
Cè says: The transformation of the hidden *jiāo* is the manifestation of sincere intent.
The *Wángběn* sometimes reads *yǒu* (有), while the *Xiǎo Sòngběn* sometimes reads *rén yīnyán* (人言或阴) or *rényán yīn* (人言或阴); now following the Sòng, Lù, and Fàn editions.
*Hé* (和), pronounced *wò* (胡卧切).
Fàn [Commentary] says: The *jiāo* hides in the water, lays eggs on high banks, then returns to the depths—*qi* responds and interacts, and only then does it split and transform.
Guāng says: The second position is the middle of thought, corresponding to day.
When a sage’s sincerity reaches its peak, nothing remains impenetrable.
The *Yì* says: *"The cuckoo cries in the Yin, and its young respond in harmony."*

**Third Position:**
The subtle and refined *qi* moves back and forth; omens and spirits are aware first.
Cè says: The movement of subtle *qi* is an omen of misfortune.
The third position is the end of thought, corresponding to night.
At the juncture of heaven and humanity, when *qi* and omens interact, if humans stray from the Dao, omens and spirits become aware first.

**Fourth Position:**
The lower garment has a child’s clothing, men’s eyes are like pearls, women sneeze with hooked breath—auspicious.
Cè says: The lower garment having a child’s clothing is the Yin responding to the Yang.
The *Wángběn* reads *shèng* (盛) for *gǎn* (感); now following the other editions.
*Rú* (襦), pronounced *rú* (儒), meaning short clothing.
*Hàn* (啑), pronounced *jiǎ* (色甲切). The *Wú* edition reads *tì* (嚏), pronounced *dì* (帝).
**Fifth Position:**
Yellow rides on *Pǐ* (否)—auspicious.
Cè says: Yellow riding on *Pǐ* is not to be partnered with.
*Pǐ* (乘), pronounced *shí* (时证切).
Wang says: Though the fifth position is central, it is in the Yin at night, improper and riding on the middle position—thus, "Yellow rides on *Pǐ*, auspicious."
Guāng says: Improper in position, insufficient for union.

**Sixth Position:**
The Dark and Yellow meet in welcoming; their intentions are mutually responsive.
Cè says: The Dark and Yellow meeting in welcoming is responding by kind.
Fàn says: Heaven is Dark, Earth is Yellow.
When Heaven and Earth meet in welcoming, winds and rains adjust in season;
When ruler and minister meet in welcoming, governance and teaching follow measure.
Guāng says: The sixth position is the utmost extent, the peak of responsiveness.
From Heaven and Earth down to all things, from ruler to subject, from husband to wife, from friend to friend—none do not respond by kind.

**Seventh Position:**
Far [is] ——, near [is] *pǒu* (掊), welcoming *fù* (父) unexpectedly meets.
Cè says: Far ——, near *pǒu*—losing the kind of father.
The *Xiǎo Sòng* reads ——; now following the other editions.
——, pronounced *hòu* (许候切), meaning angry, glaring.
*Pǒu* (掊), pronounced *hòu* (普后切), meaning to strike.
*Jiā* (迦), same as *xiè* (邂), pronounced *xiè* (蟹).
*Hòu* (逅), pronounced *hòu* (后), meaning to meet unexpectedly.

**Eighth Position:**
Blood is seen entering the gate; bowing to welcome in the central courtyard.
Cè says: Blood seen entering the gate is using virtue for self-defense.
The Sòng and Lù editions read *jiāo* (椒) for *jiān* (捬); now following the Fàn and Wáng editions.
*Jiān* (捬), pronounced *fǔ* (府), meaning to defend.
The eighth position is the midpoint of misfortune, hence "blood is seen entering the gate"—those who cause harm have arrived.
At the day’s noon, one who can defend with virtue, welcoming and repelling in the central courtyard—things cannot harm, hence "defending welcome in the central courtyard."

**Ninth Position:**
Moisture welcomes at the bed’s foot, accumulating at walls and houses.
Cè says: Moisture welcoming at the bed’s foot is ruin within.
The *Xiǎo Sòng* and *Wángběn* read *zé* (泽) for *shī* (湿); now following the Sòng, Lù, and Fàn editions.
The Fàn and Wáng editions read *fǎn* (罦) for *lěi* (累); now following the Sòng and Lù editions.
Fàn says: *Fǎn* means "to cover."
Now following the Sòng, Lù, and Fàn editions for *diān* (颠), though the *Wángběn* reads *yuàn* (愿).
*Lěi* (累), pronounced *ruì* (良瑞切).
The ninth position is the extreme of misfortune, corresponding to night.
It is through which petty men and women can overturn states—not the work of a single day or night, but a gradual process.
Like moisture welcoming at the bed’s foot, seeping upward to accumulate at walls and houses, beyond remedy.
Examining the origins of ruin, it arises from within.

Two regions, two states, three departments, one household encountering
**Yang Fire**—corresponds to *Guà* (姤).
Upon entering, meeting the first day, the sun rests at *Liǔ* (柳).
Yin-qi begins to arrive, Yang-qi begins to depart, their comings and goings meeting.

**First day:** *Yōu* (幽) meets *Shén* (神) and *Shī* (师), the omen is *zhēn* (贞).
**Cè** says: *Yōu* meeting *Shén* is the thought of attaining principle.
**Wáng** says: At the start of *Yōu*, the spirit meets but has not yet manifested—it is the symbol of a hidden communion with the divine.
**Guāng** says: One is the beginning of thought, and it corresponds to day.
With sincere devotion, one attains communion with *Shén* and *Shī*, even if it manifests in dreams, yet it does not lose its rectitude—just as when King Zhōu dreamed of Fù Yuè.
Dreams are matters difficult to verify.
Yet when sincerity reaches its peak, even dreams may be verified—how much more so when consulting with advisors and receiving blessings?

**Second day:** *Chōngchōng* (冲冲) child meets, an ambiguous decree.
**Cè** says: *Chōngchōng* child is the unworthy son.
The *Fàn* edition reads *bù shòu dìng* (不受定) for *bù dìng* (不定), now following the other editions.
**Wáng** says: To meet without intention is *chōngchōng*.
A child is a term for one in ignorance and confusion.
Thus, what certainty can there be in what it meets?
This is so because it is out of place, corresponding to night, and does not meet appropriately.
The unworthy son is clearly one without understanding.

**Third day:** Neither going nor coming, attaining the virtue of *shì* (士) and *nǚ* (女).
**Cè** says: Neither going nor coming is the proper conduct of *shì* and *nǚ*.
The *Fàn* and *Wáng* editions read *bù wǎng bù lái bù qiú* (不往不来不求), but **Cè** says *bù wǎng bù qiú* (不往不求); now following the *Sòng* and *Lù* editions.
**Sòng** says: *Zé* (则) means law.
**Guāng** says: Those who boast and self-promote are the shameful conduct of *shì* and *nǚ*.
Thus, neither going nor coming yet meeting naturally is the proper conduct of *shì* and *nǚ*.

**Fourth day:** [Blank], the *Duì* (兑) person meets rain—**calamity**.
**Cè** says: The *Duì* person meeting rain is self-inflicted ruin.
**Wáng** says: [Blank], the appearance of boldness without propriety.

**Fifth day:** Hunting meets beasts, none forbid it.
**Cè** says: Hunting meets beasts—this is truly worth emulation.
Five is the peak of fortune, and it corresponds to day.
As when hunting meets beasts, if captured, who can resist taking them?

**Sixth day:** *Bǐ* (俾) the spider weaves its snares, meeting a bee.
Though the gain is great, one cannot follow.
**Cè** says: The spider’s snares bring harm near at hand.
The bee is the same as *fēng* (蜂).
**Fàn** says: To command the spider to weave snares and catch a bee.
The bee is a stinging insect, thus unable to be controlled.
Though it may seem profitable, one cannot follow and take it.
**Guāng** says: Six is the highest blessing, yet it corresponds to night.
The petty man, seeing gain, is eager to seize it, yet cannot attain his desire.

**Seventh day:** Shaking its horns, the lord and father meet insult—this is not the proper command.
**Cè** says: Shaking its horns is the straight path in action.
Seven is the beginning of calamity, and it corresponds to day.
If the lord and father unfortunately meet insult, then the virtuous man shakes his horns, follows the straight path, and dies—it may not be the proper command, yet dying righteously leaves no choice.

**Eighth day:** Two *sì* (兕) battle, one loses its horn—unable to prevail, suffering loss.
**Cè** says: Two *sì* battling means losing a horn and suffering loss.
Victory, *shì* (诗证切).
Loss, *sàng* (息浪切).
Eight is the midpoint of calamity, and it corresponds to night.
As when two *sì* meet and battle, one loses its horn, it cannot prevail and suffers death.
The horn symbolizes the warrior who resists insult.
**Ninth day:** Someone takes its horn, meeting below and damaging the foot.
**Cè** says: Someone takes its horn—how can it be withstood?
The *Fàn* and *Wáng* editions read *dǐ qí jiǎo* (抵其角) for *huò shì qí jiǎo* (或氏其角); now following the *Sòng* and *Lù* editions.
*Dǐ* (抵), *dū lǐ* (都礼切).

---
Two regions, two states, three departments, two households at the stove:
**Yin Wood**—corresponds to *Dǐng* (鼎).
Entering the stove, the ninth position, twenty-eight degrees twenty-eight seconds, the sun’s course at *Jūn Huǒ* (鹑火), the *xiǎo shǔ* (小暑) *qì* responds.
The *Dòu* constellation aligns with *Wèi* (未), the *lǜ* (律) resonates with *Lín Zhōng* (林钟).
The *Dǐng* boils abundantly to nourish sages and worthies, thus the stove abounds with symbols of nurturing the virtuous.
Though Yin may pour and scatter, Yang still holds and harmonizes it.
*Hé* (龢), an ancient form of *hé* (和); same below.
**Sòng** says: The stove harmonizes Yin and Yang, thus sometimes it pours, sometimes it harmonizes—thus it is so.
**Guāng** says: Yin scatters, Yang harmonizes—this is the symbol of cooking.

**First day:** The stove lacks substance, begging from neighbors.
**Cè** says: The stove lacks substance—it has an empty name.
**Wáng** says: At the start of the stove’s position, out of place and corresponding to night, it lacks substance.
Having lost the way of cooking, it cannot provide food, thus it begs from neighbors.
**Guāng** says: One is the beginning of thought, yet it corresponds to night—the petty man, inwardly lacking substance, steals others’ virtues to make a name for himself.

**Second day:** The yellow *Dǐng* stands firm, its interior spacious—neither drinking nor eating, yet sincere and without harm.
**Cè** says: The yellow *Dǐng* stands firm—its central integrity is virtuous.
**Wáng** says: The *Dǐng* is the stove’s vessel.
*Jiè* (介) means standing alone.
Its spacious interior signifies broad tolerance and receptivity.
**Guāng** says: Two is in the lower body’s center and corresponds to day, a position of *fāng jǔ* (方沮), yet not yet attaining lower blessing.
The virtuous man guards the middle path, retreats to cultivate himself, and does not take food lightly.
Nourishing his resolve inwardly, he does not covet externals, thus though he neither drinks nor eats, his sincerity truly harms nothing.

**Third day:** The stove lacks firewood, yet gold flows nearby.
**Cè** says: The stove lacks firewood—it is unused.
*Pín* (濒), pronounced *pín* (频).

**Fourth day:** The *Gē* (鬲) is filled with food, attaining its labor and effort.
**Cè** says: The *Gē* filled with food is timely nourishment for me.
*Gē* (鬲), pronounced *lì* (历).
**Wáng** says: *Gē* is the smaller cooking vessel.
**Guāng** says: A *Dǐng* with a broad base is called *Gē*.
Four is the lower blessing, and it corresponds to day.
The virtuous man uses blessings to nourish the worthy, even if little, yet attains their labor and effort.

**Fifth day:** The *Dǐng* is large enough for libation—yet neither solemn nor orderly.
**Cè** says: The *Dǐng* large enough for libation—feasting without sincerity.
*Qí* (齐), pronounced *cè* (侧) and *qiè* (皆).
**Wáng** says: There is no intent of grand feasting with solemnity.
**Guāng** says: *Shāng* (觞), should be read *shāng* (商), meaning "to cook."
Five occupies the position of abundance, and thus can nourish the worthy, hence it is said: The *Dǐng* is large enough for libation.
Yet if, though it corresponds to the day’s night, one cannot treat the scholars of the world with propriety, then all scholars will refuse to return.

The *Hong Fan* says: "All those who are upright, when they have grown rich, will then govern the people."

**Sixth day:** The five flavors are blended, harmonized like fine soup—this is the feast of the great sage.
**Cè** says: The feast of blended flavors—this is the duty of the prime minister.
**Wáng** originally wrote *měi* (美) as *gēng* (羹), but the *Xiǎo Sòng* version reads: "Five flavors harmonized, rich as fine soup," now following the *Sòng, Lù, and Fàn* editions.
**Wáng** says: The duty of the prime minister is to blend the five flavors; when the great sage finds the right person, he feasts them accordingly.
**Guāng** says: Six is the highest blessing, corresponding to day.
The virtuous man assists the state, offering what is right and rejecting what is wrong, advancing the worthy and removing the unworthy, harmonizing governance like blending flavors into fine soup, and presenting it to the ruler.
If the ruler then feasts them, the world will be in great order.

High King Zǒng’s command says: If you make the blended soup, you must use salt and plum sauce.
The Three Dukes serve the ruler, bearing the symbol of the *Dǐng*.

**Seventh day:** The cow’s fat is properly rendered, yet without washing the axe before boiling, the sickness of *Ōu* and *Yù* will come.
**Cè** says: The cow’s fat and *Ōu-Yù*—this is an unclean resolve.
*Fāng* (肪), pronounced *fāng* (方).
*Ōu* (欧), pronounced *wū-hòu* (乌后).
*Yù* (鴂), pronounced *cuī-dōu* (衰都).
**Fàn** says: *Ōu-Yù* is the sound of vomiting.
**Wáng** says: *Pí-niú* (脂牛) is a fat cow.
**Guāng** says: *Fāng* (肪) is fat. *Ōu* (欧) is to vomit. *Yù* (鴂) is when the heart dislikes something and one vomits.
The fat of a fat cow is used to illustrate fine blessings.
Though the ruler may nourish scholars with fine blessings, if he does not approach them with sincere and pure resolve, the scholars will surely despise him.
Seven marks the beginning of calamity, the omen of losing the hearts of the worthy.

**Eighth day:** Eating its leftovers, though one calls out, it is not ruined.
**Cè** says: Eating its leftovers—this is to cover one’s virtue.
*Wěi* (委), pronounced *yú-wěi* (於伪).
*Jiào* (噭), same as *jiào* (叫).

**Ninth day (uppermost):** The stove’s fire is extinguished—this is the family’s calamity.
**Cè** says: The stove’s fire extinguished—this is the state’s traitor.
The stove’s fire extinguished illustrates not nourishing the worthy.
Those who do not nourish the worthy are self-made traitors to their state.
Examples include: Jiè killing Guān Lóngróng, Zhòu killing Bǐ Gān, Fú Chā killing Wú Yuán, and Xiàng Yǔ driving Fàn Zēng—they are all of this kind.

*(Note: The final line appears fragmented; "二方二州三部三家大 阳家金，准丰" may require further contextual clarification for precise translation.)*

**Hexagram 34: *Dà* (大) – The Great**

**Initial Line:**
*Yīn* emptiness within, *yáng* swelling without—
Things conform like a basin and its lid.
*Fàn* and *Wáng* originally read "within" for the first part; now following *Sòng* and *Lǔ*.
*Fàn* originally read "swelling" as *féng* (逢); now following *èr Sòng*, *Lǔ*, and *Wáng*.
*Sòng* says: *Péng* (蓬) means "to fill."
*Guāng* says: *Yǔ* (與) means "all."
All things like a basin and its lid—
Outer grandeur vast, inner emptiness.

---
**First (initial) line:**
The abyss profound and overflowing—
Encompassing without bounds, obscure.
**Cè** says: The abyss profound and overflowing—
A treasure wrapped without measure.
*Wáng* reads *guǒ* (裹) as *huái* (怀); now following all other texts.
*Hǎng* (潢), pronounced *huáng* (黄).
One: water, also the beginning of thought, corresponding to day.
The noble’s heart, like the abyss profound and overflowing,
Embraces all without exclusion—
Thus its greatness.
Residing in the lowest body, obscure—
Hidden yet unseen.

---
**Second line:**
Enlarge his thoughts, his person burns in the furnace.
**Cè** says: Enlarging his thoughts—
His mind is injured by excess.
*Lú* (炉), pronounced *lǜ* (虑).
*Wáng* says: In times of greatness, misplaced at night,
Contrary to what is fitting—
Yet he strives to broaden his thoughts,
But his wisdom cannot encompass all,
And he harms himself in return.
*Lú* is a grinding tool.
Over-grinding brings injury.
*Guāng* says: Two is the middle of thought, corresponding to night.
A petty man with small wisdom yet grand schemes.

As the *Shī* says:
*"No plowing of the fields, only weeds grow tall and proud.
No thought for distant men, laboring with weary heart."*

---
**Third line:**
Great yet not self-aggrandizing—
Beneficial to achieve greatness.
**Cè** says: Great yet not self-aggrandizing—
Building from the small.
*Wáng* says: In proper position at day,
Thus he does not exalt himself,
Preserving humility,
Using the small to accumulate,
And thereby attaining true greatness.
*Guāng* says: Three is the upper of thought, corresponding to day.
The noble’s ambition vast, his heart modest—
Thus he fulfills his greatness.

---
**Fourth line:**
Enlarge his gates and outer precincts—
His blade lost, a hollow cry.
**Cè** says: Enlarging his gates and outer precincts—
Replacing substance with renown.
*Wáng* says: Sound without substance—
Thus it is called a "hollow cry."
*Guāng* says: Gates are external, precincts distant.
The blade is for severing.
A "cry" is the spread of name.
Four is external excess, corresponding to night.
A petty man neglects his inner self,
Yet labors to enlarge his outer facade—
Abandoning the near for the distant.
His blade lost, he cannot sever.
Though his name resounds far,
His essence remains hollow.

---
**Fifth line:**
Encompassing the barren yet centered—
Overcoming.
**Cè** says: Encompassing the barren yet centered—
Subduing the Nine *Yí*.
*Wáng* reads *jiǔ* (九) as *sì* (四); now following all other texts.
*Fàn* says: *Kè* (克) means "able."
*Guāng* says: Five occupies the central position, corresponding to day.
The Sage upholds the Great Middle Way,
Capable of embracing all under Heaven.

---
**Sixth line:**
The great loses the small,
The many loses the few.
**Cè** says: The great loses the small—
Calamity arises from the subtle.
*Wáng* reads *cè* (测) as *huò zhōng fā* (祸中发); now following all other texts.
*Fàn* says: Six is water.
Water’s loss lies in its crevices.
Affairs arise from the minute;
Calamity begins subtly.
*Guāng* says: Six is the middle of excess, corresponding to night.
The onset of great loss.

---
**Seventh line:**
Great extravagance and remoteness—
Self-diminished by the *gū*,
Yet sometimes benefited by *bū*.
**Cè** says: Extravagance and remoteness self-diminished—
Able to censure himself.
*Gū* (觚), the odd and solitary.
*Bū* (餔), pronounced *bó gū* (博孤).
*Fàn* says: *Gū* is law.
*Guāng* says: *Shē* (奢), like *tuō* (哆).
*Yú* (迂), distant.
*Bū*, food.
Seven is the beginning of calamity, corresponding to day.
Greatness already excessive, reaching distant extravagance.
The noble, perceiving the subtle,
Knows calamity is near,
And with law restrains himself—
Thus he receives further blessings and provisions.

---
**Eighth line:**
Abundant walls, steep foundations—
Three years without reinforcement, they collapse.
**Cè** says: The steepness of abundant walls—
Their collapse is not delayed.
*Zhǐ* (址), same as *zhǐ* (趾).
*Fàn* says: *Fēng* (丰), great.
*Qiào* (峭), steep.
*Zhǐ*, the foot—meaning the base.
Three, completion.
Great walls with steep foundations,
If not reinforced year after year,
Risk crumbling.
Just as the noble’s Way—
If its roots are not nurtured,
Its branches will surely perish.
*Guāng* says: Eight is within calamity, corresponding to night.
The petty man, unaware of impending ruin,
Seeks only to magnify himself,
Neglecting his foundation.

---
**Ninth (terminal) line:**
Greatness completed yet diminished—
*Pǐ* departs beyond Heaven.
**Cè** says: Greatness completed yet diminished—
The small becomes the great’s resource.
*Wáng* originally lacked *yǐ* (以); now following all other texts.
*Sòng*, *Lǔ*, and *Fàn* read *zī* (资) as *zhì* (质); now following *Yú* and *Wáng*.
Two regions, three states, one department, one family—*kuò* (廓).
*Yīn* family, water—also *fēng* (丰).
Entering *kuò*, the fourth line: the seven stars of day.
*Yīn* qi buried yet pondered,
*Yáng* yet expansive and vast.
*Sòng* and *Lǔ* originally read "obscured and hidden"—
Now following *Fàn* and *Wáng*.
*Chén* says: *Yì* (瘗), pronounced *kǔ xié* (苦协).
*Yīn* ( ), pronounced *hé* (合).
Thus *yì* is written as .
*Wú* says: *Yì*, pronounced *yú jì* (於计), meaning "stillness."
Thus the character should follow #6.
*Sòng* says: , meaning "to gather."
At this time, *yīn* qi responds by concealing itself below,
While *yáng* qi remains robust and vast above.
The diagram says: "Empty within, vast without—this is *kuò*."
*Fàn* says: *Yì*, to harmonize.
, to unite.
*Wáng* says: *Yīn* qi still weak,
Buried and harmonized.
, the ancient *shè* (歙) character.
*Guāng* says: *Yīn* qi concealed and unified,
Enclosing all things,
While *yáng* qi yet strives to expand—
Thus *kuò*.
*Kuò* means to stretch and enlarge.

On the first day, *kuò* expands and spreads, yet its foundation is not set aright.
**Cè** says: *Kuò* expands and spreads—its foundation is already tilted.

**Wáng** says: At the outset of *kuò*, one loses one’s proper place at night. Though it expands and spreads, it cannot correct its beginning.

**Guāng** says: One (the first day) is the thought of origin, yet it falls to night.
The petty man strives to expand and spread, yet fails to establish his foundation, hence his actions are prone to collapse.

On the second day, gold beams and jade pillars support the *kuò*—fortified within the city.
**Cè** says: Gold beams and jade pillars—proliferating and upright support.

**Fàn** says: Gold and jade are both of fine quality.

**Wáng** says: Two resides in the lower body’s center, occupying its proper place by day.
Gold and jade as beams and pillars—this is the attainment of worthy ministers.
Beams and pillars are the tools of *bǎn zhù* (板筑, rammed-earth construction).

**Guāng** says: The tools of *bǎn zhù*—the side is called *gàn* (干), the lintel *chuí* (桢).
Two is the thought of the middle, yet it falls to day.
The noble one can use worthy and wise ministers as support, expanding his virtue to defend himself.

On the third day, *kuò* has no heir, its chamber a stone maiden.
**Cè** says: *Kuò* has no heir—how then can there be descendants?

**Wáng** says: To take a stone maiden as wife—there is no way to continue the line.

**Guāng** says: Three is the thought of the upper, yet it falls to night.
The petty man’s thoughts expand and spread, yet he cannot sustain them, hence *kuò* has no heir.
To take a stone maiden as wife—this means seeking a spouse and finding only a stone maiden.

On the fourth day, he expands his gates and doors, using *yù* (圉) to repel bandits and barbarians.
**Cè** says: To expand his gates and doors—this is great enterprise.
Originally *yù* was used to mean "to defend," but the *Wáng* text reads *yòng gù* (用固), and the *Xiǎo Sòng* text reads *yòng yù* (用御)#7. Now following the *Sòng* and *Lǔ* texts.
*Yù* is the same as *yù* (御, to defend).

Fourth is the external "other," yet it falls to day.
The noble one expands his rites and propriety to defend against the petty, hence: "Expands his gates and doors, using *yù* to repel bandits and barbarians."

On the fifth day, the Heavenly Gate opens wide, expanding the hall’s steps—yet there are flaws in its birth.
**Cè** says: The Heavenly Gate opens wide—his virtue cannot fill the hall.
The *Wáng* text reads *huī dāng* (恢当) instead of *huī táng* (恢堂), and the *Èr Sòng*, *Lǔ*, and *Wáng Cè* texts lack the *táng* (堂) character. Now following the *Fàn* text.

Fifth is within the *kuò* family yet occupies the peak position, hence: "The Heavenly Gate opens wide, expanding the hall’s steps"—speaking of openness and exaltation.
Yet during the day’s night, the petty cannot enjoy this great fortune, his virtue failing to fulfill his station, inevitably leading to errors and downfall.

On the sixth day, *wéi fēng wéi chóng* (维丰维崇), the myriad ministers *féng féng* (冯冯), their virtue rising.
**Cè** says: *Wéi fēng wéi chóng*—this is true peace.
The *Wáng* text reads *yù* (欲) instead of *zī* (兹). Now following the other texts.
*Féng* is the ancient form of *píng* (凭).
*Féng féng*—abundant and flourishing.

Sixth is the utmost greatness, yet it falls to day.
The noble one expands his virtue to achieve true peace.
Abundant and towering, all states submit in obedience, *féng féng*—teeming and thriving, like swift steeds converging from all directions.

On the seventh day, its exterior is vast and *gē* (杚), yet its interior is lost.
The noble one retreats to the wilds, the petty man enters the hall.
**Cè** says: Its exterior is vast and *gē*—there is no one within.
The *Fàn* and *Xiǎo Sòng* texts read *kū* (扢)#8. Now following the *Sòng*, *Lǔ*, and *Wáng* texts.
The *Sòng* and *Lǔ* texts read *zhì shì* (至室), while *Wáng* reads *zài shì* (在室). Now following the *Fàn* text.

**Chén** says: *Kū* (扢), pronounced *qì* (讫), meaning "to rub."
**Wáng** says: *Gē* is a *dǒu gài* (斗概, beam end).
**Wú** says: *Kū* is the same as *gài* (概), pronounced *kē ài* (柯爱).
The *Shuōwén* (说文) reads *gù mò* (古没), meaning "level."
**Guāng** says: *Gē* should be read as *gē* (圪), pronounced *yú yǐ* (鱼乙), meaning "tall and robust."

Seventh is within the *kuò* family yet occupies the upper body, hence: "Its exterior is vast and *gē*—speaking of its expansive and towering grandeur."
Yet during the day’s night, the petty man, though in a position of greatness, grows arrogant, distancing the worthy while nearing the unworthy—chaos arises from within, hence: "Yet its interior is lost, the noble one retreats to the wilds, the petty man enters the hall."

Another saying: *Kū* (扢), pronounced *xǔ qì* (许讫), meaning "joy."

On the eighth day, it expands its exterior, empties its interior—profitable for drumming and *zhēng* (钲).

「The *Zè* commentary states: When the exterior is expansive and the interior is empty, then it can be heard.

The eighth hexagram resides in the exterior and corresponds to the day.
The noble one expands the exterior to manifest **virtue/power**, empties the interior to receive all things, thus enabling their name to be heard afar.
It is like the drum and *zhēng* (a ceremonial bell) — both expand outward and are hollow within, yet produce sound.
The noble one’s **virtue/power** lies in the exterior, and when it is broad and expansive, it is solely for the sake of their reputation.

The ninth hexagram, at its peak, reaches the extreme of expansiveness in *gāoyōng* (高庸), and for three years, there are no children.
The *Zè* commentary states: To reach the extreme of expansiveness in *gāoyōng*, it will ultimately have no one to serve.
*Yōng* (庸) is the same as *táng* (塘, pond/dike).
The *Guāng* commentary says: When residing at the highest extreme yet corresponding to night,
The petty man strives to make their walls and dikes grandly expansive yet lacks any children or laborers within.
High without people, regret is inevitable.

The *Guāng* commentary further states: The ninth hexagram resides at the uppermost extreme, yet corresponds to night.
The petty man is obsessed with building lofty, expansive structures yet does not know when to stop, hence: "Expansive at *gāoyōng*."
Unable to lower their ambitions and connect with those beneath them, the lower ones will rebel, hence: "For three years, there are no children."

---
**Two sides, three states, one section, two families:**
The *Yáng* (陽) family is associated with fire, akin to *hǎn* (涣, disperse).」

「The *Yáng* (陽) family takes *hǎn* (涣) as *huàn* (焕, radiant), thus naming its first section **Wén** (文).

Upon entering **Wén**, the ninth hexagram, it is said: *"Day abandons its splendor."* (舍张)
Yin (陰) gathers its substance, while Yang (陽) disperses its form.
Form and substance intermingle, and all things shine brightly.

The first line: *"Pure and unadorned"* (祫).
What is *màn* (缦)? It is *yùzhēn* (玉贞, jade-like purity) #9.
The *Guāng* commentary states: *"Pure and unadorned"* (拾何缦), for form is within.
*Màn* (缦), pronounced *huì* (会), is akin to *zhuì* (缋, embroidered silk).
*何* (何), pronounced *bèi* (被, to cover), is *màn* (缦) without adornment.
The ninth line marks the beginning of thought, and it corresponds to daytime.
The sage cultivates inner form and outer substance, like applying embroidered designs to a plain garment (衿) and covering it with unadorned silk (缦服).
Pure yet containing subtle patterns, like the true nature of jade—its beauty is perfect. (纯素含章, 如玉之正, 美之至也)
The *Zhōngyōng* (中庸) says: *"Wearing brocade yet valuing plainness, for one fears the ostentation of its patterns."* (衣锦尚絅, 恶其文之着也)

The second line: *"Form flourishes, substance is lacking."* (文蔚质否)
The *Guāng* commentary states: *"Form flourishes, substance is lacking"* (文蔚质否), for they cannot coexist harmoniously.
The *Guāng* commentary further explains: *"Arising"* (蔚) means delicate and intricate in appearance.
The second line represents the midpoint of thought, yet corresponds to night.
Though the petty man’s form may be ornate, his substance cannot truly match it.
*Níng Yíng* (宁嬴) of the *Fù* (父) section #10 says: *"Ornate yet lacking substance—where resentment gathers."* (华而不实, 怨之所聚也)

The third line: *"Great form yet more substance, trust seems insufficient."* (大文弥朴, 孚似不足)
The *Guāng* commentary states: *"Great form yet more substance"* (大文弥朴), for substance has excess.
The *Guāng* commentary further clarifies: *"Primal"* (弥) is here taken as *"zhēn"* (珍, treasured), and *"pǔ"* (朴) as *"pú"* (璞, uncarved jade), following the readings of *Sòng* (宋), *Lù* (陆), and *Fàn* (范).
*Náfù* (孚) means great trust.
The third line represents the end of thought, yet corresponds to daytime.
The sage’s great form appears simple, yet his trust is profound—seeming insufficient yet truly boundless.

The fourth line: *"Adorned like *bīn* (彬), striped like *fěi* (斐), patterned like *bīn* (彬) and *fěi* (斐) as tiger and leopard."* (斐如邠如, 虎豹文如)
It is not what Heaven bestows, hence: *"Lacking."* (匪天之享, 否)
The *Guāng* commentary states: *"Adorned like *bīn* (斐邠) yet lacking"* (斐邠之否), what is there to praise?
*Bīn* (邠) is akin to *bīn* (彬, harmonious).
*Fěi* (否), pronounced *bǐ* (鄙, vulgar), is here taken as *"fěi"* (斐, striped).
*Fàn* (范) says: *"Tigers and leopards are prized for their patterns."* (虎豹之兽, 以其文贵)
*Fěi* (斐) and *bīn* (邠) describe form in its most elaborate state. (文盛貌也)
The *Guāng* commentary further explains: The fourth line is at the midpoint yet corresponds to night, akin to tigers and leopards—using form to harm oneself, not what Heaven bestows, hence: *"Lacking."* (盖同虎豹, 以文害其躬, 匪天所享, 故曰否也)
*Xiǎng* (享) means to face or turn toward.

The fifth line: *"Shining like *biāo* (彪), resplendent with form, distinguished with virtue, and adorned with plainness."* (炳如彪如, 尚文昭如, 车服庸如)
The *Guāng* commentary states: *"Shining like *biāo* (彪)"* (彪如在上, 天文炳也).
The *Guāng* commentary further clarifies: *"Resplendent with form"* (尚文昭如) is taken as *"shàng wén zhāo rú"* (尚文昭如), following the readings of *Sòng* (宋), *Lù* (陆), and *Fàn* (范).
The *Guāng* commentary explains: The fifth line resides at the central position, with a proper place corresponding to daytime, as the master of form and virtue, radiantly observable.
*Shàng wén zhāo rú* (尚文昭如): The sage-king values and displays the form of rituals and virtue, making them clearly distinguishable.
*"Adorned with plainness"* (车服庸如): It speaks of using carriages and garments to display the virtuous deeds of the worthy, for the greatest use of form is found here.
The *Shùn Diǎn* (舜典) says: *"Spread their words in speech, test their virtue with deeds, and adorn them with carriages and garments according to their merit."* (敷纳以言, 明试以功, 车服以庸)

The sixth line: *"Vast form without measure, unrestrained at the river."* (鸿文无范, 恣于川)
The *Guāng* commentary states: *"Vast form without measure"* (鸿文无范), for one acts at will.
*Fàn* (范) means law or standard.
The *Guāng* commentary further explains: *"Wild geese flying"* (鸿雁之飞) may resemble script or patterns, yet they lack any law or standard #11; encountering a river, they simply act as they please.
The sixth line exceeds the central position yet corresponds to night.
The petty man’s form is without law and acts arbitrarily.

The seventh line: *"The pheasant’s misfortune, yet the rooster covers the valley."* (雉之不禄, 而鸡盖谷)
The *Guāng* commentary states: *"The pheasant’s misfortune"* (雉之不禄), for it is difficult to nurture in seclusion.
The *Guāng* commentary further clarifies: The seventh line, though exceeding fullness, is in its proper place and corresponds to daytime.
Like the *zhái* (翟) or pheasant, which possess patterns yet harbor an unyielding and upright nature (耿絜之性), refusing to be tamed by others, hence: *"Misfortune"* (不禄), for they never match the rooster’s devotion to feeding on the grain of the valley.
*Jì* (荩) means to advance or progress.
The *Guāng* commentary explains: The seventh line is at the outer position yet corresponds to daytime.
The sage has form yet remains upright and uncompromising, avoiding the world and not seeking office.
The seventh line is the stage of calamity, hence the worthy retreat.

The eighth line: *"Carved and striped, the grain and cloth vanish in due time, and form becomes chaotic."* (雕签, 谷布亡于时, 文则乱)
The *Guāng* commentary states: *"Carved and striped"* (雕韱之文), for it is merely a waste of time.
The *Guāng* commentary further explains: The eighth line is near the extreme yet loses its proper place and corresponds to night.
If one pursues elaborate carvings and striped patterns, wasting time without substance, causing the grain and cloth to vanish in due course, then the harm of such form is chaos itself.

The ninth line: *"At the extreme of form, dense and intricate, easily transforming into *fúfú* (黼黻)."* (极文密密, 易以黼黻)
The *Guāng* commentary states: *"The extreme of form is easily transformed"* (极文之易), for it should be replaced with substance.
The *Guāng* commentary further explains: The ninth line resides at the extreme of form yet obtains its proper place and corresponds to daytime, able to change the harm of extreme form.
The utmost refinement of form reaches *mìmì* (密密, dense and intricate).
Yet when it becomes too delicate and minute, it can be transformed, making it like *fúfú* (黼黻, ritual patterns) which have proper order.
The *Guāng* commentary explains: The ninth line represents the exhaustion of harm yet corresponds to daytime #12; hence this image.
*Fú* (黼) is white and black, while *fú* (黻) is black and blue.
Though *fúfú* (黼黻) are patterns, compared to elaborate carvings and striped designs, they are truly substance.

**Two sides, three states, one section, three families of Rites:**
The *Yīn* (陰) family is wood, akin to *lǚ* (履, tread/conduct).
Upon entering **Rites** (礼), the fourth hexagram, it is said: *"Six divisions, three sections, great summer’s energy responds."* (六分三秒, 大暑气应)
Yin is below, while Yang is above; the upper and lower positions are correct, and things have proper rites.
The *Xiǎo Sòng* (小宋) reading of *"Yang in the upper"* (阳在上) is taken as *"Yang increases"* (阳益高); here, it follows the readings of the other families.

The first line: *"Treading at the tip of the foot, honoring one’s ancestors."* (履于跂, 后其祖祢)

「测曰：履于跂，退其亲也。宋、陆本歧作跛，今从范、王本。
王曰：居礼之初，失位当夜，是始而乖礼者也。履于跂，非敬之道也。
光谓：跂，举踵也。一居下体之下，履之至卑者也，乃欲举踵强高，居下而僭上之象也。凡臣之事君，犹事父也；事大君，犹事祖也。在下位而礼僭於上，是犹退其祖父使居己后也，不亦悖乎？

次二，目穆穆，足肃肃，乃贯以棘。
测曰：穆穆、肃肃，敬出心也。
王曰：棘，取赤心也。
光谓：穆穆、肃肃，皆恭谨貌。二为思中，而当昼。外貌之恭，必贯之以诚，然后善也。

次三，画象成形，孚无成。
测曰：画象成形，非其真也。
王曰：忠信，礼之本。既乖於本，何礼之有？
光谓：三为思末而当夜，饰外貌而无内实者也，故曰画象成形，孚无成。

次四，孔雁之仪，利用登于阶。
测曰：孔雁之仪，可法则也。
虞曰：雁飞成行，孔雀亦成行也。
光谓：孔雀有文章，雁有行字，皆威仪之象。阶，谕进而登位也。四为下禄，而当昼。君居位有威可畏，有仪可象，其下畏而爱之，则而象之。是以其教不肃而成，其政不严而治也。

次五，怀其违，折其匕，过丧锡九矢。
测曰：怀违折匕，贬其禄也。
王本过丧锡九矢作过鬯锡，今从诸家。
范、小宋本贬其禄作贬天禄，王本作败其禄，今从宋、陆本。

次六，鱼鳞差之，乃大施之，帝用登于天。
测曰：鱼鳞差之，贵贱位也。
范、小宋本大作矢，今从宋、陆、王本。六为极大，而当昼。君子制礼，使贵贱有序，差若鱼鳞。执此道而大施之於天下，天下莫不治也。帝者用此，则可以格于皇天矣。

次七，出礼不畏，入畏。
测曰：出礼不畏，人所弃也。七为祸始，而当夜。小人逾越礼法而不顾者也。由其不畏，所以入畏，谓陷刑戮也。《书》曰：弗畏入畏。

次八，冠戚，履全履。
测曰：冠戚履贱，不可不上也。
二宋、陆、王本全作金，今从范本。
范本冠戚，明不可上也。王本冠戚，不可不上也。今从小宋本。
范曰：戚，以谕败也。冠虽败，宜加之首；履虽全，宜践之足。
光谓：八在上体而为祸中，冠败之象也。当日之昼，故下不能陵也。

上九，戴无首，焉用此九？
测曰：无首之戴，焉所往也？焉，於虔切。九，阳之盛也。礼主卑让，故虽天子，必有礼也。九为亢极而当夜，上无所戴，高而必危。譬如戴冠而无首，焉用此盛极之位哉？

二方三州二部一家逃
阳家金，准遁。」

The **Yin-Qi Chapter** grows strong, the **Yang-Qi** retreats in concealment, and all things are on the verge of decline.

The **Song** says: This refers to the waxing and waning of all things with the **Yang’s** flourishing and decline.

At the seventh division, the hexagram becomes **Retreat (Tùn)**.
The subtle **Yin** within is about to emerge from the earth.

The **King** says: *Chapter* means clear, strong, and great.

**First (Initial), Escape the Smooth Water, Erase Its Tracks.**
The **Zè** says: Escape the smooth water—no tracks are left behind.
The first is the beginning of contemplation, occurring in daylight.
The **Junzi** avoids calamity before it sprouts, flees evil before it takes form, and restrains it at the very start of deliberation. If no one sees its tracks, how could misfortune ever reach them?
It is like fleeing into water—water follows and smooths out, sealing its marks, so no one can perceive its traces.

**Second, The Heart Trembles, Feet in Gold Sandals, No Will to Enter the Ravine.**
The **Zè** says: The trembling heart—its resolve is not firm.
The **Fàn** text reads *惕*, the **King’s** text reads *惕*, noting that *惕* is the ancient character; here, we follow the **Song** and **Lù** texts.
The **Song** and **Lù** texts read *含* for *金*, while the **King’s** text reads *望*; here, we follow the **Fàn** and **Xiǎo-Sòng** texts.
The **Song** says: *Jiāng* means "to proceed," unable to resolve the heart with principle before acting.
The **Guāng** says: *Jīn* symbolizes firmness and endurance.
The second is the midpoint of contemplation, occurring at night.
Though the petty person sees disaster approaching, trembling in fear, they cannot resolve themselves with principle. Clinging to their honors and emoluments, they linger and do not depart, unaware that the ravine lies beneath their feet—soon they will stumble and fall.
Confucius said: *"To see what is right and not do it is cowardice."*

**Third, Trembling at the Thighs, Whipping the Horse, Bandits Glance at the Door, Fleeing for Gain.**
The **Zè** says: Trembling at the thighs and whipping the horse—disaster is near at hand.
The **Fàn** text reads *望* as *望*; here, we follow the **Song**, **Lù**, and **King’s** texts.
The **Chén** says: *望* is the ancient character for *望*.
The **Song** says: *Near at hand* means the bandits are already close.
The **Guāng** says: The third is the end of contemplation, occurring in daylight.
*To flee* is what is fitting.
Trembling at the thighs—fear.
Whipping the horse—desire for swift departure.
Bandits glance at the door—disaster is imminent.
At this moment, it is advantageous to flee.

**Fourth, The Towering Tree is Shaken, Flying Birds Pass Over, Some Alight and Descend.**
The **Zè** says: Birds of the towering tree—if they wish to stop, they descend.
The **Song**, **Lù**, and **King’s** texts read *枞* for *摐*; here, we follow the **Fàn** text.
*Or alight and descend*—the **Song** and **Lù** texts read *止之*, while the **Fàn** text reads *或降*; here, we follow the **King’s** text.
The **Fàn** says: *Shaking upward* is called *摐*.
A tree shaken upward is where birds do not gather, so they pass and depart.
The **Guāng** says: *摐* depicts tall and dense growth.
The fourth is the lower emolument, occurring at night.
The petty person’s resolve to retreat is not steadfast; encountering fine emoluments, they halt and do not depart, thus not escaping calamity.

**Fifth, Seeing the *Zhuàn* in the Forest, the Otter Enters the Abyss—an Auspicious Omen.**
The **Zè** says: Seeing the *Zhuàn* and the otter—deep retreat from disaster.
The **Fàn** text reads *鹭* as *鹭*, while the **King’s** text reads *鹭*; here, we follow the **Song** and **Lù** texts.
The **King’s** text reads *利征* for *征*; here, we follow the **Song**, **Lù**, and **Fàn** texts.
*Zhuàn* is the ancient character for *年*; another reading is *鹰*.
*Zhuàn* is pronounced *cí-xù* (慈恤).
The **Song** says: *Zhuàn* harms birds; the otter harms fish.
The **Wú** says: *Zhuàn*—to tread.
The **Guāng** says: The fifth is the middle emolument, occurring in daylight.
Though the **Junzi** holds a high position and enjoys rich emoluments, seeing petty men wielding power in court and knowing they will bring chaos, they depart.

**Sixth, Many Fields but No Harvest, Exhausting My Labor.**
The **Zè** says: Many fields but no harvest—labor is spent in vain.
The **Song** and **Lù** texts read *会* for *费*; here, we follow the **Fàn** and **King’s** texts.
*功* is pronounced *hù-jiā* (户佳).
The **Fàn** says: *Who will eat?* is *功*.

The **King** says: The sixth hexagram, when overfull, loses its position at night, and does not know how to escape appropriately.

If one cultivates fields in the hexagram of Many Fields, yet does not manage them properly, only consuming food and labor without benefit.

The **Guāng** says: The sixth hexagram is the highest emolument, representing abundance and extremity, yet it occurs at night.

The petty man’s virtue is thin though his position is high, his strength is little though his responsibilities are heavy. Though he may possess abundant resources, he cannot govern them well—accomplished men all abandon him and flee, consuming food and labor in vain. How could he achieve success?

**Seventh, Seen in Bonds, Only Then Able to Fly.**
The **Zè** says: Seen in bonds—almost not enough to rise high.

*Jǐ*, pronounced *jī* (as in *jì*).
The **Song** says: *Jǐ* means "near."

Already seen in bonds, barely escaping, this is why it is said to be near. The **King** says: In times of fleeing danger, the seventh hexagram marks the beginning of calamity—one who flees only after the time has passed, hence "seen in bonds."

Yet when in position during the day, one can preserve ultimate well-being, hence "only then able to fly," escaping his misfortune.

The **Junzi**, though entangled in constraints, does not dwell in a chaotic realm. Ultimately, he must withdraw and distance himself, hence "only then able to fly."

**Eighth, The Neck Added to the Snare, Bound by Its Cord.**
The **Zè** says: The neck added to the snare—no self-effort remains.

The **King** says: *Měng*, the same as *yì* (wing).
The eighth hexagram loses its position at night, unable to avoid misfortune, hence the head is caught in the snare.

Though it flaps its wings within the net, there is no possibility of flight. The **Guāng** says: *Měng* is the arrow used in hunting.

The eighth hexagram is the midpoint of calamity, occurring at night, unable to flee far from disaster. The snare is already on the neck—though it struggles and tugs at the cord, how could it escape? Only self-exhaustion remains.

**Ninth, It is advantageous to flee, limping and halting; the thief’s virtue clings to the city.**
The **Zè** says: The thief’s virtue clings to the city—why flee at all?
The **King**’s text reads *virtue-thief*, the small **Song** text reads *thief-obtains*; here, we follow the **Song**, **Lù**, and **Fàn** texts.

The **Chén** says: *Pǐ*, pronounced *píng* (as in *bīng*), also *pú-xián* (蒲贤).
The **Zìshū** says: *Pǐ* means hardened calloused skin.

Two regions, three states, two departments, two families of the Tang dynasty—**Yīn** family earth—also corresponds to *dùn* (遁).

**Lù says:** The *Táng* is a great dispersion—all under Heaven flee.
Flee is also dispersion.
**Guāng says:** *Táng* is like *dàngdàng*—unrestrained, with the sense of loss.
Yin-qi comes here, Yang-qi departs—things sway *dàngdàng*.
**Sòng says:** *Zī* means "to augment."
**Lù says:** *Dàngdàng* is the appearance of emptiness exhausted.

**First (crawl):** *Táng* within—do not act; perilous.
**Cè says:** *Táng* within—no steadfastness.
One is the beginning of thought, yet it falls to night.
Within, there is nothing to hold—movement brings danger.

**Second (treading):** *Táng* in darkness—advantageous to use eastward campaigning.
**Cè says:** The advantage of *Táng* in darkness is to seek the Bright Way.
Darkness is obscurity.
East is where the sun rises.
Two is the middle of thought, dwelling in the lower body’s depths—thus *Táng* in darkness.
Its heart is swaying, lost in where to go.
Yet in the sun’s day, the superior person seeks the Bright Way to advance himself.

**Third (abrupt):** *Táng* in whiteness—untrue, losing *lónglíng*.
**Cè says:** Losing *lónglíng*—not yours to lose.
**Wáng says:** *Lónglíng*—the sound of jade.

**Fourth (youthful):** *Táng* without destination—Dao and virtue as refuge.
**Cè says:** *Táng* without destination—only virtue grants.
Destination—*dīnglì* (丁历) cut, same below.
Refuge—*bìyì* (必益) cut.
Grant—*yúlǚ* (余吕) cut.
**Wáng says:** Four obtains position in daylight, fitting *Táng*’s nature—swaying, without destination or refusal, taking Dao and virtue as refuge.
Knowing where to go—*Táng*’s excellence.
Refuge—lord.
**Guāng says:** Destination—inevitability.
Four is smooth and flourishing, yet in daylight.
Confucius said: The superior person in the world has no fixed destination, no refusal—only virtue aligns with him.
Thus it says *Dao and virtue as refuge*—speaking of its highest eminence.

**Fifth (dashing):** Fleeing deer, cradling the shrew—gain not even *zī*.
**Cè says:** Fleeing deer, cradling the shrew—insufficient achievement.
**Fàn, Wáng** texts read *xīzúgōng* (奚足功), now following **Sòng, Lù** texts.
**Wú says:** *Qú* (鼷)—*hùjī* (户鸡) cut, a small mouse.
**Wáng says:** Five occupies the peak position, should be the leader, yet loses position at night.
Without the virtue of lordship, swaying and unguarded, confused between profit and harm.
Chasing deer in flight, yet turning to the shrew-mouse—what is gained is insufficient as provision.
*Zī*—same as *zī* (资).

**Sixth (gentle):** *Táng* not self-sufficient—substituting Heaven’s rank and rewards.
**Cè says:** *Táng* not self-sufficient—no private capacity.
**Wáng says:** Obtains position in daylight, as *Táng*’s lord—swaying, without private gain, not seeking self-sufficiency, sharing Heaven’s blessings with all—thus able to substitute Heaven in bestowing rank and rewards.
A sage ruler in his time, dwelling in *Táng*’s excellence—none surpasses this.
**Guāng says:** Six is upper reward, abundant and flourishing, yet in daylight.
The superior person does not monopolize Heaven’s blessings but shares them with all worthy.

**Seventh (oppressing):** Hunt the three flights—brightly manifest in campaigning, yet all day not returning—lost.
