**Cè says:** Hunt the three flights—destination without guidance.
**Wáng says:** Dwelling in excess, yet losing position at night.
Unclear in the Way, self-inflicting peril and ruin.
Like hunting one flight yet seeking three—nowhere to turn.
Self-illuminated yet wandering until evening, swaying and confused—what good remains?
**Guāng says:** Seven is lost aspiration, yet at night.
The petty man wavers in virtue, losing this for that, swaying without steadfastness, laboring in vain.
From youth to old age, ultimately gaining nothing.

**Eighth (binding):** *Táng* collects rewards—spirit of the community halts its weeping, some receive their *mù*.
**Cè says:** *Táng* collects rewards—yet again lost.
*Fù*—*fúmù* (符目) cut, same below.
**Wáng says:** Obtains position in daylight, skilled in *Táng*’s Way—thus able to reclaim its rewards.
**Guāng says:** Eight is calamity’s midpoint, yet fortune and rewards have scattered.
Yet in the sun’s day, able to revive decline and restore ruin—thus *Táng* collects rewards.
The spirits of land and grain, once more receiving blood-sacrifice, thus halt their weeping.
The people, already withered, yet again receive nourishment—thus some receive their *mù*.

**Ninth (shedding):** Bright pearl shot at flying flesh—what is gained does not compensate.
**Cè says:** Bright pearl shot at flesh—expenditure unmatched.
*Dàng*—*dīnglàng* (丁浪) cut.
**Fàn says:** Flying flesh—birds and owls.
The pearl is heavy, the owl light—seeking the light with the heavy, thus no compensation.
**Wáng says:** Nine occupies the extreme peak, yet loses position at night—swaying, heard in the great reckoning.
Like shooting a pearl at a sparrow—what is gained does not replace what is lost.
**Guāng says:** Nine is calamity’s extremity, yet at night.
The petty man indulges desires to excess, swaying and forgetful.

Two regions, three states, two departments, three families—constant.
**Yáng** family water—corresponds to *héng* (恒).

「Entering the *Cháng* (常) phase, the twenty-third day of the twenty-sixth degree—*Lìqiū* (立秋) aligns with the *qi* (气). The day corresponds to **Qúntǎo** (鹑尾), the **Fùxiàng** (斗) constellation marks the **Shēn** (申) position, and the **Yízhé** (夷则) musical pitch resonates.

**Ninth (shedding):** The day relinquishes its **Yì** (翼).
By **Yīn** (阴) one discerns the **Bì** (辟) [as **Bì**, pronounced *bì*, or "wall"#17], by **Yáng** (阳) one discerns the **Jūn** (君) [as "sovereign"].
The **Jūn** (君) and **Chén** (臣) [as "minister"] **Dao** (道) is immutable across the ten thousand ages.
**Bì** (辟), when read as **Jūn** (君), is "sovereign."

**Song** (宋) says: **Bì** (辟) is the **Jūn** (君).
This is the beginning of **Lìqiū** (立秋).
As autumn succeeds summer, it is the **Dao** (道) of Heaven.
Thus at this time, **Yīn** (阴) knows to serve as **Chén** (臣), **Yáng** (阳) knows to act as **Jūn** (君).
**Guāng** (光) says: As autumn succeeds summer, as **Yīn** (阴) succeeds **Yáng** (阳), as **Chén** (臣) succeeds **Jūn** (君)—this is the symbol.

**First (day):** Wearing the **Dàishén** (戴神) [as "donning the divine"] **Mò** (墨) [as "black ink"], stepping on the **Lǚlíng** (履灵) [as "treading the sacred"] **Shì** (式) [as "pattern"], with one to govern ten thousand, yet never failing in **Wú** (吴) [as "harmony"].
**Cè** (测) says: Wearing **Dàishén** (戴神) **Mò** (墨) is to embody one form.
**Wáng** (王) and **Wú** (吴) both say: **Jì** (稷) is the same as **Wú** (吴).
**Mò** (墨) and **Shì** (式) are both **Fǎ** (法) [as "principles"]—the **Shén** (神) and **Líng** (灵) [as "divine and numinous"] are revered.
One is the beginning of thought, yet it occurs during the day.
The **Jūnzǐ** (君子) [as "noble one"]’s heart clings to one principle as the constant **Fǎ** (法), responding to all transformations of the ten thousand things, yet never lacking in **Wú** (吴) [as "harmony"].
The *Yì* (易) [as *Book of Changes*] says: "All movement under Heaven is governed by steadfast adherence to the One."
**Second (day):** The **Cháng** (常) within is **Wēi** (微) [as "subtle"], **Nǚzhēn** (女贞) [as "female steadfastness"] **Lì** (厉) [as "perilous"].
**Cè** (测) says: The **Cháng** (常) within is **Wēi** (微), the **Nǚ** (女) [as "female"] is not steadfast.
**Wáng** (王) originally wrote **Chángnèi** (常内) **Wēi** (微), but now follows the other schools.
**Wáng** (王) says: During the **Cháng** (常) phase, one should uphold the proper **Dao** (道) of **Jūn** (君) and **Chén** (臣), **Fùfù** (夫妇) [as "husband and wife"].
The second day loses its position at night#18—this is the **Cháng** (常) phase subtly yielding to a lowly **Nǚ** (女) [as "female"] within.
The symbol of unsteadfastness is near danger, hence **Nǚzhēn** (女贞) **Lì** (厉) [as "perilous"].

**Third (day):** The day upholds its **Dé** (德) [as "virtue/power"], three years without eating.
**Cè** (测) says: The day upholds its **Dé** (德), the **Jūn** (君)’s **Dao** (道) is clear.
Three is self-so, yet it occurs during the day.
The day upholds its **Dé** (德), thus escaping the calamity of meager nourishment.
The **Jūn** (君) upholds his **Dé** (德), thus avoiding the guilt of reckless usurpation.
**Yīyǐn** (伊尹) admonished **Tàijiǎ** (太甲), composing the *Xián yǒu yī Dé* (咸有一德).

**Fourth (day):** The month does not uphold, sometimes losing it in **Xíng** (行) [as "conduct"].
**Cè** (测) says: The month does not uphold, the **Chén** (臣) loses his **Xíng** (行).
**Xíng** (行) is read as it is written.
The month has waxing and waning, hence it does not uphold.
**Xíng** (行) is the **Dao** (道).
Four is **Wàitā** (外佗) [as "external excess"], yet it occurs at night.
If the **Chén** (臣)’s **Dé** (德) is not constant, he loses the **Dao** (道).

**Fifth (day):** Its **Cóng** (从) [as "following"] and its **Héng** (横) [as "crossing"], the constant **Dao** (道) of Heaven and Earth.
**Cè** (测) says: Its **Cóng** (从) and its **Héng** (横) are the constants of **Jūn** (君) and **Chén** (臣).
**Cóng** (从), pronounced *róng* (容).
**Fàn** (范) says: Heaven follows Earth’s crossing—this is their constant **Dao** (道).
**Wáng** (王) says: The fifth day occupies the middle position, as the master of **Cháng** (常).
Obtaining its position during the day, it follows and crosses the **Jīngwéi** (经纬) [as "vertical and horizontal principles"], all in accord with the constant **Dao** (道) of Heaven and Earth, attaining the immutable **Dao** (道) across the ten thousand ages.
**Guāng** (光) says: Five is the **Zhōnghé** (中和) [as "central harmony"], the peak of **Cháng** (常).
The **Dao** (道) of **Jūn** (君) and **Chén** (臣) is their **Jīngwéi** (经纬).

**Sixth (day):** Obtaining seven and nine, the **nuò** (懦) [as "weak"] and **náo** (挠) [as "flexible"] overcome the **gāng** (刚) [as "rigid"], unable to uphold the **Cháng** (常).
**Cè** (测) says: Obtaining seven and nine is to abandon **Shèng** (盛) [as "peak"] and ride upon **Cuī** (衰) [as "decline"].
Seven is the peak of **Yáng** (阳).
Nine is the decline of **Yáng** (阳).
The sixth day passes the middle and occurs at night—abandoning peak and riding decline.
The petty man is weak, bending and corrupting his rigidity, unable to guard his constant **Dao** (道).

**Seventh (day):** **Tāotāo** (滔滔) [as "flowing"] **wǎnglái** (往来) [as "movement"], with **Cháng** (常) decline as **Rú** (如) [as "pattern"], able to uphold the **Zhēn** (贞) [as "steadfast"].
**Cè** (测) says: **Tāotāo** (滔滔) **wǎnglái** (往来) is to uphold the proper **Dao** (道) in response to the improper.

**Eighth (day):** The **Cháng** (常) is **Jí** (疾) [as "illness"] yet not **Jí** (疾), the **jiù** (咎) [as "fault"] is formed yet not **Jí** (诘) [as "rectified"].
**Cè** (测) says: The **Cháng** (常) is **Jí** (疾) yet not **Jí** (疾)—it cannot self-govern.
Eight is the midpoint of calamity, yet it occurs at night.
The **Cháng** (常) **Dao** (道), long practiced, now has weariness—hence **Chángjí** (常疾).
If one who is ill knows his illness, it may still be treated.
If one does not know his illness yet abhors hearing of it#20, then it is certainly incurable#21.
The petty man does not know his calamity yet abhors hearing of it, until the **xiōngjiù** (凶咎) [as "grievous fault"] is formed#22—it cannot be rectified again.

**Upper Ninth (day):** **Jí** (疾) its **Jí** (疾), **Wūyī** (巫医) [as "diviners and healers"] do not fail.
**Cè** (测) says: **Jí** (疾) its **Jí** (疾)—able to self-heal.
**Wáng** (王) says: The ninth day occupies the extreme peak, yet obtains its position during the day—one who can **Jí** (疾) his **Jí** (疾).
Good at self-admonition, thus the **Wūyī** (巫医) [as "diviners and healers"] do not fail in treating him.
**Wūyī** (巫医) refers to wise counselors.
**Guāng** (光) says: The ninth is the end of calamity, yet it occurs during the day.
The **Jūnzǐ** (君子) [as "noble one"] can reflect on misfortune and preemptively guard against it, receiving loyal advice and seeking wise counsel to aid himself—thus calamity may be resolved.

Two regions, three states, three departments, one family—**Dù** (度) [as "measure"].
**Yīn** (阴) family **Huǒ** (火) [as "fire"], corresponds to **Jié** (节) [as "regulation"].
**Fǎ** (法) [as "principles"] serve as models, **Dù** (度) [as "measure"] serves as the standard.
**Yīn** (阴) *qi* (气) grows increasingly **Zào** (躁) [as "agitated"], **Yáng** (阳) *qi* (气) grows increasingly **Shě** (舍) [as "retreating"].
**Zào** (躁) and **Zào** (躁) **Shě** (舍) and **Shě** (舍), each attains its **Dù** (度).
**Shě** (舍), pronounced *shě*, or read as it is written.
**Sòng** (宋) says: **Zào** (躁), movement.
**Shě** (舍), concealment.
**Wáng** (王) says: **Shě** (舍), cessation.
**Guāng** (光) says: **Zào** (躁) refers to **Yīn** (阴) advancing in vigor.
**Shě** (舍) refers to **Yáng** (阳) withdrawing.
The **Zào** (躁) grows more **Zào** (躁), the **Shě** (舍) grows more **Shě** (舍)—each guards its constant **Dù** (度).

**First (day):** The central **Dù** (度) alone is lost.
**Cè** (测) says: The central **Dù** (度) alone is lost—it cannot achieve success.
One is the beginning of thought, yet it occurs at night.
The petty man’s measure in the heart has already strayed and been lost—how could he achieve success?
The *Shāngshū* (商书) [as *Book of Documents*] says: "If one anticipates danger and sets traps, then going to inspect and rectify according to **Dù** (度) will release [the situation]."

**Second (day):** The **Zé** (泽) [as "benefits"] does not **Shě** (舍) [as "retreat"], **Míng** (冥) [as "obscure"] within the **Dù** (度).
**Cè** (测) says: The **Zé** (泽) does not **Shě** (舍)—only then can it be proper.

**Third (day):** The small **Dù** (度) is **Chāchā** (差差) [as "erratic"], the **Dà** (大) **Lǎi** (攋) [as "collapse"] is the stage.
**Cè** (测) says: The small **Dù** (度) is **Chāchā** (差差), the great **Dù** (度) is **Qīng** (倾) [as "tilting"].
Small **Sòng** (宋), **Chén** (陈), and **Wú** (吴) manuscripts originally read **Tā** (獭) [as "otter"], but **Wáng** (王) read **Qīng** (倾), and now follows **Sòng** (宋), **Lǚ** (陆), and **Fàn** (范) manuscripts.
**Fàn** (范) manuscripts read **Chāchā** (差差) as **Zhīchā** (之差), but now follows **Sòng** (宋), **Lǚ** (陆), and **Wáng** (王) manuscripts.
**Lǎi** (攋), pronounced *lài* (赖).
**Guāng** (光) says: **Lǎi** (攋) is "ruin and fracture"#28.

The **Third (day)** is the completion of thought, and the time is night.
Thought not in accord with **Dù** (度) will lead to erratic and failed matters.
Hence it is said: Small **Dù** (度) is **Chāchā** (差差), the great **Lǎi** (攋) is the stage of ruin—
for though what is lost may be small, what is destroyed will be great.

**Fourth (day):** **Gànzhēng** (干桢), advantageous for city construction.
**Cè** (测) says: The advantage of **Gànzhēng** (干桢) lies in its **jīngyíng** (经营).
The **Fàn** (范) and **Wáng** (王) manuscripts read *gān* (干) as *gàn* (干#29); now follows the **Sòng** (宋) and **Lǚ** (陆) manuscripts.
The **Fourth (day)** is the bestowal of blessings below, and the time is day.
When the **junzi** (君子) has attained his station,
he must always use **fǎdù** (法度) as his **Gànzhēng** (干桢),
thus he can safeguard his people and undertake great **jīngyíng** (经营).

**Fifth (day):** **Gàn** (干) not **Gàn** (干), ruin to his undertakings.
**Cè** (测) says: **Gàn** (干) not **Gàn** (干) means he cannot have peace.
**Wáng** (王) says: One who has lost his station at night cannot be the leader of the people.
A **cái** (才) that is **Gàn** (干) yet not truly **Gàn** (干) will inevitably topple all he has undertaken.
**Guāng** (光) says: A man who lacks **Dù** (度) leaves the people without order—
just as **Gàn** (干) not **Gàn** (干) will destroy what he has undertaken, bringing no stability.

**Sixth (day):** Great **Dù** (度) **jiǎnjiǎn** (检检), manifesting **xiàng** (象) to Heaven, and setting its standard.
**Cè** (测) says: Great **Dù** (度) **jiǎnjiǎn** (检检) is the **chéngxiàng** (垂象) of rectitude.
**Fàn** (范), same as **Fàn** (范).
**Wáng** (王) says: The **Sixth (day)**, occupying the most exalted position,
is the greatest **Dù** (度)—one who attains his station at day.
He who is clear in **fǎzhì** (法制) and uses **Dù** (度) to examine things
will take Heaven’s **xiàng** (象) and set its **fǎ** (法) for the world.
**Guāng** (光) says: The **junzi**’s establishment of **fǎdù** (法度)
is not drawn from his own heart.
Instead, he observes **xiàng** (象) in Heaven
to set a standard for the world,
hence it is said: Heaven demonstrates **xiàng** (象), and it sets its standard.
The *Yì* (易) says: Heaven demonstrates **xiàng** (象), the sage follows it.

**Seventh (day):** Without **Dù** (度), **guī** (规) is not applied—ghosts will mock it.
**Cè** (测) says: Without **Dù** (度), **guī** (规) is not applied—ghosts will laugh and ridicule.
**Zǐ** (訾), pronounced *zhǐ* (紫), also read as *jí* (即) *yí* (移).
**Wáng** (王) says: The **Seventh (day)**, residing in excess,
loses its station at night—hence it is not **Dù** (度).
**Dù** (度) is the one who establishes **guīzhì** (规制).
**Zǐ** (訾) is mockery and ridicule.

「Praise says: Ghosts mock, *Cè* says: Those who keenly observe.
The **幽明** (Yōumíng—"dark and bright," i.e., the unseen and the seen) share in laughter,
hence the parallel phrasing reveals its meaning.

**Guāng** (光) says: The **Seventh (day)**, marked by ruin and decay, falls at night.
The **xiǎorén** (小人—"petty/ignoble person") grows arrogant and extravagant,
not restraining himself by **fǎdù** (法度—"law and measure"),
so the ghosts mock and ridicule him, and disaster will befall him.

Those who keenly observe—none surpass the ghosts.
Among the foolish of humanity, some may not yet know it,
but the ghosts first perceive the coming calamity.

**Eighth (day):** The **stone** (石) does not lose its **chì** (赤—"redness"),
a steadfast person’s essential virtue.

**Cè** (测) says: The **stone** (石) does not lose its **chì** (赤—"redness"),
and thus one may entrust him with great matters.

**Yào** (要), pronounced as *yáo* (一遥切).
**Fàn** (范) says: The stone cannot lose its firmness,
the cinnabar cannot lose its redness.

**Wáng** (王) says: The **Eighth (day)**, at the end of **Dù** (度—"measure"),
secures its station at dawn—one who must uphold his **Dù** (度—"measure")
and never alter it.

**Guāng** (光) says: **Yào** (要) means "to bind by agreement."
The **Eighth (day)**, though in the midst of disaster, stands at dawn.
The **junzi** (君子), even amid calamity and chaos,
does not change his **Dù** (度—"measure"),
holding fast to his resolve with unyielding clarity—
unmovable, unshakable.
Hence, one may make a binding agreement with him,
saying: "To entrust him with a hundred *lǐ* (里—"li, a unit of distance") of command,
to place him as guardian of the six *chǐ* (尺—"feet") of an orphan."

**Ninth (day):** The **accumulation of errors** (积差) is forgiven,
yet ten years pass without return.

**Cè** (测) says: The **accumulation of errors** (积差) is forgiven,
but no further progress can be made.

**Fàn** (范) originally wrote "difference" (差) as "goodness" (善),
but now follows the readings of the *Èr Sòng* (二宋—"Two Song editions"),
*Lù* (陆), and **Wáng** (王) editions.
The **Wáng** (王) edition writes "ten" (十) as "seven" (七),
but now follows the consensus of other editions.
**Lù** (陆) says: **Zào** (造) means "to reach/attain."
**Wáng** (王) says: At the extreme of **Dù** (度—"measure"),
losing its station at night—one who has utterly abandoned **Dù** (度—"measure").
He accumulates his errors and deviations,
gradually drifting farther astray.
"Forgiven" (贷) means leniency and delay,
coming too late in time.
Though seven years may pass—how long!—
his **Dù** (度—"measure") can never be restored.

**Guāng** (光) says: "Forgiven" (贷) should be read as *tuōdé* (吐得切),
same as **tè** (忒—"exceedingly, overly"),
the utmost degree of error.
The *Yì* (易) says: "To return in confusion is inauspicious."
Even ten years of campaigning cannot conquer it.
The two **Sòng** (宋) editions, **Lù** (陆), **Fàn** (范), and **Wáng** (王)
all liken it to the **Héng** (恒—"constancy") hexagram.
**Wú** (吴) says: "Constant head" (常首),
likening it to the **Héng** (恒—"constancy") hexagram.

The **Seventh (day)**, marking the start of autumn.
**First (day):** Corresponds to the **226th day**,
advancing the **Yì** (翼—"auxiliary") by **15°**,
aligning with the **Héng** (恒—"constancy") hexagram in the *Yì* (易) for the day's significance.
**Fourth (day):** The next **auxiliary** (翼), likening it to the **Jié** (节—"propriety") hexagram.
**First (day):** Having already advanced **Yì** (翼—"auxiliary") by **2°**,
now reaching **Yǒng** (永—"enduring").

The **Seventh (day)**, corresponds to the **238th day**,
advancing **Yì** (翼—"auxiliary") by **9°**,
aligning with the **Tóngrén** (同人—"harmony with others") hexagram in the *Yì* (易) for the day's significance.
Now following this.

**Yīn** (阴—"dark, feminine, passive") uses **wǔ** (武—"martial, force") to take,
**Yáng** (阳—"bright, masculine, active") uses **wén** (文—"cultural, refinement") to give.
The **Dao** (道—"Way") may endure forever.

**Sòng** (宋) says: **Yīn** (阴—"dark, feminine, passive") is the **qì** (气) of punishment,
hence it is spoken of in terms of **wǔ** (武—"martial, force").
**Yáng** (阳—"bright, masculine, active") is the **qì** (气) of **virtue/power** (德),
hence it is spoken of in terms of **wén** (文—"cultural, refinement").
**Wǔ** (武—"martial, force") supplements **wén** (文—"cultural, refinement"),
and **wén** (文—"cultural, refinement") supplements **wǔ** (武—"martial, force"),
the **Dao** (道—"Way") of **yīn** (阴) and **yáng** (阳) in taking and giving—
hence its **Dao** (道—"Way") can endure forever.

**First (day):** Without **tì** (替—"replacement") or **shuàng** (爽—"freshness, clarity"),
the enduring principle of the eldest son.

**Cè** (测) says: Without **tì** (替—"replacement") or **shuàng** (爽—"freshness, clarity"),
it is the eternal continuation of the **Dao** (道—"Way").

**Wáng** (王) edition writes **tì** (替) as **lì** (历—"to pass through"),
saying it is an ancient **jiàn** (僭—"usurp") character,
but now follows the readings of **Sòng** (宋), **Lù** (陆), and **Fàn** (范) editions.
**Cháng** (长), pronounced as *zhīzàng* (知丈切).
**Yǒng** (永) means "long-lasting, enduring,"
having the symbol of succession.
**Tì** (替) means "to abandon, to discard."
On the first day of dawn, one who attains the **Dao** (道—"Way") of endurance.

The **institutions of the ancient kings** (先王之制),
establishing the **dí** (嫡—"legitimate heir") to endure,
to secure the **Zōngmiào** (宗庙—"ancestral temples") and **chóngshèjì** (社稷—"altars of soil and grain"),
without replacement or loss of clarity—
the eternal law for ten thousand generations.
No **Dao** (道—"Way") of endurance surpasses this.

**Second (day):** Harboring within himself **tì** (替—"replacement") and **shuàng** (爽—"freshness, clarity"),
he will forever lose the **Zhēnxiáng** (贞祥—"auspicious virtue").

**Cè** (测) says: Harboring within himself **tì** (替—"replacement") and **shuàng** (爽—"freshness, clarity"),
how can such stability endure?
On the second day, at night, he loses the **Dao** (道—"Way") of endurance.

A **confused and deluded ruler** (昏惑之君),
violating propriety by favoring a concubine,
replacing the legitimate heir with a bastard,
harbors within himself the **qì** (气) of **tì** (替—"replacement") and **shuàng** (爽—"freshness, clarity").
If he treats his eldest son in this manner,
he will forever lose the **Dao** (道—"Way") of upright goodness.

**Third (day):** Enduring his **Dao** (道—"Way"),
yet still without blame.

**Cè** (测) says: Enduring his **Dao** (道—"Way"),
truly it may be preserved.
The **Third (day)**, marking the completion of intention,
stands at dawn.
The **junzi** (君子) has already established his **Dao** (道—"Way") and deeds,
yet has not yet ascended to fortune and rank.
If the **junzi** does not fear lack of position,
but fears how he establishes himself,
constantly renewing his **Dao** (道—"Way"),
enduring without weariness—
though he may not yet attain blessings and prosperity,
what blame is there?

**Fourth (day):** The son does not follow his proper order,
the guest precedes, and the host forever loses his rightful place.

**Cè** (测) says: The son does not follow his proper order,
this is not the **Dao** (道—"Way") of endurance.
**Wáng** (王) says: The **Fourth (day)**, near the **Fifth (day)**,
yet occupies a **yīn** (阴—"dark, feminine, passive") position in **yáng** (阳—"bright, masculine, active"),
and falls at night—
he loses the **Dao** (道—"Way") of what may endure.
If the son does not occupy his proper place in order,
then sacrifices and guests will forever lack a host.
Hence, the guest precedes, and the host forever loses his rightful place.

**Fifth (day):** The **three bonds** (三纲) are attained at the central peak,
Heaven forever grants its blessings.

**Cè** (测) says: The endurance of the **three bonds** (三纲),
its **Dao** (道—"Way") is long.
**Wáng** (王) says: Occupying the center and embodying correctness,
as the head of the household,
securing his station at dawn,
possessing the **virtue/power** (德) of a ruler—
he can make the **three bonds** (三纲) all attain their proper place within him.
Heaven aids him, hence his blessings endure forever.

**Guāng** (光) says: The **three bonds** (三纲) are:
the ruler as the bond for his subjects,
the father as the bond for his children,
the husband as the bond for his wife.
The **Fifth (day)**, marking central harmony,
stands at dawn.
The ruler establishes the **three bonds** (三纲) to found the **Huángjí** (皇极—"Supreme Polarity"),
forever preserving Heaven’s blessings.

**Sixth (day):** Greatly enduring in blessings,
he reverses the **empty court** (虚庭),
entering the **dark obscurity** (酋冥).

**Cè** (测) says: Greatly enduring in blessings,
blessings turn to ruin.
**Fàn** (范) edition writes "entering the **dark obscurity** (入于酋冥)" as "entering the **obscurity** (入酋冥)",
the *Xiǎo Sòng* (小宋—"Lesser Song") edition reads "at the **obscurity** (於酋冥)",
but now follows the readings of **Sòng** (宋), **Lù** (陆), and **Wáng** (王) editions.
The **Sixth (day)**, exceeding the center and falling at night.
The **xiǎorén** (小人—"petty/ignoble person"), relying on the endurance of blessings,
becomes proud and licentious, boastful and arrogant.
When blessings reach their end, disaster follows.
Hence, he reverses the **empty court** (虚庭),
speaking of gaining nothing.
Entering the **dark obscurity** (酋冥),
speaking of his ignorance of remorse and fear—
once disaster has already befallen,
he reaches the utmost of **obscurity** (冥).

**Seventh (day):** The **old wood** (老木) produces shade,
enduring its own form.

「**Kūn** (昆—"to gather/cluster"), pronounced *shì lì* (时吏切).
**King Wén** (王) says: The seventh hexagram marks the completion of the **Kūn** cycle, its symbol being **old wood** (老木).

Yet when it holds its position during the day, it attains the **perfecting Dao** (永道), hence it generates **Kūn** (莳).
**Kūn** (莳) refers to the **lateral growth** (旁生) of planting.
Once it bears **blossoms and leaves** (华叶), it further attains **prolonged duration** (长久),
beginning with **nothingness** (无今有), hence it is said to **entwine what does not exist** (缠其所无).

**Eighth (day):** The **perfecting Dao** (永) lacks **proper measure** (轨),
and **disaster** (凶亡) ensues, flowing onward to the future.
**Zhōu** (测) says: The **perfecting Dao** lacking **proper measure** (永不轨),
its **fate is exhausted** (命剂).
**Jì** (剂), pronounced *cái zhì* (才诣切).
**Sòng** (宋) says: **Jì** (剂) means **to trim** (翦).
**King Wén** says: Dwelling in the position of **extremity** (极之位),
while the **yin** (阴) occupies the **yang** time (阳时) and it is night—
this is an act where the **perfecting Dao** (永) does not follow **proper measure** (法).
How much less can it avoid bringing **disaster** (凶亡) to itself alone?
The **disaster** (凶亡) will then **flow into the future** (流于后).

**Guāng** (光) says: The eighth hexagram is the **midpoint of calamity** (祸中),
and it falls at night.
The **petty/ignoble person** (小人) persistently acts without **proper measure** (不法).
Once his body meets **disaster** (凶亡), the **remaining misfortunes** (余殃) will **flow into the future** (流后).

**Uppermost ninth (day):** The **perfecting Dao** (永) concludes with **docile submission** (驯首).
**Zhōu** says: The **perfecting Dao** concluding with **docile submission** (永终驯首),
this is the **enduring virtue of harmony** (长恺悌).
**Xún** (驯) means **to comply** (顺).
**Kǎi** (恺) means **joy** (乐).
**Tì** (悌) means **ease** (易).
The ninth line, at the **end of the perfecting Dao** (永终), falls during the day.
The **Dao of the noble person** (君子之道) is **enduring, joyful, and easy** (永长乐易),
and he **treats the end as the beginning** (慎终如首若一).
Thus, the **perfecting Dao** is **fulfilled** (全).

---
**The two directions, three regions, three divisions, and three families** (二方三州三部三家) **all gather** (昆).
**Kūn** (昆), pronounced *hù gǔn* (户衮切), or as the character itself.
**Yin-family metal** (阴家金) also aligns with **the people** (同人).
**Lù** (陆) says: **Kūn** (昆) also means **to align** (同).
The **yin** (阴) is about to **depart** (离), while the **yang** (阳) still **gathers** (昆).
The **Dao of gathering** (昆道) still aligns.

**Sòng** (宋) says: The **yin-qi** (阴气) is about to **kill** (杀),
hence it **departs and scatters all things** (离散万物).
The **yang-qi** (阳气) is about to **converge** (藏),
hence all things **return and align** (同归).
**King Wén** says: The **yin-qi** (阴气) governs **killing** (杀),
and it **departs from all things** (离万物).
The **yang-qi** (阳气) embodies **benevolence and love** (仁爱),
still **gathering and aligning** (犹尚昆而同之).

**First (day):** **Gathering in darkness** (昆于黑),
he does not know **light** (白).
**Zhōu** says: **Gathering in darkness** (昆于黑),
this is not to be called a **person** (人).
**Sòng** says: **White** (白) means **wisdom** (智).
**Black** (黑) means **folly** (愚).
**Guāng** says: **Darkness** (黑) signifies **filth and turbidity** (垢浊),
**light** (白) signifies **purity and integrity** (廉洁).
The first hexagram marks the **beginning of contemplation** (思始),
and it falls at night.
The **petty/ignoble person** (小人) aligns himself with **filth and turbidity** (垢浊),
and does not recognize **purity and integrity** (廉洁) as **beauty** (美).

**Second (day):** **White and black intermingle** (白黑菲菲),
three **beasts** (禽) share one **horn** (角) with **aligned tails** (同尾).
**Zhōu** says: Three **beasts** (禽) sharing one **horn** (角),
this brings no **harm to the heart** (害心).
**Fěi** (菲), pronounced *fū wěi* (敷尾切), is the same as **fěi** (斐).
**King Wén** says: It holds its position during the day.
**White and black intermingling** (白黑菲菲) signifies the **distinction between clarity and obscurity** (分别明白之义).
Three **beasts** (禽) refer to **three people** (三人).
One **horn** (角) with **aligned tails** (同尾) means **beginning and end are all aligned** (终始皆同),
with no **contrariness or divergence** (无乖异).

**Guāng** says: **Intermingling** (菲菲) describes the **appearance of white and black blending** (白黑相杂貌).
A **horn** (角) is what **repels assaults** (拒斗) at the head.
A **tail** (尾) is the **end of the body** (体之终).
The second hexagram marks the **midpoint of contemplation** (思中),
and it falls during the day.
The **noble person** (君子) interacts with others,
**pure yet not piercing** (廉而不刿),
**obscure yet not defiling** (浊而不污).
In **intermingling harmony** (菲菲), they **coexist without disorder** (群居而不乱).
One **horn** (角) signifies **united defense against insult** (共御侮).
**Aligned tails** (同尾) signify **preserving the end** (全其终).

**Confucius** says: The **noble person aligns yet does not conform** (君子和而不同).

**Third (day):** **Gathering in light** (昆于白),
he **loses what does not align with darkness** (失不黑),
with **boundless alignment at the tail** (无际一尾) and **three horns** (三角).
**Zhōu** says: **Gathering in light** (昆白) and **not aligning with darkness** (不黑),
this is **lacking mutual affinity** (不相亲).
The **Fàn** (范) and **Wáng** (王) editions read **际** (际) as **chú** (除),
but now follows the **Sòng** (宋) and **Lù** (陆) editions.

**Fourth (day):** A **bird** (鸟) **entrusts its nest** (托巢) to the **withered grass** (菆),
a **person** (人) **entrusts his life** (寄命) to the **public** (公).
**Zhōu** says: A **bird entrusting its nest** (鸟托巢),
the **public** (公) will have no **poverty** (无贫).
The **Fàn** (范) and **Xiǎo Sòng** (小宋) editions read **菆** (菆) as **cóng** (丛),
but now follows the **Sòng** (宋), **Lù** (陆), and **Wáng** (王) editions.
**Chóu** (菆) is the ancient form of **cóng** (丛).
**Sòng** says: **Public** (公) means **equitable** (均).
**Guāng** says: The **public** (公) embodies the **Dao of great unity** (道大同),
with no **distinction of self and others** (无彼我也).
The fourth hexagram marks the **lower position of gathering** (下禄),
and it falls during the day.
If the **noble person** (君子) can **extend the heart of great unity** (推大同至公之心)
to **treat others** (以待人),
then all will **return and entrust their lives** (皆归往而寄命).
Hence, it is said: The **public** (公) will have no **poverty** (无贫).

**Fifth (day):** The **valley** (谷) **loses its spacing and order** (失疏数),
and the **crowd consumes** (众牦) the **jade** (玉).
**Zhōu** says: The **valley losing its spacing and order** (谷失疏数),
how can it **fulfill its equitable measure** (奚足旬)?
The **Fàn** (范) and **Xiǎo Sòng** (小宋) editions read **谷失疏数** (谷失疏数) as **谷不谷** (谷不谷),
**losing its spacing and order** (失疏数).
The **Wáng** (王) edition reads **毂不毂** (毂不毂),
**losing its spacing and order** (失疏数).
Now follows the **Sòng** (宋) and **Lù** (陆) editions.
**Gǔ** (谷) and **gǔ** (毂) are ancient characters interchangeable.
**Lù** says: **Xún** (旬) means **equitable** (均).
**King Wén** says: The fifth line dwells in the **position of preeminence** (盛位),
and should act as the **master of things** (物主).
Yet the **yang** (阳) occupies the **yin position** (阴位) and it is night—
it does not attain the **Dao of harmonious blending** (混同之道).
The **hub** (毂) is **surrounded by spokes** (为众辐所凑).
Since the fifth line has **lost the Dao** (失道),
it cannot **encompass all things**.
Hence, it is said: The **hub loses its function** (毂不毂).

The **lightness of the crowd’s consumption** (众耗之轻),
when accumulated, becomes **great** (成多),
and will inevitably lead to **the jade being ruined** (毁玉之患),
as when **many mouths melt gold** (众口铄金).

**Guāng** says: The **hub** (毂) receives the **spokes** (辐),
and **spacing and order must be equitable** (疏数必均),
only then can it **rotate and function** (运行).
The fifth line, though a **petty/ignoble person** (小人),
dwells in the **position of preeminence** (盛位).
Without the **heart of great unity** (至公之心),
his **likes and dislikes are private** (好恶任私).
Hence, **slander and deceit** (谗慝) advance together,
and the **crowd consumes the jade** (众牦毁玉).

**Sixth (day):** **Gathering at the well and marketplace** (昆于井市),
**wagons of the same script align on the same tracks** (文车同轨).
**Zhōu** says: **Gathering at the well and marketplace** (昆于井市),
this signifies **unity in rank and order** (同一伦).
The **well and marketplace** (井市) are where **people congregate** (人所聚).
The sixth hexagram marks the **height of abundance** (盛多) during the day.
The **Dao of the noble person** (君子道) is **profound in virtue and abundant** (隆德盛),
and the **four seas converge** (四海会向).
**Script is unified** (书同文), **wagons share the same tracks** (车同轨),
with no **far or near** (无有远迩),
all **blending into one category** (混为一类).

**Seventh (day):** The **cover is lopsided and does not shelter** (盖偏不覆),
the **late rain does not rescue** (晏雨不救).
**Zhōu** says: The **cover is lopsided and does not shelter** (盖偏不覆),
this signifies **virtue not being equitable** (德不均).
**Fù** (覆), pronounced *fū jiù* (敷救切), same below.
**Yàn** (晏), means **late** (晚).
Rain that comes **late** (晚) means
the **young plants flourish** (稚者昌) while the **established ones are harmed** (植者伤).
The seventh hexagram marks **decay and loss** (败损) during the night.
The **petty/ignoble person’s virtue** (小人之德),
cannot achieve **great unity** (大同),
his **intentions are skewed** (用心颇僻),
with **favoritism and prejudice** (有爱有恶).
Like a **lopsided cover** (盖偏),
there will be **things left unsheltered** (物有不覆者).
Like **late rain** (雨晚),
there will be **crops left unrescued** (禾有不救者).

**Eighth (day) and second (day) of Kūn:** **Gathering in peril and adversity** (二昆于危难),
now **turning to shelter and safety** (乃覆之安).
**Zhōu** says: **Safety in peril and adversity** (危难之安),
this is the **fruit of prior benevolence** (素施仁).
The eighth hexagram marks the **midpoint of calamity** (祸中),
**exhaustion and peeling away** (为耗为剥落),
and carries the **symbol of peril and adversity** (有危难之象).

Thus, during the day of **Kūn** (昆),
the **noble person** (君子) naturally practices the **Dao of universal unity** (昆同之道),
spreading **benevolent love** (泛爱) and **embracing the multitude** (容众),
treating all with **equal regard** (一视同仁).
Therefore, in times of **peril and adversity** (危难之际),
he can **shelter and protect** (庇覆於物) all things,
bringing them to **safety and ease** (使之就安).

**Ninth line (day):** **Kūn** at the point of **death** (昆于死),
abandoning the **bandit** (寇) and discarding him.
**Zhōu** says: **Kūn** at death (昆于死),
this is to **abandon one’s own body** (弃厥身).
**遗** (遗), pronounced with the *yǒu* (醉) rhyme.

**King Wáng** (王) says: To **abandon one’s body** (弃身) and **discard the bandit** (遗寇),
this is the **height of misfortune** (凶之甚).
**Guāng** (光) remarks: **Evil deeds differ in kind** (为恶不同),
yet all lead to **chaos and disorder** (同归于乱).
The **ninth line** (九) stands at the **extreme of calamity** (祸极),
and corresponds to the **night** (当夜),
thus bearing this **symbol** (象).

---

**The Fivefold Division of the Day (三方一州一部一家减):**
**Yang** (阳) **family** (家) **Water** (水), **harmonizing with decline** (准损).
**First line (day):** **Decline enters** (入减), **beginning at one** (初一),
forty-one **degrees and seventeen seconds** (分一十七秒),
aligning with the **Lesser Heat** (处暑) **qi** (气).

**Second line (day):** **The day relinquishes its sorrow** (日舍轸).
**Yin** (阴) **qi** (气) **wanes** (息),
**Yang** (阳) **qi** (气) **fades** (消).
When **Yin prevails and Yang wanes** (阴盛阳衰),
all things **grow subtle and faint** (万物以微).
The **King Wáng** (王) text reads: **Yin wanes, Yang fades** (阴息阳消),
while the **minor Sòng** (小宋) text reads: **All Yin wanes** (群阴息),
**Yang fades** (阳气消).
Now following the **Sòng, Lǚ, and Fàn** (宋、陆、范) texts.

**First line (day):** **Good decline, no decline** (善减不减),
**obscurity** (冥).
**Zhōu** says: **Good decline, no decline** (善减不减),
this is **constant self-abnegation** (常自冲).
**Lǚ** (陆) says: **Suāng** (冲) means **emptiness** (虚).
**Guāng** (光) remarks: **One** (一) marks **the beginning of reflection** (思始),
yet corresponds to the **day** (当昼).
Constantly **abasing oneself in humility** (自谦冲),
this is **good decline** (善减).
The **self-abased** (自损者),
others **benefit them** (益之),
thus there is **no decline** (不减).
**One** (一) is **lowest of the low** (下下),
**good decline** (善减) as **humility** (谦),
**no decline** (不减) as **benefit** (益),
both rooted in **obscure depths** (冥昧之中),
where **none can see** (人莫得而见),
hence the term **obscure** (冥).

**Second line (day):** **Decline of the heart** (心减),
**self-abased from within** (自中),
to **manifest in form upon the body** (以形于身).
**Zhōu** says: **Decline of the heart, form upon the body** (心减形身),
this is **inner constraint** (困诸中).
The **Sòng and Lǚ** (宋、陆) texts read **form as punishment** (刑),
now following the **Fàn and Wáng** (范、王) texts.

When a person **advances in virtue and cultivates their path** (进德修业),
they must **first strengthen their heart** (必自强於心),
then **display it outwardly** (然后显着於外).
**Two** (二) marks **the midpoint of reflection** (思中),
yet corresponds to the **night** (当夜).
**Intent** (志) **declines first** (先减矣),
how then can **virtue and deeds** (德业) **increase** (何从而益)?
This is **constraint from within** (是其中先自困).

The **Master** (子) said to **Rǎn Qiú** (冉求):
*"Those lacking strength* (力不足者),
*fail midway* (中道而废),
*yet you persist in your designs.*" (今汝画)

**Third line (day):** **Decline in one’s bearing** (减其仪),
**advantageous to use brightness** (利用光) **on the steps** (于阶).

「The **Diviner** (测) said: **Declining in one’s bearing** (减其仪) is **self-restraint** (欲自禁也).
**Three** (三) marks the **interaction between lower and upper** (下上),
**the culmination of reflection** (思终),
**and the advancement of the people** (进人).
Residing in the upper position **brings excess pride and arrogance** (则多骄盈),
**culminating in reflection** (思终) **manifests outwardly** (形於外#2).
When one’s bearing is declined, it is **self-deprecation and restraint** (自贬损，禁约也).
The **steps** (阶#3) are **the means of advancement** (所以进也#4).
Through self-deprecation yet attaining advancement, there is **radiance and splendor** (有光辉也).

**Fourth line (day):** **Decline at the threshold** (减於艾),
**lowering one’s station** (贬其位).
The **Diviner** (测) said: **Decline at the threshold** (减於艾) is **unfit to govern the multitude** (无以莅众也).
The **Fàn** (范) text renders 艾 as 乂, now following the **Sòng, Lǚ, and Wáng** (宋、陆、王) texts.
艾 and 乂 share the same pronunciation.
**Fàn** (范) states: 乂 is **governance** (治也#5).
**Guāng** (光) explains: **Four** (四) represents **lowering in fortune** (下禄),
yet corresponds to the **day** (当昼).
The **petty person** (小人) who **lacks diligence in governance** (不勤於为治),
**cannot govern the multitude** (无以临众#6),
and thus **cannot preserve their station** (则不能保其禄位矣).
Hence it is said: **Decline at the threshold** (减於艾),
**lowering one’s station** (贬其位).

**Fifth line (day):** **Declining the yellow constancy** (减黄贞),
**the lower supporting the upper** (下承于上),
**tranquility** (宁).
The **Diviner** (测) said: **Declining the yellow constancy** (减黄贞) is **the proper way of the subject** (臣道丁也).
The **Fàn** (范) text reads **declining its yellow constancy** (减其黄贞),
now following the other texts.
**Sòng** (宋) states: 丁 means **appropriate** (当也).
**Wáng** (王) says: **One attains station during the day** (得位当昼),
**able to decline in serving the master** (能为减主),
**and also gains the central yellow** (又得黄中),
**the path of upright constancy** (贞正之道),
**where all people converge** (众所咸归),
hence the lower supports the upper, and **tranquility is achieved** (而获安宁之福也).
**Guāng** (光) explains: **Five** (五) is **the peak of harmony** (中和莫盛乎),
hence it is said **yellow** (黄).
**Uprightness in station** (以阳当位) is why it is called **constancy** (贞).
When **prosperity reaches its peak** (夫盛极),
**it must decline** (则衰),
**and decline is inevitable** (不可不减).
If the subject **employs this path to support their superior** (臣用此道以承其上),
then **they may attain their own tranquility** (乃可自安),
hence it is said: **The way of the subject is appropriate** (臣道当也).

**Sixth line (day):** **Gathering in obscurity** (幽阐积),
**neither closed nor spread** (不缄不施),
**unyielding like stone** (石).
The **Diviner** (测) said: **Obscurity not spread** (幽阐不施) is **the waters not level** (泽不平也).
施 is pronounced as **shī** (失豉切).
**Fàn** (范) states: **Six** (六) is **water** (水),
hence **gathering in obscurity** (积幽).
**Xiǎo Sòng** (小宋) says: **Great openness is called 阐** (大开曰阐).
**Guāng** (光) explains: **Six** (六) and **the family’s nature** (家性) are both **water** (水),
hence it is said **obscurity** (幽).
**Six** (六) represents **lowering in fortune** (上禄),
hence it is said **阐** (阐).
It also signifies **abundance and extremity** (又为盛多为极大),
hence it is said **gathering** (积).
The **Noble Person** (君子) should **augment the few and diminish the many** (当裒多益寡),
**adjusting to things and spreading evenly** (称物平施).
Now, **neither diminishing nor spreading** (今乃不减不施),
**their obstinacy is like stone** (其顽如石),
hence it is said: **the waters are not level** (泽不平也).

**Seventh line (day):** **Declining their affliction** (减其疾),
**reducing their care** (损其恤),
**harshness does not reach** (厉不至).
The **Diviner** (测) said: **Declining their affliction** (减其疾) is **avoiding peril** (不至危也).
**Fàn** (范) states: **恤** (恤) means **grief** (忧也).
**厉** (厉) means **peril** (危也).
**Wáng** (王) says: **Seven** (七) **resides in overfullness** (居过满),
**the path of peril** (危之道也).
Yet **one attains station during the day** (而得位当昼),
**able to decline their afflictions** (是能自减其疾患),
**reduce their grief** (损其忧戚),
then **harshness has no way to reach** (则厉无从而至矣).
**Guāng** (光) explains: **Seven** (七) is **the beginning of calamity** (祸始),
yet corresponds to the **day** (当昼),
**lying in the realm of the declining family** (在於减家),
hence this **symbol** (故有是象).
The *Yì* (易) says: **Reduce their affliction,
so that they may swiftly have joy** (损其疾，使遄有喜).

**Eighth line (day):** **Flowing waves** (浏涟涟),
**declining at the source of life** (减于生根).
The **Diviner** (测) said: **The decline of flowing waves** (浏涟之减) is **the roots being destroyed** (生根毁也).
The **Wáng** (王) text lacks the **下涟** (下涟) character,
now following the **Sòng, Lǚ, and Fàn** (宋、陆、范) texts.
浏 is pronounced as **liú** (音留) or **lìjiǔ** (力久切).
涟 is pronounced as **lián** (音连).
**Fàn** (范) states: **Eight** (八) is **wood** (木).
**Wood begins to decline in autumn** (秋木始衰).
**浏** (浏) is **dripping** ().
**涟涟** (涟涟) is **the appearance of dripping** (垂之貌).
**Dripping** () is an ancient form of **流** (流).
**Guāng** (光) explains: **Eight** (八) is **wood** (木),
**the sinking of heaven** (沈天),
**lingering sickness** (疾瘀),
**consumption** (耗),
**and peeling away** (剥落).
**The sinking of heaven** (沈天) refers to **the transition between autumn and winter** (秋冬之交).
**Wood’s vitality** (木之所以生者) depends on **its roots** (根也).
**Its flourishing** (所以荣者) relies on **its nourishing fluids** (滋液也).
Now, **the nourishing fluids drip downward** (今滋液下流),
**yet the roots, the source of life, are declined** (涟涟，然减其资生之根),
**how then can it still flourish?** (则何以复能盛荣乎?)

**Uppermost ninth line (day):** **The culmination of decline** (减终),
**advantageous to ascend the Western Mountain** (利用登于西山),
**and overlook the great river** (临于大川).
The **Diviner** (测) said: **Ascending in the culmination of decline** (减终之登) is **truly achievable** (诚可为也).
**Fàn** (范) states: **Being in the West signifies metal** (在西为金),
hence it is said **Western Mountain** (西山).
**Metal generates water** (金生水),
hence it is said **great river** (大川).
**Guāng** (光) explains: **When things reach their extreme** (物极则反),
**the culmination of decline must increase** (故减终必增).
**Ascending the mountain and overlooking the river** (登山临川) is **to stand high over the low** (以高临下也#7).
**The West** (西) is **the completion of things** (物之成).
**Standing high over the low** (夫登高临下) is **rarely without peril** (鲜有不危),
yet **declining in this position makes it achievable** (以减处之，故可为也).

**Three regions, one state, two families** (三方一州一部二家唫).
**Yin family, fire** (阴家火),
**corresponding to the negative** (准否).」

The **Chen** (陈) pronunciation is **Qín** (钦), also **Qín** (琴), pronounced as *jùjǐn* in both Small Song and Wu dialects. The *Shuōwén Jiězì* states: "To speak urgently, the same as *jǐn* (噤)." We follow this today.

Lu (陆) says: **"Lì"** (唫) means "closed and sealed." When **Yin** (阴) does not transform, and **Yang** (阳) does not act, all things are sealed.

In general, when **Yang** (阳) applies its essence and **Yin** (阴) shapes its form, all things come to life. But during the **Chùshǔ** (处暑) season, when **Yin** (阴) does not transform and **Yang** (阳) does not act, all things are sealed.

On the first day, **"Lì"** (唫) does not give, and men dwell like women. **Cè** (测) says: **"Lì"** (唫) does not give, which is contrary to human nature. **"Yǔ"** (予) is the same as **"yǔ"** (与). **Wáng** (王) says: **"Lì"** (唫) is to close oneself off and not engage with others, like a man imitating a woman’s seclusion. **Guāng** (光) says: One is the beginning of thought, occurring at night, dwelling in the depths below. The **junzi** (君子) contemplates the **Dao** (道) and seeks to extend it to others, yet now he is sealed and does not engage—like a man dwelling as a woman does.

A solitary person without kin will be shunned by all, hence it is said, "contrary to human nature."

On the second day, **"lì"** (唫) is in the blood, nourishing the **Qián** (乾) bones. **Cè** (测) says: **"Lì"** (唫) in the blood makes one emaciated yet fattened. **Fàn** (范) originally wrote **"zāi"** (臞), pronounced *yūfù*, but all other versions write **"zāi"** (臞), with Small Song pronouncing it *jù* and Wu *qú*; we follow this. **Wáng** (王) says: **"Lì"** (唫) in the blood nourishes the **Qián** (乾) bones, fearing emaciation and seeking to fatten oneself. **Guāng** (光) says: Two is the middle of thought, occurring in the day, able to accumulate **virtue** (德) to beautify the body. The *Dàxué* (大学) says: *"Wealth moistens the house, **virtue** moistens the body."*

On the third day, the face does not engage, the mouth **chā** (唭), and **"lì"** (唫) has no words. **Cè** (测) says: The face does not engage, and human conduct is faint. **Fàn** (范) and **Wáng** (王) omit the mouth radical; we follow Small Song, Lu, and the second Song versions. **Chā** (唭) is pronounced *qùlì*, and **"yù"** (唭) is pronounced *yújì*. **Fàn** (范) says: **Chā** (唭) is sound without words. **Wáng** (王) says: Human conduct nearly reaches extinction. **Guāng** (光) says: Three is the end of thought, and also the completion of intention. Once thought is complete, speech and appearance can engage with others. But if the family nature is sealed, even during the day, it remains closed and unengaged—human conduct nearly vanishes. The *Yì* (易) *Jié* (节) hexagram, line two, says: *"Not going out of the gate or courtyard—disaster."*

On the fourth day, **"lì"** (唫) is in the grains, neither thriving nor common, burdening the old and the clan. **Cè** (测) says: **"Lì"** (唫) in the grains means they cannot be shared.

On the fifth day, neither central nor supervising, the stingy decay of rot and worms. **Cè** (测) says: Neither central nor supervising—this **"lì"** (唫) is wrong. Small Song says: *"Salaries await the worthy, yet granaries overflow with rotting grain—still they are stingy with their **lì** (唫)—this is wrong."* **Guāng** (光) says: **"Dū"** (督) is also **"zhōng"** (中). The **junzi** (君子) accumulates and knows how to disperse; if it should be sealed, then seal; if it should be applied, then apply. In the fifth position of abundance, the family nature is sealed, straying from the middle way during the day—thus rotting yet still stingy.

On the sixth day, springs and sources flow abundantly, sealed in hills and gardens. **Cè** (测) says: The sealing of springs and sources is not to be criticized. The second Song, Lu, and **Wáng** (王) versions write **"yì"** (议) instead of **"jī"** (讥); we follow **Fàn** (范). **Fàn** (范) says: Six is water, hence springs and sources. Hills and gardens are used to illustrate elevation. **Guāng** (光) says: Six is the peak of abundance, the utmost. To store water in high places and wait for the time to apply it means its reach is far. Today’s sealing is the means for tomorrow’s blessings—thus it is not to be criticized.

---

The seventh day, stinginess affects the body, and the yellow flesh is ruined.
**Cè** says: Stinginess affecting the body is harm to bone and flesh.
The **Wáng** version renders "yellow flesh" as "yellow bone and flesh," but we follow the **Sòng**, **Lù**, and **Fàn** versions.

Seven represents decay and ruin, and it is also the time of night.
Yellow is the central point.
Bone and flesh reside in the center, hence "yellow flesh."
**Qi** and blood nourish the body; if stinginess does not extend to the four limbs, then bone and flesh are ruined.
Enlightenment and blessings bind kin; if stinginess does not extend to the nine clans, then inner and outer relations are estranged.

The eighth day, stinginess meets misfortune; prayers with an ox resolve it.
**Cè** says: Stinginess encountering misfortune is the proper cost.
**Fàn** says: The ox is a great expense.
**Wáng** says: Though excessive stinginess invites disaster, one can pray with an ox to avert calamity.
**Guāng** says: Eight is the midpoint of misfortune, hence "stinginess meets disaster."
The night’s omen is thus: "Prayers with an ox resolve it."
To not spare expense in resolving disaster is the right way.

The ninth day, stinginess brings no rain; faith lies in dried meat.
**Cè** says: Stinginess without rain—what can be hoped for?
**Fàn** says: Faith is trust.
**Wáng** says: Nine is the extreme of stinginess, yet it is night.
Yin and Yang are both closed, hence no rain.
**Guāng** says: When nourishment is exhausted and no hope remains, it is indeed true that flesh dries into jerky.
This speaks of the ruler’s blessings failing, and the people suffering.

Three regions, one state, and one clan guard together:
The Yang family guards wood—this is also the principle of negation.
The Yin guards the door, the Yang guards the gate; nothing disturbs them.

The first day, closing the doors and windows, guarding what is original.
**Cè** says: Closing the doors and windows is a good way to hold what one has.
The **Wáng** and **Xiǎo Sòng** versions render "closing the doors and windows" as "closing the bright windows," but we follow the **Sòng**, **Lù**, and **Fàn** versions.
**Fàn** says: Doors and windows are companions and associates.
**Guāng** says: The *Yì* says: "Wandering to and fro, companions follow your thoughts."
When the mind is moved by something, the thing responds in kind.
Windows are for observing the outside.
Original is the beginning.
The *Lèjì* says: "Man is born and remains still, this is Heaven’s nature; moved by things, he acts, this is human nature."
One is the beginning of thought and the time of night; able to close the enticements of external companions and guard the original nature of what one possesses.

The second day, lost in self-guard, not as effective as the first’s holding.
**Cè** says: Lost in self-guard, there is no proper means.
**Sòng** says: *Yì* (以) is *yòng* (use).
Without proper use, self-guard is all there is.
**Wáng** says: The second day guards its confusion, the first guards its way, hence the second is not as effective as the first.
**Guāng** says: Two is the midpoint of thought, yet it is night; enticed by external things, lost in confusion, and failing to guard what it should.
The *Shū* says: "Only the people are born with abundance; because of things, they change."

The third day, neither loss nor gain, coming and going in silence.
**Cè** says: Neither loss nor gain is guarding one’s original state.
Three is the stage of human progress, self-composure, and fulfilled intent—night’s omen.
Virtue is completed within; able to guard without losing it.
The coming and going of external things—what loss or gain do they bring me?
Silent and fulfilled? Trust without words.
The *Yì* says: "Neither loss nor gain, coming and going in harmony."

The fourth day, the **Guān** (艮) symbol guards.
**Cè** says: The **Guān** symbol’s guard is strict and without leniency.
The **Sòng** and **Lù** versions render "the **Guān** symbol guards" as "the **Guān** (贝) symbol guards itself," while the **Xiǎo Sòng** version writes "the **Guān** (貌) symbol guards," but we follow the **Wáng** version.
**Sòng** says: *Guān* (贝) is pronounced *gǒu* (狗).
**Lù** says: *Guān* ( ) is prohibition and restraint.
The **Guān** symbol, like a dog, cannot discern what is proper, only restraining.
**Fàn** says: The **Guān** symbol is likeness, like a straw dog.
**Guāng** says: *Guān* (贝) is pronounced *gǒu* (狗); it should read "the **Guān** (艮) symbol is a mistake for *gǒu* (狗)."
The **Guān** symbol, like a dog, is akin to saying "the **Guān** symbol like a dragon."

The fifth day, guarding the center with harmony, binding the lords with sincerity.
**Cè** says: Guarding the center with harmony is the nature of the lords.
The **Sòng** and **Lù** versions render "binding the lords with sincerity" as "binding the center with one’s sincerity," while the **Wáng** version renders "binding the lords with sincerity" as "binding the lords with their sincerity," but we follow the **Fàn** version.
**Fàn** says: Five is the Son of Heaven; guarding the way of central harmony, the proper sovereign over all lords.
**Guāng** says: Sincerity is alignment.
None excel in central harmony as much as Five.
Guarding the way of central harmony, to bind the lords with sincerity, is the standard by which the lords are called and aligned.

The sixth day, chariot wheels halted, jade and jasper dusted.
**Cè** says: Chariot wheels halted means no contact with neighbors.
The **Wáng** version renders "no contact with neighbors" as "exchange with neighbors," but we follow the other versions.
**Yùn** (轫), pronounced *rěn* (而振).
**Xiǎo Sòng** says: **Yùn** (轫) is the wooden block that hinders chariot wheels.
In rituals for lords, weapons are not used; the lords themselves are the proper tools for order.
Losing sincerity in guarding, no contact with neighboring states, chariot wheels halted and not moving, jade and jasper gathering dust and unused.
**Guāng** says: The family nature is guarding; six exceeds the center and is night—a case of guarding too much.

The seventh day, the many Yang do not guard, the males’ sincerity.
**Cè** says: The many Yang’s guard is guarding sincerity and trust.
The **Wáng** version omits "do not guard," but we follow the other versions.

The eighth day, the mortar lacks its pestle, and its pestle is raised.
Heaven is dark and does not rain, the bright day destroys the heat.
**Cè** says: The mortar lacks its pestle—this is guarding poverty.
**Wáng** says: A mortar without a pestle means guarding without any use.
The pestle is raised and does not descend—guarding without any fulfillment.
**Guāng** says: Its pestle is raised—there is no grain to pound.
Heaven is dark and does not rain; there is no nourishment to hope for.
Eight is the midpoint of misfortune, of depletion, hence this symbol.

The ninth at the top: **With Tu** there is guarding, and he speaks at Lu’s head—no peril.
**Cè** says: With **Tu** there is guarding, hence it is ever renewed.
The **Sòng** and **Lù** versions read *has Tu has guarding*, while **Cè** reads *its Tu its guarding*; we follow the **Fàn**, **Wáng**, and **Xiǎo Sòng** versions.
**Tu**, pronounced *tú*.
**Sòng** says: *Què* means "to surpass."
**Fàn** says: **Tu** is white. **Lú** is black. Nine is the West, hence white.
**Guāng** says: **Tu** is the flourishing of thatch, its color white.
Nine marks the end of calamity and faces the day—one who repents his misfortune.
Thus, he contemplates the old and wise with white hair, guarding his former Dao,
and declines the black-haired, deceitful men of false speech—
so the state does not fall into peril.
The *Qín Shì* says:
*"The brave warriors, their strength exhausted, yet I still have them.
The valiant fighters, their archery and charioteering unerring, yet I do not desire them."*

**Yǐn** family gold, conforming to **Xùn**.
Entering **Xùn**, five degrees, eighteen minutes thirty-four seconds—white dew qi responds.
The day follows the Star of Longevity, the Dipper in the **Yǒu** position, the **Nán Lǚ** pitch.
**Lù** says: **Xùn** is "enter."
**Yù** is also "enter."
**Wáng** says: **Yù** means "to gather."
**Guāng** says: **Xùn** is the rooster, hence the image of many flying crows gathering.

When **Yīn** comes, it acts contrary; when **Yáng** goes, it acts in harmony. Things retreat, descend, and assemble.
The **Sòng**, **Lù**, **Wáng** versions read *"Yáng goes with harmony"*—we follow the **Fàn** version.
**Sòng** says: **Yīn** comes from below, hence it is called "contrary."
**Yáng** goes from above, hence it is called "harmony."
**Guāng** says: When **Yīn** rises, it harms things, hence "contrary."
When **Yáng** descends, it nurtures things, hence "harmony."

The first day: **Raging** surges in darkness, gathering his resolve.
Though he might wish to wander freely, Heaven does not grant it.
**Cè** says: **Raging** surges in darkness—Heaven has not yet favored him.
The **Sòng** and **Lù** versions read *"gathers his resolve"* as *"with gathering resolve"*—we follow the **Fàn** version.
The **Fàn** version reads *"wander freely"* as *"adroitly at ease"*—we follow the **Sòng**, **Lù**, **Wáng** versions.
**Wáng** says: *Zī* is the ancient form of *zī* (to nourish).
**Guāng** says: One is the beginning of thought, and it faces night.
The petty man harbors evil intent, raging and surging in the murky depths, gathering his resolve inwardly.
Though he might wish to wander freely, Heaven does not nourish it.

The second day: Gathering in darkness, he shoots true.
**Cè** says: Gathering in darkness—this is upright yielding.
The **Wáng** version reads *"upright yielding"* as *"upright bow"*—we follow the other versions.
Yielding is the same as *yǔ* (to give).
Two is the midpoint of thought, facing the day.
The virtuous man has a good heart, also gathering inwardly in the murky depths.
Like shooting—his resolve is upright, and when he releases, it never misses. Hence, *"shoots true."*
Upright yielding means "only the upright is yielding."

The third day: Gathering to eat, **zhuó zhuó**.
**Cè** says: Gathering to eat, **zhuó zhuó**—beneficial as dancing.
**Zhuó**, pronounced *zhǔ*.
**Fàn** says: **Zhuó zhuó** is the appearance of swift eating.
**Wáng** says: **Zhuó zhuó** exhausts the grain—extreme greed.
Desiring swift benefit, like dancing to the rhythm.
**Guāng** says: Three is the completion of intent, facing night, near fortune.
The petty man, seeing fortune, is greedy and strives to seize it, leaving nothing untried.

The fourth day: Gathering his wings, beneficial to act.
**Cè** says: Gathering his wings—friends assist.
Four is lower fortune, also external "other," facing the day’s light.
Like crows gathering their wings, beneficial to act.
The scholar gains friends’ aid, beneficial for advancement.

The fifth day: Gathering his belly, avoiding the grain fields.
**Cè** says: Gathering his belly—this is not how one gains renown.
The **Fàn**, **Wáng**, and **Xiǎo Sòng** versions read *"avoiding the grain fields"* as *"avoiding the golden grain fields"*—we follow the **Sòng** and **Lù** versions.
The **Fàn** and **Xiǎo Sòng** versions read *"renown"* as *"advancement"*—we follow the **Lù** and **Wáng** versions.
Five is central fortune, also the belly, facing the night—
he can only gather his blessings for himself.
Thus, *"gathering his belly."*
One who hoards for himself will surely be deprived—
this is the way to avoid fortune, much less fame.

The sixth day: Yellow heart, vast wings—gathering to the heavens.
**Cè** says: Yellow heart, vast wings—beneficial to gain support.
The **Sòng**, **Lù**, and **Wáng** versions read *"beneficial"* as *"harmonious"*—we follow the **Fàn** version.
Six is middle-high, hence called yellow.
**Hóng** is the bird that flies highest.
Six is also abundant and flourishing, dwelling in the peak of fortune, facing the day.
The virtuous man takes the Middle Way as his heart, with many supporters.
Like the vast wings of the **Hóng**, his flight knows no bounds.

The seventh day: Gathering the snare, **zhuó zhuó**.
**Cè** says: Gathering the snare, **zhuó zhuó**—suffering separation and harm.
**Zhuó**, pronounced *zhuó*.
**Fàn** says: Seven is the cord, the arrow.
The arrow uses a cord—this is the snare.
**Guāng** says: Seven is the stage of calamity, facing night, hence suffering separation and harm.

The eighth day: Its **fàn**, severing its **jiān**—peril.
**Cè** says: **Fàn**, severing **jiān**—dangerous yet fulfilled.
**Fàn** is the same as *huī* (to swing).
**Jiān**, pronounced *xiàn*.
**Wáng** says: In daylight, one can still be in danger—
though not reaching ultimate calamity, it is extreme peril.
**Xiǎo Sòng** says: **Fàn** is an overturned cart.
**Jiān** is a net.
**Guāng** says: Eight is the midpoint of calamity, facing the day, hence escape is possible.

The ninth at the top: Its horn—maintaining to subdue his kin.
**Cè** says: Its horn—exterminating his kind.

---

**抵**, pronounced *zhǐ* (as in "paper").

**Fàn** says: **抵** is to strike.
**Wáng** says: Its horn#25, used to repel things—
things that share the same hatred.
**Guāng** says: Nine is extermination, ultimate calamity, ferocity, the horn,
and it faces the night.
Though calamity rages unceasing, reaching its utmost extremity,
it still seeks victory through ferocity, hence the destruction of one’s kin.

**Three regions, one state, two departments, two clans gathered**—
**Yáng**-clan’s earth, conforming to **Cùi** (Gathering).
Entering the gathering, the seventh day, discards the horn.
The *Yì* (Book of Changes) says: **Cùi**, the **Wáng** (King) feigns a temple.
Thus, the gathering abounds with spirits and gods.

**Yīn**-**qì** (vital breath) collects and gathers,
**Yáng** (brightness) does not restrain or rule—
things gather and exalt one another.
**Chóng** (exalt), also means to gather.

The first day: Spirits and gods are without form—**líng** (numinous).
**Cè** says: Spirits and gods without **líng** (numinous power), their forms are unseen.
**Xiàn** (seen), pronounced *xiàn* (as in "acclaim").
**Wáng** says: Spirits and gods take formlessness as **líng** (numinous).
**Chén** says: The gathering—**Yīn**-**qì** (vital breath) collects.
All things decay and decline; form returns to formlessness,
things return to their roots.
What is rootless? Is it not **líng** (numinous)?
The **jīng qì** (refined vital breath) becomes things,
wandering spirits as transformations—
when gathered, they become things;
when scattered, they are formless.
Thus, spirits and gods take formlessness as **líng** (numinous).

**Guāng** says: One is the lowest of the low,
also the water—
a symbol of profound darkness.
The second day: **Yàn cùi xī xī** (Gathered like swallows, joyous and merry).
**Cè** says: **Yàn cùi xī xī**, indulging in licentious revelry.
**Fàn**’s original text had *yàn* (transgression), **Xiǎo Sòng** as *yàn* (transgression),
now following **Sòng**, **Lù**, and **Wáng**’s original text.
Two is the midpoint of thought, the common folk#26;
facing the night.
Gathering in darkness and reveling in excess,
overindulgence leads to transgression.

The third day: **Zōng qí gāo nián** (Revering the high years), the gate of spirits.
**Cè** says: Revering the high years—
spirits await respect.
**Sòng**, **Lù**, and **Wáng**’s original texts all read *Zōng qí gāo xīn* (Revering the high virtue),
now following **Fàn**’s original text.
**Fàn** says: Three is the gate.

The fourth day: Dragging a sheep to offer at the **cóng shè** (shrine groves),
holding the **guī** (jewel-axe) to reverence its left thigh—**yě** (wild).
**Cè** says: Dragging a sheep at the grove,
insufficient for honor.
**Fàn**’s original text had *xìn bì qí zuǒ gǔ* (reverence and ward off its left thigh),
**Cè** says: Insufficient for labor,
now following **Sòng**, **Lù**, and **Wáng**’s original text.
**Xìn** (reverence), same as *shēn* (to extend).
The sheep is the **zhōng shèng** (central sacrificial victim).
Shrines follow groves and forests—
the lesser spirits.
Holding the **guī** (jewel-axe), a solemn rite.
The reverence should extend the right thigh.
Four is the night of the day,
though there be blessings and riches,
they cannot be utilized—
like dragging a sheep to merely display at the shrine groves,
insufficient for honor.
Holding the **guī** (jewel-axe) and bowing,
it extends the left thigh,
thus failing to escape vulgarity.

The fifth day: **Dǐng xuè zhī yòu** (The tainted blood of the cauldron),
**jiǔ zōng zhī hào** (the delight of nine clans),
thus the later has trust.
**Cè** says: The tainted blood of the cauldron,
trusting the **Wáng**’s (King’s) command.
**Wáng** says: **Yòu**, same as *yòu* (to blaze),
meaning the sacrificial firewood.

The sixth day: **Wèi qí guǐ** (Fearful of its spirits),
**zūn qí lǐ** (honoring its rites),
**kuáng zuò mí yín** (madness as licentiousness),
**wáng** (perishing).
**Cè** says: The madness of fearing spirits,
exceeding the proper measure.
**Fàn**’s original text had *mèi* (to obscure);
now following **Sòng**, **Lù**, and **Wáng**’s original text.

The **King** says: *Mí* (眯) means the eyes see nothing#28.
Madness and blindness while seeking licentious rites—then it is in darkness.

The seventh: **Sǒng cuì at the mound and tomb.**
**Cè** says: **Sǒng cuì** at mound and tomb—rites are not abandoned.
The **King** says: **Sǒng** means reverence.
**Guāng** says: Seven is high, is rites, and marks the beginning of calamity, yet it is daytime.
With reverence gathered at mound and tomb, it is the symbol of burial rites.

The eighth: **The screech-owl in the forest, trampling the many birds.**
**Cè** says: The screech-owl in the forest—what all gather upon.
The **Sòng** and **Lù** texts read *guā* (呱), the **Wáng** text reads *fèi* (吠); now following the **Fàn**, **Xiǎo Sòng** texts.
*Guā*, pronounced *jiāo* (交切), with multiple tones.
The **King** says: The screech-owl—evil bird.
Gathered in the forest—surely it will be hissed by the many birds.
**Guāng** says: Eight is the midpoint of calamity, yet it is night.
The petty man’s evil reputation has already taken root, like the screech-owl in the forest, where the many birds must gather and hiss at it.

The ninth: **Drooping tears, dripping from the nose, gathering the family’s assembly.**
**Cè** says: Drooping tears dripping from the nose—time’s command is severed.
**Xiǎo Sòng** says: *Huì* (汇) means kindred.
**Guāng** says: Nine is the extreme of calamity, is extermination.
Like the noble man who, though born with virtuous power, dies in sorrow.
When **Zhào Wénzǐ** completed his hall, he said: *"Sing here, weep here, gather the clans here"*—this is what is meant.

Three regions, one state, two departments, three families, and the accumulation#29 of **Yīn** family water—like **Dà Chù** (Great Accumulation).
**Yīn** is about to close in greatness, while **Yáng** remains slightly open.
Mountains, rivers, marshes, and swamps—all things return here.
The **King** says: Mountains and marshes are where all things are stored and hidden.
**Guāng** says: When **Yīn** is strong and **Yáng** is weak, all things reach the end of **Yáng** and return to be hidden in mountains, rivers, marshes, and swamps, piled within them.

The first: **Dark accumulation of *pǐ* (否), forming the foundation of light.**
**Cè** says: Dark accumulation of *pǐ*—beginning in evil.
The **Sòng** and **Lù** texts read *"beginning in evil"* as *"already in evil"*, the **Fàn** text lacks *"already"*, and the **Xiǎo Sòng** text lacks *"already"*—now following the **Wáng** text.
The **Wáng** text reads *zī* (资) for *jī* (基); now following the other texts.
*Pǐ* is pronounced *bǐ* (鄙).
**Fàn** says: *Pǐ* means not good.
**Guāng** says: One is the beginning of thought, yet it is night.
The petty man accumulates evil in darkness and invites punishment in light, so the hidden evil becomes the foundation for clear retribution.

The second: **Accumulation unused until it reaches great use—then the noble man steadies his heart.**
**Cè** says: Accumulation unused—it cannot be measured.
*Dù* (度) is pronounced *dài* (待洛切)#30.
**Fàn** says: *Jiè* (介) means great.
**Sòng** says: Accumulation long unused—virtuous power deeply hidden—when the time comes, it reaches great use.
**Guāng** says: Two is the midpoint of thought, yet it is daytime.
The noble man accumulates virtue within, constrained in low station, unused by the times.
Yet if the accumulation does not cease, its use must be great.
The noble man merely broadens his virtuous heart—he does not eagerly seek use.

The third: **Accumulating stones that do not eat—wasting labor and effort.**
**Cè** says: Accumulating stones that do not eat—nothing can be gained.
Three is the upper thought, yet it is night.
Wasting the heart on uselessness, laboring in vain—like accumulating stones that cannot be eaten, toiling without gain.

The fourth: **The noble man accumulates virtue until it reaches the carriage-ears.**
**Cè** says: The noble man accumulates virtue until it flourishes.
The **Xiǎo Sòng** text reads *"until it flourishes"* as *"until great flourishing"*—now following the other texts.
The **King** says: *Fán* (蕃) means progeny, pronounced *fú* (附袁切).
**Xiǎo Sòng** says: *Fán* means carriage-ears, pronounced *fú* (敷袁切).
**Guāng** says: Carriage-ears are the two crossbars.
To reach the carriage-ears—speaking of its full accumulation being visible.

The fifth: **The hidden does not fill, the thief does not profit.**
**Cè** says: The hidden fills, the thief profits—it only harms itself.
The **Wáng** text reads *jiǎn* (减) for *cáng* (藏); now following the other texts.
*Cáng* is pronounced *cú* (徂浪切).
Five occupies an exalted position, receiving great fortune, yet does not practice virtue and charity, but accumulates without end—just enough to profit the thief.
The **Qín** accumulated the **Ào Cāng** for the resources of **Chǔ-Hàn**; the **Sūi** accumulated at **Luòkǒu** for **Lǐ Mì’s** use—all are of this kind.

The sixth: **Great fullness and generous bestowal—obtaining people without arrogance.**
**Cè** says: Great fullness and generous bestowal—people come to it.
The **Wáng** text reads *fāng* (方) for *kàng* (亢); now following the other texts.
*Shī* (施) is pronounced *shì* (式豉切).
The **King** says: Six occupies a position of prosperity, obtaining the time of day.
The accumulation is greatly full, yet able to bestow greatly, aiding all things.
Thus, people all turn to it—its way is boundless.
**Guāng** says: Wealth dispersed gathers people—thus it obtains them.
*Kàng* means rival.
The *Shī* says: *"None rival, only the people."* Thus, it is without rival in the world.

The seventh: **Protruding and prominent, jade and silk arrayed—deciding to invite bandits.**
**Cè** says: Protruding and prominent—this is the thief’s invitation.
The **Fàn** text reads *shōu* (收) for *zhāo* (招); the **Wáng** text reads *kuài* (快) for *jué* (决)—now following the **Sòng**, **Lù**, and **Wáng** texts.
*Kuī* (魁) means the head emerges.
*Yán* (颜) means it is conspicuous.
*Bān* (班) means to array.
Seven marks the beginning of calamity, yet it is night.
Accumulation without end—protruding and conspicuous, jade and silk arrayed—though it can satisfy one’s desires for a time, it does not know that thieves will follow.

The eighth: **Accumulating virtue invites calamity—it is the guilt of the ancestors.**
**Cè** says: Accumulating virtue invites calamity—it is not one’s own fault.
The **Xiǎo Sòng** text reads *zhǎn* (展) for *chén* (辰); now following the other texts.
*Chén* means time.
A family that accumulates evil must have lingering retribution.
Eight is the midpoint of calamity, yet it is night.
Though the body accumulates virtue and suffers the calamity of the times, it is the guilt of their ancestors.

The ninth: **The petty man accumulates wrong until it reaches his descendants.**

「The eighth: **Accumulating virtue invites calamity—it is the fault of one’s forebears.**
**Cè** says: Accumulating virtue invites calamity—it is not one’s own guilt.

The ninth: **The petty man accumulates wrong until it extends to his descendants.**
**Cè** says: The petty man accumulates wrong, and calamity is thereby deferred. The *Wáng* text renders *miáo* (苗) as *wáng* (亡), while the *Xiǎo Sòng* text renders it as *dí* (笛), pronounced like *dí* (迪). Now following the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and *Fàn* texts.
*Miáo* (苗), anciently written as *wěi* (委), signifies deferred consequences.

Fàn says: In cases where evil is great, calamity will extend even to the descendants.
Guāng says: When wrong is accumulated to its extreme, calamity reaches its full measure—deferred and deferred, it thus drags on to affect the descendants. #31

**Three regions, one state; three departments, one family adorned.**
The *Yáng* family’s fire—corresponding to *Bēn* (贲).
Upon entering adornment, the eighth hexagram, **thirty-six degrees with fifteen seconds**, the *Qiūfèn* (秋分) *qi* aligns, hence it also corresponds to *Duì* (兑).
*Duì* signifies speech and tongue, thus adornment displays the symbols of excessive words.

The *Yáng* (阳) adorned in white, the *Yīn* (阴) in black—each fulfills its role, and adornment marks their entering and exiting.
The *Sòng*, *Lù*, and *Fàn* texts render *jué* (厥) as *qí* (其). Now following the *Sòng* and *Lù* texts.
*Sòng* says: When *Yīn* *qi* emerges, it governs above, hence white is used for adornment.
When *Yáng* *qi* enters, it governs below, hence black is used for adornment.
*Lù* says: *Yīn* governs the west, hence white; *Yáng* retreats to the north, hence black.
*Wáng* says: White signifies manifestation, black signifies concealment.
White and black divide in form—their adornment is thus revealed.

The first: **Speech without words—words without speech.**
**Cè** says: Speech without words—silent yet trustworthy.
The *Wáng* text omits *zàncí* (赞辞) and reads only *yán bù yǐ yán* (言不以言). Now following the other texts.
One signifies the beginning of thought, and it is daytime.
The virtuous man inwardly upholds his utmost sincerity, submerged in profound silence, regarding speechlessness as true speech.
This is so because silent trustworthiness is the essence of speech.
Confucius said: What does Heaven speak?
The four seasons proceed in their course, and all things are born.

The second: **Adornment without substance—first the fine words, then the loss of proper conduct.**
**Cè** says: Adornment without substance, first the fine words—this is the loss of sincerity.
The *Wáng* text renders *zhēn* (贞) as *zhēn* (真). Now following the other texts.
*Wáng* says: Without its foundation, seeking adornment, though it may begin with elegant words, it will ultimately lose its proper conduct.
Guāng says: Two signifies the midpoint of thought, yet it is night.
The petty man lacks inner sincerity, merely indulging in outward adornment.
At first, his words and appearance may seem trustworthy and beautiful, but in the end, he loses his true conduct.
Conduct refers to the manifestation of virtue in outward form.

The third: **He spits forth a yellow tongue, grasps a yellow brush—advantageous to see the wise.**
**Cè** says: The advantage of a sharp tongue and brush—this is the advantage of discerning people.
The *Sòng* and *Lù* texts render *tǔ huáng shé* (吐黄舌) as *tǔ huáng qiú shé* (吐黄酋舌), and the *Fàn* and *Xiǎo Sòng* texts render *qián* (钳) as *gān* (柑). Now following the *Wáng* text.
*Qián* (钳) is pronounced *jūlián* (渠廉).
*Zhī* (知) is used interchangeably with *zhì* (智).
*Qián* (钳) means to hold.
*Yù* (聿) means brush.
The virtuous man speaks and writes, never straying from the central path—only the wise can understand this, while the foolish are unworthy of discussion.
*The Fǎyán* (法言) says: Speech is the voice of the heart.
Writing is the drawing of the heart.
Voice and drawing reveal form—the virtuous and the petty are thus distinguished.

The fourth: **A sharp tongue, blustering and clamorous—this is the proper conduct of the merchant.**
**Cè** says: The proper conduct of blustering and clamorous speech—this is advantageous to the merchant.
The *Sòng* and *Lù* texts render *lì shé wāwā* (利舌哇哇) as *shé wāwā* (舌哇哇), while the *Fàn* and *Xiǎo Sòng* texts render it as *lì kǒu wāwā* (利口哇哇). Now following the *Wáng* text.
*Wā* (哇) is pronounced *wūjiā* (乌佳).
*Wáng* says: Merely adorning words with empty rhetoric to dazzle and sell—this is the proper conduct of the merchant, not the virtuous man’s true path.

---

**The Fifth:** Speech humbles like water, yet its essence is the **Heavenly Vagina** (天牝).
**Cè** says: Humble speech like water—it can wash away impurities.
Now following the **Wáng** text, where *tàn* (牝) is rendered as *bǐ* (比).
The *Xiǎo Sòng* text states: Though firm in principle, one can empty the heart to receive sound counsel, heeding advice like flowing water—what would not be embraced?
**Guāng** says: *Tàn* (牝) means "valley." The **Heavenly Vagina** refers to the **sea**.
Five holds the exalted position yet aligns with daytime, humbling itself to accept others' words.
Thus, people vie to tell it of the **Dao** (善道), like water flowing into the sea.
The sea, though low, receives all streams, and so achieves its vastness;
The **virtuous ruler** (君), though humble, gathers all goodness, and so achieves his **sainthood** (圣).

**The Sixth:** Speech has no retraction—it soars like the wind, the **Great Person** (大人) a thunderous gust.
**Cè** says: Speech that cannot be retracted—this is also to exalt it.
The **Great Person** refers to one in a noble position.
Six passes the midpoint yet aligns with night, marking one who errs in speech.
A single misspoken word cannot be undone by four horses’ tongues—hence, "speech has no retraction."
Though one cleanses it, the more foul it becomes—this is the **mouth** (口) #32.
Even if one tries to suppress it, it has already soared—hence, "it soars like the wind."
Moreover, power and blessings lie within oneself—when words issue forth, swift as wind and thunder, they bring life or ruin to all things.
How can one not be cautious?

**The Seventh:** When the time for speech is not ripe, words are faint, and the **superior** (上) grows suspicious.
**Cè** says: If the time for speech is not ripe, how can it be clear?
The *Xiǎo Sòng* text omits the *bù* (不) character; now following the other families.
*Jiàn* (见) is pronounced *xián biàn* (贤遍).
**Fàn** says: *Dīng* (丁) means "proper" or "due."
A faint wind is all that can be done—it cannot be clear and evident.
**Guāng** says: Seven marks the beginning of calamity, yet aligns with daytime.
The **virtuous man** (君子) serving under a **tyrannical ruler** (暴君) cannot speak directly, so he uses faint words, a gentle wind.
If he speaks clearly, the ruler will surely doubt him.
Confucius said: The **virtuous man** (君子) must first be trusted before offering counsel.
If he is not trusted, his words will be taken as slander against himself.

**The Eighth:** Murmuring like cicadas, ceaselessly, blood flows from the mouth.
**Cè** says: Murmuring like cicadas—this is self-inflicted harm.
*Chuì* (蛁) is the same as *tiáo* (蜩).
*Yōng* (喁) is pronounced *yǒng* (颙).
**Wáng** says: *Tiáo* (蜩) is the insect that chirps incessantly.
**Guāng** says: *Yōng yōng* (喁喁) is like *zhūn zhūn* (谆谆).
Eight marks the midpoint of calamity, yet aligns with night.
When the ruler does not accept counsel, the minister forcefully presses his words, risking either humiliation or punishment.
Like the cicada’s ceaseless chirping, though blood flows from the mouth, who would listen?
It only harms oneself.

**The Ninth (Upper):** A **white tongue** (白舌) speaks *yú yú* (于于), bent at its root, the **virtuous man** (君子) loses faith.
**Cè** says: A **white tongue** speaking *yú yú*—this is truly a lasting way.
The *Sòng* and *Lù* texts render **Cè**’s words as: "A **white tongue** (信舌不白)."
**Wáng** renders *yú yú* (于于) as *gān gān* (干干), and **Cè** says: "This marks speech that is not trusted."
