The *Xiǎo Sòng* text reads: "A **white tongue** (白舌不白), bent at its root."
Now following the **Fàn** text.
*Fǒu* (否) means "contradict," pronounced *fāng jiǔ* (方九).
*Yú yú* (于于) describes a **bent** or **submissive** posture.
Nine marks the extreme of calamity, yet aligns with daytime.
The **virtuous man** (君子) in an age of **non-Dao** (无道), when his words are not believed, should only bend his tongue and keep silent.
This is truly a lasting way.
The *Yì* (易) says: "If words are not trusted, even the mouth will be exhausted."
Three families, one state, three departments, and two households have doubts.
Wood and **accommodation** (贲) are also aligned.
This doubt is like the **wind** (巽) or **thunder** (震)—the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and **Wáng** texts all take it as the **wind** (巽), while **Fàn** takes it as **thunder** (震).
Both are incorrect.
When **yin** and **yang** clash, all things are injured and scattered—this is doubt.
*Dǐng* (硙) is pronounced *wǔ duì* (午对).
**Sòng** says: When things clash and are scattered, it is called *dǐng*.
At this time, **yin** and **yang** clash, their proportions balanced, and day and night equal.
**Lù** says: *Diāo* (雕) means "injured," and *lí* (离) means "scattered."
When **yin** is low yet dominant, and **yang** is exalted yet neglected, this is doubt—this is injury and scattering.

**The First:** Doubt is restless—**virtue/power** (德) and **truth** (贞) are lost.
**Cè** says: Doubt that is not upright—how can it be settled?
The *Xiǎo Sòng* text renders *huí* (徊) and *chuàng* (恛) as *jǔ* (呬), with the pronunciation *xū lì* (虚利).
*Jǔ jǔ* (呬呬) means "laugh."
Now following the other families.
**Fàn** renders *bù zhèng zhī yí* (不正之疑) as *yí kǎi* (疑怬失贞).
Now following the other families.
*Chuàng* (恛) is pronounced *huí* (回).
**Fàn** says: *Shǐ* (矢) means "straight."
**Wáng** says: *Chuàng chuàng* (恛恛) describes a **confused** and **chaotic** state.
**Guāng** says: One marks the beginning of thought, yet aligns with night.
The **petty man** (小人) has no upright heart, filled with doubt yet hesitant to decide, ultimately settling on nothing.

**The Second:** Doubt turns inward—**trust** (孚) is near.
**Cè** says: Doubt turns inward—this is turning from **clarity and tranquility** (清静).
Two marks the midpoint of thought, a **repetition** (反复), yet aligns with daytime.
The **virtuous man** (君子) with doubt should screen out desires and interests, balance away love and hatred, quiet his heart, and reflect upon himself.
If it is **righteousness** (义), he will act; if not, he will abandon it.
With this to resolve doubt, how far can it be?

**The Third:** Doubt is forcefully illuminated—**receptive compassion** (慈闵) is met, yet it troubles the heart’s root.
**Cè** says: Doubt is forcefully illuminated—this is **darkness** (冥) within the heart.
**Fàn** renders *qiáng* (强) as *qiǎng* (强), now following the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and **Wáng** texts.
*Qiáng* (强) is pronounced *qí liǎng* (其两).
**Wáng** says: Though in doubt, one forcefully illuminates—this is **darkness** (暗) yet **forceful clarity** (强明).
It is fitting that such **receptive compassion** (慈闵) troubles the heart’s **root** (祖).
**Guāng** says: Three marks the completion of thought, yet aligns with night, hence this symbol.
Confucius said: "To know is to know; to not know is to not know—this is wisdom."
The *Qǔ Lǐ* (曲礼) says: "In doubtful matters, do not **question** (质)."
**Cè** says: Doubt is considered—this is **consulting the past** (旧) first.

---

**Wáng** says: When the heart holds doubts, yet examines and verifies the ancient texts to clarify them, then doubts will surely be resolved.

**Guāng** says: Four is the **"external"** (外它), the **"clear and flowing"** (条畅), yet aligns with day, hence this symbol.

Ancient texts and old persons alike may be consulted.
When one consults and encounters true sincerity and trustworthiness, then it is fitting to follow them.

Confucius said: **"Doubt, ponder, and inquire."**

Next, **Five**: **Hūn** (赨) and **Huáng** (黄), doubting the **center of *Jīn*** (金中).
**Cè** says: *Hūn* (赨) and *Huáng* (黄) in doubt—this is **perversion seizing the upright** (邪夺正).

The *Sòng* and *Lù* texts render *hūn* (赨) as *xià* (吓), which does not appear in the character dictionaries;
**Fàn** renders it as *fáng* (妨); now following the **Wáng** and **Chén** texts.
*Hūn*, pronounced *tú dōng* (徒冬).

**Fàn** says: *Hūn* is the **crimson** (赤).
**Wáng** says: *Hūn* is the **vermilion** (彤), same as *tóng* (彤).

Though in a position of prestige, yet dwelling in darkness and aligning with night,
one cannot discern and resolve the doubts.
The vermilion and yellow hues, yet doubting them for *Jīn* (金).
The *Xiǎo Sòng* (小宋) says: *Hūn* (赨), pronounced *xióng* (雄), is the **雄黄** (*xiónghuáng*, orpiment).
The *hūn* (赨) and *huáng* (黄) hues are radiant and resplendent—
one doubts there might be traces of *Jīn* (金) within them.

**Guāng** says: Five occupies the **honored position** (尊位), yet aligns with night.
In doubt yet unable to clarify, the **greatly deceptive** (大佞) resembles loyalty—
thus perversion can seize the upright.

Next, **Six**: **Oaths of sincerity** (誓贞) may be heeded, yet doubts may still hold **truth** (诚).
**Cè** says: *Oaths of sincerity* (誓贞) may be heeded—this clarifies the **Sovereign’s command** (王命).

**Wáng** says: Six attains its position and aligns with day,
able to resolve all doubts.
With **proclamations and oaths** (诰誓) to set things right,
all may heed and discern what confounds them.
Even if doubts persist and understanding has not yet dawned,
there is the **Way of sincere clarity** (诚明之道) to penetrate them—
then all **foolishness and confusion** (愚迷) will be dispelled.
This is the **reiteration of the Sovereign’s command** (申明王命)
to **cut through the masses’ doubts** (断众疑).

**Guāng** says: All things bear **doubts** (疑),
hence one must **swear oaths** (誓) to them.
When oaths are **upright** (正), then all may heed them.
Yet if people still **doubt** (疑) the oaths,
then one must **reiterate them with utmost sincerity** (申之以至诚).
**Sincerity** (诚) makes them follow;
**lack of sincerity** (不诚) makes them not follow.

「Six is the uppermost fortune (上禄), hence it is called **Sovereign’s Command** (王命).

Next, **Seven**: Ghostly apparitions (鬼魂) harbor doubts (疑), sighing and wailing (嚘鸣).
Catching birds of the **proclaimed wood** (弋木之鸟) or foxes from the **marked holes** (射穴之狐),
inverts the senses—**eyes see as ears** (反目耳), and **ears hear as eyes** (厉).
**Guāng** says: Ghostly apparitions (鬼魂) are vague (恍惚), flickering between presence and absence (若有若无), truly deserving of doubt (疑).
**Black not as crow** (莫黑匪乌), **red not as fox** (莫赤匪狐)—these are distinctions easily made.
**Eyes see, ears hear** (目视耳听) is the natural order of things.
Seven signifies **losing one’s resolve** (失志), **decay** (消), **ruin and harm** (败损), and occurs at night.
Since one’s own **illumination is obscured** (己既不明), many are **bewildered** (惑).
Whether they be **ghosts or spirits** (若鬼若魂), unable to discern, one can only sigh in lament (叹嗟而已).
Though crows and foxes are easily distinguished, still there may be **mistakes or omissions** (或得失), leaving doubt unresolved.
If one’s own **ears and eyes** (己之耳目) are not even trusted,
then to **see with ears and hear with eyes** (反以耳视而目听) is truly perilous (宜其危矣).

Next, **Eight**: **Inverted doubts** (颠疑) meet with **obstructive guests** (遇干客), three years without hunting.
**Guāng** says: When **inverted doubts** (颠疑) encounter guests (客), it is an occasion of utmost reverence (甚足敬也).
**Hunting** (射) is pronounced *yì*.
**Fàn** says: **Hunting** (射) here means **satiation** (厌).

At the **uppermost Nine**, **doubt without trust** (疑无信) leads to **drawing the bow like a deer** (控弧拟麋), resulting in **nothingness** (无).
**Guāng** says: **Doubt without trust** (疑无信), when carried to its extreme (终), yields no **fate or name** (所名).
The **Sòng, Lǔ, Fàn, Wáng** commentaries all originally read *九疑无信*, now following the **Wáng and small Sòng** versions.
**Fàn** says: **Without trust** (无信) means **nothing worthy of trust** (无所信).
**Drawing the bow like a deer** (控弧拟麋) and **nothingness** (无) further emphasize the **depth of doubt** (疑之甚).

**Guāng** says: Nine represents **doubt at its peak** (疑极), hence this **symbol** (象).
If doubt persists without resolution (终疑不决), then **no fate or name** (成名) will be attained.

**Three directions, one state, three departments, three families observe** (三方一州三部三家视):
The **Yáng family** (阳家) sees **gold** (金), following the **observations of the *Zhuan* texts** (准观).
The **Yīn family** (阴成) sees **the forming of vital essence** (魄), the **Yáng family** (阳成) sees **the forming of ancestral spirits** (妣),
and all **manifestations of things** (物之形貌) are visible.

**Wáng** says: At this time, the **forms of all things are fully manifest** (万物形貌已成), all visible.
**Guāng** says: The **Yīn of autumn equinox** (秋分之阴) is like the **moon taking shape** (月成魄).
**Mǐ** (妣) should be read as **配** (match), with the pronunciation *pǐ-jì* (匹计切).
At the **midpoint of Yīn and Yáng** (阴阳中分), **pairing is complete** (成配偶).

**First day (初一)**, **concealing one’s illumination** (内其明), not using its **radiance** (光).
**Guāng** says: **Concealing one’s illumination** (内其明) means **self-examination in darkness** (自窥深).
**Fàn** says: **Inner reflection without remorse** (内省不疚), then what **worries or fears** (忧惧) can there be?

**Guāng** says: One (一) marks the **beginning of contemplation** (思始), occurring at **day’s dawn** (当昼).
**Retreating vision inward** (收视内明), not relying on **external radiance** (外光).

Next, **Second day (次二)**, **the noble sees within** (君子视内), **the petty sees without** (小人视外).
**Guāng** says: The petty **seeing outward** (视外) cannot **perceive the heart** (见心).
**Body’s flourishing or decline** (身之荣悴), **people’s virtue or lack thereof** (人之贤不肖)—none escape this truth.

Next, **Third day (次三)**, **observing virtue** (视其德) can **support the Sovereign’s realm** (可以干王之国).
**Guāng** says: **Observing virtue** (视德) to **uphold the state** (干), ensures **completeness and integrity** (乃能有全).
Three (三) signifies **intent fully formed** (成意), and also **advancing others** (进人), occurring at **midday** (当日之昼),
when **virtue is manifest and externalized** (德成而外形).
Hence, the **Sovereign observes virtue’s scale** (王者视德之大小),
assigning **rank and position** (任以爵位) to serve as **the pillars of the state** (为国家之桢干).

Next, **Fourth day (次四)**, **powdering the forehead** (粉其题), **moistening the mustache** (雨其渥须),
**the vision is without charm** (视无姝).
**Guāng** says: **Powdering the forehead and moistening the mustache** (粉题雨须) is **unbearable to behold** (不可忍瞻).
The **Sòng and Lǔ** versions lack the word *忍*, the **small Sòng** version lacks *可*,
now following the **Fàn and Wáng** versions.

**Wáng**’s version reads *顺* (顺), meaning **facial expression altered** (面权).
The **small Sòng** version reads *面无色* (面无色), pronounced *mì-jué* (匹迥切), meaning **face without hue** (面无色).
Now following **Fàn**’s version.
**题** (题), pronounced *fěn-biàn* (粉变切), means **forehead** (额).
**面** (面), pronounced *mì* (匹), means **face** (面).
**姝** (姝), pronounced *chūn-jū* (春朱切), means **beautiful** (好).
**Fàn** says: **题** (题) is the **forehead** (额).
**面** (面) is the **face** (面).
**姝** (姝) means **beauty or grace** (好).

「Guāng says: The fourth **color (sè)** is **white (bái)**, which signifies **low rank and fortune (xià lù)**, **external but not intrinsic (wài tāi)**, and is the adornment of **petty people (xiǎo rén)** who embellish their outward appearance to gain fortune. **Prolonged pretense must fail (wěi jiǔ bì bài)**, just as **powdering the forehead (fěn tí)** and encountering rain causes the **mustache to cling with dampness (wū xū)**,—how could others find it pleasing (hǎo) when they see it?

The fifth **color (sè)** is **luan-fèng (鸾凤) in riotous display (fēn rú)**, and its **virtue/power (dé)** shines **brilliantly (huī rú)**. **Guāng says:** *Luan-fèng in riotous display (鸾凤纷如)* means **virtue/power radiant and pure (dé guāng hào)**. The **small Sòng (小宋)** version reads *huī (晖)* as *shí (时)*, but now following the **other versions (jū jiā)**.

The fifth **color (sè)** occupies the **highest rank (zūn wèi)**, receives **great blessings (shèng fú)**, yet is seen at **day (dāng zhòu)**. When the **Sovereign’s virtue/power shines (zhōng dé huī rú)** and **auspicious omens (jiā ruì)** gather, then the *luan-fèng* appear in abundance. **Jiā Yì (贾谊)** says: *The phoenix soars at a thousand rèn (仞), gazing upon the radiant virtue/power and descending (览德晖而下之).*

The sixth **color (sè)** is **white chariot with emerald canopy (sù chē cuì gài)**, which is **harmful to vision (wéi shì zhī hài)**, yet **pure (zhēn)**. **Guāng says:** *White chariot with emerald canopy (素车翠盖)* is merely **loveliness of outward appearance (tú hǎo wài)**. The sixth **color (sè)** signifies **high fortune (shàng lù)**, yet it appears at **night (dāng yè)**. **Petty people (xiǎo rén)** without virtue/power (dé) yet holding fortune (lù) are **lovely without but ugly within (wài hǎo nèi chǒu)**, like riding a **white chariot (sù chē)** while displaying an **emerald canopy (cuì gài)**—their outward appearance seems splendid, but inwardly they lack refinement. **The pure (zhēn)** must be **judged with upright vision (dāng yǐ zhèng shì)**, then their **beauty and ugliness will be self-evident (zhī hǎo chǒu fēn fēn)**.

The seventh **color (sè)** is **to observe its flaws (shì qí xiá)**, yet **without impurity (wú huì)**. **Guāng says:** *To observe its flaws (视其瑕)* means **one can correct oneself (néng zì jiāo)**. The **small Sòng (小宋)** version reads *wú (无)* as *wǎng (罔)*, but now following the **other versions (jū jiā)**. **Wáng (王)** says: The seventh **color (sè)** occupies a **position of excess (guò mǎn zhī dì)**, yet one still holds rank at **day (dāng zhòu)**—this is the ability to **examine one’s flaws in accordance with the times (yīn shí ér zì shì qí xiá)**, to **guard against faults before they arise (jiè yú wèi méng)**, so that **blame and regret do not take root (guā zǔi bù shēng)**, and thus **impurity never appears (gù wéi wú huì)**. **Guāng says:** *This is why the seventh **color (sè)** is seen at **day (dāng zhòu)***—it marks the **beginning of calamity (huò shǐ)**.

The eighth **color (sè)** is **feicui (翡翠) in flight (yú fēi)**, **detaching its wings (lí qí yì)**; the **fur of the fox (hú máo)** becomes **one’s own undoing (gōng qí zéi)**. **Guāng says:** *Feicui and fox fur (翡翠狐鼠)* are **loveliness in form (hǎo zuò)**, yet **a cause for blame (jiù)**. **Má (鼦)**, pronounced *dū liáo (都聊)切*, is the same as *diāo (貂)*. **Fàn (范)** says: **Each uses its patterned fur (wén máo)**, yet **both bring about disaster (shì shēn zhī huò)**. The **small Sòng (小宋)** version reads: *Wǎng (罔) detaches its wings (lí qí yì)*. **Guāng says:** *The eighth **color (sè)** is the **midpoint of calamity (huò zhōng)**, yet it appears at **night (dāng yè)**. **External beauty (wài guān zhī měi)** becomes **a personal misfortune (shì shēn zhī zāi)**. **Mèngzǐ (孟子)** says: *A man like Běnchéng (盆成) may have some talent (xiǎo yǒu cái)*, yet **if he has not heard the Great Dao/Way of the Noble (dà dào)**, then **it is enough to ruin his life (bì zhì shēn ér wéi yǐ)**.

The ninth **color (sè)** is **the sun setting its light (rì méi qí guāng)**, **glowing in the east (bīn yú dōng fāng)**, **observing its origins (yòng shì qí shǐ)**. **Guāng says:** *The sun setting and glowing in the east (rì yù bīn dōng)*, **finally turning back to its beginning (zhōng gù shǐ)**#38. **Fàn (范)** says: *Bīn (贲)* means **yellow-white hue (huáng-bái sè)**. The **small Sòng (小宋)** version reads: *The sun as it sets glows in the east (rì zhī jiāng méi bīn dōng fāng)*. **Guāng says:** *The ninth **color (sè)** is the **end of vision (shì zhī zhōng)**, yet it appears at **day (dāng zhòu)**. **The Noble (jūnzǐ)** cultivates **virtue/power (dé)** and establishes **achievements (lèng gōng)**, **treating the end as the beginning (shèn zhōng rú shǐ)**. As the sun sets, it **reflects back upon the east (fǎn zhào dōng fāng)**. **The *Yì* (易) says:** *To observe one’s steps (shì lǚ)*, **to examine omens (kǎo xiáng)**, **and to return to their roots (qí xuán yuán jí)** is **ultimately auspicious (shǐ zhuān yuán jí)**. **The three regions, two states, one department, and one family (sān fāng èr zhōu yī bù yī jiā)** are submerged in **yin (yīn)**, **water (shuǐ)**, and **observation (guān)** is also appropriate here.

The fourth **color (sè)** in **yin (yīn)** is **the sun abandoning its family (rì shě shì)**. **Yīn (沈)** means **to descend or sink (xià shì)**. The **other versions (jū jiā)** assume this to be **yīn duì (沈兑)**, but this is incorrect. **Yīn (阴)** and **yáng (阳)** proceed separately, yet **both yearn for the other (xiāng sī)**. Their **intentions lie in the Mysterious Palace (xiān gōng)**. **Sòng (宋)** says: *Xiāng (怀)* means **to ponder (sī)**. **Yīn (阴)** and **yáng (阳)** have long been distinct, yet **they both converge in autumn (qiū zhōng)**, **mutually yearning (xiāng sī)**, so their **intentions are both fixed upon the Mysterious Palace (xiān gōng)**.

The first **color (sè)** in **yin (yīn)** is **to sink one’s ears in the inner chamber (yīn ěr yú guī)**, **not hearing the pure (bù wén zhēn)**. **Guāng says:** *To sink one’s ears in the inner chamber (沈耳于闺)* means **losing the essence of virtue/power (shī dé tǐ)**. **Fàn (范)** says: *The first **color (sè)** is the **ear (ěr)**, **which is submerged in water (shuǐ zhōng)**, hence **sinking (yīn)***. **Guāng says:** *The first **color (sè)** is the **lowest of low (xià xià)**, yet it appears at **night (dāng yè)**. **Petty people (xiǎo rén)** delight in **sinking their ears in the inner chamber (yīn ěr yú guī)**, **listening secretly (qián tīng)**, **to uncover others’ hidden affairs (yǐ xià rén zhī yǐn sī)**, yet **they do not hear the Noble’s upright Dao/Way (zhēn dào)**. **Even if they fill their ears with tassels (chuí liáng chōng ěr)**, **they still cannot perceive hidden truths (wù wú yǐn qíng)**.

The second **color (sè)** in **yin (yīn)** is **sinking vision to self-perception (yīn shì zì jiàn)**, **superior to the one-eyed’s glance (xiàn yú miǎo zhī miǎn)**. **Guāng says:** *Sinking vision’s perception (沈视之见)* attains **true beauty (dé zhèng měi)**. **Fàn (范)** says: *The second **color (sè)** is the **eye (mù)**, yet **it belongs to the sinking family (yīn jiā)**, hence **sinking vision (yīn shì)***. The **small Sòng (小宋)** version reads: *Miǎo (眇)*; **one-eyed and blind (yī mù máng)**. **Miǎn (眄)** is **oblique vision (yé shì)**. **Guāng says:** *The second **color (sè)** is **mid-thought (sī zhōng)**, yet it appears in the **yin (yè)** phase. **Sinking vision (yīn shì)** within oneself **perceives both good and evil (zì jiàn shàn è)**, **attaining its true beauty (dé qí zhèng měi)**. It is **superior to petty people (xiàn yú xiǎo rén)**, who **cannot inwardly reflect (bù néng nèi xǐng)** yet **spy on others’ right and wrong (páng kuī tā rén zhī shì fēi)**. Like **the one-eyed (miǎo mù)**, **they themselves are unclear (jǐ zé bù míng)**, yet **they delight in oblique vision (hǎo yé shì)**.

The third **color (sè)** in **yin (yīn)** is **to sink in beauty (yīn yú měi)**, **losing the true arrow (shī zhēn shǐ)**. **Guāng says:** *To sink in beauty (沈于美)* means **acting like the deaf and blind (zuò lóng máng)**. **Fàn (范)** says: *Shǐ (矢)* means **straightforwardness (zhí)**. **Guāng says:** *The third **color (sè)** is **upper thought (sī shàng)**, yet it appears at **night (dāng yè)**. **Petty people (xiǎo rén)** **are drowned in the beauty of sounds and colors (yīn yì shēng sè zhī měi)**, **losing their upright nature (shī qí zhèng zhí zhī xìng)**. **Lǎozǐ (老子)** says: *The five tones (yīn) make one’s ears deaf (ér lóng), the five colors (sè) make one’s eyes blind (mù máng)*. This means **what one hears and sees is not truly perceived (suǒ wén jiàn jiē bù dé qí zhèng)**, **like the deaf and blind (rú lóng máng)**.

The fourth **color (sè)** in **yin (yīn)** is **the phoenix chick (yuān chú) sinking its vision (yīn shì)**, **selecting bitter food (shí kǔ zhēn)**. **Guāng says:** *Yuān chú (宛雏) sinking its vision (沈视)* means **choosing the direction of nourishment (zé shí fāng)**. The **small Sòng (小宋)** version reads *yuān (宛)* as *miǎn (冕)*, but now following the **other versions (jū jiā)**. **Wáng (王)** says: *Yuān chú (宛雏)* belongs to the **phoenix (fèng)**. **Guāng says:** *The fourth **color (sè)** is **low fortune (xià lù)**, yet it appears in the **yin (yè)** phase. **The Noble (jūnzǐ)** **chooses fortune to sustain (zé lù ér shí)**, **holding fast to bitter purity (shǒu kǔ jié xún zhèng dào)**. Like the **phoenix chick (fèng xià)**, **it does not feed unless it finds the right place (bì zé kě shí zhī fāng)**, then **it gathers (rán jié yǐ)**. **The fourth **color (sè)** in **yin (yīn)** is **the phoenix chick (yuān chú) sinking its vision (yīn shì)**, **selecting bitter food (shí kǔ zhēn)**. **Guāng says:** *Yuān chú (宛雏) sinking its vision (沈视)* means **choosing the direction of nourishment (zé shí fāng)**. The **small Sòng (小宋)** version reads *yuān (宛)* as *miǎn (冕)*, but now following the **other versions (jū jiā)**. **Wáng (王)** says: *Yuān chú (宛雏)* belongs to the **phoenix (fèng)**. **Guāng says:** *The fourth **color (sè)** is **low fortune (xià lù)**, yet it appears in the **yin (yè)** phase. **The Noble (jūnzǐ)** **chooses fortune to sustain (zé lù ér shí)**, **holding fast to bitter purity (shǒu kǔ jié xún zhèng dào)**. As the **phoenix chick (fèng chú)**, **it gazes downward at the four lands (xiàng dī yú sì fāng)**, **and only feeds on bamboo and grain (fèi zhú shí)**, **selecting the place where it can feed (bì zé kě shí zhī fāng)**, **then gathering (rán jié yǐ)**.

The fifth **color (sè)** in **yin (yīn)** is **the carved eagle (diāo yīng) soaring high (gāo xiáng)**, **sinking its belly (yīn qí fù)**, **delighting in foul gruel (hǎo è jiù)**. **Guāng says:** *The carved eagle (diāo yīng) soaring high (高翔)* is **found in rotten grain (zài fǔ liáng)**. The **Sòng (宋)** and **Lǔ (陆)** versions read *jiù (粥)* as *shéng (绳)*, but now following the **Fàn (范)** and **Wáng (王)** versions. **Wú (吴)** says: *Jiù (粥)* is an ancient form of *yù (育)*. **Guāng says:** *The *Guānzǐ (管子)* says: *A woman does not abandon her husband (fù)*#42. **Jiù (粥)** shares the same **heart (xīn)** as *yù (育)*, with **good and evil (hǎo è)** read as the character itself. The fifth **color (sè)** holds **eminent rank (zūn wèi)**, yet it appears at **night (dāng yè)**. **It looks upward yet its aspirations are low (wàng gāo ér zhì q

As the carved eagle and fierce crow,
though its flight soars high,
it gazes downward at the rotting rat—
its intent fixed on seizing it
to fill its belly.
At first it seems virtuous,
yet ends in depravity—
thus it delights in *yù* (孕) yet spurns *yù* (育).

Next, the sixth:
Grain seen as piled treasure,
beneficial when rightly bestowed upon the king.
**Guāng says:** Grain piled high—
its Dao is manifest.
The **Fàn (范)** and **Wáng (王)** versions read *sù (粟)* as *piào (票)*;
now following the **Sòng (宋)** and **Lǔ (陆)** versions.
The **Fàn (范)** version reads *lěi (累)* as *lěi (累)*;
now following the other editions.

Next, the seventh:
**Lí** like **Lóu** (离娄),
with crimson flesh and owl-like wings.
**Lì** says: **Lí** and **Lóu** with crimson flesh—
their food is unwholesome.
The seventh hexagram:
Red in use, resembling feathers.
Again, the seventh signifies lost resolve,
and occurs at night.
Though vision is as keen as **Lí** and **Lóu**,
seeing crimson flesh and taking it,
one gains only the foul bird.
To eat without choosing one’s fortune—
this is the path of peril.

Next, the eighth:
**Pàn** obtains its medicine,
beneficial for auspicious movement.
**Guāng says:** **Pàn** obtains its medicine—
auspicious for advancing.
The **Fàn (范)** and **Xiǎo Sòng (小宋)** versions read *lì zhēng (利征)* as *zhēng lì (征利)*;
now following the **Sòng (宋)**, **Lǔ (陆)**, and **Wáng (王)** versions.
The eighth hexagram is at the midpoint of calamity,
yet occurs in daylight—
thus it obtains medicine and auspicious movement.

At the top, the ninth:
Blood like **Gāng (刚)**,
sinking into the brow—
before death, then mourning.
**Guāng says:** Blood like **Gāng (刚)**, sinking into the brow—
it ends in ruin through greed.
The **Sòng (宋)** and **Lǔ (陆)** versions read *xuè rú gāng (血如刚)*, *shěn yú méng (沈于颡)* as *rú xuè rú gāng (如血如刚)*, *shěn yú zhī méng (沈于之颡)*;
the **Wáng (王)** version reads *rú xuè rú gāng (如血如冈)*, *shěn yú zhī méng (沈于之颡)*;
the **Xiǎo Sòng (小宋)** version reads *rú xuè rú gāng (如血如冈)*, *shěn yú méng (沈於颡)*;
now following the **Fàn (范)** version.
**Wáng (王)** says: The path of peril and annihilation (#43),
arriving in succession—
thus before death, then mourning.
**Guāng** says: Blood—
like fat and moisture.
**Gāng (刚)** should be read as **Gāng (冈)**.
*Sinking*—
it bears the symbol of fishing profit.
The ninth hexagram is the extreme of calamity,
the exhaustion of resources,
the brow.
Fishing profits without end,
draining the people’s fat and moisture,
piling up like hills and ridges,
until it sinks into the brow, unaware—
thus peril and annihilation follow in turn.

Three regions, two states, one department, two families within—
**Yáng** family, **Huǒ** (Fire),
**Zhǔn** returns to **Méi** (darkness).

「Upon entering the inner [region], the third [division]—
thirteen degrees and twenty-two seconds [of celestial longitude],
the Sun aligns with **Dà Huǒ** (大火, the Great Fire star).

The **qi** of **Hán Lù** (寒露, Cold Dew) responds,
the **Dòu** (斗, Big Dipper) establishes its **shù** (戍, Station) position,
and the **lǜ** (律, musical pitch) corresponds to **Wú Shè** (无射, No Shooting).

Yin withdraws from within and remains without,
Yang withdraws from without and remains within—
the **completion** (既) of all things.

**Sòng** (宋) says: **Completion** (既) means **exhaustion** (尽).

On the first day, **cautious with one’s spouse** (谨于媐),
first **auspicious**, then **peaceful** (宁).

**Cè** (测) says: **Cautious with one’s spouse** (谨于婴),
it refers to **governing female affairs** (治女政).

The **Fàn** (范) and **Wáng** (王) versions of *governing female affairs* (治女政) read *shǐ nǚ zhèng* (始女政),
now following the **Sòng** (宋), **Lù** (陆) versions.

**Mǎi** (媐), an ancient form of **fēi** (妃, consort) or **chóu** (仇, rival).

**Fàn** (范) says: [媐] means **match** (匹).

To be **cautious with one’s spouse** (谨其妃匹),
the **Dao** of male and female aligns,
and the **proper order** (正室) of households is established.

**Guāng** (光) says: **Inner** (内) symbolizes the **household and family** (室家).

At the **beginning** (初) of dwelling within,
thus the **two warnings** (二戒) are given.

In the *Book of Changes* **[Hexagram] □** **[Line] Jiā Rén** (家人, Family),
the first nine: **Leisurely at home** (闲有家), **regret vanishes** (悔亡).

On the second day, **erroneous within** (邪其内主),
**wandering far** (迂) in the **yellow groves** (黄林).

**Cè** (测) says: **Erroneous within** (邪其内主),
far from **peace** (宁).

The **Fàn** (范) version reads *nǎi* (乃) instead of *hú* (乎),
now following the **Sòng** (宋), **Lù** (陆), and **Wáng** (王) versions.

**Fàn** (范) says: **Inner** (内) **main** (主) refers to the **wife** (妇).
**Wander** (迂) means **far** (远).
**Yellow** (黄) means **central** (中).

**Xiǎo Sòng** says: The **bed** (床) is where one finds **rest** (安).

**Line Three**: *"You conduct yourself with propriety yet weep"* (尔仪而悲), *"the pit beneath my western stair"* (坎我西阶).

**Cè** says: *"You conduct yourself with propriety yet weep"* (尔仪而悲) — this is the **filial sentiment for one’s mother-in-law** (代母情).

**Fàn** says: *"The pit"* (坎) means **sorrow** (忧).
In the **ceremony of the bride’s reception** (亲迎之道), the **bride ascends the western stair** (妇升西阶).
There is the **virtue of substituting for one’s parents** (代亲之义), hence the **weeping** (故悲).

**Wáng** says: *"Conduct"* (仪) means **matching** (匹).
**Xiǎo Sòng** cites *The Rites of Marriage* (**《昏义》**) thus:
*"At dawn"* (厥明), the **father-in-law and mother-in-law** (舅姑) **feast the bride** (共飨妇), **offering the ritual libation of one cup** (以一献之礼奠酬#44).
The **father-in-law and mother-in-law descend first from the western stair** (舅姑先降自西阶), the **bride descends from the eastern stair** (妇降自阼阶), **to signify substitution** (以着代).

**Line Four**: *"Delighting in trifles, delighting in peril"* (好小好危), *"losing her coarse robe"* (丧其缊袍), **dangerous** (厉).

**Cè** says: *"Delighting in trifles, delighting in peril"* (好小好危) — this is **unworthy of honor** (不足荣).

The **Fàn** version reads *yùn* (蕴) instead of *yùn* (缊), the **Wáng** version reads *bǎo* (饱) instead of *páo* (袍); now following the **Sòng** and **Lù** versions.

**Line Five**: *"The dragon sinks into the mud"* (龙下于泥), *"the virtuous person gains advantage by taking a wife"* (君子利用取媐), *"meeting the common and the equal"* (遇庸夷).

**Cè** says: *"The dragon sinks into the mud"* (龙下于泥) — **yang descending into yin** (阳下阴).

The **Wáng** version reads *fū* (肤) instead of *yōng* (庸); now following the other versions.
*"Taking"* (取) is the same as *"to wed"* (娶).

**Fàn** says: *"The dragon"* (龙) **illustrates yang** (以谕阳).
**Yang descending into yin** (阳下于阴) is the **meaning of the bride’s reception** (亲迎之义).

**Guāng** says: In the **ceremony of the bride’s reception** (亲迎之礼), the **groom drives the carriage three circuits** (婿御轮三周) — this is **yang descending into yin** (阳下阴).
**Yōng** (庸) means **obtaining the norm** (得其常).
**Yí** (夷) means **equality** (等夷).

**Sixth Hexagram:**
*"At dusk, the bird flies and tucks its wings inward.
Though it would fill the palace, its mate is unseen."*
**Cè** says: *"At dusk, tucking its wings"*—it cannot restrain itself.
**Wáng** says: The sixth position is at its peak yet loses its proper place at night, contrary to the principle of dwelling within.
*"At dusk, the bird flies"*—it has no fixed abode.
*"Tucks its wings inward"*—it cannot restrain itself within.
*"Though it would fill the palace"*—its desire is boundless.
*"Its mate is unseen"*—it loses the way of pairing, gaining nothing in the end.

**Seventh Hexagram:**
*"On a crumbling wall, weeds sprout.
A white-haired man takes his young wife—prosperity."
**Cè** says: *"Weeds on a crumbling wall"*—things rejoice in finding their kind.
*(Wáng version reads *xiù* [秀] for *yǒu* [莠]; now following other versions.)*
*(Fàn, Xiǎo Sòng versions read *wù* [勿] for *wù* [物]; now following Sòng, Lù, and Wáng versions.)*
**Hù** (皬) is pronounced *hú-jué* [胡觉].
**Fàn** says: *"White but impure"* is called *hù*.
**Guāng** says: The seventh marks the beginning of calamity, yet occurs in daylight—declining yet reviving.
*"Prosperity"* means *wealth*.
*"Things rejoice in finding their kind"*—creatures delight in their own.
The *Yì* says: *"A withered willow bears grain,
An old man takes a young wife—
No misfortune remains."*

**Eighth Hexagram:**
*"Within, he cannot conquer his wife,
Bringing ruin to household and state,
Wading deep waters, unmeasured."
**Cè** says: *"Cannot conquer his wife"*—a state’s sin.
*(Fàn version reads *bùkěcè* [不可测] for *bùcè* [不测]; now following other versions.)*
**Fàn** says: *"Conquer"* means *overcome*.
**Ninth (Uppermost) Line:**
*"Rain descends to earth—
It cannot stop, cannot pass.
**Cè** says: *"Rain descends to earth"*—proper moderation of moisture.
*(Three regions, two states, one department, three families depart—
The Yin house’s wood, aligned with *Wúwàng* [无妄]).*

「The Han scholars interpreted *Wúwàng* [无妄] as "no further expectations," so Yángzǐ [扬子] thus criticized their reading.

**Cè** [测] says: *"Meng* [妄] means *reckless action*; *wàng* [望] means *not what one expects*—this takes both meanings together.

When *Yin* [阴] departs from its *Yáng* [阳], and *Yáng* departs from its *Yin*, all things flourish and disperse in wild confusion.
*Tì* [倜] (pronounced *zhāngliú* [张留]) and *chàng* [倡] (pronounced *chāng* [昌]).
At this time, *Yin* and *Yáng* are both departing, their roles yet undetermined, and all things are in wild confusion, unsure of their place.

**Wáng** [王] says: *Tì* [倜] is the same as *zhuó* [侜], meaning all things are rampant and scattered.

**First (Lowermost) Line:**
*"Depart from this* **Língyuān** [灵渊], *abandon that* **Kūyuán** [枯园].
**Cè** [测] says: *"Depart from this* **Língyuān** [灵渊]"—not by the virtue of humility, but by arrogance.
**Fàn** [范] says: *One* [一] represents *water*, which is lowest in rank, hence *"Língyuān"* [灵渊].
To depart from the low is to seek the high—this is not the Dao of humility.

**Xiǎo Sòng** [小宋] says: *"Kūyuán"* [枯园] is like *"high above"* [高上].
**Guāng** [光] says: *"Kūyuán"* [枯园]—when trees wither, it is because the land is high and lacks nourishment.

*One* [一] is the beginning of *thought* [思], occurring at *night* [夜].
The common folk, weary of lowliness, think of the high, desiring to depart from this and follow that—this will surely bring calamity.

**Second Line:**
*"Depart from that* **Kūyuán** [枯园], *abandon this* **Língyuān** [灵渊].
**Cè** [测] says: *"Abandon this* **Língyuān** [灵渊]"—this is the radiance of the humble Dao.
*Two* [二] represents *thought in the middle*, occurring at *day* [昼].
The sage considers those beneath him, hence the radiance of humility.

**Third Line:**
*"He raises his steps,
To the hall with dew upon them."
**Cè** [测] says: *"Raising his steps with dew"*—this is reckless action.
*(Fàn [范], Xiǎo Sòng [小宋] originally read *mengshēng* [妄升]; Wáng [王] read *ānxíng* [安行]; now following Sòng [宋] and Lù [陆] versions.)*
Dew clinging to one’s garments is an omen of misfortune and disgrace.
*Three* [三] is the fulfillment of *intent*, occurring at *night* [夜].
The common folk, seeing high rank, strive toward it,
Unwittingly stepping into misfortune and disgrace.

**Fourth Line:**
*"Depart from son and father,
Depart from minister and lord."
**Cè** [测] says: *"Depart from son and father"*—this is not what one expects.
*(Fàn [范] originally read *qù yú fùzǐ* [去于父子]; now following Xiǎo Sòng [小宋], Lù [陆], and Wáng [王] versions.)*
**Wáng** [王] says: *Four* [四] has already attained its proper place and occurs at *day* [昼].
To depart from it is fitting.
Departing from the son to follow the father,
Departing from the minister to follow the lord—
This aligns with the order of reverence and subordination,
And thus no blame or regret arises.

**Guāng** [光] says: *Four* [四] is the beginning of *fortune*, occurring at *day* [昼].
To depart from the low and attain the high is fortune beyond expectation,
Hence it is said *"not what one expects."*

**Fifth Line:**
*"He grasps his garments,
To the courtyard where there are* **mí** [麋].
**Cè** [测] says: *"Grasping his garments with* **mí** [麋]"—this is also cause for dread.
**Xiǎo Sòng** [小宋] says: *"Grasp"* [攓] is pronounced *qiān* [愆], meaning to lift.
**Guāng** [光] says: *"Grasping his garments and moving"*—there are thorns and brambles in the courtyard.
A *mí* [麋] deer wandering in the courtyard is a sign of a ruined state.
Fear and dread arise from these emotions,
And *five* [五] represents *garments*, holding an elevated position but occurring at *night* [夜].
He is about to depart from his station and lose his state—how could he not fear?

**Sixth Line:**
*"He himself departs from* **chéng** [成], *heaven bestows its name upon him."
**Cè** [测] says: *"He himself departs from* **chéng** [成]"—this is the virtue of *retreating without lingering*.
*(Xiǎo Sòng [小宋] and Lù [陆] originally read *chéng* [城]; now following Fàn [范] and Wáng [王] versions.)*
*Rǎng* [攘] is the ancient form of *ràng* [让].
*Yí* [遗] is pronounced *yuè* [醉].
**Fàn** [范] says: *Six* [六] is the position of *uppermost blessing*, and the nature of his family is to depart.
When his work is complete, he retires—thus heaven bestows his name upon him.

**Seventh Line:**
*"He departs from* **dézhēn** [德贞], *three deaths do not cease."
**Cè** [测] says: *"Depart from* **dézhēn** [德贞]"—this is the end of shameful ruin.
**Fàn** [范] says: *"Lìng* [令]" means *good*.
**Wáng** [王] says: *Seven* [七] is in a position of excess and has already lost its place, occurring at *night* [夜].
*Guāi* [乖]—it is fitting to depart from it.
To depart from what is *evil* is nothing compared to abandoning one’s *virtue, power, and uprightness* [德贞].
Thus, even though three deaths may come,
There remains the name of *"not ceasing"* [不令]—it is not extinguished.
*Three* [三] here means *many*.

**Eighth Line:**
*"The moon is high in its arc,
The fire is near its suspension—
One cannot stir, stirring brings* **qiān** [愆].
**Cè** [测] says: *"The moon arcs, the fire suspends"*—this is cause for fearing blame.
*Jī* [几] is pronounced *qí* [祈] or *jī* [居衣].
*Suspension* [县] is the ancient form of *xuán* [悬].
**Fàn** [范] says: *"The moon is high in its arc"*—this is beyond the twenty-day mark.
*Fire* [火] refers to *Dàhuǒ* [大火].
When the fire is near suspension, the year is drawing to a close.
*Eight* [八] is the position of *old age and infirmity*, corresponding to eighty years in one’s lifespan.
As the moon wanes when stirred, and the fire’s days decline,
He retires from office, leaving his *chēng* [成] (accomplished work) behind,
And entrusts his teachings to the younger generation—
He cannot stir recklessly, lest he bring calamity.

Guang says: *"Within the fire, cold and heat withdraw.*
Eight marks decay, like peeling—
As when a man grows old and frail,
He should remain still, not stir.

*Upper Nine*: Seeking myself, I find none—
I turn toward the west-north.
Cè says: *"Seeking myself, how can I endure?"*
Little Song cites *Xuántú* [玄图]: *"No yang is born like the Child—
West-north, and the Child’s beauty is spent."*

Guang says: *"West-north is the yin position;
Nine marks exhaustion, annihilation—
At the peak of departure, facing night.
Already gone, so I seek in vain.
Like the sun and the year,
Moving from west into north—
How can they endure long?"*

Three regions, two states, two departments, one household grow dark.
The Yang family’s gold aligns with *Míngyí* [明夷].

Entering darkness, the seventh rank—
The sun lodges in *Fáng* [房].
Yin ascends upon yang, yang descends into yin—
All things lose their brightness.
*Hom* [丧], pronounced *xīlàng* [息浪].

Wang says: *"All things rely on yang for brightness;
When yang descends, they lose their light."*

*First Rank*: Like the void, yet alone seeing *yōuzhēn* [幽贞].
Cè says: *"Like the void, yet alone seeing—
This is the central illumination."*

Wang says: *"In the dark and dim,
Yet occupying the proper position,
Thus: ‘Like the void, yet alone seeing.’
Guarding oneself in the depths of obscurity,
Without losing its rectitude."*

Guang says: *"One marks ‘thought below,’
Also ‘below below,’
Fitting the sun’s position,
Thus able to see *yōuzhēn*."*

*Second Rank*: Blind, advancing into *pǐ* [否].
Cè says: *"Blind, advancing into *pǐ*—
Brightness does not see the Way."*

*Pǐ*, pronounced *fāngjiǔ* [方久], differs from *bù* [不同].
Wang says: *"In darkness and dimness,
Yet losing the proper position for night—
Further darkened.
Then, blindly advancing,
There must be fear of stumbling."*

Guang says: *"Two marks ‘thought in the middle,’
Yet facing night,
Thus this symbol arises."*

*Third Rank*: Yin moves, yang follows—
Favorable to action, not ominous.
Cè says: *"Yin moves, yang follows—
Matters extend outward."*

Wang and Range’s text reads *"must"* (*bì* [必]), now following Song and Lu’s *"action"* (*zuò* [作]).

*Fourth Rank*: Its kind grows dark—
Losing the *jīnkuì* [金匮].
Cè says: *"Its kind grows dark—
Laws and measures decay."*

Range says: *"Four is metal.
Yet in its course,
In an age of darkness,
Thus: ‘Its kind grows dark.’"*

Guang says: *"Laws and measures uphold the state,
Like the *kuì* [匮] securing gold.
Within a single state,
There may be darkness and brightness—
Laws and measures may still be upheld.
But if darkness prevails and is further obscured,
Conforming to flattery, clustering like one,
Then laws and measures decay.
Four marks ‘lower fortune,’
‘External otherness,’
Yet facing night—
Thus this symbol arises."*

*Fifth Rank*: The sun stands central,
The moon reaches its peak—
The virtuous man hides himself, does not enter *qióng* [穷].
Cè says: *"Sun central, moon at its height—
Brightness fears being blunted."*

Wang says: *"Five marks central harmony,
Yet also occupies the proper position.
At the sun’s zenith and moon’s fullness,
Yet restraining excess,
Hiding his traces.
Having grasped the principles of waxing and waning,
Flexion and extension,
His Way does not reach exhaustion."*

*Sixth Rank*: The *Xuānnǎo* [玄鸟] bears sorrow—
Brightness descends into obscurity.
Cè says: *"The *Xuānnǎo* bears sorrow—
It will descend into dimness."*

Wang says: *"The *Xuānwū* [玄乌], the knowing crow.
Six is at peak fullness,
Yet loses the proper position for night—
A symbol of departing brightness toward obscurity."*

Little Song says: *"The *Xuānwū* is *yǐ* [乙].*
Guang says: *"Six marks *kuòtiān* [廓天],
Venturing into early autumn—
Thus this symbol arises."*

*Seventh Rank*: *Tí* [提] the brightness—
Or follow its path.
Cè says: *"*Tí* the brightness—
Virtue will follow its course."*

Song and Lu’s text reads *"shèng"* [睄], pronounced *xījǐng* [息井], meaning *"provincial"* (*shěng* [省]), or *"awaken"* (*xǐng* [醒]).
Little Song pronounces it *miǎo* [眇], meaning *"one-eyed"* (*yīmáng* [一目盲]).
Song and Lu’s *"tí"* reads *"dí"* [𠫗], pronounced *dōulí* [都黎].
All other commentaries omit *"virtue"* (*dé* [德]).
Range’s text reads *"shèng tí míng dé"* [睄提明德], mistakenly following Cè.
Range says: *"*Shèng* [睄] and *shào* [哨]—
Blindness of the eyes."*
Meaning aligns with *miǎo* [眇].
Now following Wang’s text.

---

**Wang’s text** omits the *"follow"* (*zūn*) character in *Cè*’s commentary. Now following the other commentaries.

**Wang says#57:**
*"Shèng* [睄]—
The ancient term for *xiāo* [宵] (night).*
When the seventh line attains its proper position, it should be day.
Yet within the night, *tí* [提] the brightness shines forth on its own,
or follows its course—
thus it does not lose the *Dao/Way* (*dào*).

*Tí* [提] the brightness—
refers to the kind of *candle* (*zhú* 烛).

**Guang says:**
*"The seventh line represents *consumption* (*xiāo* 消) or *decay* (*bài sǔn* 殆损),
and bears the symbol of night,
yet it corresponds to the day’s position,
hence it is called *tí* [提] the brightness.

*Virtue/Power* (*dé* 德)—
refers to those with *bright virtue* (*míng dé* 明德).
In times of darkness and confusion,
those with bright virtue will lead the masses,
and all will follow them.
Like a candle in the night,
all will follow its light.

**Eighth Rank:** *Shì* [视] not its true form,
*plucking* (*yí* 夷) its right eye—
*ruin of the state* (*miè guó* 灭国), *loss of the family* (*sāng jiā* 丧家).

**Cè says:**
*"Shì* [视] not its true form, *plucking* (*yí* 夷) its eye—
the state will suffer loss."

All other texts render *true* (*zhēn* 真) as *straight* (*zhí* 直).
Now following **Song and Lu’s text**.
All other commentaries render *yí* [夷] as *pluck* (*shì* 视直夷目),
but now following **Range’s text**.

**Range says:**
*"Yí* [夷]—
*injury* (*shāng* 伤)."
**Guang says:**
*"The eighth line represents *severe illness* (*jí yū* 疾瘀) or *exhaustion* (*hào* 耗),
and yet it corresponds to night,
hence this symbol arises.

**Ninth Rank:** *Huì míng míng* [晦冥冥]—
*advantageous for the unenlightened* (*bù míng* 不明) in *constancy* (*zhēn* 貞).

**Cè says:**
*"The advantage of *huì* [晦] and *míng* [冥]—
it cannot stand alone in brightness."

**Song, Lu, and Wang’s texts** render *"the advantage of *huì míng míng*"* as *"*huì* in the upper and lower"* (*huì zài shàng xià* 晦在上下).
**Little Song’s text** renders it as *"*huì míng míng*"* (*晦冥冥*).
Now following **Range’s text**.

The ninth line reaches *huì*’s extreme,
yet corresponds to day.
The *gentleman* (*jūnzǐ* 君子) may employ *huì* (obscurity) as his method,
and thus it will be sufficient.

The *Yì* (*Book of Changes*) says:
*"When internal troubles arise yet one can steadfastly uphold the will,
Jiūzǐ [箕子] used this."*

It also says:
*"Jiū’s *Míng Yí* [明夷], advantageous in constancy."*

**Three regions, two states, two departments, two families** of *mèng* [瞢].

*Mèng* [瞢]—
ancient term for *mèng* [梦] (dream), also pronounced *mò dēng* [莫登].

**Yáng**’s family *tǔ* [土] (earth) also aligns with *Míng Yí* [明夷].
**Entering Pǔ**’s commentary, the sixth rank—
**31°13′**#59, corresponding to the *Qi* of *Frost’s Descent* (*Shuāng Jiàng Qì* 霜降气).

**Eighth Rank:** *Rì shě xīn* [日舍心].
*Huì* [晦]—
*outer darkness* (*wài àn* 外暗);
*Mèng* [瞢]—
*inner confusion* (*yīn hūn* 内昏).

Some say:
*"Huì* twice—
*blind* (*máng* 盲) *negation* (*pǐ* 否), not inner confusion.
*Mèng* twice—
*bright* (*míng* 明) *abdomen* (*fù* 腹), *lost in the sky* (*rǎn tiān* 睒天), not outer darkness."

It says#60:
*"The constant has no steadfast *virtue/Power* (*dé* 德),
the *restraint* (*jié* 节) has no restraint.
If the line and the hexagram oppose each other,
it is contrary.
*Blind* (*máng* 盲)—
contrary to *bright* (*míng* 明).
*Bright* (*míng* 明)—
contrary to *confused* (*mèng* 瞢).
The *yīn* (*yin*) line portends *south*,
the *yáng* (*yang*) line portends *north*.
When things lose their *bright constancy* (*míng zhēn* 明贞),
none escape *confusion* (*mèng* 瞢).

**Wú’s text** renders *portends* (*zhēng* 征) as *extends* (*yán* 延),
saying *zhēng* is the ancient term for *yán*.
**Wang’s text** renders it as *approaches* (*jìn* 近)—
a mistake.
**Lu says:**
*"The *yáng* (positive) line in the south
means all things gather to be seen at *Lí* [离].
Now it is in the north,
hence it is called *mèng* [瞢]."

**First Rank:** *Mèng fù rǎn tiān* [瞢腹睒天]—
*not seeing its boundaries* (*bù dǔ qí zhēn* 不睹其畛).

**Cè says:**
*"Mèng fù rǎn tiān* [瞢腹睒天]—
*no ability to see*."

**Range’s text** renders *"*mèng fù* [瞢腹]"* and *"*míng fù* [明腹]"* as *"repeated"* (*fù* 复).
It renders *boundaries* (*zhēn* 畛) as *sorrow* (*zhěn* 轸)—
now following **Song, Lu, and Wang’s texts**.

*Rǎn* [睒]—
pronounced *shī rǎn* [失冉].
*Zhēn* [畛]—
pronounced *zhāng rěn* [章忍], also *zhēn* [真].

**Range says:**
*"Rǎn* [睒]—
*glimpse* (*kuī* 窥)."
**Wang says:**
*"Rǎn* [睒]—
*temporary vision* (*zàn shì* 暂视)."

When the line loses its proper position, corresponding to night,
its heart and abdomen are in confusion,
temporarily gazing at the sky—
how could it know the boundaries?

**Guang says:**
*"The sky is vast beyond measure,
yet difficult to discern.
The first line represents the *beginning of contemplation* (*sī shǐ* 思始),
and yet it corresponds to night,
hence this symbol arises.

**Second Rank:** *Míng fù rǎn tiān* [明腹睒天]—
*seeing its roots* (*dǔ qí gēn* 睹其根).

**Cè says:**
*"Míng fù rǎn tiān* [明腹睒天]—
*only the center is bright*."

**Range says:**
*"The second line represents the *eyes* (*mù* 目),
hence it is *bright* (*míng*).
*Làn* [烂]—
*bright* (*míng*).
**Wang says:**
*"Attaining its proper position and corresponding to the day,
it reveals the brightness within the abdomen,
gazing upward at the sky—
it must surely investigate its fundamental roots."*

**Guang says:**
*"The second line represents the *midpoint of contemplation* (*sī zhōng* 思中),
and yet it corresponds to the day,
hence this symbol arises.

**Third Rank:** *Shī huò dǎo shè* [师或导射]—
*its target* (*qí zhì* 其埻) *like a piglet* (*tún* 豚).

**Cè says:**
*"Shī huò dǎo shè* [师或导射]—
*no means to execute*."

**Wang’s text** renders *dǎo* [导] as *zūn* [遵]#61—
now following **Song, Lu, and Range’s texts**.
**Wang and Little Song’s texts** render *tún* [豚] as *dùn* [遁]—
ancient characters were interchangeably used.

*Zhì* [埻]—
pronounced *yǐn* [尹].

**Range says:**
*"Shī* [师]—
*a blind guide* (*gǔ shī* 瞽者).
*Tún* [豚]—
*to flee* (*dùn* 遁).
*Zhì* [埻]—
*the mark of the target*."

**Guang says:**
*"The third line represents the *height of contemplation* (*sī chóng* 思之崇),
as *advancing* (*jìn rén* 进人) or *stocks and arms* (*gū gāng* 肱股),
yet it corresponds to night.
Like a blind guide instructing others in shooting,
it must surely distance its target,
and cannot hit.
*Tún* [豚]—
*just as ‘distant’* (*yuǎn* 远) is meant.

**Fourth Rank:** *Jiàn zhēn bù mí* [鉴贞不迷]—
*advantageous for the people* (*yú rén yú zī* 于人攸资).

**Cè says:**
*"Jiàn zhēn bù mí* [鉴贞不迷]—
*truly trustworthy*."

**Range says:**
*"The fourth line represents *metal* (*jīn* 金),
hence it is *mirror* (*jiàn* 鉴).
The upright mirror—
though it does not mislead itself,
how much more so when it aids the virtuous in governance?
*Zī* [资]—
*to take*."

**Wang says:**
*"Attaining its proper position and corresponding to the day,
it is the one who can rectify the mirror,
without confusion.
Not only does it rectify itself,
but it also serves as a resource for others.
*Zī* [资]—
*to take its brightness*."

**Fifth Rank:** *Bèi míng fǎn guāng* [倍明反光]—
*encountering* (*chù* 触) *Móng* [蒙] *in confusion* (*hūn* 昏).

**Cè says:**
*"Bèi míng fǎn guāng* [倍明反光]—
*betrayal by the people*."

**Song and Lu’s texts** render it as *"*people’s peril*"* (*rén suǒ jīn* 人所濒),
**Range’s text** renders it as *"*people may frequent*"* (*rén kě pín* 人可频),
**Little Song’s text** renders it as *"*people’s frequenting*"* (*rén suǒ pín* 人所频).
Now following **Wang’s text**.

**Wang says:**
*"Fǎn* [反]—
ancient term for *fǎn* [返] (to return).
The fifth line is in the *proper position* (*chéng wèi* 盛位),
yet it dwells in *yin* (negative), corresponding to night—
*lost and not recovering*,
hence it bears the symbol of *turning away from brightness*."

**Song and Lu’s texts** render *"*frequent*"* as *"*maō* [姽]"*.
**Little Song’s text** renders *"*yù* [且]"* as *"*qí* [其]"*,
and *"*maō*"* (*qí yī* 其腰) as *"*yī* [腰]"*,
pronounced *yú* [居] *yí* [宜] (cutting),
meaning *slender-waisted beauty* (*xì yāo měi fù* 细腰美妇).
Now following **Range and Wang’s texts**.

*Maō* [姽]—
pronounced *niú wěi* [牛委]#63.

**Translation:**

*"Maō* [姽]—
pronounced *guǒ* [果] *wǔ* [五] *èr* [二] (cutting).
**Range says:**
*Maō*—small in appearance.

**Wang says:**
Sixth line in the *yin* position;
also corresponds to *huà* [画] (the *guà* hexagram),
when *máng* [瞢] (darkness) is dominant—
it governs those lost in confusion, setting them aright.
*Mángmáng* [瞢瞢] *lí* [离]—
from darkness to clarity.
*Yíng* [荧]—small light;
there is bewilderment.
*Yǐ* [且]—weakness.
**Light says:**
From darkness to clarity—
without vigor, it cannot prevail,
hence it is unsuited to *yíng* and *yǐ*.
Seventh line: *máng* [瞢] *hǎo* [好], revealing what is detested.
**Light says:**
The detested in *máng* is not obscured.
**Range’s text** reads *"not obscured"* (*zhuó bù mèi* 著不可昧),
**Wang’s text** reads *"obscured"* (*zhuó mèi* 著昧).
Now following **Song and Lu’s texts**.

Eighth line: *hūn* [昏] *chén* [辰] benefits the moon,
small *zhēn* [贞], not yet reaching the stars.
**Light says:**
*Hūn chén* benefits the moon—
still worthy of aspiration.
**Range’s text** reads *"not yet reaching"* (*wèi jì xīng* 未及星),
**Wang’s text** reads *"not yet"* (*mò yǒu jì xīng* 末有及星).
**Song says:**
The moon follows the sun,
so it remains worthy of longing.

Upper ninth line: *shí* [时] *cuōcuō* [蹉蹉],
not attaining its blessings.
For men: broken *jì* [笄];
for women: changed *kuài* [哿].
**Light says:**
Not attaining blessings—
men die, women lament.
**Wang’s text** reads *suàn* [算] for *jì*.
**Wang** takes *cuō* [磋] as ancient *jiē* [嗟].
*Kuài* is *jiā* [珈], pronounced *jiā* [加].

**Three regions, two states, two departments, three families:**
*Yang* family—water, *zhǔn* [准] in *kùn* [困].
Entering *qióng* [穷] at the ninth line,
the sun rests at the tail.
*Yīn* qi blocks the *yǔ* [宇] (heavenly expanse),
*Yang* loses its place—
all things reach their utmost exhaustion.
**Little Song’s text** reads *"grouped yīn"* (*qún yīn* 群阴) for *yīn* qi,
**Wang and Little Song’s texts** read *"exhausted"* (*qióng chù* 穷处) for *qióng jù* [穷遽].
Now following **Song, Lu, and Range’s texts**.
The *liù hé* [六合] (six extremes) is called *yǔ* [宇].

First line: *qióng* its *qióng*,
and the people favor the middle.
**Light says:**
*Qióng* its *qióng*—
emotion resides in the center.

Second line: *Qióng* not *qióng*,
and the people do not center.
**Light says:**
*Qióng* not *qióng*—
deceit may flourish.

Third line: *Qióng* seeks *dá* [达].
**Light says:**
*Qióng* seeks *dá*—
the teacher is in the heart.
**Wang’s text** reads *sī* [思] for *shī* [师],
**Little Song’s text** reads *zhōng* [中] for *xīn* [心].
Now following **Song, Lu, and Range’s texts**.
Third line signifies completion,
advancing the person—
those in *kùn* who study it
can exhaust their minds,
and thus nothing remains unreached—
what need for a distant teacher?

Fourth line: *Tǔ* [土] not harmonized,
*shù* [木] *kē* [科] *tuǒ* [椭].
**Light says:**
*Tǔ* not harmonized—
a disease among the people.
*Tuǒ*—pronounced *tú hé* [徒和].
**Wang says:**
*Kē tuǒ*—wood’s affliction.
**Light says:**
Fourth line is *xià lù* [下禄] (lower fortune),
yet it corresponds to night.
The petty gain position,
exhausting the people for self-indulgence.
When the people are exhausted, the state is in peril—
like barren soil, the roots are diseased.
**Yǒu Zǐ** says:
If the people lack, who shall have abundance?

Fifth line: *Gēng* [羹] without *sàn* [糁],
its belly *kǎnkǎn* [坎坎],
not losing its *fàn* [范].
**Light says:**
Food without *sàn*—
yet it does not stray from rectitude.
**Range’s text** reads *"praise"* (*zàn* 赞) and *"Light"* (*guāng* 光) both as *"gēng without sàn"* (*gēng wú sàn* 羹无糁).
Now following **Song, Lu, and Wang’s texts**.
*Sàn*—pronounced *sāng gǎn* [桑感].
**Wang says:**
Holding the central position,
it is the master of *qióng* (exhaustion),
not straying from the propriety of *qióng*.
*Gēng* without *sàn*—
its belly hollow and impoverished,
yet it does not lose the model of the sage.
The beauty of dwelling in *qióng* is unmatched.
**Chén** says:
*Sàn*—broken rice.

Sixth line: *Shān* [山] without *jiǎo* [角],
*shuǐ* [水] without *lín* [鳞],
*kùn* [困] offends the body.
**Light says:**
*Shān* without *jiǎo*—
exhausting the people.
Burning mountains for hunting,
draining marshes for fishing—
though gains may be plentiful,
there will be none to follow.
Heavy taxation exhausts the people,
and when the people are exhausted,
they rebel—disaster will surely strike the ruler.

Seventh line: *Zhèng* [正] its foot,
*dì* [蹛] at *bì yú* [狴狱],
seen in three years.
**Light says:**
*Zhèng* its foot—
danger turned to safety.
*Bì*—pronounced *bì* [弊].
**Wang says:**
*Dì*—same as *dié* [跌].
Holding position yet encountering *huà* [画],
*zhèng* foot meets *dié*.
This exhaustion is not self-invited.
*Lù* [录]—lenient recording, read as *lǜ* [虑].
Exhaustion without losing rectitude—
it is fitting that danger yields to safety.
**Little Song** says:
*Dì*—pronounced *dài* [带], meaning *tà* [蹈].
**Light says:**
*Bì*—a prison.
Seventh line avoids blame yet incurs disaster,
hence this symbol.

Eighth line: *Shè* [涉] through frost and snow,
*léi* [累] the *tóu* [项] at *xī* [膝].
**Light says:**
*Léi* the *tóu* at *xī*—
also insufficient for life.
**Song and Lu’s texts** read *dǐng* [顶] for *tóu*.
Now following **Range, Wang, and Little Song’s texts**.
*Xī*—same as *xī* [膝].
**Wang says:**
Losing position at night,
unable to steady its foot,
wading through frost and snow,
encountering peril.
*Léi* the *tóu* at *xī*—
the severity of imprisonment.

Upper ninth line: *Pò* [破] *bì* [璧], *huǐ* [毁] *guī* [圭],
saying *"zào* [灶] produces *wā* [蛙]",
*tiān* [天] brings calamity through others.
**Light says:**
*Pò bì huǐ guī*—
meeting misfortune.
**Range’s text** reads *á* [鼋] for *wā*,
pronounced *měng* [猛].
Now following **Song, Lu, and Wang’s texts**.
*Wā*—pronounced *wū wā* [乌娲].
**Wang says:**
The time has reached its extreme,
unable to return to openness—
though holding position at day,
it is not spared from calamity.
*Pò bì huǐ guī*—
losing its treasures.
*"Zào* produces *wā*"—
nothing left to eat.
Great calamity from others—
blame is not self-made.
**Light says:**

**Translation:**

*"Pò bì huǐ guī*—though his person is precious,
he meets with misfortune in his time.
The extremity of calamity,
the utmost of adversity—
thus this symbol arises.

**End of *Collective Commentary* on *The Great Mystery Classic*, Volume Five.**

---
*(The following annotations and corrections are omitted as per instructions.)*

**Translation:**

**#56–#64** (originally missing; restored from *Fan* edition and *Bei Yao*, *Quan Shu* versions)
#58 *De* (originally *Ming*; corrected according to *Bei Yao* and *Quan Shu*)
#61 *Dao* (originally *Dao*; revised per textual context)
#62 *Yun* (originally *Yun*; adjusted for meaning)
#63 *Qie* (originally *Mao*; corrected according to *Bei Yao* and *Quan Shu*)
#64 *Mo* (originally *Wei*; corrected according to *Bei Yao* and *Quan Shu*)

---
**Collective Commentary on *The Great Mystery Classic*, Volume Six**
*Three Directions, Two Provinces, Three Departments, One Household—Severance*

**Yin House, Fire Phase** (aligned with *Bao*):
When *yin qi* severs, *yang* form is executed—
in seven days, near exhaustion.

*Song* and *Lu* editions read *xíng* (刑, "punishment"); here, following *Fan*, *Wang*, and *Xiao Song* editions.

*Xiao Song* edition reads *qún yīn* (群阴, "collective *yin*").
*Song* says: *"Shā"* (杀) means *"shuāi"* (衰, "to decline"), sharp and precise.
*Wang* says: When *yin qi* prevails, it slays all things.
*Yang* form decays, thus it is executed—
slain by *yin qi*.

Read as written.

**First Day:** Sever its ears and eyes, its heart and belly—*calamity*.
*Ce* says: Sever its ears and eyes—
the inner lacks the outer.

*Wang* edition reads *zhōng wài wú* (中外无, "inner and outer nonexistent"); here, following *Song*, *Lu*, and *Fan* editions.

Ears and eyes are what guard the heart and belly.
Severed, the heart and belly are in peril.
One—*thinking* begins—
and the night follows.

The petty man hates loyalty, fears counsel,
self-destroying his wisdom.

**Second Day:** Sever its warts and excrescences—*advantageous, without filth*.
*Ce* says: Sever its warts and excrescences?
It cannot grow great.

*Wart* (*yōu*), pronounced *yōu*.
*Excrescence* (*zhuì*), pronounced *zhī-yīn*.

*Wang* says: Warts and excrescences are the body’s evils.
Severed and removed, they no longer grow.
*Guang* says:

The second day’s *thinking* reaches the *drawing*—
the noble one attacks his own vices,
preventing them from growing great.

The third day: Sever nose to feed mouth—
losing its *qi*-master.
*Ce* says: Severing the nose to lose the master—
to diminish without honor.
The *Yu* edition reads *food* as *sweetmeat*; here, following *Lu*, *Fan*, and *Wang* editions.

*Fan* says: The nose is the master of *qi*-breath.
*Wang* says: Severing it improperly is like severing the nose to feed the mouth.

The fourth day: Butchering evenly, balanced.
*Ce* says: Butchering evenly, balanced—
it can achieve completion.
*Wang* says: The fourth day marks the height of ministers,
yet it faces the *drawing*—
butchering things with equitable *virtue*.
*Guang* says: The fourth day is the beginning of lower blessings,
when the noble one first attains position.

The fifth day: Sever its thighs and arms—
losing its war-horses.
*Ce* says: Severing its thighs and arms—
losing great ministers.
The *Song* and *Lu* editions read *forget* as *t*; the *Wang* edition reads *none*—
here, following the *Fan* edition.
*Wang* says: The fifth day holds the ruler’s position,
yet it faces the *night*.
Without the ruler’s *virtue*,
it is severing its thighs and arms to seek order—
properly losing what it rides upon.

*Guang* says: War-horses are what bear the yoke and flank the axle,
bearing heavy loads and traveling far—
also symbols of great ministers.

The sixth day: Severing without wounding—
feasted by the four quarters.
*Ce* says: Severing without wounding—
the Dao can be divided.
The sixth day holds a position of prosperity,
yet it faces the *day*.
Distributing its Dao, teaching without end,
nurturing the people boundlessly—
the four quarters lack nothing,
yet the Dao suffers no loss.

The seventh day: Purple *zhuó* and *yú* clouds,
companions encircling the sun—
its sickness cannot be severed.
*Ce* says: Purple *zhuó* and *yú* clouds—
they do not know to be carved away.
The *Song* and *Lu* editions read *not advantageous to carve*—
here, following the *Fan* edition.
The *Wang* edition reads *companions* as *bright*—
here, following the *Song*, *Lu*, and *Fan* editions.
*Yú*, pronounced *yú-lǜ* (余律切).
*Wang* says: Purple *zhuó* is ominous *qi*.
*Yú* clouds are the *qi* of a sunbeam piercing the sun.
*Guang* says: The seventh day is ruin and loss,
yet it faces the *night*.
Like petty men forming factions to deceive their ruler,
becoming a calamity for the state—
the ruler cannot sever and remove them.

The eighth day: Sever its *dù*—
obtaining my heart’s sickness.
*Ce* says: Severing its *dù*—
beneficial to the state.
The *Wang* and *Small Song* editions read *dù* as *yú*—
here, following the *Song*, *Lu*, and *Fan* editions.
*Benefit*, pronounced *bù-biān* (步边切).
The eighth day is the midst of calamity,
yet it faces the *day*.
Able to sever and remove deceitful *dù*,
obtaining what my heart detests.

The ninth day: Sever flesh to take bone—
extinguished in blood.
*Ce* says: Severing flesh and extinguishing blood—
it cannot preserve itself.
The extreme of severing—
the people are already exhausted,
the ruler cannot preserve himself—
thus, extinguished in blood.

Three regions, two states, three departments, two houses—
*Yang* family, *Gèn* wood.

The *yin* ceases things above, the *yang* ceases things below—
both above and below cease.

The *Small Song* edition reads: *"Great Yin ceases things above,"*
but here we follow the other editions.
The *Song* commentary says:
*"At this time, things above are obstructed by *yin* qi,
below they return to *yang* qi,
each ceasing in its place and not moving further."*

**First hexagram:** Ceasing at Ceasing—
inner clarity, no blame.
*Ce* says:
*"Ceasing at Ceasing—
wisdom is sufficient and clear."*

The *Small Song* edition reads *"Ceasing at Inner"*—
here we follow the other editions.
One is the beginning of thought, yet aligned with *Cease*’s proper place.
He who ceases where ceasing is appropriate
then his inner clarity remains unblamed.

The *Yijing* says:
*"Gèn ceases—
ceasing in its proper place."*

The *Great Learning* says:
*"Knowing where to cease, only then is there steadfastness.
Steadfast, only then can one be tranquil.
Tranquil, only then can one find ease.
Ease, only then can one deliberate.
Deliberate, only then can one attain."*

Things have roots and branches, affairs have ends and beginnings.
Knowing what comes first and last—
then one draws near to the Dao.

**Second hexagram:** The chariot’s axle halts,
the horse’s head ceases.
*Ce* says:
*"The chariot’s axle halts—
it cannot proceed."*

The *Fan* edition reads *"The chariot’s axle, the horse ceases"*—
here we follow the *Song*, *Lu*, and *Wang* editions.
*Wu* says:
*"Axle (*rèn*)—pronounced *rèn-yín* (而振切)—
the blocking wood of the wheel."*

*Guang* says:
*"Two is the midpoint of thought, yet it faces night.
The petty man’s wisdom is insufficient to discern principle—
to cease is permissible, but to act is perilous."*

**Third hexagram:** Bar its gates and doors,
using ceasing to restrain mad *guǒ*.
*Ce* says:
*"Bar its gates and doors—
repelling what is improper."*

The *Wang* edition reads *"repelling"* (*yù*) as *"enclosing"* (*yù*),
but here we follow the *Fan* edition.
*Guang* says:
*"Guǒ—delusion.
Repelling—also ceasing."*

Three is the gate, again the height of thought,
again the completion of intent, yet it faces day.
The superior man deliberates thrice before acting—
if madness and delusion are improper, they cannot be concealed.
Thus, at the time of completion, one must be cautious before acting.

**Fourth hexagram:** Ceasing at *tóng* wood,
seeking its sparse grains.
*Ce* says:
*"Ceasing at *tóng* wood—
its seeking is exhausted."*

The *Fan* edition reads *"seeking its exhaustion"*—
here we follow the *Song* and *Lu* editions.
*Wang* says:
*"Tóng wood—
that which stands bare, without branches or trunk."*

*Sparse grains—*
the edible grasses and plants.
*Guang* says:
*"Four is the lower *lù* (fortune), yet it faces night.
The petty man seeks fortune but lacks the proper way—
he will surely gain nothing."*

**Fifth hexagram:** The pillar stands firm in *Lú*,
the cover covers the chariot,
the nave aligns with the spokes.
*Ce* says:
*"The pillar and cover, the nave—
nobility in the center."*

The *Fan* edition reads *"nave"* (*gǔ*) as *"valley"* (*gǔ*)—
here we follow the two *Song*, *Lu*, and *Wang* editions.
*Valley* and *nave* are anciently interchangeable characters.
*Wang* says:
*"Deliberate—steady.
As the pillar steadies in the *Lú*’s center,
finding its proper place to cease.
The cover’s cover rests upon the chariot;
the chariot moves, yet the cover does not.
The nave aligns its spacing,
to gather the spokes.
Though the spokes move, it remains ever at the center.
All these use ceasing as their function.
Five occupies the center, attaining its position,
excellently embodying the Dao of ceasing—
thus taking its symbol."*

*Guang* says:
*"Lú—
the round shelter of grass.
First, a pillar is planted at the center to steady it.
The cover has a *dòu* (fighting vessel) to hold the bow.
The wheel has a nave to align the spokes."*

The mind’s resting place,
long holding fast to its center—
thus these three are precious.

Next, the sixth: square wheels, slender axles,
the carriage jolting.
**Zè** says:
*"Square wheels jolting—
their own trembling returns."*

The *Fàn* edition reads *lián* (broad) for *liǎn* (slender); we follow the other editions.
The *Fàn* and *Xiǎo Sòng* editions read *kǎnkē* (坎轲) for *kǎnkē* (坎坷); we follow the *Sòng* and *Lù* editions.
Both *kē* (坷) and *kē* (轲) are pronounced *kě*.

**Wáng** says:
*"Square wheels must stop;
slender axles must sway.
Using them to travel—
the carriage jolts violently,
yet never finds its way."*

**Guāng** says:
*"Six is upper fortune,
abundance and excess,
yet it belongs to night.
The petty man advances without the Dao—
though he rests in a position of splendor,
he will never find peace."*

Next, the seventh: the carriage laden with its *zhuì*,
the horse stumbling in its stride—
resting in constancy.
**Zè** says:
*"The carriage laden, the horse stumbling—
its movement can be corrected."*

The *Sòng* and *Lù* editions read *jiǎo* (侥) for *zhuì* (傂); the *Fàn* edition reads *zhuì*; we follow the *Xiǎo Sòng* edition.
The *Xiǎo Sòng* edition reads *lín* (怜) for *lín* (邻); we follow the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and *Fàn* editions.
*Zhuì* is pronounced *zhī* in Chén, *chí* in Wú.
*Zhuò* (擸) is pronounced *liángxié* (良涉切); some editions mistakenly write it as *liè* (猎), a scribal error.
**Fàn** says:
*"Zhuì—wheels."*
*"Zhuò—overstep."*

**Wáng** says:
*"Zhuì—what is bound.
Zhuò—what is hung.
The laden carriage, the stumbling horse—
it is fitting to halt and seek rectitude."*

**Guāng** says:
*"Seven is lost aspiration,
decay and ruin,
and it belongs to day—
thus the laden and stumbling.
The virtuous halt at danger,
and thus do not lose their rectitude."*

Next, the eighth: the good bow reverses,
the bad bow reverses.
The good horse rears,
the bad horse rears.
The snapped string breaks the carriage—
it will never rest.
**Zè** says:
*"The bow reverses, the horse rears—
it can never be used."*

The *Wáng* edition reads *"the good bow reverses, the good horse rears, the bad horse rears."*
The *Sòng* edition reads *"the good bow reverses, the bad bow reverses, the good horse rears, the bad horse rears."*
The *Fàn* edition reads *hèn* (恨) for *hěn* (很); we follow the other editions for *hěn*, and the *Fàn* for the rest.
*Pàn* (弸) is pronounced *bóméng* (薄萌切).
**Sòng** says:
*"The snapped string breaks the carriage—
thus it cannot be used."*

**Fàn** says:
*"Pàn—string.
Yǎn—rest."*

**Wáng** says:
*"The bow’s reversal—
it cannot shoot.
The horse’s rearing—
it cannot be guided.
Yet if one forces their use,
there will be snapped strings and broken carriages—
it will never rest in peace."*

**Guāng** says:
*"Though the bow be good yet prone to reverse,
though the horse be good yet prone to rear—
it can never be used.
What then of the bad?
Eight is misfortune’s core,
peeling away,
and it belongs to night.
The petty man, perverse and obstinate,
does not know when to halt."*

From *Xúnzǐ*:
*"The bow must first be tuned before seeking strength;
the horse must first be broken before seeking excellence;
the scholar must first be trustworthy before seeking wisdom."*

Next, the ninth: broken at the stump,
collapsed at the grinding stone—
resting.
**Zè** says:
*"Broken wood, grinding stone—
the virtuous halt here."*

The *Sòng* and *Lù* editions read *biān* (砭) for *duì* (砋), pronounced *yàn* (验切);
the *Xiǎo Sòng* edition reads *xiàn* (硟), pronounced *xiān* (仙);
the *Wáng* edition reads *duì* (砋), pronounced *zhǐ* (止), saying *"the stone for pounding silk."*
We follow this.
**Wú** says:
*"Duì—pronounced *kǎi* (恺), obstruction."*

**Guāng** says:
*"Nine is limit,
is stone,
the exhaustion of misfortune,
and it belongs to day.
When the Dao of the virtuous cannot proceed,
then they must halt."*

**Three regions, two states, three departments, two families—firm.**
The *Yīn* family is metal, also corresponding to *Gèn*.
At the beginning of firmness, one *fēn* and twenty *máo*—
the sun’s course splits wood,
the winter solstice’s *qi* responds.
The Dipper stands in the *Hài* position,
the musical pitch *Zhōng* resonates with *Zhōng*.

**Lù** says:
*"Gèn is mountain and stone,
also wood with many knots—
all depict firmness."*

The *Yīn* form is calloused and dense;
the *Yáng* loses its thread—
all things contend in strength.
The *Xiǎo Sòng* edition reads *xiàng* (貌) for *xíng* (形), *chāng* (昌) for *mào* (冒); we follow the other editions.
*Pián* (胼) is pronounced *bùtián* (部田切).
*Sàng* (丧) is pronounced *xìlàng* (息浪切).
**Sòng** says:
*"Pián—abundance.
Xù—endeavor."
Meaning: at this time, the *Yīn* *qi* is robust and abundant,
while the *Yáng* loses its endeavor,
and cannot act.
Thus all things, relying on the *Yīn* *qi*, contend—
meaning strength.*

**Fàn** says:
*"Pián—fixed."*

**Wáng** says:
*"Pián-mào—dense and abundant.*
At the first day,
the swirling stone is fixed within—
unchangeable constancy.
**Zè** says:
*"The swirling stone is fixed within—
it cannot be transformed."*

The *Xiǎo Sòng* edition reads *jiān pán* (坚盘) for *pánshí* (盘石);
the *Sòng* and *Lù* editions read *bùzhēn* (不贞) for *bùhuàzhēn* (不化贞);
the *Wáng* and *Xiǎo Sòng* editions read *bùhuà qí zhēn* (不化其贞);
the *Sòng* and *Lù* editions read *"swirling within the fixed—
it cannot be transformed."*
We follow the *Fàn* edition.

**Guāng** says:
*"One is the lowly man,
the beginning of thought,
and it belongs to night.
The petty man is stubborn and foolish,
his heart like the swirling stone’s firmness—
unchangeable, unable to enter rectitude."*

Confucius said:
*"Only the utterly foolish are unmoved."*

Next, the second: firm and white as jade,
its inner nature transforms—
constancy.
**Zè** says:
*"Firm and white as jade—
it can be transformed."*

The *Sòng* and *Lù* editions omit the *huà* (化) character;
the *Xiǎo Sòng* edition reads *zhuàng* (状) for *xíng* (形);
we follow the *Fàn* and *Wáng* editions.
Two is the middle of thought,
and it belongs to day.
Though the virtuous heart be firm and pure as jade,
when they see goodness, they change;
when they have faults, they amend—
their inner transformation renews daily,
seeking rectitude.
*The Classic of Changes* says:
*"Resting on stone—
not even a day—
constancy brings good fortune."*

Next, the third: firm yet not overbearing,
or its center may drain.
**Zè** says:
*"Firm yet not overbearing—
it cannot maintain harmony."*

The *Sòng* and *Lù* editions read *líng* (陵) for *líng* (凌); we follow the *Fàn*, *Wáng*, and *Xiǎo Sòng* editions.
**Sòng** says:
*"Harmony—centrality."*

**Fàn** says:
*"In the month of firm ice,
today it does not overbear—
perhaps the *Yáng* *qi* drains within."*

**Guāng** says:
*"Three is completed intention,
and it belongs to night.
The petty man’s heart is not firm;
good and evil war within him,
his virtue wavers—
he has a beginning but no completion,
unable to practice steadfast thought,
faithfully holding to the center."*

Like ice that seeks to harden on the outside
while its *yáng* *qi* leaks within—
it can never truly take form.

Next, the fourth: the small wasp buzzes busily,
its *shān* and *yǐng* unsealed.
**Zè** says:
*"The small wasp buzzes busily—
this is its lineage."*

The *Sòng* and *Lù* editions read *yíng* (荧) for *yíng* (营), omitting *zài* (在).
The *Wáng* edition reads *fèn* (分) for *jiè* (介).
**Wú** says:
*"Wasp—same as *fēng* (蜂)."*
**Fàn** mistakenly writes *pén* (盆).
*Shān* (螮) is pronounced *dūjì*.
*Yǐng* (蛡) is pronounced *shù* or *yì*.
We follow the majority for *fēng*, and *yíng* and *jiè* from the *Fàn* edition.
**Sòng** says:
*"Lineage—root."*
**Fàn** says:
*"Shān—virtue.
Yǐng—state."*
**Wáng** says:
*"Shān—the wasp’s brood.
Yǐng—its nest."*
**Guāng** says:
*"Shān—same as *dì* (蒂), meaning the wasp’s nest’s base.
Jiè—great.
Shān is used to speak of virtue,
yǐng to speak of state.
The fourth is lower fortune,
and it belongs to day.
The superior man cultivates virtue to preserve his station.
A state does not depend on size,
but on diligent virtue to fortify its root.
It is like the small wasp buzzing to build its nest—
it must first make its base.
The nest does not depend on size,
but if the base is firm, it will not fall."*

Next, the fifth: the *yǐng* is large, the *shān* small—emptiness.
**Zè** says:
*"The *yǐng* large, the *shān* small—
the state is hollow."*

**Fàn** says:
*"When a state is small but virtue great,
the people are numerous and prosperous;
when a state is great but virtue small,
the people are hollowed out."*
**Guāng** says:
*"None surpass the fifth in central harmony,
yet it falls to night.
The petty man enjoys great fortune but cannot hold it.
Though a state be vast, if virtue is thin,
like a wasp’s nest large but its base small—
its fall will not be long.
Emptiness—this speaks of outward strength,
while inwardly there is nothing."*

Next, the sixth: the *qiān shān* weaves delicately,
suspended over the Nine Regions.
**Zè** says:
*"The *qiān shān* suspended—
the people thrive thereby."*
The *Xiǎo Sòng* edition reads *shā* (纱) as *yōu* (幽), meaning "subtle appearance," but we follow the majority.
*Tiě* (铁) is the same as *xiān* (纤).
*Xuán* (悬) is the same as *xián* (县).
**Wáng** says:
*"Shā—same as *miǎo* (眇)."*
**Guāng** says:
*"The sixth is high fortune,
abundance, extreme greatness,
and it belongs to day.
The Son of Heaven holds firm virtue—
one man’s blessing,
ten thousand people rely upon it,
like the delicate base suspending a great nest."*

Next, the seventh: firmness topples, striking the mound.
**Zè** says:
*"Firmness topples, striking the mound—
it does not know its path."*
*Diān* (颠) means "top."
The seventh is lost resolve,
ruin, descending the mountain,
and it belongs to night.
The petty man is strong yet ignorant,
like using his firm top to advance,
colliding with the mound—
he does not know where to go.*

Next, the eighth:
Firmness brings calamity—
only the *jiě* *huò* can rectify it.
**Zè** says:
*"Firmness brings calamity—
this is the use of straightness and rectitude."*
*Huò* (祸) is the same as *hù* (怙).
*Jiě* (解) is the same as *xièzhì* (獬豸).

The King says:
*"**Jiě** (解) is the beast that strikes the perverse."*

**Zè** (测) explains:
*"Eight is calamity at its peak, striking during the day—
it relies upon its firmness yet provokes disaster."*

Yet the **Way** of the virtuous lies in striking the perverse;
though firm and stepping into calamity,
they do not lose their rectitude.

Next, the ninth:
Firmness brings calamity—
only the **jiě** **huò** (解祸) can rectify it.
**Zè** says:
*"Firmness brings calamity—
this is the use of straightness and rectitude."*
**Huò** (祸) is the same as **hù** (怙).
**Jiě** (解) is the same as **xièzhì** (獬豸).

---

The King says:
*"**Jiě** (解) is the beast that strikes the perverse."*

**Guāng** (光) states:
*"Eight is at the height of calamity, striking during the day—
it is one who leans upon firmness yet incurs disaster."*

Yet the **Way** of the virtuous lies in striking the perverse;
though firm and stepping into calamity,
they do not lose their rectitude.

Next, the upper ninth:
Bees burn their wings,
perishing upon the corpse.
**Zè** says:
*"Bees burn their wings—
their reliance is lost."*
*(The text of Sòng (宋) and Lù (陆) reads: "Bees consume their wings," but we follow Fàn (范), Wáng (王), and Xiǎo Sòng (小宋) here.)*
**Shāng** (丧) is pronounced *xī wàng* (息浪).
Fàn (范) says:
*"**Shī** (尸), the master."*
**Guāng** states:
*"Wings (**yì** 翊), should be read as **yǐ** (翼)."*
Nine is calamity’s utmost,
striking during the night.
The petty man’s firmness in vice
reaches the point of ruining his house—
like bees burning their own nest,
losing their master.

The *Yì* (易) says:
*"A bird burns its nest—
three directions, three states, one family becomes yang."*
*(The yang family perishes, the water is exhausted—
this follows the pattern of *Jì* (既济).)*
Upon entering the ninth, the day is called *"Shě Jī"* (舍箕).
The **yin-qi** is now pure,
the **yang-qi** concealed within the **Líng** (灵).
Things achieve completion in form.
*(Fàn’s text reads: "The yin-qi is now exhausted," while Xiǎo Sòng reads: "The great yin is now pure"—
we follow the Sòng, Lù, and Wáng texts here.)*
Sòng says:
*"The earth is called **Líng** (灵)."*
Lù says:
*"Pure (**qīng** 清), means cold."*
**Guāng** states:
*"The yang-qi hides within the earth,
**Gǎng** (港) is the master of things.
Things depend upon it to achieve completion,
thus attaining their form."*

First, the one:
If completion is like ruin,
its use will not cease—
**míng** (冥).
**Zè** says:
*"If completion is like ruin,
this is why it does not fail."*

Wáng’s text reads only one *"gǎi"* (改),
but we follow the other versions.
Wáng says:
*"At the start of completion,
though in proper position during the day,
if the work is like ruin,
and one does not boast of its success,
then its use will not cease—
and **míng** (冥) will align with the **Dao**."*

**Guāng** states:
*"One is thinking downward,
striking during the day.
The **Way** of the virtuous,
though silent, shines forth daily.
**Míng** (冥) is hidden yet not displayed.
The *Lǎozǐ* (老子) says:
*'When success is achieved, do not dwell upon it.*
*He who does not dwell upon it,
will not be abandoned by it.'*"*

Next, the two:
Completion is slight, yet change is repeated—
not yet complete yet already in peril.
**Zè** says:
*"Completion is slight yet change is repeated—
it cannot fulfill itself."*

Wáng’s text has only one *"gǎi"* (改),
but we follow the other versions.
Wáng says:
*"Having lost its position at night,
though completion is still slight,
if it is changed again,
then the matter will surely not complete,
and danger will ensue."*

**Guāng** states:
*"Two is reversal,
striking during the night.
The petty man’s heart is not steadfast,
and thus he will never succeed.
The *Yì* (易) says:
*'Jìn advances like the weasel or rat—
rectitude is perilous.'*"*

Next, the three:
Completion leaps yet contracts,
completion flies yet does not pursue.
**Zè** says:
*"Completion leaps yet contracts—
the virtue of completion is strong."*

Wáng says:
*"When the matter is already complete,
it leaps forward yet again,
then contracts and retreats—
knowing self-restraint and caution,
it advances without fault."*

**Guāng** states:
*"Three is completion in intent,
striking during the day.
The virtuous, when facing affairs, are cautious—
leaping and contracting, undecided.
This is so because,
