when the matter is complete,
it is like a bird taking flight—
no longer able to be driven back,
thus advance and retreat must be measured with care.
The *Yì* (易) says:
*'Or leaps into the abyss—
no fault.'*"*

Next, the four:
On the verge of completion, arrogance leads to ruin.
**Zè** says:
*"The arrogance of one on the verge of completion—
this is a flaw in the **Way** of completion."*

Wáng says:
*"Having lost its position at night,
this is one on the verge of completion,
yet arrogant—
it will surely ruin its success.
The **Way** of success
hates arrogance and self-aggrandizement."*

**Guāng** states:
*"Four is the decline of fortune,
thus it is on the verge of completion.
Striking during the night,
hence it is arrogant.
At the **Kuìqiū** (葵丘) gathering,
Qi Huánggōng (齐桓公) was arrogant and boastful,
and nine states rebelled against him."*

Next, the five:
Completion stands alone in the center—
greatness.
**Zè** says:
*"Completion stands alone in the center—
it can dwell in the middle."*

*(Xiǎo Sòng’s text reads: "Completion is able to stand in the center," but we follow the other versions.)*
Five is the utmost of **harmony** and **center**,
striking during the day—
the **Son of Heaven**’s work is complete,
alone wielding the **Huángjí** (皇极),
to govern the four quarters,
a radiant virtue and achievement.*

Next, the six:
Completion is **arcane** and **fragmented**,
using completion to gain calamity.
**Zè** says:
*"The arcane fragmentation of completion—
it does not act with humility."*

*(Xiǎo Sòng’s text reads: "Completion is arcane and fragmented, fragmented," but we follow the other versions.)*
*(Fàn’s text reads: "humility (**qiān** 谦)" as "yielding (**ràng** 让)," but we follow Sòng, Lù, and Wáng here.)*
Wáng says:
*"Six dwells in fullness yet loses its position at night,
though it is the chief of completion,
it harbors fragmented and petty actions within—
it will surely fall from its success and incur calamity."*

**Guāng** states:
*"Six is the utmost of completion,
striking during the night.
When great success is achieved,
many desire to share in its glory.
The one who leads it,
having already exhausted their greatness,
must distribute even the minutiae to others,
and thus all will rejoice.
If they wish to retain both greatness and small,
they will be hated by the multitude,
and instead, through their success,
they will gain calamity."*

Next, the seven:
Completion is **obstructed**—
**repair** (补).
**Zè** says:
*"The repair of completion’s obstruction—
it is indeed difficult to uphold."*

*(Sòng and Lù’s text reads: "depend on (**yī** 依)," but we follow Fàn and Wáng here.)*
Fàn says:
*"Seven is lost resolve,
hence it is obstructed.
The **Way** of the virtuous
is good at repairing faults,
thus when there is obstruction,
they repair it."*

Wáng says:
*"Beneath completion,
it is difficult to uphold—
one must consider the **Way** of repairing errors."*

Next, the eight:
The time for completion is ripe yet not achieved—
**Heaven** descends **perilous rectitude**.
**Zè** says:
*"The time for completion is ripe yet not achieved—
it alone loses the center."*

Wáng’s text reads *"zhǐ"* (止) instead of *"wáng"* (亡),
but we follow the other versions.
Fàn says:
*"Eight is wood,
the harvest of autumn."*

**Guāng** states:
*"The petty man,
when the time for completion is ripe yet not achieved,
lacks the **virtue** to complete it,
losing the center of time,
thus **Heaven** descends blame
and he loses rectitude."*

Upper ninth:
Completion reaches **exhaustion** and enters ruin—
**destroying** completion,
the virtuous do not complete.
**Zè** says:
*"Completion reaches exhaustion and is destroyed—
the virtuous bring it to an end."*

*(Xiǎo Sòng’s text reads: "The petty man destroys completion," but we follow the other versions.)*
Nine is the **end of exhaustion**,
striking during the day.
The sun at its zenith then declines;
the moon at its fullness then wanes—
completion reaches exhaustion and enters ruin,
the natural order of things.
Ruination then destroys its completion.
The virtuous, knowing that completion must be ruined,
constantly restrain and diminish themselves,
so as not to reach completion,
thus ensuring their lasting fortune and blessings.*

「Yǒng Yóu Jī#12 departs a hundred paces from willow leaves and shoots them,
a hundred strikes and a hundred hits—
one man stands beside him, saying: *"Well done!
You do not rest on this virtue#13—
soon your qi#14 will falter and your strength will weary,
your bow will snap and your arrows will bend,
not a single strike will hit,
and your hundred shots will all come to naught."*
This is the type of case described.

**Three regions, three states, one section, two families#15—
The Yin family#16 and the Fire family#17,
corresponding to the hexagram **Shì Hè#18**.
Fàn and Wáng read it as *zhìmì*—
dense and secret.
Chén reads it as *guī*—
closed doors, with the pronunciation *zhìlì*.
Wú says: *wù*—
also like *zhì*, same as *zhì*.
Lù says: *hè* means "to join,"
and *shì* also refers to **Yin and Yang harmonizing#19**.
When **Yin and Yang clash#20**,
joining into one, their calamity floods all things.
Sòng says: *"At this time, the Yin clings and ascends in a closed manner above,
while the Yang clings and declines in a guarded manner below,
the lower and upper both close and guard—
their joining is dense as one."*

Guāng says: *"Clash (dì), like exceeding beyond measure."*

First line: **A circle piercing a square, a round peg in a square hole#21—
within, there is **disunity and discord#22**.
Shì says: *"A circle piercing a square, a round peg in a square hole—
within, they lose their harmony."*
The pronunciation: *yǔ* (for 杌), *niè* (for 棿), *kuǎn* (for 窾).
Fàn says: *"Water in the Fire family,
mutually restraining each other.
Like a round peg in a square hole,
the circle and square do not fit."*

Guāng says: *"Shì (合) means 'to join.'
One is the beginning of thought,
and it occurs at night—
its heart does not harmonize."*

Second line: **Unbroken continuity#23**.
Shì says: *"The unbroken continuity of **Shì (合)**—
one becomes two."*
Fàn says: *"Two Fire families join,
their continuity is dense as one."*
Guāng says: *"Two is the middle of thought,
and it occurs during the day.
Their joining is without interruption—
two as one."*
The *Yì* says: *"Two persons with the same mind,
their strength can cleave metal."*

Third line: **A dragon assaults a place not its own,
its light vanishes in the chamber#24**.
Shì says: *"A dragon assaults a place not its own—
it loses its natural course."*
Wáng and Xiǎo Sòng’s versions lack the *dragon* character,
we follow Sòng, Lù, and Fàn’s versions.
Wáng says: *"Assault (xí) means 'to enter.'"*

Fourth line: **The stench of fat extinguishes the nose,
beneficial for virtuous beauty and integrity#25**.
Shì says: *"The integrity where the nose is extinguished—
there is no place for fragrance."*
Sòng and Lù’s versions read: *"no place for the square,"*
Fàn and Xiǎo Sòng’s versions read: *"no place for labor."*
We follow Wáng’s version.

Corresponding to **Shì Hè#18**,
there is the image of **eclipses#26**.
Four is the beginning of **fortune#27**,
and it occurs during the day.
When food and drink arrive,
one first perceives **stench and fragrance#28**.
Seeing what is obtained, one must ponder its meaning—
one must not lose what is upright.

Fifth line: **Gnawing bone breaks teeth,
a full jar#29**.
Shì says: *"Gnawing bone breaks teeth—
this is great greed for profit."*
Five is the **midpoint of fortune#30**,
and it occurs at night—
the petty man greedily pursues profit and harms himself.
The *Yì* says: *"To gnaw the meat of a sacrifice,
one encounters poison."*

Sixth line: **Drinking sweat, gulping with a sound of *hōng-hōng*#31,
obtaining its **smooth and nourishing essence#32**.
Shì says: *"Drinking sweat with a sound of *hōng-hōng*—
the Dao/Way#33 is fully indulged."*
Wáng’s version reads *hōng-hōng* as *shǔn-shǔn*.
Xiǎo Sòng’s version reads *sweat* as *wū*#34,
with the pronunciation *wū* (污).
*Gulping* (吭) is read as *yǐn*#35,
with the pronunciation *shānyuè* (山劣反),
meaning *yǐn-yǐn*—
small drinking.
*Fully indulged* is read as *dàodé shì*#36.
We follow Sòng, Lù, and Fàn’s versions.
Fàn says: *"Sweat is moisturizing.
Much moisturizing makes the gulping sound *hōng-hōng*."*
Guāng says: *"Six is the peak of abundance,
the utmost extreme.
What the virtuous indulge is the Dao/Way#33—
though they take much, it does no harm."*

Seventh line: **Its differences—
front joins, back separates#37**.
Shì says: *"Its differences—
its joining and separating."*
Seven is **dissipation#38**,
**ruin and loss#39**,
and it occurs at night—
thus **Shì (合)** is dense from here onward,
before joining and after separating.

Eighth line: **Supporting its fracture,
covering its defect,
the person shines and is **resolute and courageous#40**.
Shì says: *"Supporting the fracture and covering the defect—
it can still be beneficial."*
Sòng and Lù’s versions read *覆* (廅) as *lú*,
we follow Fàn and Wáng’s versions.
Chén’s pronunciation: *廅* as *wūhé* (乌合切).
Xiǎo Sòng: *resolute and courageous* (偈) is pronounced *jié*.
Fàn says: *"To shelter and protect its flaws,
to cover its defects."*
Wáng says: *"To support and assist its fractures,
to cover and conceal its defects."*
Xiǎo Sòng says: *"Resolute and courageous (偈) refers to **bravery and valor#41**."*
Guāng says: *"Eight is **exhaustion#42**,
**peeling away and decline#43**,
and it occurs during the day—
thus there is this image."*

Upper ninth: **Yin and Yang begin to open,
their changes turn **dark to light and light to dark#44**.
Shì says: *"Yin turns dark and Yang turns light—
the extreme then reverses."*
Fàn’s version reads: *"Yin and Yang begin to open,
the extreme then reverses."*
Wáng’s version reads: *"Yin and Yang dark to light,
the extreme is reversal."*
Xiǎo Sòng’s version reads *open* (启) as *qǐ*,
with the pronunciation *qǐ*.
*Dark* (赤) is read as *yān*#45,
with the pronunciation *wūxián* (乌闲反).
Shì says: *"Yin dark and Yang light—
the extreme is reversal."*
We follow Sòng and Lù’s versions.
The pronunciation: *shì* as *wǔhé* (五禾切).»

Wáng says: **"** *Yǐ* **is an ancient character for *huà* (化).**
Wú says: *"** *Wū* **is *huà* (化)."**
Guāng says: **"** *Yǐ* **is the symbol of Yin and Yang being blocked and uncommunicative.**
When things reach their extreme, they reverse—thus they change again and open, generating all things, sprouting red and white.

**Three regions, three states, one department, and one family have lost their Yang.**
The Wood family of the Yang line is measured against *Dàguò* (大过).
**Entering, losing sequence four, twenty-six minutes eleven seconds;**
the *xiǎo xuě* (小雪) qi responds.
**Yin grows great as a thief, and Yang cannot prevail—**
things sink into the unfathomable.
Sòng says: *"This means that at this time, Yang is robbed and attacked by Yin and cannot reclaim anything."*

**First line: Piercing the void, the blade is extinguished.**
Shì says: *"Piercing the void, the blade is extinguished—*
this is deep contemplation from the subtle.
**The subtle is the incipient movement, the precursor of auspiciousness and inauspiciousness.**
**One is the subtle essence of thought—**
before the birth of the spirit, it is the first principle, and it occurs during the day.
Though a gentleman may sometimes err,
if he deeply contemplates and plans ahead,
correcting from the subtle signs before they manifest,
it will leave no trace—
like piercing the void with a blade,
though the blade is extinguished, there is no injury."*

**Second line: Contemptuous of virtue, the subtle is lost.**
Shì says: *"The loss of contemptuous virtue—*
this is failing to revere the subtle."
The Sòng, Lù, and Fàn versions read *zhēng* (征) for *wēi* (微); we follow Wáng’s version.
**Contemptuous (*miǎo*)—**
like distant and small.
**Two is the midpoint of thought, hence ‘contemptuous of virtue.’**
The mechanisms of gain and loss are both subtle and refined,
yet at night, the petty man cannot be cautious of the subtle,
leading to great loss."

**Third line: Concluding and conforming, anxious and reverent,**
**in his heart, he takes it as his ancestor.**
Shì says: *"Concluding and conforming—*
this is the ability to self-correct."
Wáng says: *"Concluding (*zú*) is finality;*
conforming (*cóng*) is submission.
Anxious (*xù*) is worry;
reverent (*sǒng*) is respect."
At the time of loss, occupying the correct position during the day,
one who concludes by submitting to the Great Way,
worries over past mistakes,
and adds reverence—
this is taking it as the guiding principle of the heart.
Guāng says: *"Three is the completion of intent during the day,
thus there is this image.
One corrects before it manifests;
three corrects after it is complete."*

**Fourth line: Trusting in excess, not eating,**
**until he hides in the shadows.**
Shì says: *"Trusting in excess, not eating—*
this is losing the rectification of fortune."
Fàn’s version reads *"rectifying fortune"* (*zhèng lù* 正禄); we follow Sòng, Lù, and Wáng’s versions.
Wáng says: *"At the time of loss, occupying the wrong position at night,
unable to rectify his mistake,
he trusts in his erroneous conduct and does not eat.
‘Until he hides in the shadows’ means all day long.
To go all day without eating is to risk losing one’s life.
‘Eating’ also metaphorically means fortune, hence Shì’s reciprocal phrasing."
Sòng says: *"The sun’s decline is ‘hiding in the shadows,’*
the sun’s setting is ‘concealed.’"
Guāng says: *"Four is the lower fortune, yet it occurs at night,
thus there is this image."*

**Fifth line: The yellow-haired child is central,**
**and he prospers.**
The gentleman uses this to **purify his faults.**
Shì says: *"The yellow-haired child is central—*
this is rectifying excess through purification."
**Child (*ér*)**—pronounced *wǔ qǐ* (五稽切).
Fàn says: *"Yellow-haired refers to old age with yellow hair,*
a sign of the child-teeth."
Sòng says: *"Hair turning white and then black is called yellow,*
teeth falling out and regrowing are called child-teeth."
**Prosper (*fān*)**—like a fence.
One who maintains centrality and rectitude
can erect a protective boundary.

**Sixth line: His granaries are full, his fields overgrown.**
**He consumes the fruit, but does not nourish the roots.**
Shì says: *"Granaries full, fields overgrown—*
this is failing to cultivate the foundation."
Fàn says: *"Not cultivating virtue while occupying high fortune,*
granaries full and fields overgrown,
the people exhausted, consuming only the fruit,
suffering at the roots—
the foundation is not secure, and the loss is severe."
Guāng says: *"Six is the upper fortune, abundance, and extremity,
yet it occurs at night, thus there is this image.
When the people are well-fed, the ruler is well-fed—
this is like nourishing the roots while consuming the fruit."*

**Seventh line: If sick, take medicine; if bewitched, consult the shaman.**
Shì says: *"Sickness treated with medicine, bewitchment with divination—*
disaster can be averted."
Fàn says: *"The shaman appeases the offense;*
when the offense is removed, the illness is cured,
and divination is used to bestow blessings."
Guāng says: *"Seven is the descent of disaster,*
separating from calamity but encountering misfortune,
and it occurs during the day, thus there is this image."*

**Eighth line: The hen crows at dawn,**
**the female has horns, the fish grows wood.**
Shì says: *"The hen crows at dawn—*
this is the reversal of correctness."
Fàn says: *"The hen should not crow at dawn.*
The female should be docile, not have horns;
the fish should dwell in water, not grow wood—
the loss is severe."
**Upper ninth: The passing of the sun and moon,**
**rectification at the corpse.**
Shì says: *"Rectification at the corpse—*
this is not far off."
Fàn says: *"The Way of the gentleman, executed in the world,
though he dies, still endures.
Though aged, the sun and moon have passed;
his gains and losses, though in the coffin,
are still remembered for self-rectification."
Wáng says: *"The admonitions of the ancients are profound—*
even if rectification comes after death,
it is still not far off."
Guāng says: *"The mistakes of life, corrected in death by warning descendants,
are still not far off."
When the King of Chu was dying,
he instructed the Prime Minister to present the *sù* (筫) cup and expel the Marquis of Shen.
Liú Xiàng says: *"Thus he awakens his descendants and future generations,
which is better than dying without enlightenment."
**Sù**—not found in the character dictionary.
**Marquis of Shen**—a mistake in the *Xīn Xù* (新序); he was a minister of King Wén of Chu.

**Three regions, three states, two departments, and one family are severely affected.**
The **Yin family’s Metal** is also measured against *Dàguò* (大过).

「Entering the **extreme** phase, fourfold#13, the day rests upon the Southern Dipper.
The King says: *"Extreme" (劇) is the utmost of excess and deviation.*
Guāng says: *"Dàguò (大过), the age of sagging beams and pillars;
**Extreme** (劇), also the omen of disunion and chaos.
When Yin reaches its utmost, it weeps upon the Yang—
without **introduction** (介), the omen of disunion’s extremity.
The *Wáng* and *Xiǎo Sòng* manuscripts read *"位"* (position) for *"泣"* (weep).
The *Fàn* manuscript omits the *"於"* (at).
The *Wáng* manuscript reads *"分"* (divide) for *"介"* (introduction).
The *Fàn* manuscript reads *"俦"* (companion) for *"信"* (trust); the *Xiǎo Sòng* reads *"仍"* (follow), pronounced *"惹"* (provocation). We follow the *Sòng* and *Lù* manuscripts.
*"俦"* (companion), pronounced *"俦"* (companion).
*Sòng* says: *"Weep upon the Yang"*—to make the Yang weep.
Meaning: at this time, the Yin-qi is at its utmost, overwhelming and weakening the Yang, causing it to weep, and no longer possessing even the slightest **introductory virtue** (介) for all things.
Guāng says: *"俦"* (companion) is the ancient form of *"类"* (kind).
All things that receive Yang and live are the Yang’s **kindred** (类).
Once Yin exhausts its utmost influence, all the Yang’s kindred suffer the extreme calamity of disunion.

**First phase:** Bones oppress the flesh—**obscure**.
*Cè* says: *"Bones oppress the flesh"*—the **inner conduct** of the virtuous is harmed.
The *Sòng*, *Lù*, and *Fàn* manuscripts read *"骨景其肉"* (bones illuminate the flesh), **obscure within**.
We follow the *Wáng* and *Xiǎo Sòng* manuscripts.
Bones and flesh are **affectionate** substances.
One is the **beginning of thought**, and it occurs at night.
The root of calamity and disorder arises from within,
like bones binding and oppressing the flesh—**hidden and unseen by people**.

**Second phase:** Blood flows in **eclipse**—**disasterous omens**.
*Cè* says: *"Blood flows in eclipse"*—the **virtuous one is internally wounded**.
The *Fàn* manuscript reads *"君子伤之"* (the virtuous one is wounded by it); we follow the *Sòng*, *Lù*, *Wáng* manuscripts.

**Third phase:** Wine arises, **virtue is lost**, ghosts **glance** at their dwellings.
*Cè* says: *"Wine arises, virtue is lost"*—**unable to govern**.
The *Fàn* manuscript reads *"持"* (hold) for *"将"* (govern); we follow the *Sòng*, *Lù*, *Wáng* manuscripts.
*"睒"* (glance), pronounced *"失冉"* (shī rǎn).
The King says: *"睒"* (glance) means **to gaze**.
**Fourth phase:** Feasting at the **extreme**, parents arrive with **leftovers**, **harmonious**.
*Cè* says: *"Feasting at the extreme with harmony"*—**submission to blessings**.
The *Fàn* and *Xiǎo Sòng* manuscripts originally read *"采"* (harvest); we follow the *Sòng*, *Lù*, *Wáng* manuscripts.
*"馂"* (leftovers), pronounced *"俊"* (jùn).
*"若"* (harmonious), pronounced *"于为"* (yú wéi).
*Fàn* says: *"馂"* (leftovers) are **cooked food**.
*"若"* (harmonious) means **submission**.
Guāng says: The **fourth phase** is **lower blessings** and occurs by day.
The virtuous one serves in a chaotic age, seeking blessings to feed their parents,
without failing in submission.

**Fifth phase:** Venturing into the wild, seeing **desolation**, with tigers herding pigs, **seizing** breeches and **short robes**.
*Cè* says: *"Venturing into the wild, seeing desolation"*—**nowhere to place one’s feet**#14.
The *Wáng* manuscript reads *"唐"* (Tang) for *"虎"* (tiger); we follow the other manuscripts.
*"虚"* (desolation) is the same as *"墟"* (ruins).
*"攓"* (seize), pronounced *"愆"* (qiān).
*"绔"* (breeches) is the same as *"袴"* (trousers).
*"襦"* (short robe), pronounced *"儒"* (rú).
The King says: *"Venturing into the fields and seeing barren hills."*
Guāng says: The **fifth phase** is **middle blessings**, occurring at night.
The petty man rides the chaos of the age, residing in a position of splendor,
engaging in greed and violence to plunder the people—
like tigers herding pigs, leaving the people **nowhere to place their hands or feet**.

**Sixth phase:** The four states are filled—**settling**.
*Cè* says: *"The four states are filled"*—**seeking a secure dwelling**.

**Seventh phase:** **Anxious and abundant**, donning the **face of calamity** while **grieving**.
*Cè* says: *"Anxious and abundant"*—**bearing the comparison of calamity**.
The *Fàn* manuscript reads *"半"* (half) for *"丰"* (abundant); the *Wáng* reads *"牛"* (ox), the *Xiǎo Sòng* reads *"羊"* (sheep); we follow the *Sòng* and *Lù* manuscripts.
*"麃"* (anxious), pronounced *"普表"* (pǔ biǎo).
*"丰"* (abundant), pronounced *"敷容"* (fū róng).
*"较"* (comparison), pronounced *"角"* (jué).

**Eighth phase:** **Jars** piled upon **baskets**, **omens of withering**#15.
*Cè* says: *"Piled upon jars"*—**their duties are urgent**.
The *Fàn* manuscript reads *"瓶累于繘"* (jars piled upon baskets), **their duties are urgent**.
We follow the *Sòng*, *Lù*, and *Wáng* manuscripts.
*"繘"* (baskets), pronounced *"余律"* (yú lǜ).»

**Ninth (uppermost) line:** The sea’s waters swarm aloft, exhausted at Heaven’s pivot.
*Cè* says: *"The sea’s waters swarm aloft"*—**utterly beyond speech**.

The *Fàn* manuscript reads *"蔽"* (obscured) for *"弊"* (exhausted); the *Wáng* and *Xiǎo Sòng* manuscripts read *"落"* (fall) for *"语"* (speech). We follow the *Èr Sòng* and *Lù* manuscripts.
The *Wáng* manuscript reads *"天"* (Heaven) as *""* (omitted); we follow the other manuscripts.

**Three regions, three states, two departments, two households**—**tamed by the Yang family**, aligned with *Kūn*.
*Sòng* says: *"Kūn"*—**compliance**.
*"驯"*—also **compliance**.
When *Yīn* qi is supremely compliant, the chaos is undifferentiated, its source unseen.

*Sòng* says: *"This speaks of Yīn’s fulfillment, when compliance is complete, and the time returns to Yang. Its work is undifferentiated, without beginning or end, its roots unseen."*

**First day:** Yellow Spirit’s hidden virtue—**compliant**.
*Cè* says: *"Yellow Spirit’s hidden virtue"*—**compliance with rectitude**.
The *Tǔ* family’s essence is **yellow**#17, the birth of the divine is **Spirit**, the lowest of the low is **hidden**, the day’s proper time is **rectitude**.
At the **beginning of contemplation**#18, **these four virtues**#19 are present—**compliant yet rectifying**.

**Second day:** Its **fat**—**the toil of women**; without stillness, **life perishes**.
*Cè* says: *"The loss of fat"*—**incapable of purity**#20.
The *Sòng* and *Lù* manuscripts read *"绳"* (cord); the *Xiǎo Sòng* manuscript reads *""* (omitted).

**Third day:** The **female’s rectitude** is ever **benevolent**, guarding its root.
*Cè* says: *"The female’s rectitude is ever benevolent"*—**never forgetting its source**.
The *Yì* says: *"Constant in virtue, rectitude—**the woman prospers**."*
Kūn is **Mother**; the three natures delight in **emotion and kindness**, and it governs **many children** while presiding over **drawing**#23, hence *"the female’s rectitude is ever benevolent."*
It is said that **constant benevolence** is the **proper path** for women.
Three is **Wood**, **thought ascending**, **fulfilling intent**, **self-so**; it is **able to never forget its root**.

**Fourth day:** **Yielding to toil**, it does not match the **five’s splendor**.
*Cè* says: *"Yielding to toil"*—**cutting down virtue**.
The *Xiǎo Sòng* manuscript reads *"not as the five-five’s splendor"*, now following the other texts.
Wang says: *"Splendor"*—**virtue encompassing a thousand**.
Guāng says: Four is **low fortune**, yet it presides over **night**.
The **petty man** serving his lord **cuts down his merits**, not matching the **five’s luminous embrace**, daring not to **boast of himself**.

**Fifth day:** The **luminous pouch, vast embrace**, its virtue **precious and central**.
*Cè* says: *"The luminous pouch, vast embrace"*—**daring not to boast**.
The Wang manuscript reads *"虚"* (emptiness) for *"灵"* (luminous); now following the other texts.
The *Xiǎo Sòng* manuscript reads *"灵括巨橐"* (luminous binding, vast pouch); now following the other texts.
Wang says: *"Precious"*—**what is valued by things**. *"Central"*—**obtaining the meaning of balance**.
Guāng says: Five is **pouch**, **embrace**; Earth, as a thing, **contains and expands with radiance**, hence *"luminous pouch, vast embrace."*
Five occupies the **position of fullness**, yet presides over **drawing**.
The **noble** as a subject, though **high in rank and great in merit**, does not **boast**; thus it has this symbol.
The *Yì* says: *"Yellow robes—**great good fortune**."*

**Sixth day:** The **pouch loses its binding**, spilling **precious vessels**.
*Cè* says: *"The pouch loses its binding"*—**the subject’s mouth overflows**.
The *Xiǎo Sòng* manuscript also reads *"橐"* (pouch); now following the other texts.
Six is **cave**, **passage**; **exceeding the center** while presiding over **night**.
The **petty man** cannot **contain virtue** to follow the king’s affairs, like a pouch **losing its ties** and spilling **precious vessels**.
The *Yì* says: *"If the subject is not discreet, he loses himself."*

**Seventh day:** **Square and firm**, transgressing **rectitude**, advantageous for the subject’s **rectitude**.
*Cè* says: *"Square and firm, transgressing rectitude"*—**guarding proper conduct**.
Seven is **Lǐ**, **guilt**, **transgressing**, yet presiding over **drawing**.
The **noble** serves above, **offering what is right and removing what is wrong**, acting with **square firmness**; though there may be **transgression**, nothing is **hidden that loses its rectitude**—this is the **proper path** for a subject; hence it is **advantageous**.

**Eighth day:** **Compliant yet not rectified**, it does not **preserve its life**.
*Cè* says: *"Compliant yet not rectified"*—**lacking unity**.
Eight is **disease and stagnation**, **decay and peeling**; yet it presides over **night**.
The **petty man** serving above **hesitates before and behind**, **right and wrong**, **yes and no**, **only complying with the lord**, unable to **guard the Way and maintain unity**, hence it does not **preserve its life**.

**Ninth day:** **Compliant in righteousness**, forgetting life, **relying on Heaven’s rectitude**.
*Cè* says: *"Compliant in righteousness, forgetting life"*—**receiving the mandate is certain**.
Nine is the **exhaustion of calamity**, yet presides over **drawing**.
The **noble** serves his lord with **loyalty unto death**, **no second thoughts**; **compliant in righteousness**, forgetting life—what he relies on is **Heaven’s rectitude alone**.

---
**Three regions, three states, two departments, three generals**
The **Yin family’s water**, aligning with *Wèi Jì*.
The **general enters**, descending to **eighth**, the day to **Shén Jì**, the **Great Snow** in resonance with *qi*.

The **Fight Star** stands in **Zǐ**, the **Lü** in **Yellow Bell**.
Lù says: *"General"*—**Yin and Yang reach their peak, then reverse**; just as *Fù*, *Shēng*, and *Jì Zhì* follow.
It is said: *"Things cannot be exhausted,"* hence it is followed by *Wèi Jì*—its meaning is the same.
**Yin’s qi** completes things **above**; **Yang’s mandate** begins **below**, ready to **replenish**.

The Wang manuscript reads *"如"* (as); now following the other texts.
Sòng says: *"At this time, Yin completes things above; those who have fulfilled their function withdraw, so Yang’s qi begins anew below."*

**First day:** The **general constructs deceit**, **original peril**.
*Cè* says: *"The general constructs deceit"*—**danger takes command**.
Fàn says: *"Peril"*—**danger**.
*"Original"*—**beginning**.
Wang says: *"At the start of the general’s term, yet out of place at night, constructing deceit."*
Taking **danger as foundation**, hence *"takes command"*.
Guāng says: One is **thought’s beginning**, yet presiding over **night**, hence this symbol.

**Second day:** The **general has no flaw**, **original clarity**.
*Cè* says: *"The general has no flaw"*—**easy to follow**.
Fàn says: *"Clarity"*—**purity**#21.
Wang says: *"Obtaining position while presiding over drawing, the general few in faults, hence ‘the general has no flaw.’"*
The **essence of two** is pure and clear, hence *"original clarity"*.
Beginning without flaw, **later it will be easy to continue**.
Guāng says: Two is **thought’s middle**, yet presiding over **drawing**#22, hence this symbol.

**Third day:** **Furnace and potter’s wheel**—**disaster**; advantageous to **halt and use**.
*Cè* says: *"Furnace and potter’s wheel"*—**internal injury from transformation**.
The Sòng and Lù *Cè* reads *"钧不化"* (wheel does not transform); now following Fàn and Wang’s texts.
Fàn says: *"Smelting"* is **furnace**, *"pottery"* is **wheel**.
Wang says: *"Furnace and wheel"*—**the beginning of creation**.
Beginning yet **not by its proper method**, advantage lies in **quick cessation**.
The **general’s path grows strong**, yet **out of place at night**, a **general not acting by principle**.
Guāng says: *"Disaster"*—**unfavorable**, pronounced *bǐ*.
Three is **fulfilling intent**, yet presiding over **night**, hence this symbol.

**Fourth day:** The **general flies and gains wings**, advantageous to **ascend to Heaven**.
*Cè* says: *"The general flies and gains wings"*—**its aid is strong**.
Four is **low fortune**, **external**, yet presiding over **drawing**.
The **noble obtains position**; **people assist him**; like a **general flying and gaining wings**.

**Fifth day:** The **great bird flies**, **plucking its feathers**.
Though feathers are many, they **cannot reach their destination**.
*Cè* says: *"The great bird plucks its feathers"*—**unreliable aid**.

The **Sage** says: *"Five dwells in a place of prominence, ought to be the foremost lord, yet loses position at night, contrary to what is proper. Like a great bird about to take flight, yet plucking its six wings—though feathers be many, how could it ever fly?*

**Guāng** says: *"The Duke of Jìn’s words to Pínggōng: ‘I have over three thousand guests in my service—can I still be said to neglect the worthy?’*

**Gùsāng** #23 says: *"The wild goose soars to the heavens, relying on its six wings alone. The down beneath its belly, the fur upon its back—if even a handful were removed, its flight would neither rise nor fall. Does not your lordship’s retinue consist of these six wings, or merely the down and fur of belly and back?"*

**Sixth day:** The sun loses its brilliance, the noble begins to decline.
*Cè* says: *"The sun loses its brilliance"*—**its radiance is at its peak**.
The **Sòng and Lǔ** manuscripts read *"shànglóng"* (ascend/flourish) for *"shuāngjiàng"* (decline), while the **Wáng** manuscript also reads *"lóng"* for *"jiàng"* (decline). I follow the **Fàn** and **Xiǎo Sòng** manuscripts here.
**Xiǎo Sòng** reads *"shī"* (lose) as *"wú"* (brute), which I follow among the other manuscripts.
**Fàn** says: *"Jiàng"* means **to descend**. Five is the **midday sun**, hence six is the **waning sun**. *"Lièliè"* means **abundance**. The sun’s heat is greatest only after midday, hence *"lièliè"* is spoken of.

**Guāng** says: *"Shī"* is homophonous with *"yì"* (waning), pronounced *tújié*. Six is **high fortune**, yet exceeding midday and presiding over **drawing**, though possessing great abundance, the noble knows it must decline, able to humble itself—thus its radiance remains unwaned.

**Seventh day:** The boat **lurches**, the carriage **stumbles**—its harm is not distant.
*Cè* says: *"The boat lurches, the carriage stumbles"*—**harm is near**.
The **Wáng** manuscript reads *"gōng"* (arm) for *"chuán"* (boat) and *"yuǎn"* (distant) for *"é"* (far), which I follow the **Sòng, Lǔ,** and **Fàn** manuscripts here.
*"Zhuǎ"* is pronounced *qùxiè*.
*"Bá"* is pronounced *púbó*.

「**Fàn** says: Seven is **the loss of aspiration**. The boat and carriage are the means of governance; the worthy are also the means to govern the world. The one who loses aspiration is the master of calamity, hence the **lurching and stumbling**; abandoning the means of governance brings self-harm, so the danger is not distant.

**Guāng** says: Seven marks the **beginning of disaster** and corresponds to **night**, hence this symbol. When a state is about to fall, it first abandons its worthy advisors.

**Eighth day:** The **young** are in the **abyss**, the **elder** abandons the boat.
**Cè** says: The elder abandons the boat—**to rescue the drowning world**.
**Fàn** says: The young refer to the **common people**. In times of calamity, as though in an abyss, the elder abandons the boat to save them.
**Guāng** says: Eight is **the midst of disaster**, yet corresponds to **day**, hence this symbol.

**Ninth day:** The **scarlet silkworm** clings to the **dead mulberry**, its cocoon not **yellow**.
**Cè** says: Clinging to the dead mulberry and not yielding yellow—**the silkworm’s labor is ruined**.
The **Fàn** manuscript reads *"clings to the dead mulberry, not yellow"* as *"clings to the dead mulberry"* and *"its cocoon not yellow"*, while the **Wáng** manuscript reads *"dead mulberry, not yellow"*, and I follow the **Sòng** and **Lǔ** manuscripts here.
**Fàn** says: When the silkworm first emerges, its **bristles** are red like those of the aged, hence the **scarlet**.
**Wáng** says: Nine dwells at the **ultimate peak**, yet loses its position and corresponds to **night**, with nowhere to turn. The **scarlet silkworm** is the **ailing silkworm**. But clinging to the **dead mulberry**, how could it ever form its cocoon? **Not yellow** means **unfruitful**.

**Threefold calamity in three states and three regions**—**one household’s ruin**#25.
**Dàn** (danger) is pronounced *nǎi* (to endure).
**Yang** household calamity is measured by **obstruction**; **Yīn** *qi* is **threefold difficulty**, with water **congealing** and earth **cracking**, while **yang** is weak in the abyss.
The **Xiǎo Sòng** manuscript reads *"Yīn qi fāng nǎn"* (difficulty of **Yīn** *qi*) as *"Tài yīn nǎn"* (ultimate **Yīn** difficulty), and I follow the other manuscripts here.
**Xiǎo Sòng** says: When **Yīn** reaches its **ultimate extremity**, **yang** *qi* arrives with **reassurance**, and **Yīn** fears **yang**, causing **great peril and difficulty**. At the very start of the first day:

**First day:** **Calamity** befalls me, **obscure and formless**.
**Cè** says: **Calamity** befalls me, **obscure and formless**—**it is yet unseen**.
The **Fàn** manuscript reads *"it is not yet seen"*, and I follow the **Sòng, Lǔ**, and **Wáng** manuscripts here.
**Calamity** is the symbol of **obstruction and suppression**. One is the **slightest hint of thought**, yet it corresponds to **day**. **Obstructing and suppressing** disaster and evil when they are still **unseen**—what greater **illness** could there be?

**Second day:** **Frozen** at the **ice ford**, the **wild horse** drags the **wood**#28.
**Cè** says: The **wild horse** drags the **wood**—**reckless birth**.
The **Fàn** manuscript omits *"at"* and reads *"drags"* as *chǔ* (to hold), and I follow the **Sòng, Lǔ**, and **Wáng** manuscripts here.

**Third day:** **Firm in the center**, **calamity** arises from **extraordinary forces**.
**Cè** says: **Firm in the center**, **never to be toppled**.
The **Wáng** manuscript reads *"toppled"* as *gù* (to look back), and I follow the other manuscripts here.
Three is **the completion of intent**, yet it corresponds to **day**. The **heart** can **fortify and endure**, obstructing and suppressing **extraordinary forces**.

**Fourth day:** The **egg cracks the stone**, **ruined**#29.
**Cè** says: The **egg cracks the stone** and is **ruined**—**the calamity of the lowly**.
The **Sòng, Lǔ**, and **Wáng** manuscripts read *"calamity"* as *zá* (mixed), and I follow the **Fàn** and **Xiǎo Sòng** manuscripts here.
**Ruined** is pronounced *túwàn* (to play idly).
**Wáng** says: In times of **calamity**, losing its position and corresponding to **night**, one does not know the **way of calamity**. Hence, when the **egg cracks the stone**, it is **ruined**, and **unfruitfulness** is certain.

**Fifth day:** **Calamity** without **interval**, though great, it is **not laborious**.
**Cè** says: **Calamity** without **interval**—**the heart is sealed and closed**.
**Wáng** says: Obtaining its position in the center, it becomes the **master of calamity**. It can **block and seal** the onset, leaving no **interval**, so that disaster has no way to enter. Even in the midst of **great calamity**, one avoids harm without exertion.

**Sixth day:** The **great carriage** moves **heavily**#30, **upward** on the **mountain**, **downward** into the **river**.
**Cè** says: The **great carriage** moves **heavily**—**upward and downward, obstructed**.
The **Sòng, Lǔ**, and **Wáng** manuscripts read *"heavily"* as *chuánchuán*.
**Wú** says: **River** is pronounced *biān* (ford), but the **Fàn, Wáng**, and **Xiǎo Sòng** manuscripts read *"obstructed"* as *yù* (to block), and I follow the **Sòng, Lǔ** manuscripts here.
**Wú** says: **To block** is pronounced *kǔhǎi* (bitter sea).
**Yù** is pronounced *érzhèn* (to shake).
**Fàn** says: **Heavy movement** is the **appearance of delay**.
**Wáng** says: Six dwells in **abundance and fullness**, yet loses its position and corresponds to **night**, unable to avoid **calamity’s proper course**.

**Seventh day:** **Pulling up the stone**, **strength is exhausted** in the effort.
**Cè** says: **Pulling up the stone**—**seizing the moment**.
The **Fàn** manuscript reads *"exhausted"* as *jìn* (to finish), and I follow the **Sòng, Lǔ**, and **Wáng** manuscripts here.
**Pulling** is pronounced *zhìrén* (the person).」

「The **Sòng** says: **"Time"** refers to the **day** (daytime).
**Fàn** says: **Stone** is used to signify **difficulty**. **川川**—the **appearance of difficulty** (or calamity).

**Wáng** says: When **Yin-qi** reaches its extreme, even if frozen and solidified like **congealed ice**, there is **fearful dread** (戁) and **injury** (恐) from **external assaults**. Though **Yang-qi** is still faint, it will **collide** (邸) with the **obscure** (冥) and **encounter** (触) its **darkness** (昧), revealing its **strength** (膂力) within. As **Yin-qi** reaches its peak and **Yang-qi** begins to rise, the **symbol** (象) reflects **toil and exertion**.

**First line:** **Toil in deliberation**, **obstructed integrity** (否贞).
**Cè** says: **Toil in deliberation** and **obstructed integrity**—**the center is not upright** (中不正也).
The **Fàn, Wáng**, and **Xiǎo Sòng** manuscripts lack the character **"deliberation"** (谋), which I adopt from the **Sòng** and **Lǔ** manuscripts here.
**Wáng** says: At the **beginning of toil**, yet **losing position** (失位) as **night**, it is **toil without adherence to the Dao/Way** (勤而不以其道者也). **Obstructed integrity** means **not upright** (不正之谓也).
**Guāng** says: **One** marks the **start of thought**, the **beginning of toil and exertion**. As it belongs to the **toil family** yet aligns with **night**, hence this **symbol**.

**Second line:** **Toil with favor**, **exertion earnest and sincere** (勤悾悾), the **noble one finds harmony** (君子有中).
**Cè** says: **Toil with favor** and **earnest exertion**—**it encompasses all emotions** (有诸情也). **悾悾**—pronounced *kōng* (empty), akin to **reverent** (款款).
**Wáng** says: **Two** **gains position** (得位) as **day**, **adhering to the Dao/Way of toil**. **Toil with favor**—**exertion that is not wasted** (勤不虚施者也). **悾悾**—**reverent and sincere**, **unflagging in toil** (勤之而款款, 不倦其勤者也). Moreover, **the noble one’s harmony** (君子之中道) is attained, so what **fault or regret** (咎悔) could possibly arise?
**Guāng** says: **Harmony** (中)—**sincere devotion** (至诚). **Two** marks the **midpoint of thought**, aligning with **day**. The **noble one** **fulfills loyalty** (尽忠) toward others; **favor and toil** (恩斯勤斯) are sincere and **earnest** (悾悾), not merely superficial expressions.

**Third line:** **Bound and constrained** (羁角), **my lament is incessant** (泣呱呱), **yet lacking embrace and support** (未得襁扶).
**Cè** says: **Bound and constrained**—**unable to fulfill its mandate** (不得命也).
The **Xiǎo Sòng** manuscript reads *"yet lacking"* (未得) as *"yet without"* (未有), which I follow from the **Sòng, Lǔ, and Fàn** manuscripts here. The **Sòng** and **Lǔ** manuscripts read *"support"* (扶) as *"staff"* (杖), which I follow from the **Wáng** manuscript here.
**呱呱**—the sound of weeping.
**Wáng** says: **Male** (角) and **female** (羁)—**ornaments for children**. **吾吾**—**restless and aimless**. **呱呱**—**cries**. **襁**—to **embrace**; **扶**—to **hold**. If a child **cries in distress** (吾吾然无所归之貌), yet **cannot be embraced or held** (不得长者抱持之) by elders, even if **toiling in sorrow**, it will **never find resolution**. This illustrates **three**, when **toil and difficulty** (劳苦) are at their height, yet **losing position** (失位) as **night**, **with nowhere to turn**; hence the **symbol**.

**Fourth line:** **Toil in strength**, **abandoning subservience** (放陪), **forgetting sustenance** (忘食), the **great person achieves victory** (大人有克).
**Cè** says: **Toil in strength**, **forgetting sustenance**—**the virtue/power of the great person** (大人德).
The **Fàn** manuscript reads *"subservience"* (陪) as *"doubling"* (倍), which I follow from the **Sòng, Lǔ, and Wáng** manuscripts here.
The **Sòng** and **Lǔ** manuscripts read *"virtue/power"* (德) as *"attainment"* (得), which I follow from the **Fàn, Wáng, and Xiǎo Sòng** manuscripts here.
**放**—pronounced *fǔwàng* (to abandon).
**Wáng** says: As **toil in the Dao/Way** grows **profound**, yet **gains position** (得位) as **day**, **without losing its proper course** (不失其宜). This is when, **amidst toil and difficulty**, one can **strengthen its efforts** (勤其力), **abandoning subservience** (放乎陪隶之事), and **forgetting the rewards of sustenance** (忘其禄食之报). Only **the great person** (大人) can **achieve this**, leading to **success**.

**Guāng** says: **Four** marks the **lower rank of sustenance**, yet **aligns with day**; hence this **symbol**.

**Fifth line:** **Frenzied and stumbling** (狂蹇蹇), **calamity is near** (祸迩), **fortune is distant** (福远).
**Cè** says: **Frenzied stumbling**—**fortune is far away** (远乎福也).
The **Sòng, Lǔ, and Fàn** manuscripts read *"frenzied"* (狂) as *"to go"* (往), which I follow from the **Wáng** and **Xiǎo Sòng** manuscripts here.
**Wáng** says: **Five** **dwells in a position of abundance**, yet **loses position** (失位) as **night**. **Toil** (勤) **greatly departs from its proper course** (大失其宜), hence the **symbol of frenzy and stumbling**. **Fortune is distant** (福远), **while calamity is near** (祸迩).

**Sixth line:** **Toil achieves success**, **approaching the heavens** (几于天).
**Cè** says: **Toil achieves success**—**heavenly aid arrives** (天所来辅).
The **Sòng, Lǔ, and Wáng** manuscripts read *"approaching the heavens"* (几于天) as *"heavenly nearness"* (天夹).
**Sòng** says: **夹**—**nearness**.
**Lǔ** says: **夹**—**harmony**.
The **Xiǎo Sòng** manuscript reads *"heavenly nearness"* (天夹), which I follow from the **Fàn** manuscript here.
**Wáng** says: **Six** **dwells in a position of abundance**, yet **gains position** (得位) as **day**; hence, **"toil achieves success"** (勤有成功). **几**—**nearness**.

**Seventh line:** **Toil drags, yet not by the nose**, **but by the tail** (于尾), **exhausted** (弊).
**Cè** says: **The exhaustion of toil**—**its Dao/Way is contrary** (其道逆也).
**Fàn** says: **Dragging an ox by the tail** (牵牛不其鼻, 而尾) **causes exhaustion**; hence, **toil is exhausted** (故劳弊也).

Guāng says: **"Seven"** is **a rope**, is **a cord**, and also **lost resolve**—yet it corresponds to night, hence this symbol.

**Eighth line:** **Toil is restless** (踖踖), **the heart is weary** (心爽), **the blindfolded ox refuses to move** (蒙柴不却).
**Cè** says: **Toil wearies the heart**—**it is a sacrifice to the state**.

**Tread** (踖), pronounced *wú zī xī* (吴资昔切), **Xiao Sòng** reads it as *què* (鹊).

**Ninth line, at the apex:** **His toil, his toil!** **He carries a chariot into the abyss**, **drags a boat up the mountain**.
**Cè** says: **His toil, his toil!** **Toil finds no reward**.
**Wáng** says: **Nine dwells in the extreme height**, yet **loses its proper time** (当夜), **toiling without the Dao/Way**.

**Three directions, three states, three regions, three households**—**nurturing the Yang family’s gold**, **aligned with the chin** (准颐).
**At the end of Nine**, **heaven’s measure of qi remains**, **yet still has sixty minutes, twenty-four seconds**.
**Tread** (踦) **should be forty minutes sixteen seconds**, **Excess** (赢) **should be twenty minutes eight seconds**.
**Yin swells upon the wilds**, **Yang nurtures all things**, **their hue is red below**.
**Sòng and Lǔ** read **弸 (弸)** as **胎 (tāi)**, **Wáng** as **殆 (dài)**; **Xiao Sòng** reads **赤 (chì)** as **殷 (yīn)**, now following the **Fàn** manuscript.
**弸 (弸)** has no entry in the character dictionaries.
**弧 (hú)**, pronounced *pú méng* (蒲萌切).
**芦 (lú)**, pronounced *chén wú hóu* (陈吴侯切), in **Wú** as *fú* (敷).
**Fàn** says: **弸 (弸)**—**fullness at its peak**.
**蓲 (蓲)**, **concealment**. **When things first emerge**, their color is red.
**This refers to the time when Yin-qi swells to its extreme in the fields** (於田野), **so Yang conceals itself**, and all things are red below.
**Lǔ** says: **蓲 (蓲)**, pronounced like **沤 (yōu)** in **沤菅 (yōu jiān)**.
**It speaks of Yang nourishing the roots of all things**, making them all red.

**Guāng** says: **弸 (弸)**, **fullness**.

**First line:** **Hide the heart in the abyss**, **nurture its sacred root**.
**Cè** says: **Hide the heart in the abyss**—**the spirit does not stray**.
**Xiao Sòng** reads **美厥灵根 (měi jué líng gēn)** as **美厥灵元 (měi jué líng yuán)**, now following the other manuscripts.
**Guāng:** **One is thinking of the lower realm**.
**Preserving the spirit and securing the root**—**this is how one nurtures life**.

**Second line:** **Silent nourishment of evil**, **the origin contains no integrity**.
**Cè** says: **Silent nourishment of evil**—**the heart is corrupt**.
**Fàn** manuscript reads **匪 (fěi)** as **否 (pǐ)**, now following **Sòng, Lǔ, and Wáng**.
**Wú** says: **墨 (mò)**, same as **默 (mò)**.
**函 (hán)**, pronounced *hú nán* (胡男切).
**Wáng** says: **Losing its position at night**, **losing the proper way of nurturing**, **silently following the crooked path**.
**Guāng** says: **元 (yuán)**, **beginning**. **Two is thinking of the middle**, yet **it corresponds to night**.
**The wickedness of the petty man**, though not yet manifest in speech or action, **nurtures his evil inclinations**; **internally harboring what is not upright**, he can never enter the path of the noble.

**Third line:** **Manure the mound to fertilize it**, **nurture its roots and shoots**.
**Cè** says: **Manure the mound to fertilize it**—**the center shines brightly**.
**荄 (tì)**, pronounced *gǔ āi* (古哀切).
**Wáng** says: **Obtaining its position at day**, **skilled in the Dao of nurturing**, hence the symbol of **manuring the fertile mound** to **nurture the roots and shoots of grasses and trees**. **Thus their growth and flourishing can be awaited without delay**.

**Fourth line:** **The swallow eats sparingly**, **its resolve is firm**, **advantageous to go on a trading expedition**.
**Cè** says: **The swallow eats sparingly**—**its resolve is in reliance**.
**Xiao Sòng** reads **在 (zài)** as **有 (yǒu)**, now following the other manuscripts.
**扁 (biǎn)**, pronounced like **篇 (piān)**.
** (jué)**, pronounced *jū huò* (居蠖切).
**贾 (gǔ)**, pronounced like **古 (gǔ)**.
**Sòng** says: **赖 (lài)**, **advantage**.
**Guāng** says: **Four is lower fortune**, yet **it corresponds to night**.
**The petty man obtains his position**, his resolve is in **seeking advantage for self-nurturing**. **Like the swallow flying in a sparse manner**, **eating merely to survive**. **This is merely the way of the merchant**.

**Fifth line:** **Yellow heart within the belly**, **white bones grow flesh**, **sincere virtue does not return**.
**Cè** says: **Yellow heart within the belly**—**upper virtue aligns with Heaven**.
**Fàn** manuscript reads **上德 (shàng dé)** as **上得 (shàng dé)**, now following **Sòng, Lǔ, and Wáng**.
**Sòng** says: **It speaks of upper virtue like Heaven’s bestowal**.
**Guāng** says: **Yellow**, **the middle**. **Bone**, **the withered thing**. **Sincere**, **the thorough trust**.
**Five is fortune’s center**, yet **it corresponds to day**, **the master of nurturing**.
**Firmly holding the middle**, **to nurture all under Heaven**, even **white bones can grow flesh**, **how much more so for people?** **How much more so for birds, beasts, grasses, and trees?**
**Its virtue is like Heaven’s**—**clouds move, rain descends**, **governing the four quarters**, **and no creature can repay such virtue**.

**Sixth line:** **Repeatedly, daily three meals**, **the sacrificial ox’s omen**, **fat but not advantageous**.
**Cè** says: **Repeatedly fed**, **fat without substance**.
**Wáng** manuscript omits **次次 (cì cì)**, **Xiao Sòng** reads **沉沉睢睢 (chén chén suī suī)**, also saying **fat and lost in substance**, now following **Sòng, Lǔ, and Fàn**.
**Wú** says: **次 (cì)**, same as **趑 (zī)**, pronounced *zī*.
**Fàn** says: **次次**, **the appearance of unrest**.
**The ox already divined**, **awaiting fattening before use**, hence **no substance**.
**Lǔ** says: **Six dwells in overfullness**, **loses its position at night**, **nurturing too excessively**, **yet not appropriately**.
Hence the symbol of **daily three meals**, **to enrich its food**, like the **sacrificial ox of the ancestral temples**, **fed beyond measure**, **already answering the divined omen**, yet **the fatter it grows**, **the more it harms its body**.
**Guāng** says: **Six is upper fortune at night**, hence this symbol.

**Seventh line:** **The young boy leads the elephant**, **the woman is fierce**, **the noble nurtures his illness**.
**Cè** says: **Leading the elephant to nurture illness**—**they do not correspond**.
**Sòng and Lǔ** read **徽 (huī)** as **微 (wēi)**, now following the other manuscripts.
**Wáng** says: **Seven dwells in the overfull position**, **near danger**.
Yet **obtaining its position at day**, **the noble in it is auspicious**, **the petty man and woman in it are inauspicious**. **Like the young boy leading the elephant**, **his strength cannot control it**, **necessarily bringing peril**.
Only the noble **knows the limits of time**, **nurturing his illness with the Dao**, thus **can he attain final auspiciousness**.
**One auspicious, one inauspicious**, **two paths opposed**, hence **they do not correspond**.

**Guāng** says: **徽 (huī)**, **a great rope**, meaning **to tether and bind**.

「**Měng**—**a fierce beast**. Seven brings ruin and misfortune, **the onset of calamity**, hence **illness**.
When the time is day, **it belongs to the noble**. **Over-nurturing it brings fortune’s peak and disaster’s arrival**.
The petty man, **not measuring his strength**, yet still **clings stubbornly to his cultivation**.
The noble **knows the limits of time**, **harmonizing with its waxing and waning**, **like tending an illness**—thus **no blame attaches**.

**Next, Eight**: **a lodged obstruction**, **untamed**, **poisonous sickness erupts**, **ghosts ascend the mound**.
**Cè says**: **The eruption of the lodged sickness** **returns to the tomb**.
Eight is **the heart of disaster**, **at night**.
The petty man **persists in his nurturing without cease**, **like a bone lodged in the throat**, **unable to dislodge it**, **thus inviting great calamity**.
Like **poisonous sickness erupting**, **beyond remedy**.

**Uppermost Nine**: **Stars align as the year aligns**, **returning to the beginning’s succession**.
**Cè says**: **Stars aligning as the year aligns**—**completing the nurturing of the start**.
The **Rán** manuscript reads **"completing the start’s nurturing"**—now following the **Sòng, Lǔ, and Wáng** manuscripts.
Nurturing at **Uppermost Nine**, **dwelling at the end of the *Zàn***,
The sun **reaches its limit in the next hexagram**, the moon **in its cycle**, the stars **revolve in heaven**, the year **shall renew its beginning**.
**Completing the nurturing of the start**, **beginning to succeed the end**, **cycling endlessly**—this is **how the Dao of Heaven is boundless**.

**Zàn of the One: Water.**»

「**Zàn of the Two: Fire.**
**Zàn**, insufficiency also. The **year** is **fixed at three hundred sixty-five days plus one-fourth of a day**.
**Xuán** has seven hundred twenty-nine **Zàn**, corresponding to three hundred sixty-four days and a half.
Its insufficiency is half a day, which is called **Zàn of the Two**.
**Zàn**, pronounced *jù-yí* (居宜切).

**Frozen** ascends to the **crimson sky**, **dusk** descends into the **dark spring**.
**Cè says**: *"Frozen ascends to the crimson sky"*—**this marks the beginning of the yin**.
**Fàn says**: *"Frozen"* is **the utmost cold**; **yet the sky** is **the utmost height**.
*"Dusk"* is **the utmost heat**; **yet the spring** is **the utmost depth**.
**Frozen** is at the **sky’s zenith**, hence it marks the **beginning**.
**Guāng says**: *"Crimson"* is **the peak of yang**; *"dark"* is **the extreme of yin**.
**All things reach extremes and reverse**—since the **beginning of time**,
**Yin and yang** mutually **generate**#38,
**Day and night** mutually **follow**#38,
**Good and evil** mutually **lean against**,
**Order and chaos** mutually **persist**,
**Gain and loss** mutually **multiply**,
**Fortune and misfortune** mutually **contradict**#39—
**these are all the natural principles of Heaven and humanity**.

**Yíng Zàn of the Two: Fire.**
**Yíng**, excess also. Beyond the **three hundred sixty-five days** of the **year**, there is an excess of one-fourth of a day, which is called **Yíng Zàn**.
**One insufficiency, one excess**—**the Zàn of the Two** are what they **produce**.
**Cè says**: *"One insufficiency, one excess"*—**the cycles never cease**.
The **Fàn** and **Xiǎo Sòng** manuscripts read *"Zàn of the Two"* as *"Zàn of the Odd"* (踦奇), and the **Fàn** manuscript reads *"cycles"* (儃) as *"meditation"* (禅); now following the **Sòng, Lǔ, and Wáng** manuscripts.
*"Cycles"*, anciently *"meditation"* (禅), pronounced *shèn* (时战切).
**Lǔ says**: *"When yin reaches its extreme and yang arises, they mutually cycle and replace each other without end."*
**Guāng says**: *"The calculations of insufficiency and excess*#35#36—**even Heaven and Earth cannot equalize them."*
**Only when they are unequal** can **transformation and change** occur, **endlessly generating**.
Thus it is said: *"Twenty-nine days of insufficiency*#27#35#36, **and the moon shifts to the next cycle**;
*Twenty-seven days of insufficiency*#27#35#36, **and it completes the Heavenly revolution**.*
Then there are **the dark and new moons**, **the ten celestial stems**, **the twelve terrestrial branches**,
Then there are **the six divisions of pregnancy**—this is **how they endure forever**.
**The Great Commentary on the *Tàixuán* Scriptures concludes here.**

#35#36*"Guāng says"*#37, *"the calculations"*#35#36—originally missing, supplemented from the **Fàn** and **備要、全書** manuscripts.
#37*"This"*—originally missing, supplemented from the **備要、全書** manuscripts.
#38*"Follow"*—originally *"become"* (成), corrected according to the **備要、全書** manuscripts and context.

---
*(Note: The annotations #35–#38 refer to textual corrections in the original, not part of the translation itself.)*

「#39 *"Reflect"* originally *"follow"* (承), corrected according to the **備要、全書** manuscripts.

**Chapter Seven: Commentary on the *Tàixuán* Scriptures – *Shànglíng* Xu Hàn’s Annotations**
**Preface to *Xuānshǒu* (The Mysterious Origin)**

The main text has already been presented earlier; the preface is omitted to avoid repetition.

The **mysterious patterns** of the **cosmic whole**—
One yin and one yang interweave,
Yet harmonized by the **Three**—this is how they function.

The **Mysterious** encompasses **Three Systems**,
And within them, one yang governs one system,
Giving rise to all things.

From the **regions, states, districts, and households**,
Each follows the **One, Two, or Three**—
This is called **the dispersion of the Three Positions**.

It is said: *"Arrange the Nine Nines,*
To calculate the birth of all things."

The **Nine Circuits** flow cyclically with voids,
Establishing divisions to disperse—
Life perpetuates without end.

**Praise to the Supreme Principles**—
This is the **opening discourse**.

Now it gathers names,
Systematizing the **Mysterious lineage**.

**The Mysterious Origin**—
The opening text is scattered beneath the scriptures and is omitted here to avoid repetition.

The *Yì* divides the **hexagrams** to govern the calendar:
Each line represents one day,
And sixty hexagrams complete the **360-day cycle**.

**Kǎn, Lǐ, Zhèn, and Duì** serve as the **Four Cardinal Directors** in their seasons.

The **solstices and equinoxes** mark the four seasons,
Each line representing one **qi**,
And four hexagrams completing the **24 seasonal qi**.

The *Commentary* states:
*"The *Jiǎzǐ* hexagram-qi begins with *Zhōngfá*."*

Seven parts of the **80% day**—
This is the **beginning of the Mysterious Scriptures**.

*Zhōngfá* governs the **first sign of Winter Solstice**—
The so-called *"earthworms knotting."*

From the **first day of the cycle** to the **third day of the revolution**,
It is then **received by *Fù* (Repetition).**

*Fù* governs the **second sign**—
The so-called *"deer antlers shedding."*

From the **fourth day of the revolution** to the **sixth day of *Lún* (Abundance)**,
It is then **received by *Tún* (Difficulty).**

*Tún* governs the **third sign**—
The so-called *"waters and springs moving."*

From the **seventh day of *Lún*** to the **ninth day of *Xián* (Dispersion)**,
The **three hexagrams’ qi** are now complete.

Each hexagram has a remainder—
Seven parts of the **80% day**—
Overlapping and interwoven to form the whole.

At the year’s end, the **sixty hexagrams** govern the **Eighty-One Mysterious Origins**,
And the **Mysterious qi** permeates thus.

Therefore, some hexagrams recur—
For the **qi’s numbers** flow between Heaven and Earth,
Encountering obstacles that remain unresolved,
Or erupting chaotically without control—
Thus the hexagrams mirror these phenomena.

They accumulate and connect sequentially,
Hence the **twenty-one Origins** share similarities.

Scholars who dismiss the **four-seasonal hexagrams**
And claim *"Lǐ corresponds to separation, Zhèn to doubt, Duì to stagnation, Kǎn to diligence"*
Fail to grasp the **calendar’s true function**.

**Preface to *Xuāncōng* (The Mysterious Collision)**

The main text has already been presented earlier; the preface is omitted to avoid repetition.

One day, one **daytime**, one **nighttime**—
Uniting the **Dao of day and night** in understanding,
This is the **divine clarity of the Sun**,
Hence its symbols are modeled after it.

By day, the **yang** promotes the **Five Blessings** in ascending order,
By night, the **yin** descends through the **Six Extremes**.

Ascension and descent are interdependent,
Thus the **Origins** are established to measure **transformations**.

One and Six form the **North**,
Two and Seven form the **South**—this is the **vertical axis**.

Three and Eight form the **East**,
Four and Nine form the **West**—this is the **horizontal axis**.

The **Central Five** mark the **Pole**,
Also serving as the **vertical axis**.

The **Six *Jiǎ* (Stems)** strike between them,
While the **Sun** moves inversely,
Meeting the **Big Dipper**—
For the Dipper moves in order.

The Dipper aligns with the **month** to establish the **four seasons**,
And the **Sun** meets it once,
Completing **366 days** in the year.

When **years, months, days, and times** remain unaltered,
All grains flourish—
Thus the **Mysterious Collision** governs the world,
Illuminating the **bright**, nurturing the **talented people**,
And bringing **peace and prosperity** to households.

**The Mysterious Collision (Text)**
The **divination text** is scattered beneath the various **praises** and is omitted here to avoid repetition.

Xu Hàn’s annotations are incomplete.
**The Mysterious Collision**.»

The **Central** marks the **beginning of Yang**,
The **responding** gives rise to **Yin**.

**Zhou** returns to **virtue**;
**Ying** opposes **punishment**.

**Steep sorrow** is **great**,
**Encountering** is **small desire**.

**Loneliness** is solitary, yet the **hearth** is neighborly.

**Yang** is **virtue**,
**Yin** is **punishment**.

**Virtue** is first **arduous**, then **easy**,
**Punishment** is first **beneficial**, then **obstructed**.

Great sorrow and solitude—
**Uprightness** has not yet prevailed.

Small desire and neighborliness—
**Benefit** is just emerging.

**Minority** is **subtle**;
**Majority** is **abundant**.

**Steadfastness** is **internal return**;
**Expansiveness** is **external deviation**.

Internal sorrow and external solitude,
Yet failing to **subtle** it—then **harm** occurs.

To **subtle** it is to **return** and **touch**—
This is the **Way** of Heaven.

Encountering the **hearth** and becoming **abundant**,
**Expanding** yet **increasing emptiness**—then **loneliness**.

Uprightness cannot prevail,
Yet **many causes** give rise to it.

Families without the **‘also’** character—only the **Song** edition has it.

**Above**, **touching** the **plain**;
**Text**, **many causes**.

**Many causes** must be **adorned** and **curved** to completion—
Thus it is **text**.

When **plain** is **tied** and **straightened**, **substance** prevails.

**Dryness** is **madness**;
**Rite** is **rectitude**.

**Hunting** brings **approach**,
**Fleeing** brings **loss**.

To **dry** without **yielding**—
The **madness** of **seizing**—not the **rectitude** of **rite**.

Yet the **Noble One** sometimes **dries**,
Only to **save the world**.

As **Mencius** says:
*"To flatter the great, they scorn you—*
This is the speech of the **mad**."

**Approach** should be **gradual**,
**Loss** should be **swift**.

**Above**, **dryness** generates **madness**,
**Ying**, **excess** generates **curvature**.

The **Records** say:
*"Rite is shaped by the land."*

From **response** to **rite**, it is fully realized in **royal governance**.

Yet if **uprightness** cannot prevail, one **flees**—
This is the **joy of retreat**.

**Rite** and **text** arise from **external deviation**,
Then **strengthen the world**.

Families without the **‘also’** character—**Song** has it.

**Excess** is **private curvature**;
**Tang** is **public** without **desire**.

**Difference** is **error**;
Yet **constant grain**.

From **excess**, private curvature—
**Movement** errs and **oversteps**.

From **Tang**, public without desire—
**Guarding constancy** and **abundance**,
Thus the **cultivator of heart** takes warning.

**Tang** is the **great heart**.
**Constancy** is the **Way** of **ten thousand ages** of **lord and subject**.

The **preface** of **Ru Lǐ Gōng** on the **lord and subject’s Way** says:
*"From this beginning, the year shall have [its harvest]."*

The **Noble One** has **abundance**,
**Transmitting** to descendants.

Speaking of **constant abundance** in **responding** to things by **kind**,
And the year having its **autumn**.

**Rite** withdraws the self to **publicize** the world,
Until the **phases of fleeing** and **Tang** have passed,
Yet the **rectitude** of **lord and subject** does not change.

Then **rite** is seen—
The **strengthening** of the world’s **function**.

This is called **retreat as progress**,
**Publicizing private** to fulfill its **private**.

**Youthful** and **few**—
**Preeminent** yet **without lack**.

**Few** is **excessive**;
**Without lack** is **moderation**.

**Youthful**—**benefit** returns in **excess**.
**Measure**—**skill** in **holding abundance**.

**Increase** begins to **flourish**,
And **endures** to **lengthen**.

**Sharp** holds **oneness**,
And **Kun** achieves **great harmony**.

**Reaching** daily **benefits** its **kind**;
**Reduction** daily **diminishes** its **gathering**.

**Exchange** is **following**;
**Lip** is **not communicating**.

**Lip**—**awe**;
**Guarding**—**unassailable**.

**Song** has the **‘also’** character,
And **‘guarding above’** also has it—families lack it.

**Rui** emerges,
**Xī** retreats.

**Following** is **dispersing**;
**Plummeting** is **gathering**.

Families have the **‘also’** character—**Ding** lacks it.

**Advancing** is **many schemes**;
**Accumulating** is **many riches**.

**Madness** and **simplicity** support **Ying**,
**Excess** and **brightness** err and **miss**—
Returning to their **true nature**, the **youthful** bring **auspice**.

**Youthful** with **few**—
**Excessive** yet **rarely erring**.

From **youthful** to **increase**,
**Pure virtue** flourishes.

Holding **oneness** and **reaching** it,
Exchanging with **kind**—then **movement** arises.

Thus **awe** must be present,
And **excess** expelled,
**Error** warned against,
While **Ying** and **Rui** preserve **virtue**.

The **Commentary** says:
*"To have and act upon it—*
Then **ease** takes it—
**Hao Tian** should not take from here."

**Following**—its people **divide** things likewise,
**Unfolding** thoughts to **dispel** moral **patterns**.

Advancing with **many schemes**,
Yet none dare **set** within **oneness**—
**Extending** it to **reach** its **ultimate** is all.

The **Great Learning’s** governance,
By which the world is **pacified** and **joyous**—
This is its **essence**.

**Losing rectitude** and **fleeing**,
**Forgetting worry** and **Tang**—
Yet **constancy** and **measure** are not **replaced**,
The **function** of **rite**.

**Measure** holds **abundance**,
Until **lengthening** and **great harmony**,
Then **changing** to **reduction**,
**Lip** with all things,
**Returning** and **guarding** itself.

**Guarding** yet **not firm**,
Then **entering** to **accumulate**—
The **common people’s** affairs.

The **decadent Zhou** poetry says:
*"Like a merchant’s threefold gain—*
The **Noble One** recognizes this,
**Adapting** to the times."

When **greed for profit** prevails,
And **virtue and righteousness** decline,
Then what the world **relies on** to act
Is only **adornment**—
Not **reaching** **harmony**,
Thus **truth** shines **bright** across the world.

Hence **emptiness** and **deception** confuse the people,
**Right** and **wrong** are in **chaos**,
Causing the **outer** to be **dazzled** by **decay and separation**,
While the **inner** loses its **calm stillness**—
The world **tilts**.

This is the **age** of the **Noble One’s** **deep concealment**.

The world’s **birth**—
**One** of **order**, **one** of **chaos**—
Its **Way** is thus.

**Release** is **extension**;
**Adornment** is **decay**.

Things **approach** to **shed burdens**,
**Pushed** to **release**;
**Substance** decays, yet **adornment** is **achieved**.

**Extension** arises from the **inner**,
**Adornment** is **externally** set.

Human **truth**—
**Accumulated force** over time **hangs**, **resolves**, and **shines forth**,
Coming to its time **unbeknownst**.

**Gathering** is **decayed accumulation**,
Thus the **aged** warn against **obtaining**.

**Format**—**liking** is **affirmation**;
Yet **doubt**—**disliking** is **negation**.

**Liking** is already **settled**,
**Doubt** is **negation** yet **persuaded**—
**Liking** and **disliking** are **fixed**,
Called **formatting things** to **take** this.

**Level** and **flat**—**viewing** the **tilted**.

**Level**—each **finds its own flatness** and **ease**.
**Viewing**—**mutual tilting**.

The **Spring and Autumn Commentary** says:
*"Yet there is **observation**."*

**Holding level** and **cautious of tilting**,
Can **cultivate virtue**,
Can **nurture life**.

**Joy**—**uplifting**;
**Submergence**—**hidden storage**.

**Contention**—**scholar’s equality**;
**Inner**—**woman’s womb**.

Things **halt** at their **level**,
Then **joy** in their **emergence** and **contend** in their **guard**.

**Scholar’s equality**—**public’s ultimate**.

「**Woman’s womb**—the **ultimate of private affection**.

**Public triumph**,
Then all things **reveal themselves mutually**.
**Private triumph**,
Then all things **withdraw from each other**.

**Diligence** brings **joy**—
Scholars translate **joy** as *yí* (喜), Xu (许) and Huang (黄) as *xǐ* (憙).
**Departure** brings **sorrow**.
Song has no *shì* (事) character—
*Shàng* (尚) is **pursuit**,
*Huì* (晦) is **restraint**.

**Change**—**mutual transformation and laughter**;
**Obscurity**—**prolonged worry**;
**Disruption**—**excessive matters**;
**Exhaustion**—**no joy**.

From **joy** and **contention** arise **diligence** and **pursuit**,
From **change** and **harmonization** comes **rectification** for the **world**,
Only **level and tilting** can be **mastered**—
The **great person** alone can **bear this**.

I **judge decisively**,
Then the **multitude listens**,
And **matters converge**.

**Harmony and ease** flow through things,
Yet **exhaustion** is **unattainable**—
This is **joy**.

**Joy** must have **diligence**,
**Diligence** must have **remorse**,
**Pursuit** must have **change**,
When **change** occurs, **judgment** and **rectification** prevail.

**Resolute and daring**—
**To temper softness** with **severity**.

**Displacement**—**relocating settlements**;
**Restraint**—**no movement**.

**Multitude**—**warmth and gentleness**;
**Firmness**—**cold and rigidity**.

**Resolute** in **great affairs**,
**Displacement** seeks **departure**—
Like **Zhou’s encampment** at **Luoyi**—this is **rectification**.

When the **four directions’ folk** gather in **great harmony**,
It is the **symbol of warmth and gentleness**.

**Flourishing and decline** reach their **extremes**,
A **necessary destiny**.

Thus, **above**—**resolute and daring**—**actions are taken**,
**Below**—**the multitude follows with ease and harmony**,
While **displacement** lies within.

**Governance** should **peak at King Cheng’s grandeur**,
Yet **plan for King Ping’s declining era**,
**Displacement** awaits the **opportune moment**—
This is why **Duke Zhou** alone **perceived the subtle threads**.

**Softness restrained**—**no movement**,
**Firmness upheld**—**to stabilize the tilt**.

**Secrecy**—**unquestionable**;
**Completion**—**irrevocable**.

**Daring with resolve**—**displacement**,
While **warmth and gentleness** align with **secrecy**,
Ascending by **kindred**.

**Softness tempered by severity**—**restraint**,
**Cold rigidity preserving its completion**,
Descending by **examination**.

**Secrecy** is **uninterrupted**—**human nature**;
**Completion** is **irrevocable**—**heavenly mandate**.

**Human nature**—
**Resolute yet unharmful**,
**Naturally inclined to kindness**.

**Heavenly mandate**—
**Severity tempered yet unbroken**,
**Capable of nurturing life**.

**Kindness**—**kindness in virtue**;
**Severity**—**severity in grace**.

**Restraining** gains **fortune**,
**Losing** brings **misfortune**.

**Strength**—**virtue without weariness**;
**Excess**—**evil without cessation**.

**Vigilance**—**the **Sovereign’s Way**;
**Submission**—**the **Minister’s safeguard**.

**Flourishing** yet **approaching decline**,
**Kindness** must come **before restraint**,
**Restraint** must come **before strength**.

Then the **Sovereign’s Way** is **rectified**,
**Peaking in an era of prosperity**,
**Establishing law for all under heaven**.

Having **received completion**#3,
**Losing excess** yet **returning to submission**,
**Preserving what is gained**.

Then **yin** transforms with **aging change**,
**Yang** comes with **non-conformity**,
**Struggle** and **labor**—
The **subtle struggle** of **aging**.

Scholars translate **struggle** as *yǎn* (也),
Song as *shì* (事).

**Residence**—**attaining one’s station**;
**Hardship**—**encountering obstruction**.

**Attaining station**,
Then **no hardship** can **prevail**.

**Trust**—
One who **conforms to the Way** achieves **trust**,
**Hardship**—**awaiting the time** alone.

**Law**—**easily shared with all under heaven**;
**Labor**—**toil without reward**.

From **centrality to levelness**,
The **great person’s rectification**.

From **joy to law**,
The **sage’s governance**.

**Law** is **shared with all under heaven** alone,
Thus **easy**.
Yet if it were **not easy**,
Who could **harmonize with all under heaven**?

**Labor** follows **heaven’s Way**,
**Exertion** without **toil**—**reward** without **suffering**.

**The Way** not in its **proper time**,
**Toil without reward**—
Then **only cultivation** remains.

**Cultivation** receives **abundant surplus**,
The **noble cultivates auspiciousness**,
The **petty cultivates malevolence**.

Eighty-one chapters,
Seven hundred twenty-nine **cultivations**.
**Matters** of **kindred**—
**Celebration** or **calamity**—
This is **abundant surplus**,
And **cultivation** receives it as **seed**,
**Auspiciousness** and **malevolence** flourish.

**Mystic interweaving**—
**Centrality** begins **cyclical motion**,
**Excess** curves **resolute uprightness**.

**Fragmented** and **ornate** **ways**—
Sometimes **pure**, sometimes **variegated**.

**Centrality** and **cyclical motion**—**Dao/Way and virtue**,
**Excess** and **resolute uprightness**—**timely affairs**.

**Centrality** generates **Dao/Way**,
**Cyclical motion** returns **virtue**.

**Excess**—**confused by cyclical motion**,
**Resolute uprightness**—**guarding centrality’s exterior**.

**Uprightness** produces **vigilance**,
**Curvature** produces **ornateness**.

**Strength**—**vigorous**,
**Weakness**—**frail**.

Sometimes *ruǎn* (傒) has *shì* (事) above it.

**Accumulation**—**many**,
**Scarcity**—**few**.

**Vision**—**to see**,
**Obscurity**—**to be blind**.

**Youth**—**without knowledge**,
**Flourishing**—**with surplus**#4.

**Departure** leaves the **old**,
Yet **the new** is **approaching**.

**Youth’s ignorance**,
Like **vision** amid **blindness**,
Though sometimes **obscured**,
**Vision** remains within.

To **make clear and bright**,
Simply **unfurl it**.

**Flourishing with surplus**,
Reverses to **scarcity**.

**Frailty with weakness**,
**Scarcity without accumulation**—
This is the **mask of scarcity**,
Yet **not yet departed from unity**.

**Departure** leaves the **old** while **the new** approaches—
**Learning’s support**.

**Great**—**external**,
**Contracted**—**internal**.

**Advance**—**progress**,
**Retreat**—**withdrawal**.

**Joy**—**idleness and ease**#5;
Ancient *dàng* (荡) as *tì* (逖).

**Labor**—**stumbling and struggling**.

**Perception**—**thoughts harmonized**;
**Exhaustion**—**thoughts strained**.

Song translates **Perception** as *sī* (思) **harmonized**,
**Exhaustion**—*sī* (思) **strained**.

**Morning qi** is **sharp** and **active**,
**Evening qi** **returns to ease** and **internal repose**.

Scholars translate **qi** as *xí* (席),
Lin as *xì* (夕).

**Self-loathing**—**difference**;
**Self-adornment**—**ornament**.

**Difference**—**internal discord** is **evil**,
**Ornament**—**external display** is **beauty**.

**Rectification** does not **tolerate**,
Yet **Kun** is **broad and yielding**.

**Increasing days** **benefit**,
**Decreasing days** **harm**.

**Submission**—**obeying orders**,
**Rebellion**—**contradicting**.

**Relaxation**—**gentleness**,
Yet **rigidity**—**unyielding**.

**Rigidity** like **leather soles**,
**Loses gentleness and harmony**.

**Sound** ascends—
**Leather soles** belong to **ascension**.

Huang translates as *yǐn* ().

**Levelness and ease**—**simple**,
Yet **hardship**—**obstructed and stubborn**.

**Cutting through doubts** yet lingering in uncertainty—

**Stop** not **sharp**—**clarity** is lost.

Thus the **virtuous** in **advancing** find **ease**—
The **Dao** stretches far;
In **stopping**, they are **stern**—
**Virtue** stands **victorious**.

**Vastness**—**boundless**,
**Endeavor**—**unconflicted**.

Only what **now** suits;

**Ancient** ways suffice.

**Endure**—**seeing** what comes **after**.

**Follow**—**dragged along**,
**Hold**—**steadfast**.

**Struggle**—**extracting** from **difficulty**,
**Severity**—**no** **mercy**.

When **subtle yang** first stirs,
Even in **deepest yin**, it **extracts** from **difficulty**.
When **pure yin** **severs** **yang**,
Its **end** is **unforgiving**.

**Tang**—**vast and open**—
Yet **secluded** and **obstructed**.

**Renew**—**crafting** anew;
**Constant**—**relying** on the **old**.

**Loss**—**great** **ruin**;
**Retreat**—**small** **gain**.

**Law**—**hates** **harshness**.

**Ritual**—**comprehensive**,
Yet **dwelling**—**confined**.

**Gather**—**empty** **endeavors**;
**Multiply**—**solid** **achievements**.

**Gather** by **yin’s** **withdrawal**—
Thus its **endeavors** are **empty**;
**Multiply** by **yang’s** **spread**—
Thus its **achievements** are **solid**.

**Empty**—**easily** **consumed**,
**Solid**—**hard** to **overcome**.

The **Ode** of **King Wu** proclaims:
*"Truly, you are the **just** and **faithful** teacher."*
This is **solid** **multitude**.

Receiving **armies** from **Muye’s** **camp**,
**Deer Terrace’s** **wealth**,
**Jiuqiao’s** **grain**—
These are **empty** **gatherings**.

**All** **unite** the **two**,
Yet **secrecy** **fulfills** the **one**.
**Two**—**human** **affiliation**;
**One**—**Heaven’s** **pure** **essence**.

Ten thousand things **take form**,
Their **secrecy** **unbroken**—
All **manifest** therein.

**High**—**aspiring** **mind**,
**Low**—**humble** **mind**.

**Sociable**—**many** **friends**,
**Reserved**—**few** **associates**.

**Sharp**—**slanderous**,
**Blind**—**hesitant**.

*Slander*—*Song* reads *zàn* (谗).
*Hesitant*—*Zǔ* (剪) *gǎn* (跙).
*Gǎn*—*Cǎi* (才) *yǔ* (与) **cut**.

Ancient scholars say:
*Slanderous* and *hesitant*—
**Movement** **stagnates**.

With **blindness** **foresight**,
Thus its **steps** are so.

**Near**—**clinging** to **distant**,
**Sever**—**drawing** **blood**.

**Encounter**—**meeting** **difficulty**,
**Prepare**—**waiting** for **opportunity**.

**Exert**—**self** **exhausted**,
**Nurture**—**self** **sustained**.

**Completion**—**labor** **finished**,
**Unchangeable**.

*Fan Wang*’s *Xuan* [*Error*] ends with:
**Confrontation**—**contrary**,
Yet **Kun**—**harmonious**.
**Increase**—**benefit**,
**Decrease**—**loss**.

**Completion**—**labor** **finished**,
**Unchangeable**.

*Song Zhong* supplements these.
Yet some say *Lu Ji* originally had **Completion** first,
Now cross-referencing the *Secret Treasury*’s *Lu* text—
**None** exist.
Recently, *Song Weiguan* obtained an ancient text,
Also missing these five stanzas.
Yet today’s text again has:
**Confrontation**—**unendurable**,
Yet **Kun**—**broad and yielding**.
**Increasing days** **benefit**,
**Decreasing days** **harm**—
**Unknown** whence derived.

Thus alone deleting *Song Zhong*’s four supplements,
The rest **doubtful**—
**Dare not** remove,
Awaiting scholars’ **investigation**.

**Mysterious**—*zhān* (摛), **expanding**—
**Mysterious**—**penetrating** ten thousand forms **unseen**.
**Nurtured** by **emptiness** yet **born** of **principles**—
**Divine** and **settled**.
